69. Six hours after undergoing uncomplicated carotid endarterectomy, a 77-year-old man with hypertension reports left shoulder pain and difficulty breathing. He was admitted to the hospital 1 year ago for congestive heart failure. His temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 90/min and irregular, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 100/70 mm Hg. Pulse oximet [Y on 2 Umin of oxygen by nasal cannula shows an oxygen saturation of 92 percent. The surgical site is clean, dry, and intact. Crackles are heard at the left lung base. Heart sounds are normal. An ECG is shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Acute myocardial infarction</br>b. Congestive heart failure</br>c. Dissecting aortic aneurysm</br>d. Pulmonary embolism</br>e. Spontaneous pneumothorax</br></br>7O. One day after undergoing elective left total hip replacement, a hospitalized 75-year-old woman is somnolent and has slow, shallow breathing. She has had severe postoperative pain and has been receiving intravenous morphine. She has hypertension and gastroesophageal reflux disease. Her other medications are amlodipine, omeprazole, and enoxaparin. Temperature is 37.3 C 99.1  F, pulse is 64/min, respirations are 8/min, and blood pressure is 110/62 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 88 percent. Examination shows a clean, dry surgical incision without drainage or erythema. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. There is no edema of the lower extremities. Neurologic examination shows no focal findings. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?</br>a. Acetylcysteine therapy</br>b. Administration of flumazenil</br>c. Administration of naloxone</br>d. Noninvasive ventilation</br>e. Piperacillin-tazobactam therapy</br></br></br><hr /></br><p>Question 69</br>“Six hours after carotid endarterectomy a 77-year-old man has left shoulder pain and dyspnea … ECG shown”</p></br><p>Correct Answer: A. Acute myocardial infarction (AMI)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Timing: 6 h after major vascular surgery = classic window for peri-operative MI.<br /></br>• Symptoms: Elderly patients often present with dyspnea, vague epigastric/shoulder discomfort instead of crushing chest pain.<br /></br>• Physical exam: Basilar crackles → new left-sided pulmonary edema from LV dysfunction. No fever / wound problems.<br /></br>• Vitals: Mild hypotension, atrial fibrillation (irregular pulse) – common with atrial ischemia or catecholamine surge after MI.<br /></br>• ECG: (image not provided) NBME usually shows ST-segment elevation or new Q-waves fulfilling universal MI criteria.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Post-op patients are hyper-adrenergic &amp; tachycardic → ↑ myocardial O₂ demand.<br /></br>2. Carotid endarterectomy patients have diffuse atherosclerosis → unstable coronary plaque.<br /></br>3. Sudden LV dysfunction → pulmonary congestion (crackles) → dyspnea.<br /></br>Pattern: “Older, early post-op, shoulder/epigastric pain + dyspnea + arrhythmia = silent MI.”</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Type 1 (plaque rupture) or Type 2 (supply–demand mismatch) infarction.<br /></br>• Ischemic myocardium releases troponin within 3-4 h, peaks ~24 h.<br /></br>• Complications: arrhythmia, cardiogenic shock, papillary-muscle rupture, pulmonary edema.</p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Obtain STAT 12-lead ECG &amp; serial troponins.<br /></br>2. MONA-B (morphine cautiously, O₂, nitroglycerin, aspirin) + β-blocker, anticoagulation.<br /></br>3. Urgent PCI if ST-elevation.<br /></br>4. Optimize hemodynamics (IV fluids/pressors, afterload reduction).</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“MISSED” for silent post-op MI:<br /></br>• Male elderly<br /></br>• Incision within 24 h<br /></br>• Shoulder discomfort<br /></br>• SOB<br /></br>• ECG changes<br /></br>• Demand/supply mismatch</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Congestive heart failure – Would expect bilateral crackles, peripheral edema, history of volume overload; CHF is a consequence, not a diagnosis for the acute event.<br /></br>C. Dissecting aortic aneurysm – Presents with tearing chest/back pain, asymmetric pulses, mediastinal widening; not likely 6 h after neck surgery.<br /></br>D. Pulmonary embolism – Acute dyspnea &amp; pleuritic pain, tachycardia, clear lungs or focal wheeze; impossible so soon after surgery and without DVT source.<br /></br>E. Spontaneous pneumothorax – Sudden dyspnea + unilateral absent breath sounds &amp; hyperresonance; chest radiograph, not crackles.</p></br><p>Take-home: Any acute dyspnea + vague upper-body pain in the first 48 h after major surgery is an MI until proven otherwise.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Question 70</br>“One day after elective hip replacement a 75-year-old woman is somnolent, RR 8/min, SpO₂ 88%; receiving IV morphine”</p></br><p>Correct Answer: C. Administration of naloxone</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Time course: POD 1 with escalating IV morphine for severe pain.<br /></br>• Respiratory rate 8/min + low O₂ saturation = classic opioid-induced respiratory depression.<br /></br>• No focal neuro deficits; lungs clear; incision fine → rules out PE, pneumonia, stroke.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Opioids ↓ medullary respiratory center sensitivity to CO₂ → hypoventilation → hypercapnia → somnolence.<br /></br>2. FIRST step = reverse the drug effect.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• µ-receptor agonism → analgesia, miosis, constipation, respiratory depression.<br /></br>• Naloxone = competitive µ-receptor antagonist, rapid IV onset (1–2 min), short half-life → may require repeated dosing or continuous infusion.</p></br><p>Management Algorithm<br /></br>1. Airway support (jaw thrust, O₂).<br /></br>2. Naloxone 0.04–0.4 mg IV, repeat until adequate ventilation.<br /></br>3. Reassess pain control (consider PCA, lower total opioid, add non-opioid analgesics).<br /></br>4. Monitor for withdrawal or re-sedation (naloxone shorter t½ than morphine).</p></br><p>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“Low-rone for low respirations”: Naloxone fixes low RR from narcotics.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Acetylcysteine – Treats acetaminophen/hepatic toxicity; no correlation here.<br /></br>B. Flumazenil – Benzodiazepine antagonist; patient is on opioids, not benzos.<br /></br>D. Non-invasive ventilation – Temporizing measure; underlying cause still present. Use only if antidote unavailable or ineffective.<br /></br>E. Piperacillin-tazobactam – Broad-spectrum antibiotic; no infectious signs (afebrile, clear lungs, clean wound).</p></br><p>Key takeaway: In any post-op patient with bradypnea and somnolence on opioids, give naloxone immediately.</p>
71. A 34-year-old woman, gravida 3, para 2, at 36 weeks' gestation comes to the office for her first prenatal visit. She has recei ed no prenatal care, because she did not have health insurance until recently. After her last pregnancy 2 years ago, she was diagnosed with hypertension, which has been well controlled with lisinopril since that time. She takes no other medications and has no known allergies. Her pulse is 88/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 130/80 mm Hg. Fundal height is 33 cm. Fetal movement is noted. This patient is at greatest risk for which of the following pregnancy complications?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Abruptio placentae</br>b. Congenital heart block</br>c. Fetal macrosomia</br>d. Neural tube defect</br>e. Oligohydramnios</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Oligohydramnios  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Hypertension … well-controlled with lisinopril” → ACE inhibitor exposure during pregnancy.<br /></br>• “36 weeks … first prenatal visit” → prolonged fetal exposure (especially 2nd / 3rd trimester).<br /></br>• Normal maternal vitals and fundal height slightly lagging (33 cm at 36 wk), often seen with severe oligohydramnios/IUGR in ACE-fetopathy.<br /></br>Key diagnostic clue: ACE-I use is the step-1 piece of information that trumps all other data in the stem.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Identify teratogen (ACE inhibitor).<br /></br>2. Recall classic ACE-I fetal effects: inhibition of fetal RAAS → decreased renal perfusion → renal tubular dysgenesis → anuria → severe oligohydramnios.<br /></br>3. Match complication list → oligohydramnios is the direct downstream consequence; other choices do not relate to ACE-I exposure.<br /></br>Take-home: “ACE-I/ARBs after 1st trimester → fetal renal failure → oligohydramnios ± Potter sequence (pulmonary hypoplasia, limb contractures, facial anomalies).”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• ACE-I/ARB block angiotensin II production or action.<br /></br>• Fetal kidneys rely on RAAS for normal nephrogenesis &amp; GFR.<br /></br>• ↓Ang II → renal tubular dysgenesis → ↓fetal urine output (principal source of amniotic fluid after 1st trimester).<br /></br>• Result: oligohydramnios → cord compression, pulmonary hypoplasia, limb contractures, growth restriction, neonatal renal failure, neonatal death.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Considerations<br /></br>• Immediate: STOP lisinopril; switch to pregnancy-safe anti-HTN (labetalol, nifedipine, hydralazine, methyldopa).<br /></br>• Obstetric management: serial growth scans &amp; amniotic fluid index; assess lungs &amp; kidneys; plan delivery at term or earlier if non-reassuring.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>ACE-I = “ACE”<br /></br>A  → Anhydramnios (oligohydramnios)<br /></br>C  → Contractures (limb)<br /></br>E  → End-stage renal failure (fetal)  </p></br><p>or<br /></br>“PRIL drills a hole in the KID’s kidneys → no pee → no sea (amniotic ‘sea’).”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Abruptio placentae – Associated with maternal hypertension, trauma, smoking, cocaine, not with ACE-I teratogenicity. Would present with painful vaginal bleeding &amp; tender uterus, not highlighted here.<br /></br>b. Congenital heart block – Classic for maternal anti-Ro/SSA or anti-La antibodies (SLE/Sjögren). No autoimmune history given.<br /></br>c. Fetal macrosomia – Seen in uncontrolled maternal diabetes, obesity, excessive weight gain. Fundal height lagging, not excessive; no diabetes mentioned.<br /></br>d. Neural tube defect – Risk factors: folate deficiency, valproic acid, carbamazepine, hyperglycemia. ACE-I do not influence neural tube closure. Ultrasound dating would often show elevated AFP early, not provided here.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>ACE inhibitors/ARBs are contraindicated after the first trimester because they shut down fetal RAAS, leading to anuria and severe oligohydramnios— the most common and dangerous pregnancy complication in this setting.</p>
72. A 72-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 6-week history of intermittent rectal bleeding with bowel movements. She underwent a hysterectomy at the age of 42 years for leiomyomata uteri. She has hypertension treated with amlodipine. Rectal examination shows several small external hemorrhoids. Her hematocrit is 31  percent. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Reassurance and follow-up examination in 6 months</br>b. Technetium 99m scan</br>c. Colonoscopy</br>d. Angiography</br>e. Hemorrhoidectomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Colonoscopy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age 72 years – average-risk colorectal cancer (CRC) screening normally stops at 75, but any new lower-GI bleeding in an elderly patient is CRC until proven otherwise.<br /></br>• 6 weeks of intermittent rectal bleeding + iron-deficiency anemia (Hct 31%) – red flag signs; blood “with bowel movements” does NOT rule out proximal source.<br /></br>• External hemorrhoids are noted, but hemorrhoids are a common distractor; they do not explain anemia in an elderly patient unless bleeding is massive and persistent, which is rare.<br /></br>• No prior colonoscopy is mentioned. Even if she had one, any new hematochezia mandates evaluation.  </p></br><p>Together, these clues demand full colonic evaluation, not reassurance or hemorrhoidectomy.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Establish seriousness: 72-year-old + blood per rectum + anemia → high pre-test probability of colorectal malignancy, angiodysplasia, etc.<br /></br>2. Local anorectal source identified? External hemorrhoids present, but small and typically painless with minimal bleeding; do not cause anemia.<br /></br>3. Appropriate diagnostic test: The single study that can both localize and treat most lesions is colonoscopy.<br /></br>4. Next step: Schedule colonoscopy promptly.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In any patient &gt;50 years with unexplained hematochezia or iron-deficiency anemia, hemorrhoids are NOT an adequate explanation until colonoscopy has ruled out a proximal lesion.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Colorectal cancer arises through adenoma-carcinoma sequence; risk rises sharply after age 50.<br /></br>• Other potential causes of occult/visible lower GI bleeding in the elderly include angiodysplasia, diverticulosis, ischemic colitis—but all are best visualized initially with colonoscopy.<br /></br>• Hemorrhoids are dilated venous plexi distal to the dentate line; bleeding is typically bright red, small-volume, and seldom lowers hematocrit.</p></br><p>Diagnostic/Treatment Pearls<br /></br>First-line test = colonoscopy (diagnostic ± therapeutic polypectomy, cauterization).<br /></br>If colonoscopy nondiagnostic and bleeding persists → consider tagged RBC scan, CT angiography, catheter angiography, capsule endoscopy.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Bleeding + Elderly = Scope.”<br /></br>Mnemonic: B.E.S.T. – Bleeding Elderly? Scope Today!</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Reassurance &amp; 6-mo follow-up – Unsafe delay; risks missing malignancy.<br /></br>b. Technetium-99m (tagged RBC) scan – Used for brisk bleeding when colonoscopy can’t localize; not first step.<br /></br>d. Angiography – Invasive; reserved for hemodynamically significant, ongoing bleeding after colonoscopy/RBC scan.<br /></br>e. Hemorrhoidectomy – Only if hemorrhoids are symptomatic and other sources excluded; would not address possible proximal pathology nor explain anemia.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Attributing any rectal bleeding to external hemorrhoids without excluding proximal disease—especially dangerous in patients &gt;50 years or with iron-deficiency anemia.</p>
73. A 42-year-old man is brought to the emer ency department because of a 6-hour history of moderate diffuse headache, blurred vision, difficulty concentrating moderate back pain, and tingling of his hands and feet. He made three dives during 4 hours to depths of 90 feet. His symptoms began while he returned to the dock after 4 hours in the water. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. He has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 20 years and drinks four to six 12-oz beers daily. His pulse is 100/min; other vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On neurologic examination, cranial nerves are intact. Muscle strength is 5/5 in the upper extremities and 4/5 in the lower extremities. Deep tendon reflexes are 1+ in the upper extremities and 3+ in the lower extremities. Babinski sign is present bilaterally. Sensation is normal over the hands. Proprioception and sensation to pinprick, vibration, and temperature are decreased over the lower extremities. Cognition is intact. After obtaining results of a complete blood count, complete metabolic panel, and ECG, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Hyperbaric oxygen therapy</br>b. Intravenous heparin therapy</br>c. Lumbar puncture for cerebrospinal fluid analysis</br>d. MRI of the brain and spine</br>e. Plasma exchange</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Hyperbaric oxygen therapy  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key exposures: “3 dives … depths of 90 ft … symptoms began on return to the dock.” Rapid ascent after repetitive, deep dives → classic set-up for decompression illness.<br /></br>• Time course: Neurologic complaints start within hours after surfacing – typical for Type II (neurologic/spinal) decompression sickness (DCS).<br /></br>• Neuro findings: Weakness, hyperreflexia (3+), bilateral Babinski, ↓ vibration/proprioception in legs = spinal cord involvement. Headache, blurred vision, difficulty concentrating = cerebral involvement.<br /></br>• No trauma, normal vitals, normal CBC/CMP/ECG → rules out many metabolic and vascular causes.  </p></br><p>Pattern: Diver + hours-later joint/back pain or neurologic signs = DCS → urgent recompression.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Identify precipitating event (repetitive deep dives, ascent).<br /></br>2. Recognize constellation of spinal-cord and cerebral deficits = Type II DCS.<br /></br>3. Core management principle: DO NOT delay therapy for imaging or labs. Definitive treatment is rapid recompression in a hyperbaric chamber with 100% O₂.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: Any neurologic symptom that appears soon after diving mandates emergent hyperbaric oxygen therapy.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• At depth, inert nitrogen dissolves in tissues.<br /></br>• During ascent, ↓ ambient pressure → nitrogen comes out of solution → bubbles in blood &amp; tissues.<br /></br>   – Pain in joints/back (“the bends”) – Type I DCS.<br /></br>   – CNS, spinal cord, vestibular symptoms – Type II DCS.<br /></br>• Bubbles occlude small vessels, trigger endothelial damage, and physically compress nerves. Oxygen under pressure:<br /></br>   – Shrinks bubbles (Boyle’s law).<br /></br>   – Drives nitrogen back into solution and accelerates washout.<br /></br>   – Improves tissue oxygenation and prevents secondary ischemia.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line (do immediately):<br /></br>• Recompress in hyperbaric chamber (e.g., U.S. Navy Table 6) + 100% O₂.<br /></br>• High-flow O₂ by non-rebreather while awaiting chamber.<br /></br>• IVF hydration, supine positioning, symptom-based supportive care.  </p></br><p>Second-line / adjuncts: none proven superior to recompression; steroids occasionally considered for spinal edema but not first-line.  </p></br><p>Diagnostics: Imaging or LP can be done AFTER the patient is stabilized if atypical presentation, but never delay recompression for them.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Diver with the BENDS needs to be RECOMPRESSED.”<br /></br>BENDS mnemonic:<br /></br>B – Bubbles<br /></br>E – Extremity/Joint pain<br /></br>N – Neurologic deficits<br /></br>D – Dizziness/visual changes<br /></br>S – Short time after Surfacing  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Intravenous heparin therapy<br /></br>   • Used for thrombotic or embolic vascular events; nitrogen bubbles, not fibrin clots, are the problem. Anticoagulation does not shrink bubbles and poses bleeding risk.  </p></br><p>c. Lumbar puncture for CSF analysis<br /></br>   • Would delay definitive care. CSF in DCS is often normal or nonspecific; LP neither diagnoses nor treats DCS and risks herniation if intracranial pressure is elevated.  </p></br><p>d. MRI of the brain and spine<br /></br>   • May show ischemic or demyelinating-appearing lesions, but normal in many cases. Imaging cannot rule in/out DCS quickly and would postpone urgent therapy.  </p></br><p>e. Plasma exchange<br /></br>   • Indicated for antibody-mediated disorders (e.g., Guillain-Barré, TTP). No role in inert gas bubble disease.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>New neurologic or musculoskeletal symptoms within hours of surfacing from a dive = decompression sickness. Do not delay—start hyperbaric oxygen therapy immediately.</p>
74. A 3-year-old girl is brought for a well-child examination. Her mother notes that she can ascend but not descend stairs with alternating feet. She is at the 10th percentile for height and 25th percentile for weight. She copies a cross but not a square. She can count three objects correctly. She is able to stand, but not hop, on one foot. She speaks in five-word sentences. During the examination, she shares a toy with her sibling. Which of the following is the most accurate assessment of her development? Fine Motor Gross Motor Language Social Development Development Development Development	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Delayed delayed normal normal</br>b. Normal delayed normal normal</br>c. Normal normal delayed delayed</br>d. Normal normal delayed normal</br>e. Normal normal normal normal</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e.  Normal  |  Normal  |  Normal  |  Normal  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Key age anchor = 3 years old.<br /></br>1. Fine-motor clues<br /></br>   • Copies a cross (expected ≈3½–4 y) but cannot copy a square (square ≈4–5 y).<br /></br>   • Therefore at or slightly ahead of expectation for 3 y; certainly not delayed.</p></br><ol></br><li></br><p>Gross-motor clues<br /></br>   • Ascends stairs with alternating feet (3 y milestone).<br /></br>   • Cannot yet descend stairs alternately and cannot hop on one foot—both are 4 y milestones.<br /></br>   • Able to stand briefly on one foot (3 y milestone). → On time.</p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Language clues<br /></br>   • Speaks in 5-word sentences (≥3-word sentences by 3 y; 4-5 word sentences common). → On time.</p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Social clues<br /></br>   • Shares toys (parallel play gives way to associative/ cooperative play around 3 y). → On time.</p></br></li></br></ol></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process<br /></br>1. Identify the child’s age.<br /></br>2. Recall the “rule of 4s” (every 4–6 months a new shape &amp; motor feat):<br /></br>   • 3 y: circle, tricycle, climb stairs alternating feet, 3-word sentences.<br /></br>   • 4 y: cross/square, hop, descend stairs alternating feet, 4-word sentences.<br /></br>3. Compare the child’s abilities to the 3-year column—she meets or exceeds every 3-year milestone and simply has not yet achieved 4-year milestones, so development is normal.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: A skill is only “delayed” if it lags behind what ≥90 % of age-matched peers can do; lacking next-year milestones is not a delay.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Development follows predictable cephalo-caudal, proximal-to-distal progression. Variability of ±3 months is normal; girls often gain fine-motor skills earlier than boys. No pathology suggested here.</p></br><p>Treatments / Diagnostics<br /></br>None indicated—reassurance and routine follow-up.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“3 Cs at 3”: Circle, Climb (stairs up), 3-word Conversation, Cooperative sharing.<br /></br>“4 Practices at 4”: Cross/Square, hop on one Foot (4 limbs off the ground!), go down stairs, 4-word phrases.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Delayed fine + gross motor: Child is already ahead (copies cross, climbs stairs) → not delayed.<br /></br>b. Normal fine but delayed gross: Same reasoning—gross motor is on time; descending stairs alternately is a 4-year skill.<br /></br>c. Language and social delayed: Five-word sentences and sharing are age-appropriate.<br /></br>d. Delayed language only: language clearly normal.<br /></br>e. Normal/Normal/Normal/Normal fits all observations, hence correct.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Labeling absence of a 4-year milestone as a delay in a 3-year-old. Always match skills to the child’s CURRENT age bracket.</p>
75. A study is conducted to assess whether a new drug used to treat type 2 diabetes mellitus is associated with an increased risk for developing cancer. In the study, a meta-analysis is performed of six small clinical trials of the</br>new drug, and the relative risk for cancer development is determined. The results of the meta-analysis are shown in the graph. Which of the following conclusions can be drawn from these data?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. The drug is associated with a decreased cancer risk, but the result is not significant</br>b. The drug is associated with an increased cancer risk, but the result is not significant</br>c. The drug is significantly associated with a decreased cancer risk</br>d. The drug is significantly associated with an increased cancer risk</br>e. The association between the drug and cancer risk cannot be determined from the data provided</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. The drug is associated with a decreased cancer risk, but the result is not significant  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key phrase: “meta-analysis of six small clinical trials … relative risk (RR) for cancer.”<br /></br>• The forest plot (not shown here) must depict a pooled RR &lt; 1.0, implying fewer cancers in the treatment arm, but the 95 % confidence interval (CI) for that pooled estimate crosses the null value of 1.0.<br /></br>• Crossing the line of no effect (RR = 1) = lack of statistical significance.<br /></br>• Therefore: direction points toward benefit (↓ cancer risk), but we cannot reject the null hypothesis.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Look at the pooled point estimate.<br /></br> • RR &lt; 1 ⇒ suggests protective effect.<br /></br>Step 2: Examine the 95 % CI.<br /></br> • CI includes 1 ⇒ P &gt; 0.05 ⇒ not statistically significant.<br /></br>Take-home: “&lt; 1 but CI crosses 1” = “trend toward decreased risk; NOT significant”.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (brief)<br /></br>No specific disease pathophysiology is being tested; the core concept is interpretation of relative risk and confidence intervals in evidence-based medicine.</p></br><p>Diagnostic / Statistical Concepts<br /></br>• Relative risk (RR): ratio of incidence in exposed vs unexposed.<br /></br>• 95 % CI that does NOT include 1 = statistically significant (α = 0.05).<br /></br>• Meta-analysis pools data to increase power and precision; still must inspect the CI.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“RR line dance”:<br /></br> • If the CI crosses 1, the line “steps” on the null → no significant difference.<br /></br>Visual mnemonic: Picture the forest plot’s diamond (pooled effect). Diamond touching the central stem (RR = 1) = “touchdown, no score”.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Increased risk but not significant – would require pooled RR &gt; 1 with CI crossing 1. (Opposite direction.)<br /></br>c. Significantly decreased risk – RR &lt; 1 AND CI entirely &lt; 1. (CI would not touch the line.)<br /></br>d. Significantly increased risk – RR &gt; 1 AND CI entirely &gt; 1.<br /></br>e. Cannot be determined – false; both magnitude and statistical significance can be interpreted from the plot.</p></br><p>Core USMLE takeaway<br /></br>Always pair the point estimate with its confidence interval:<br /></br>• Direction (RR &lt;&gt; 1) tells you the apparent effect.<br /></br>• Whether the CI includes 1 tells you statistical significance.</p>
76. A 27-year-old man is brought to the emer ency department ED by his fiancee 30 minutes after the sudden onset of shortness of breath, chest tightness, palpitations, and severe, generalized discomfort. This is the patient's third visit to the ED during the past month for episodes of similar symptoms; each time, ECGs showed only sinus tachycardia, and he was discharged after evaluation disclosed no abnormalities. Today, he says he has been excessively worried that he will have another episode. He has hyperlipidemia treated with atorvastatin. Two months ago, he began bupropion for smoking cessation. He previously drank five 12-oz beers weekly; two weeks ago, he stopped drinking beer altogether in an effort to decrease the frequency of these episodes. He smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 6 years but quit 5 weeks ago. He appears anxious and paces the room. Temperature is 37.6 C 99.7  , pulse is 110/min, respirations are 22/min, and blood pressure is 155/89 mm Hg. Physical examination discloses no other abnormalities. On mental status examination, he is alert and fully oriented. Serum thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration and results of a complete blood count and basic metabolic panel are within the reference ranges. Blood alcohol concentration is O mg/dl. Urine toxicology screening is negative. ECG findings are unchanged from previous visits. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management while this patient is in the ED?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. CT scan of the head</br>b. EEG</br>c. Intramuscular administration of olanzapine</br>d. Oral clomipramine therapy</br>e. Oral lorazepam therapy~-</br>F. Plasma metanephrine concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>E. Oral lorazepam therapy  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Sudden onset … shortness of breath, chest tightness, palpitations, severe generalized discomfort” = classic autonomic surge seen in a panic attack.<br /></br>• “Third visit … each time ECG showed only sinus tachycardia, and evaluation disclosed no abnormalities” rules out acute cardiac/structural disease.<br /></br>• “Excessively worried that he will have another episode” = anticipatory anxiety → meets DSM-5 criterion for panic disorder (recurrent unexpected panic attacks + ≥1 month worry/behavior change).<br /></br>• Normal TSH, BMP, CBC, tox screen, and no alcohol: most common medical mimics excluded.<br /></br>• Vitals: mild tachycardia, mild fever—expected adrenergic state; no focal neuro findings.  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: Young adult with paroxysmal somatic symptoms + persistent worry + clean medical work-up → Panic disorder.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Identify condition: panic attack in ED.<br /></br>2. Immediate goal: rapidly abort current attack &amp; calm patient.<br /></br>3. Best acute agent in ED: a short-acting benzodiazepine (e.g., lorazepam 1–2 mg PO/SL or alprazolam).<br /></br>4. Long-term: SSRI/SNRI, CBT; but initiation is outpatient once medically cleared.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: For acute panic attacks, give a benzodiazepine; for chronic management, give SSRI/SNRI + CBT.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• Etiology: dysregulation of locus coeruleus &amp; limbic system → sudden sympathetic discharge.<br /></br>• Risk factors: genetic predisposition, prior anxiety, stimulant use/withdrawal (e.g., caffeine, nicotine).<br /></br>• Manifestations: abrupt onset of ≥4 symptoms (palpitations, dyspnea, chest pain, dizziness, paresthesias, fear of dying/losing control) peaking within minutes.<br /></br>• Course: anticipatory anxiety leads to ED utilization and avoidance behaviors.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics Summary<br /></br>Acute (ED):<br /></br>• Benzodiazepine (lorazepam or alprazolam) – fastest onset, reliable PO absorption.<br /></br>Chronic:<br /></br>• First-line: SSRI (sertraline, paroxetine, fluoxetine), SNRI (venlafaxine).<br /></br>• Psychotherapy: cognitive-behavioral therapy with interoceptive exposure.<br /></br>• Consider tapering benzo once SSRI effective (~6 wk).  </p></br><p>Diagnostic tests not required once appropriate medical causes excluded.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“PANIC now, SSRI later”:<br /></br>P – Propranolol not first choice<br /></br>A – Acute attack → Alprazolam/Lorazepam<br /></br>N – No organic cause on work-up<br /></br>I – Interoceptive exposure (CBT) for maintenance<br /></br>C – Chronic control → sertraline/fluoxetine/paroxetine  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. CT scan of the head – For focal neuro deficits, altered consciousness, or thunderclap headache; none present. Neuro imaging would not relieve current symptoms.<br /></br>B. EEG – Utilized for seizure work-up; patient has preserved awareness between episodes, no tonic-clonic activity, no post-ictal state.<br /></br>C. Intramuscular olanzapine – Antipsychotic for acute agitation/psychosis; patient is anxious, not psychotic or dangerously agitated.<br /></br>D. Oral clomipramine – TCA reserved mainly for refractory OCD; slower onset, anticholinergic AEs, not first-line for panic disorder.<br /></br>F. Plasma metanephrine concentration – Screens for pheochromocytoma (episodic hypertension, headaches, diaphoresis). This patient’s BP only mildly elevated, no headaches/diaphoresis; three normal ED work-ups already argue against it.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Ordering extensive repeat tests (“medical clearance fatigue”) instead of treating a clearly identifiable panic attack once life-threatening etiologies have been ruled out.</p>
77. A 19-year-old woman comes to the office 2 days after a home urine pregnancy test was positive. She has a 2-week history of mild pelvic cramping and frequent urination. She has not had vaginal bleeding. She thinks her</br>last menstrual period was 7 or 8 weeks ago. Menses previously occurred at regular 28-day intervals and lasted 7 days. Examination shows a uterus consistent in size with a 12-week gestation. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Transvaginal ultrasonography</br>d. Urine pregnancy test</br>e. Wet mount preparation of vaginal discharge</br></br></br><p>I want to double-check something before I write the full explanation, because the key you provided seems inconsistent with the usual NBME answer for this stem:</p></br><p>• The vignette describes a uterus that is larger than expected for dates (LMP 7–8 wks vs. 12-wk-size uterus), which classically prompts a transvaginal ultrasound to confirm an intra-uterine pregnancy, establish gestational age, and rule out multifetal or molar pregnancy.<br /></br>• “ABO/Rh blood grouping and antibody screen” is certainly part of the routine initial prenatal laboratory panel but is not used to “diagnose” the current discrepancy.</p></br><p>Could you please confirm whether the intended correct answer is</p></br><p>c. Transvaginal ultrasonography</p></br><p>or</p></br><p>a. Blood grouping and screening ?</p></br><p>Once I know which answer choice you definitely want justified, I can provide the full high-yield explanation using the rubric you gave.</p>
78. A 68-year-old man comes to the physician because of sadness since his wife died 1 month ago. He says he feels lost without her and cries whenever he talks about her. He is reluctant to socialize with his bridge group because the other members' spouses are still alive. He still enjoys spending time with his children and grandchildren. He has difficulty falling asleep at night but sleeps his usual 6 to 7 hours without awakening. He also takes one short nap daily. He has had a decreased appetite because he misses his wife's cooking, resulting in a 2.3-kg 5-lb weight loss. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. He does not drink alcohol. He is 178 cm 5 ft 10 in tall and weighs 84 kg 185 lb; 8MI is 27 kg/m 2. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination, he has a sad mood and tearful affect. He says he longs to be with his wife again but has not had suicidal ideation. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Antidepressant therapy</br>b. 8enzodiazepine therapy</br>c. Nutritional supplementation</br>d. Psychodynamic psychotherapy</br>e. Reassurance and follow-up examination in 1 month</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Reassurance and follow-up examination in 1 month  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Trigger: “wife died 1 month ago” → recent bereavement.<br /></br>• Core emotions: sadness, crying when talking about wife, longing to be with her.<br /></br>• Preserved functioning/pleasure: still enjoys time with children &amp; grandchildren.<br /></br>• Neurovegetative symptoms: mild insomnia (difficulty falling asleep but 6–7 hrs total), small nap, modest ↓ appetite with 5-lb loss.<br /></br>• No pathologic thoughts: explicitly denies suicidal ideation, psychosis, excessive guilt, or worthlessness.<br /></br>• Duration: only 1 month.  </p></br><p>Together these clues point to NORMAL (uncomplicated) GRIEF, not major depressive disorder (MDD).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Ask: Does the patient meet DSM-5 criteria for MDD?<br /></br>  • ≥ 5 of 9 SIGECAPS? No (has only sleep &amp; appetite changes + sadness).<br /></br>  • Duration ≥ 2 weeks? Yes, but still lacks required symptom number/severity plus preserved enjoyment of family.<br /></br>  • Pathologic features (suicidal intent, hopelessness, psychosis, pervasive anhedonia)? None.  </p></br><p>Step 2 – Any features of complicated grief (&gt;12 mo) or persistent complex bereavement? No (only 1 mo).  </p></br><p>Step 3 – Management of uncomplicated grief = empathic reassurance, normalization of reaction, offer resources (support groups), and schedule follow-up to monitor for progression or pathologic features. Hence choice e.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Recent bereavement with limited, expected symptoms and intact function requires support, not pharmacotherapy; antidepressants are reserved for superimposed MDD or complicated grief.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Uncomplicated (acute) grief<br /></br>• Within first 6–12 months after a loss.<br /></br>• Waves of sorrow triggered by reminders; mixed with positive memories.<br /></br>• Mild sleep/appetite disturbance common.<br /></br>• Function generally preserved; patient can experience pleasure.<br /></br>• Reality testing intact; no psychosis.  </p></br><p>Persistent complex bereavement (&gt;12 mo) or MDD develops in ~10–20% of bereaved individuals and warrants treatment.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line for normal grief<br /></br>1. Reassurance: validate feelings (“Your reaction is common and understandable”).<br /></br>2. Grief counseling or support groups if patient desires.<br /></br>3. Follow-up: re-evaluate in 1–2 months or sooner if red-flag symptoms emerge (suicidal ideation, pervasive hopelessness, inability to function).<br /></br>No labs or imaging are necessary unless other medical concerns are present.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>• “GRIEF = Good Recall of loved one, Intermittent Emotions, Function preserved.”<br /></br>• Red-flag mnemonic for complicated grief: SAD FACES<br /></br>  S – Suicidality / self-harm<br /></br>  A – Anhedonia pervasive<br /></br>  D – Dysfunctional daily life<br /></br>  F – Feelings of worthlessness<br /></br>  A – Active psychosis<br /></br>  C – Compulsive avoidance of reminders<br /></br>  E – Extreme duration (&gt;12 mo)<br /></br>  S – Severe weight loss (&gt;10%)  </p></br><p>If ≥1 present → evaluate for MDD or complicated grief.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Antidepressant therapy (e.g., sertraline)<br /></br>   – Indicated only if criteria for MDD or persistent complex bereavement are met. Would overtreat this normal reaction and expose to side-effects.  </p></br><p>b. Benzodiazepine therapy<br /></br>   – Used for acute, severe anxiety or insomnia unresponsive to behavioral measures. Risks (dependence, falls) outweigh benefits here.  </p></br><p>c. Nutritional supplementation<br /></br>   – Not needed; weight loss is mild (5 lb) and explained by decreased appetite; appetite typically returns.  </p></br><p>d. Psychodynamic psychotherapy<br /></br>   – Explores unconscious conflicts; not first-line for uncomplicated grief. Grief-specific counseling or support groups are more appropriate if therapy desired, but reassurance alone suffices initially.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line: A 68-year-old with expected, self-limited symptoms 1 month after his wife’s death requires empathy, education, and scheduled follow-up—NOT medication.</p>
79. A 3-year-old African American boy is brought to the office because of a 3-day history of moderate pain of his chest, back, and legs. He receives no medications. He appears ill and is in moderate distress. On cardiac examination, a grade 2/6, systolic ejection murmur is heard best along the left sternal border. Examination of the lower extremities shows no abnormalities. His hemoglobin concentration is 6.6 g/dl N=11.5-14.5, and hematocrit is 22 percent N=33 percent--43 percent. A blood smear is shown. Serum hemoglobin electrophoresis is most likely to show which of the following findings in this patient?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Absence of hemoglobin A</br>b. Absence of hemoglobin F</br>c. Decreased hemoglobin C concentration</br>d. Decreased hemoglobin S concentration</br>e. Normal hemoglobin A concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Absence of hemoglobin A (HbA)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• African-American boy, 3 y o<br /></br>• Recurrent “pain crisis” (chest, back, legs) → classic vaso-occlusive episode<br /></br>• Severe normocytic anemia (Hgb 6.6 g/dL) + flow murmur<br /></br>• Blood smear (not shown to you) would typically demonstrate sickled erythrocytes ± Howell-Jolly bodies.<br /></br>KEY CLUE: Pain crisis + ethnicity + profound anemia ⇒ think sickle cell disease (HbSS).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Homozygous β-globin point mutation (Glu→Val at position 6) → HbS replaces normal β-chains.<br /></br>2. Because BOTH β-globin genes are mutated, the patient produces:<br /></br>   • HbS: ~90–95 %<br /></br>   • HbF: variably elevated (protective, declines after 6 mo of age)<br /></br>   • HbA: 0 % (ABSENT)<br /></br>3. Electrophoresis therefore shows complete absence of HbA → choice A.</p></br><p>Take-home: In classic sickle cell anemia (HbSS) there is 0 % HbA; HbA is only present in sickle cell trait (HbAS).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Essential Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Point mutation creates hydrophobic patch → deoxygenated HbS polymerizes → RBC sickling → vaso-occlusion (pain, dactylitis, acute chest, priapism) + chronic hemolysis (anemia, scleral icterus, gallstones).<br /></br>• Autosplenectomy by early childhood → Howell-Jolly bodies on smear, infections by encapsulated organisms.<br /></br>• HbF does not participate in polymerization; higher HbF = milder disease (e.g., hydroxyurea therapy).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Management (high-yield)<br /></br>1. Confirm with Hb electrophoresis (newborn screen catches most cases).<br /></br>2. Acute pain crisis: IV fluids, NSAIDs/opioids, ± transfusion.<br /></br>3. Disease-modifying: Hydroxyurea ↑ HbF; chronic transfusions; curative bone-marrow transplant.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“SickLe ceLL = <strong>S</strong> for <strong>S</strong>ubstituted β-chain, <strong>S</strong>icky HbS, <strong>S</strong>plenic auto-infarct.”<br /></br>Mnemonic for electrophoresis migration (fast → slow): <strong>A</strong> (<strong>A</strong>lways first), <strong>F</strong>a<strong>S</strong>t<strong>, </strong>C**rawl.<br /></br>Therefore: HbA &gt; HbF &gt; HbS &gt; HbC on alkaline gel. In HbSS you see only HbS (± HbF) and NO HbA.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Answers Are Wrong  </p></br><p>b. Absence of hemoglobin F<br /></br>• HbF is NOT absent; many HbSS patients keep 1–10 % HbF (and therapeutic strategies aim to raise it).</p></br><p>c. Decreased hemoglobin C concentration<br /></br>• HbC results from a different β-chain mutation (Glu→Lys). HbSC disease would show both HbS and HbC, not the clinical severity seen here. No reason for “decreased” HbC in HbSS.</p></br><p>d. Decreased hemoglobin S concentration<br /></br>• HbS is actually markedly INCREASED (&gt;90 %) in HbSS; it is only decreased in trait or after transfusion.</p></br><p>e. Normal hemoglobin A concentration<br /></br>• Normal HbA (~95 % in healthy individuals) indicates intact β-chains; not possible in homozygous sickle cell disease.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Concept<br /></br>Homozygous sickle cell anemia (HbSS) completely eliminates production of normal adult hemoglobin A—electrophoresis shows 0 % HbA and predominantly HbS (with variable HbF).</p>
80. A previously healthy 11-month-old boy is brought to the emergency department by his mother because of cough and difficulty breathing for 2 hours. His mother says that he has been irritable and has had little to eat or drink since the onset of symptoms. He is agitated and difficult to examine. He has a deep, barking cough. His temperature is 38.5 C 101.3 F, pulse is 110/min, respirations are 36/min, and blood pressure is 100/50 mm Hg. Mild inspiratory stridor is heard. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Administration of a cough syrup containing dextromethorphan</br>b. Administration of aerosolized albuterol</br>c. Administration of inhaled cool mist humidified air</br>d. Administration of subcutaneous epinephrine</br>e. Direct laryngoscopy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Administration of inhaled cool-mist humidified air  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age &amp; seasonality clue: 11-month-old in winter-type scenario → think viral URIs.<br /></br>• Barking (“seal-like”) cough + inspiratory stridor = classic buzzwords for croup (laryngotracheitis, most often parainfluenza).<br /></br>• Fever is low-grade (38.5 °C).<br /></br>• Respiratory status: Mild inspiratory stridor only; no stridor at rest, no retractions, no hypoxia, VS stable (RR 36 for age). → Categorizes this as MILD croup.<br /></br>• Because exam is otherwise benign, immediate airway instrumentation is unnecessary.</p></br><p>Key pattern: Barking cough + mild stridor + well-appearing child = mild croup → supportive therapy (cool mist ± single dose PO/IM dexamethasone).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify syndrome: barking cough + stridor = croup.<br /></br>2. Grade severity:<br /></br>   • Mild = stridor only with agitation, no respiratory distress → supportive care.<br /></br>   • Moderate-severe = stridor at rest, retractions, distress → racemic epi nebulization + steroids.<br /></br>   • Impending respiratory failure = lethargy, cyanosis → airway stabilization/intubation.<br /></br>3. Choose lowest-intensity intervention matching severity → cool-mist humidified air.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Always match croup severity with least-invasive effective treatment; humidified air is first-line for mild disease.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Parainfluenza virus infects subglottic tracheal mucosa → mucosal edema → narrowed airway → turbulent flow → stridor and “seal” cough.<br /></br>• Infants have narrow airways; minor edema markedly increases resistance (Poiseuille’s law).<br /></br>• Usually self-limited (≤72 h).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Mild croup:<br /></br>• Humidified cool air (home “steamy bathroom” or ED mist tent).<br /></br>• One dose dexamethasone (0.6 mg/kg PO/IM) shortens symptom course, but many exam questions isolate “cool mist” as NEXT step before steroids.  </p></br><p>Moderate–severe croup:<br /></br>• Nebulized racemic epinephrine (0.05 mL/kg of 2.25 % solution; max 0.5 mL) rapidly ↓ edema.<br /></br>• Observe ≥2 h for rebound.<br /></br>• Add dexamethasone.</p></br><p>Impending failure:<br /></br>• Prepare for intubation with ENT/Anesthesia help.</p></br><p>No imaging or labs are required for straightforward presentations; lateral neck X-ray (“steeple sign”) adds radiation without changing acute management.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CROUP ladder”:<br /></br>Cool-mist → Roids (dexamethasone) → racemic epinephrine → intUbation/airway protection in extremis.<br /></br>Picture a SEAL on the bottom rung barking; each rung up the ladder is escalating therapy.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Dextromethorphan cough syrup<br /></br>• Antitussives provide no airway relief, are not recommended in infants (&lt;4 y) due to risk of respiratory depression, and do not treat stridor.  </p></br><p>b. Aerosolized albuterol<br /></br>• Useful for lower-airway bronchospasm (asthma, bronchiolitis); croup is due to upper-airway edema, not smooth-muscle constriction. No benefit.</p></br><p>d. Subcutaneous epinephrine<br /></br>• Systemic epi has slower onset, more side effects. Nebulized (racemic) epi is preferred—and only for moderate-severe disease, which this child does not have.</p></br><p>e. Direct laryngoscopy<br /></br>• Invasive; indicated when epiglottitis, foreign body, or airway obstruction with impending failure is suspected. Mild croup does not warrant instrumentation (may worsen swelling).</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: confusing epiglottitis (toxic, high fever, drooling, tripod) with croup (barky cough, usually nontoxic, responds to humidity/epi).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line: Mild croup = cool-mist humidified air (± single-dose dexamethasone). Escalate only if stridor persists at rest or respiratory distress develops.</p>
81. A 32-year-old woman comes to the office 2 days after undergoing CT scan of the abdomen because of a 1-month history of recurrent urinary tract infections and intermittent left flank pain. Three days ago, she completed a</br>course of antibiotic therapy for her most recent urinary tract infection. Today, she feels well. She has no other history of serious illness and takes no medications. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination discloses no</br>abnormalities. CT scan of the abdomen shows normal urogenital anatomy and no renal calculi, but an annular pancreas is noted. Which of the following is the most likely clinical course of this CT scan finding?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Biliary obstruction</br>b. Gastric outlet obstruction</br>c. Malignant transformation</br>d. Recurrent pancreatitis</br>e. This condition will remain benign</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Recurrent pancreatitis  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Incidental finding: The patient underwent CT for an unrelated problem; the scan “shows … an annular pancreas.”<br /></br>• Age: 32 years – an adult (not the typical neonatal presentation of annular pancreas with immediate duodenal obstruction).<br /></br>• Currently asymptomatic from a GI standpoint – no abdominal pain, jaundice, nausea/vomiting.<br /></br>Key inference: We must predict what complication adults with an annular pancreas most commonly go on to experience.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach<br /></br>1. Recognise the anatomic defect: a ring of pancreatic tissue encircling the 2nd portion of the duodenum caused by failure of the ventral pancreatic bud to rotate/ fuse correctly.<br /></br>2. Know age-dependent presentations:<br /></br>   • Neonates → duodenal (gastric-outlet) obstruction with non-bilious or bilious vomiting (“double-bubble” sign).<br /></br>   • Adults → pancreatic duct stasis/obstruction is more common than frank duodenal obstruction → repeated bouts of acute or chronic pancreatitis.<br /></br>3. Compare answer choices with epidemiology: In published adult series, ≥50 % of symptomatic adults present with pancreatitis; duodenal obstruction is less frequent, biliary obstruction and malignancy are rare.<br /></br>4. Therefore the “most likely clinical course” is recurrent attacks of pancreatitis.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In adults an annular pancreas most often manifests later as recurrent acute or chronic pancreatitis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Embryology: Two ventral pancreatic buds rotate posteriorly and fuse with the dorsal bud. In annular pancreas the ventral bud splits and encircles the duodenum, forming a fixed ring of pancreatic tissue.<br /></br>• Consequences:<br /></br>  – External compression of the duodenum (gastric-outlet obstruction) – classic in neonates.<br /></br>  – Entrapment/stenosis of the pancreatic ductal system → impaired drainage → enzyme activation → recurrent inflammation (pancreatitis) – typical in adults.<br /></br>• Risk factors/associations: Down syndrome, neonatal polyhydramnios, pancreas divisum (may co-exist), peptic ulcer disease secondary to stasis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Asymptomatic → observation.<br /></br>• Recurrent pancreatitis or obstruction → surgical bypass (duodenojejunostomy or gastrojejunostomy) rather than resection of the pancreatic ring.<br /></br>• Imaging clues: CT/MRCP shows pancreatic tissue completely or partially encircling the second portion of the duodenum; “crocodile jaw” or “ring” sign on ERCP.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorisation Tips<br /></br>“Adults PAN out”<br /></br>P – Pancreatitis<br /></br>A – Annular<br /></br>N – Neonates = obstruction (duodenal)<br /></br>If it’s an Annular pancreas, remember: Neonates throw up, Adults flare up (pancreatitis).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Biliary obstruction – Rare; the annular tissue surrounds the duodenum, not the distal common bile duct. Would present with jaundice, cholangitis, elevated conjugated bilirubin – not the most common issue.<br /></br>b. Gastric outlet obstruction – Classic neonatal complication; in adults it is less common than pancreatitis (≈40–50 % vs &gt;50 % pancreatitis in symptomatic adults). Thus not the “most likely.”<br /></br>c. Malignant transformation – Pancreatic or duodenal malignancy arising in annular tissue is exceedingly uncommon; literature limited to case reports.<br /></br>e. Condition will remain benign – While many individuals are asymptomatic, the question asks for the “most likely clinical course,” i.e., the most common complication if symptoms develop. Statistically, recurrent pancreatitis tops the list; therefore choice e is not the best answer.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In adult patients an annular pancreas most often declares itself through repeated episodes of pancreatitis; counsel the patient on symptoms of pancreatitis and arrange appropriate follow-up.</p>
81. A 52-year-old woman with metastatic breast cancer comes to the emergency department because of a 24-hour history of severe left arm pain. She rates her pain as a 9 on a 10-point scale. Her only medication is hydrocodone-acetaminophen hydrocodone 5 mg/acetaminophen 500 mg, two tablets every 6 hours. Three years ago, she had upper gastrointestinal bleeding secondary to a gastric ulcer. Five years ago, she had a bilateral mastectomy. She is 160 cm 5 ft 3 in tall and weighs 44 kg 96 lb; 8MI is 17 kg m 2. Her temperature is 36.9 C 98.4  F, pulse is 126/min, respirations are ~ min, and blood pressure is 118/72 mm Hg. Examination shows temporal wasting, protruding ribs, and well-healed bilateral mastectomy scars. There is guarding of the left upper extremity. An x-ray shows a pathologic fracture of the left proximal humerus. Which of the following is</br>the most appropriate next step in pain management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Increase the dosage of hydrocodone-acetaminophen</br>b. Add oral gabapentin to the medication regimen</br>c. Add oral ketorolac to the medication regimen</br>d. Switch to intramuscular meperidine</br>e. Switch to intravenous morphine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Switch to intravenous (IV) morphine  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues: “metastatic breast cancer,” “pathologic fracture,” “pain 9/10,” current analgesic = hydrocodone 5 mg/acetaminophen 500 mg q6 h, obvious cachexia (BMI 17), tachycardia (126/min) → all point to severe, acute cancer-related pain.<br /></br>• Current regimen = a weak opioid + acetaminophen (WHO pain ladder step 2). Her pain is uncontrolled ⇒ escalate to a strong opioid (step 3).<br /></br>• Prior upper GI bleed (gastric ulcer) makes NSAIDs risky.<br /></br>• Weight (44 kg) and poor nutritional status favor parenteral over oral when rapid, reliable analgesia is needed.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (why IV morphine now)<br /></br>1. Severity: 9/10 pain with pathologic long-bone fracture is “severe.”<br /></br>2. Current therapy failure: She’s already on the maximum safe acetaminophen daily dose (4 g if compliant); simply increasing tablets risks hepatotoxicity and still may not give adequate opioid effect.<br /></br>3. WHO/CDC cancer pain ladder:<br /></br>   – Step 1: non-opioids (acetaminophen/NSAID)<br /></br>   – Step 2: weak opioid ± non-opioid (hydrocodone, codeine)<br /></br>   – Step 3: strong opioid (morphine, hydromorphone, fentanyl) ± adjuvants<br /></br>4. IV route gives fastest onset, allowing quick titration to effect; important in the emergency department. After stabilization, convert to equianalgesic oral or PCA regimen.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Metastatic breast cancer frequently seeds bone → osteolytic lesions → fractures → severe nociceptive pain from periosteal stretching/inflammation. This is not primarily neuropathic (so gabapentinoids add little acutely).  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Initial ED management: IV morphine 0.1 mg/kg (≈4 mg in this pt) q15 min PRN until pain ≤3, watching for respiratory depression.<br /></br>• Transition to oral morphine or long-acting opioid + breakthrough short-acting dose once stable.<br /></br>• Co-analgesics: bisphosphonate or denosumab for skeletal events, radiation/surgery for fracture stabilization.<br /></br>• Always prescribe bowel regimen (e.g., senna + docusate) with chronic opioids.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Cancer pain ladder: 1-2-3 = Acetaminophen/NSAID → Weak opioid → Morphine (or equi-potent). Uncontrolled? Move up, never sideways.”  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Increase hydrocodone-acetaminophen<br /></br>   – Acetaminophen already near ceiling dose; more tablets risk hepatotoxicity and still provide only a step-2 opioid. Does not follow ladder.  </p></br><p>b. Add oral gabapentin<br /></br>   – Useful for neuropathic pain (e.g., post-herpetic neuralgia, diabetic neuropathy) but not primary therapy for acute nociceptive fracture pain. Onset is slow; would not relieve 9/10 pain now.  </p></br><p>c. Add oral ketorolac<br /></br>   – Potent NSAID but contraindicated with history of GI bleeding/ulcer. Also oral ketorolac is less effective than parenteral and still inadequate for severe cancer pain alone.  </p></br><p>d. Switch to intramuscular meperidine<br /></br>   – Meperidine metabolite (normeperidine) accumulates, causing seizures; avoided except for short-term use in rigors. IM route painful and erratic absorption. Not recommended for chronic cancer pain.  </p></br><p>Take-Home Message<br /></br>Uncontrolled severe cancer pain despite step-2 opioids requires escalation to an immediately titratable strong opioid—IV morphine is the fastest, safest, guideline-supported choice in the emergency setting.</p>
82. A 52-year-old woman with metastatic breast cancer comes to the emergency department because of a 24-hour history of severe left arm pain. She rates her pain as a 9 on a 10-point scale. Her only medication is hydrocodone-acetaminophen hydrocodone 5 mg/acetaminophen 500 mg, two tablets every 6 hours. Three years ago, she had upper gastrointestinal bleeding secondary to a gastric ulcer. Five years ago, she had a bilateral mastectomy. She is 160 cm 5 ft 3 in tall and weighs 44 kg 96 lb; 8MI is 17 kg m 2. Her temperature is 36.9 C 98.4  F, pulse is 126/min, respirations are ~ min, and blood pressure is 118/72 mm Hg. Examination shows temporal wasting, protruding ribs, and well-healed bilateral mastectomy scars. There is guarding of the left upper extremity. An x-ray shows a pathologic fracture of the left proximal humerus. Which of the following is</br>the most appropriate next step in pain management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Increase the dosage of hydrocodone-acetaminophen</br>b. Add oral gabapentin to the medication regimen</br>c. Add oral ketorolac to the medication regimen</br>d. Switch to intramuscular meperidine</br>e. Switch to intravenous morphine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Switch to intravenous morphine  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Metastatic breast cancer … pathologic fracture” → cancer-related, acute, ­severe nociceptive pain.<br /></br>• “Pain 9/10 despite hydrocodone-acetaminophen 5/500 mg, 2 tabs q6 h” → current ‘weak’ opioid at its ceiling dose (8 tabs/day = 4 g acetaminophen).<br /></br>• Tachycardia, guarding → physiologic signs of uncontrolled pain.<br /></br>• Hx “upper GI bleed from gastric ulcer” → NSAIDs contraindicated.  </p></br><p>All clues point to the need for rapid, potent analgesia that bypasses the acetaminophen ceiling and avoids NSAIDs.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (WHO Cancer Pain Ladder)<br /></br>Step 1: Non-opioid (acetaminophen/NSAID) – already maximized, NSAID unsafe.<br /></br>Step 2: Weak opioid (hydrocodone, codeine) ± non-opioid – currently failing.<br /></br>Step 3: Strong opioid (morphine, hydromorphone, fentanyl) ± adjuvant – indicated now.<br /></br>Route: For acute severe pain, parenteral administration provides fastest, reliable onset → IV morphine.  </p></br><p>Take-home: Escalate both potency (weak → strong opioid) and, when rapid control is needed, route (oral → IV).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Pathophysiology Points<br /></br>• Pathologic fracture = intense somatic pain transmitted via A-δ &amp; C fibers → responds best to μ-opioid agonists.<br /></br>• Hydrocodone product limited by acetaminophen hepatotoxicity (≥4 g/24 h).<br /></br>• Morphine: first-line strong opioid; no ceiling effect; titratable IV for quick relief.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line for severe cancer pain<br /></br>1. IV morphine (2-4 mg q10-15 min until comfort, then convert to scheduled oral long-acting opioid plus breakthrough dosing).<br /></br>2. Consider bone-stabilizing measures (radiotherapy, orthopedic fixation, bisphosphonate) after pain is controlled.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“1-2-3, PO-IV-Me”<br /></br>1 = Non-opioid<br /></br>2 = Weak opioid<br /></br>3 = Strong opioid<br /></br>If pain is ‘≥8’, jump to ‘IV’ → Morphine.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Increase hydrocodone-acetaminophen<br /></br>• Already at 4 g/day acetaminophen (max safe). Further dose ↑ risks hepatotoxicity and still provides insufficient opioid potency.  </p></br><p>b. Add oral gabapentin<br /></br>• Helpful for neuropathic pain (e.g., post-herpetic neuralgia), not for acute fracture pain; onset delayed days-weeks.  </p></br><p>c. Add oral ketorolac (NSAID)<br /></br>• Potent NSAID but contraindicated with history of upper GI bleed; also ceiling effect and slower onset than IV opioid.  </p></br><p>d. Switch to intramuscular meperidine<br /></br>• Meperidine’s toxic metabolite (normeperidine) → risk of seizures; IM route painful/unreliable; no advantage over safer opioids.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Concept<br /></br>Uncontrolled severe cancer pain requires escalation to a strong opioid (morphine, hydromorphone, fentanyl) given via the route that provides the fastest, titratable relief—intravenous morphine in this case.</p>
83. Comprehensive Clinical Science Self-Assessment A 24-year-old woman, gravida 1, para 1, comes to the physician because she has not had a menstrual period for 12 months and has been unable to conceive during this time. Her son was born by spontaneous vaginal delivery 18 months ago, and she breast-fed him for 6 months. One month after delivery, she had profuse vaginal bleeding and underwent emergency dilatation and curettage for retained placental fragments. She has not used any contraception since her delivery and currently takes no medications. She and her husband have sexual intercourse twice weekly. Analysis of the husband's semen has shown no abnormalities. The patient's vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. Pelvic examination shows a normal cervix, uterus, and adnexa. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hematocrit 42 percent</br>Leukocyte count 8800/mm 3</br>Serum</br>Thyroid-stimulating hormone</br>Follicle-stimulating hormone</br>Luteinizing hormone</br>1.1 μU/mL</br>10 mlU/mL</br>12 mlU/mL</br>Pelvic ultrasonography shows normal ovaries, no free fluid in the pelvis, and no distinct endometrial stripe. Treatment with oral progesterone for 10 days produces no uterine bleeding. Which of the following is the most</br>appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. CT scan of the head</br>b. CT scan of the pelvis</br>c. Hysteroscopy</br>d. Laparoscopy</br>e. Dilatation and curettage</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Hysteroscopy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Secondary amenorrhoea (&gt; 12 mo) after a pregnancy.<br /></br>• History of vigorous uterine curettage for retained placenta → classic risk factor for intra-uterine adhesions (Asherman syndrome).<br /></br>• Normal vital signs and normal-sized uterus/ovaries on bimanual exam.<br /></br>• Pelvic US: “no distinct endometrial stripe” (suggests endometrium is absent or obliterated rather than hypo-estrogenic).<br /></br>• Progestin challenge test negative (no withdrawal bleed) → either (1) very low oestrogen or (2) outflow tract problem/uterine cavity obliteration.<br /></br>• FSH 10 mIU/mL, LH 12 mIU/mL (mid-normal). Normal gonadotropins argue against premature ovarian failure or global pituitary failure (Sheehan).  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Rule out pregnancy, thyroid, prolactin disorders → already excluded.<br /></br>2. Negative progestin challenge → decide between low oestrogen vs. structural uterine problem.<br /></br>   • Normal FSH/LH + normal ovaries on US = oestrogen is likely present.<br /></br>   • Thin/absent endometrial stripe and prior curettage = structural (adhesions).<br /></br>3. Best diagnostic/therapeutic test for intra-uterine adhesions = hysteroscopy (direct visualisation + option for lysis).  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Secondary amenorrhoea after uterine instrumentation + thin endometrium + no bleed after progestin challenge = think Asherman → evaluate with hysteroscopy.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Asherman syndrome<br /></br>• Etiology: Basalis layer destruction (usually postpartum curettage, septic abortion, myomectomy).<br /></br>• Path: Fibrous synechiae bridge uterine walls → cavity partially or totally obliterated.<br /></br>• Clinical: Hypomenorrhoea/amenorrhoea, infertility, recurrent pregnancy loss, cyclic pelvic pain if partial obstruction.<br /></br>• Labs: Normal ovarian function → normal FSH/LH/oestrogen.<br /></br>• Imaging: HSG shows “spider-web” filling defects; saline sonogram shows irregular cavity; hysteroscopy is gold-standard.<br /></br>• Treatment: Hysteroscopic adhesiolysis ± oestrogen therapy post-op to regrow endometrium; place paediatric Foley or IUD temporarily to keep walls apart.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Highlights<br /></br>1. Confirm diagnosis with hysteroscopy (simultaneously therapeutic).<br /></br>2. Post-lysis: High-dose conjugated oestrogens or estradiol + cyclic progestin to stimulate regrowth, plus barrier (Foley balloon or copper IUD) for 1–2 weeks.<br /></br>3. Evaluate tubal patency and repeat cavity imaging if fertility remains an issue.</p></br><p>Memorisation Tips<br /></br>“ASHERMAN”<br /></br>A – After instrumentation<br /></br>S – Scarred stripe (absent on US)<br /></br>H – Hysteroscopy to diagnose &amp; heal<br /></br>E – Estrogen levels normal<br /></br>R – Retained placenta curettage = #1 cause<br /></br>MAN – Menstrual Amenorrhoea, iNfertility</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. CT scan of the head<br /></br>• Ordered if suspect Sheehan (panhypopituitarism) or pituitary tumour. Sheehan presents with inability to lactate, fatigue, hypotension, low FSH/LH/TSH/ACTH. This patient breast-fed 6 months and has normal gonadotropins/TSH, making pituitary pathology unlikely.</p></br><p>b. CT scan of the pelvis<br /></br>• Pelvic CT lacks sensitivity for intra-uterine adhesions; exposes patient to unnecessary radiation; ultrasound already showed normal adnexa and thin endometrium. Not diagnostic or therapeutic.</p></br><p>d. Laparoscopy<br /></br>• Evaluates pelvic adhesions/endometriosis, not intra-uterine cavity. Would miss uterine synechiae and offers no treatment for them.</p></br><p>e. Dilatation and curettage<br /></br>• More curettage risks worsening existing adhesions. D&amp;C is therapeutic for retained products or diagnostic for AUB, not for suspected Asherman.</p></br><p>Remember: Post-curettage amenorrhoea = think scars inside the uterus, so look inside the uterus (hysteroscopy).</p>
84. A previously healthy 7-year-old boy is brought to the office because of a 1-week history of diffuse, mild abdominal pain and bloody diarrhea. He has a constant urge to pass stools despite passing 10 small, bloody stools daily. During this time, he has had decreased oral intake. He last urinated 12 hours ago. He has not had fever or vomiting. No other family members are ill. The patient appears ti ed. His temperature is 37.7 C 99.8 F, pulse is 120/min, respirations are 26/min, and blood pressure is 120/85 mm Hg. Examination shows pallor and tacky mucous membranes. The lungs are clear to auscultation. A grade 2/6 systolic murmur is heard. The abdomen is soft and diffusely tender to palpation; there is no guarding or rebound. Examination of the perianal area shows no abnormalities. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 7 g/dL N=11.5-14.5</br>Hematocrit 21  percent N=33 percent-43 percent</br>Leukocyte count 12,000/mm 3</br>Platelet count 14,000/mm 3</br>Serum</br>Na+</br>K+</br>cI-</br>HCO 3-</br>Urea nitrogen</br>Creatinine</br>147 mEq/L</br>4.2 mEq/L</br>118 mEq/L</br>14 mEq/L</br>72 mg/dL</br>8.2 mg/dL N=0.22-0.59</br>Which of the following is the most likely infectious agent?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Campylobacter jejuni</br>b. Clostridium difficile</br>c. Escherichia coli</br>d. Giardia lamblia</br>e. Salmonella enteritidis</br></br></br><p>⚠️  Before I launch into the rubric-based explanation, I want to flag a potential answer-key error:</p></br><p>• The triad of 1) microangiopathic hemolytic anemia (Hb 7 g/dL, schistocytes implied by low Hb with normal MCV), 2) thrombocytopenia (14 000/µL), and 3) acute kidney injury (Cr 8.2 mg/dL, BUN 72 mg/dL) after a prodrome of bloody diarrhea in a child is the classic presentation of hemolytic uremic syndrome (HUS).<br /></br>• On NBME/USMLE exams, the organism most strongly associated with HUS is Shiga-toxin–producing Escherichia coli O157:H7 acquired from under-cooked beef, unpasteurized juice, petting zoos, etc.<br /></br>• Campylobacter jejuni can cause bloody diarrhea, but it is classically linked to Guillain-Barré syndrome, not HUS.</p></br><p>Because the provided “correct answer” (Campylobacter jejuni) conflicts with the classic teaching and with the key findings in the vignette, I suspect a mis-key. Please let me know whether I should:</p></br><ol></br><li>Proceed with a full, detailed explanation using Escherichia coli as the correct answer (most likely what the NBME would expect), or  </li></br><li>Provide an explanation defending Campylobacter jejuni as the correct choice despite the inconsistency.</li></br></ol></br><p>I’ll wait for your guidance so the subsequent explanation aligns with your intention.</p>
85. A previously healthy 5-year-old boy is brought to the physician because of progressive muscle weakness over the past 6 months. Examination shows a fish-like mouth with a high-arched palate, wasting of distal muscles,</br>and hypotonia. Deep tendon reflexes are intact and symmetric. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Glycogen storage disease, type II Pompe disease</br>b. Guillain-8arre syndrome</br>c. Hypothyroidism</br>d. Myasthenia gravis</br>e. Myotonic dystrophy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Myotonic dystrophy  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age/Onset – 5-year-old with slowly progressive weakness over 6 months (chronic, not acute).<br /></br>• Face – “Fish-like” (hatchet) facies + high-arched palate → classic craniofacial clue for myotonic dystrophy.<br /></br>• Pattern of weakness – Distal muscle wasting &gt; proximal; hypotonia but deep-tendon reflexes preserved → typical for myotonic dystrophy (dystrophic process, not neuropathy or NMJ).<br /></br>• Symmetric intact reflexes → argues against neuropathies (GBS) and NMJ disorders (MG often has fatigable weakness, no atrophy at this age).  </p></br><p>Key Pattern = Childhood‐onset, distal wasting, hatchet face / high-arched palate, preserved DTRs  ⇒ think myotonic dystrophy.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Tempo: chronic, insidiously progressive.<br /></br>2. Distribution: distal weakness + muscle wasting.<br /></br>3. Craniofacial signs: hatchet face, high-arched palate.<br /></br>4. Reflexes: intact (rules out neuropathic areflexia).<br /></br>5. Age: congenital–juvenile presentation fits DM1 congenital form.  </p></br><p>Core Take-away: On Step exams, “hatchet/fish-like facies” + distal wasting + high-arched palate = myotonic dystrophy.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Myotonic dystrophy type 1 (DM1) = CTG trinucleotide repeat expansion in DMPK gene → abnormal myotonin-protein kinase.<br /></br>• AD with anticipation (earlier &amp; worse in successive generations).<br /></br>• Pathology: myotonia (delayed muscle relaxation), progressive muscle wasting beginning distally, multisystem involvement (cataracts, cardiac conduction defects, testicular atrophy, insulin resistance).<br /></br>• Reflexes often preserved until late because pathology is myopathic, not neurogenic.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>• Confirm with genetic testing for CTG repeat. EMG shows myotonic discharges; CK mildly ↑.<br /></br>• Symptomatic care: physical therapy, ankle-foot orthoses, mexiletine or other Na⁺-channel blockers for troublesome myotonia; monitor EKG for conduction defects; cataract screening. No disease-modifying cure.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Quick Mnemonic<br /></br>“CTG = Cataracts, Toupee (balding), Gonadal atrophy” and “Fish face can’t let go the CTG.” Visualize a fish with a high-arched palate biting a CTG fishing line but can’t relax its jaw (myotonia).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Glycogen storage disease type II (Pompe)<br /></br>• Infant-onset cardiomegaly, macroglossia, hepatomegaly, severe hypotonia (“floppy baby”); death &lt;2 yrs if untreated.<br /></br>• No high-arched palate / distal wasting / preserved reflexes; disease is more diffuse and frequently affects heart.  </p></br><p>b. Guillain-Barré syndrome<br /></br>• Acute to subacute demyelinating polyneuropathy; ascending weakness, areflexia, sensory symptoms after infection.<br /></br>• Reflexes absent, not preserved; facial features &amp; distal wasting absent; timeline is weeks, not months.  </p></br><p>c. Hypothyroidism<br /></br>• Can cause hypotonia and delayed reflex relaxation but not distal muscle wasting or characteristic facial features; would also show systemic signs (constipation, weight gain, dry skin).  </p></br><p>d. Myasthenia gravis<br /></br>• Fatigable weakness, especially ocular/bulbar, normal bulk (no wasting), fluctuates daily; reflexes intact but no atrophy or fish-like facies; very rare at age 5 unless neonatal transient MG.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Summary Pearl<br /></br>In NBME/Step questions, combination of (1) distal muscle atrophy, (2) “hatchet” or fish-like face with high-arched palate, (3) intact reflexes, and (4) childhood onset = MYOTONIC DYSTROPHY.</p>
86. A 77-year-old man comes to the physician for an initial visit. He has a history of gout and surgical repair of a hernia. He has been told that he has a mild cardiac murmur, and he thinks that he had rheumatic fever as a</br>child. He takes no medications. His pulse is 80/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 150/92 mm Hg. A grade 4/6 systolic murmur is heard best at the second right intercostal space; it decreases in intensity</br>with Valsalva maneuver and with handgrip and increases in intensity when he squats. There is an S 4, and a systolic thrill is palpable at the base. Which of the following is the most likely cause of these findings?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Aortic insufficiency</br>b. Aortic stenosis</br>c. Hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy</br>d. Mitral regurgitation</br>e. Mitral stenosis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b.
