Aortic stenosis  </p></br><p>─────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Location/quality: “Grade 4/6 systolic murmur … best at the 2nd right intercostal space” = classic aortic area, harsh, crescendo–decrescendo.<br /></br>• Maneuvers:<br /></br>  – Decreases with Valsalva (↓ preload) and hand-grip (↑ afterload)<br /></br>  – Increases with squatting (↑ preload &amp; afterload)<br /></br>  → Exactly matches flow-dependent, fixed-obstruction lesions such as aortic stenosis.<br /></br>• S4 + palpable systolic thrill = concentric LV hypertrophy from chronic pressure overload.<br /></br>• Age 77 with remote rheumatic fever or degenerative valve calcification → both common etiologies of AS in the elderly.  </p></br><p>─────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify that the murmur is systolic.<br /></br>2. Localize to the RUSB (aortic valve).<br /></br>3. Apply maneuvers: only “fixed” outflow‐obstruction lesions (AS) get louder with squatting and softer with Valsalva, whereas HOCM behaves oppositely.<br /></br>4. Presence of an S4 and thrill further supports a stiff, hypertrophied LV facing high outflow gradients.<br /></br>Core takeaway: A crescendo–decrescendo systolic murmur at the RUSB that varies directly with preload (↑ with squat, ↓ with Valsalva) = aortic stenosis.  </p></br><p>─────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Etiology (elderly):<br /></br>• Calcific degeneration of a trileaflet valve (most common)<br /></br>• Post-rheumatic fusion or bicuspid valve degeneration  </p></br><p>LV faces chronic pressure overload → concentric hypertrophy → S4 (atrial kick into stiff ventricle) and eventual diastolic HF.  </p></br><p>Classic clinical triad (late disease): angina, syncope, heart failure.  </p></br><p>─────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Diagnostics:<br /></br>• Echo: valve area &lt;1 cm², mean gradient &gt;40 mm Hg confirms severe AS.<br /></br>• ECG: LVH with strain; CXR: post-stenotic dilatation of ascending aorta.  </p></br><p>Management:<br /></br>• Definitive: Surgical or transcatheter aortic valve replacement (SAVR/TAVR) once symptomatic or LV dysfunction.<br /></br>• Medical: Conservative only for mild disease; avoid preload/afterload drops (e.g., profound diuresis or vasodilators).  </p></br><p>─────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“AS is AStiff valve at the Aortic Site” – think “S” for:<br /></br>• Systolic crescendo–decrescendo<br /></br>• Squat louder<br /></br>• S4<br /></br>• Syncope/angina/heart failure  </p></br><p>ValSalva Softens Stenosis (both AS &amp; pulmonic stenosis), but it Strengthens hypertrophic septum (HOCM).  </p></br><p>─────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Aortic insufficiency (regurgitation)<br /></br>• Diastolic, blowing decrescendo at LSB; ↑ with hand-grip, not squat. Wide pulse pressure, bounding pulses—none present here.  </p></br><p>c. Hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy<br /></br>• Systolic murmur loudest at LSB/apex; INCREASES with Valsalva &amp; standing (↓ preload) and DECREASES with squatting—opposite pattern of vignette. Often in younger pts with FHx SCD.  </p></br><p>d. Mitral regurgitation<br /></br>• Holosystolic blowing murmur at apex radiating to axilla; louder with hand-grip; no thrill at base, not located RUSB.  </p></br><p>e. Mitral stenosis<br /></br>• Diastolic low-pitched rumble with opening snap at apex; heard best in left lateral decubitus; unrelated maneuver profile.  </p></br><p>Key difference: Only aortic stenosis gives a harsh systolic murmur at the RUSB that softens with Valsalva and has an S4 with palpable thrill.</p>
87. A 54-year-old woman comes to the office because of a 2-day history of increasingly severe pain in her right ear; she rates the pain as a 9 on a 10-point scale. She also has a mild ringing sensation in her right ear. She adds that this morning, her usual cereal tasted awful to her, and she vomited once. She has no symptoms in her left ear and has not had fever, headache, neck stiffness, or symptoms of an upper respiratory tract infection. Her most recent vaccination was 3 years ago for influenza. She has no history of serious illness, takes no medications, and has no known allergies. She is 165 cm 5 ft 5 in tall and weighs 72 kg 158 lb; BMI is 26 kg/m 2. Temperature is 37.3 C 99.1  F, pulse is 84/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 125/85 mm Hg. Examination of the left pinna and tympanic membrane discloses no abnormalities. Examination of the right ear shows erythema and edema of exterior auditory canal and pinna; light touch of the right pinna produces pain. The right tympanic membrane cannot be observed because of pain and edema. Cranial nerve testing discloses a widened right palpebral fissure. The patient has decreased ability to close her right eye, wrinkle her forehead, and smile. Visual acuity and visual field testing disclose no abnormalities. Sensation to light touch and pinprick is intact over the face. The remainder of the examination discloses no abnormalities. Which of the following conditions is most likely to develop in this patient during the next 24 hours?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Auricular vesicles</br>b. Otitis media</br>c. Swollen and painful parotid gland</br>d. Visual field defect</br>e. Xerostomia</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer: Auricular vesicles  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Increasingly severe pain in her right ear … light touch of the pinna produces pain” → classic for acute otalgia of an external‐ear process.<br /></br>• “Erythema and edema of external auditory canal and pinna” → looks like otitis externa.<br /></br>• Ipsilateral LOWER motor neuron CN VII findings (wider palpebral fissure, inability to close eye, wrinkle forehead, or smile) → facial nerve palsy.<br /></br>• Loss of taste (“cereal tasted awful”) + tinnitus/vomiting → chorda tympani and vestibulocochlear involvement.<br /></br>Pattern: Severe otalgia + external ear inflammation + ipsilateral LMN facial palsy = Herpes zoster oticus (Ramsay-Hunt syndrome). In 24–48 h the characteristic vesicular eruption over the auricle/external canal usually appears but may lag behind the pain → predict “auricular vesicles.”</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Localize the deficit: LMN facial palsy (forehead involved).<br /></br>2. Check ear: painful, inflamed external canal.<br /></br>3. Combine ear pain + LMN VII → think Ramsay-Hunt (not simple Bell palsy).<br /></br>4. Know natural history: burning otalgia precedes vesicles by 1–2 days. Therefore the most likely new finding tomorrow is a vesicular eruption on the auricle.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Ramsay-Hunt = “Bell palsy + Otitis externa-like pain → shingles vesicles on the ear.”</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Reactivation of latent Varicella-Zoster Virus in the geniculate ganglion.<br /></br>• Virus tracks along sensory fibers→ vesicles on concha, external canal, occasionally palate.<br /></br>• Motor fibers of CN VII → ipsilateral LMN facial paralysis.<br /></br>• May involve CN VIII → tinnitus, vertigo, vomiting; chorda tympani → taste loss.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line:<br /></br>• High-dose oral acyclovir, valacyclovir, or famciclovir + prednisone started ≤72 h to improve recovery.<br /></br>• Lubricating eye drops/patch to protect cornea (impaired eye closure).<br /></br>Work-up (if diagnosis uncertain): viral PCR of vesicle fluid; MRI brain/temporal bone if atypical course.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Hunt for the Vesicles”:<br /></br>H – Herpes Zoster<br /></br>U – Unilateral ear pain<br /></br>N – Nerve VII palsy<br /></br>T – Taste loss / Tinnitus<br /></br>→ expect vesicles soon.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Otitis media – Infection of the middle ear; would give bulging/tender tympanic membrane and conductive hearing loss, not LMN facial palsy. External canal edema already present; spreading inward is not typical within 24 h.<br /></br>c. Swollen &amp; painful parotid gland – Parotitis (classically mumps) presents with fever, bilateral parotid swelling, not ear canal edema or facial palsy.<br /></br>d. Visual field defect – CN VII palsy does not affect optic pathways; no mechanism for field loss.<br /></br>e. Xerostomia – Would require damage to parasympathetic supply of submandibular/sublingual glands (chorda tympani) bilaterally; not an acute 24-h development and not typical presenting/early feature of Ramsay-Hunt.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In Ramsay-Hunt syndrome, intense otalgia and LMN facial paralysis precede the pathognomonic vesicular rash on the auricle/external canal. Expect those auricular vesicles to appear next.</p>
88. A 42-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department by paramedics 30 minutes after she was struck by a car while she was riding her bicycle. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She is alert and fully oriented. She has moderate left upper abdominal and flank pain. Temperature is 37.1  C 98.8 F, pulse is 118/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 88/60 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 94 percent. There is a 5 x 7-in, tender ecchymosis over the left upper abdominal quadrant and left flank. Bowel sounds are normal. The remainder of the physical examination shows no abnormalities. Neurologic examination shows no focal findings. After administration of 2 L of lactated Ringer solution, pulse is 105/min, and blood pressure is 95/70 mm Hg. Transfusion of 2 units of packed red blood cells is begun. Thirty minutes later, pulse is 80/min, and blood pressure is 124/80 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on an F10 2 of 1.0 via nonrebreather face mask shows an oxygen saturation of 100 percent. CT scan of the abdomen shows a grade 3 splenic laceration. The patient is admitted to the surgical intensive care unit for nonoperative management. Three hours later, she has progressive difficulty breathing. Temperature is 37.2 C 99.0 F, pulse is 90/min, respirations are 30/min, and blood pressure is 110/70 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry now shows an oxygen saturation of 87 percent. On pulmonary examination, breath sounds and diffuse crackles are heard bilaterally. Heart sounds are normal. Abdominal examination is unchanged. Chest x-ray is shown. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's current condition?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Acute congestive heart failure caused by myocardial contusion</br>b. Aspiration pneumonia</br>c. Bilateral pulmonary contusions</br>d. Transfusion-related acute lung injury</br>e. Traumatic intrapulmonary hemorrhage</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>d. Transfusion-related acute lung injury (TRALI)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – key clues</br>• Recent transfusion — 2 units of PRBCs started only 3 h ago<br /></br>• Abrupt dyspnea &amp; hypoxemia — SpO₂ fell from 100 % (on 100 % O₂) to 87 %<br /></br>• Tachypnea (RR 30), bilateral crackles, diffuse bilateral opacities on CXR (image not shown in stem)<br /></br>• Normothermic/afebrile, no chest trauma described, no jugular venous distention, no S₃, BP and pulse reasonable → argues against cardiogenic pulmonary edema</p></br><p>Pattern recognition: “within 6 h of any blood product, acute respiratory distress + bilateral non-cardiogenic pulmonary edema” = TRALI.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)</br>1. Timeline → respiratory failure develops &lt; 6 h after transfusion.<br /></br>2. Physical exam → diffuse crackles, but heart sounds normal, no evidence of volume overload.<br /></br>3. Imaging (typical) → bilateral fluffy infiltrates identical to ARDS picture.<br /></br>4. Hemodynamics → normotensive, no tachycardia, no CHF signs.<br /></br>⇒ Non-cardiogenic pulmonary edema caused by transfusion → TRALI.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: TRALI is the most common fatal transfusion reaction; think of it whenever acute hypoxemia with bilateral infiltrates develops during or within 6 h of blood product administration.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Donor anti-HLA or anti-neutrophil antibodies bind recipient neutrophils → massive neutrophil activation in pulmonary microvasculature → capillary leakage → protein-rich pulmonary edema (ARDS picture).<br /></br>• Non-cardiogenic: PCWP is normal/low; no LV dysfunction.</p></br><p>Risk factors: plasma-containing products (FFP, platelets) &gt; PRBC, but can occur with any transfusion.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Management</br>1. Stop the transfusion immediately.<br /></br>2. Supportive care identical to ARDS:</br>   – High-flow O₂, possibly intubation with low-tidal-volume ventilation and PEEP.<br /></br>3. No role for diuretics; pulmonary edema is not volume mediated.<br /></br>4. Report to blood bank; future avoidance of implicated donor.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“TRALI = 6 letters → ≤ 6 hours after transfusion, Lungs fill with Injury.”<br /></br>Contrast with TACO (“Circulatory Overload”) → occurs in older / CHF patients, responds to diuretics, presents with HTN &amp; JVD.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Acute CHF from myocardial contusion<br /></br>   • Requires significant blunt chest trauma; vignette describes abdominal impact only.<br /></br>   • Would show S₃, elevated JVP, possible hypotension and cardiomegaly on CXR; edema would be cardiogenic.</p></br><p>b. Aspiration pneumonia<br /></br>   • Develops over &gt; 12 – 24 h, often febrile; infiltrates classically in dependent lobes, not diffuse bilateral edema.</p></br><p>c. Bilateral pulmonary contusions<br /></br>   • Occur immediately or within a few hours of direct chest trauma; CT at admission would have shown lung injury; no thoracic impact was described.</p></br><p>e. Traumatic intrapulmonary hemorrhage<br /></br>   • Also requires direct thoracic trauma; presentation mirrors pulmonary contusion and would not be transfusion-timed.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: confusing TRALI with TACO (volume overload). Remember:<br /></br>– TRALI: acute lung injury, normal volume status, NORMAL BNP/PCWP, no response to diuretics.<br /></br>– TACO: hypertension, S₃, high BNP, responds to furosemide.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Take-Home</br>New bilateral pulmonary infiltrates + hypoxemia within 6 h of transfusion = TRALI until proven otherwise; stop transfusion and provide supportive pulmonary care.</p>
89. A 12-year-old girl is brought to the emergency department by paramedics 10 minutes after she was stabbed in the chest with a steak knife by another girl after school. The patient's medical history is unknown. She is gasping for breath but able to say her name. Her temperature is 37.0 C 98.6 F, pulse is 140/min, respirations are 36/min, and blood ressure is 80 mm H . Pulse oximet [Y on an F10 ~ of 1.0 by nonrebreather face mask shows an oxygen saturation of 86 percent. Examination discloses a 2-cm stab wound to the left of the lower sternum. Jugular venous pressure is 12 cm H 20 N=S--9. Breath sounds are equal bilaterally. Heart sounds are decreased. The abdomen is soft, nondistended, and nontender. Pulses are decreased in all extremities. Pericardiocentesis yields 30 ml of non-clotting blood. In addition to intravenous fluid resuscitation and typing and crossmatching of the patient's blood, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Chest x-ray</br>b. CT angiography of the chest</br>c. Sternotomy</br>d. Transesophageal echocardiography</br>e. Tube thoracostomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>c. Sternotomy  (immediate median sternotomy for surgical repair of a penetrating cardiac injury)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• “Stab wound to the left of the lower sternum” = within the cardiac box (sternal notch → xiphoid, nipples laterally) → high suspicion for cardiac injury.<br /></br>• Vital-sign pattern: hypotension (80 mm Hg), tachycardia (140/min), tachypnea (36/min) = shock.<br /></br>• Neck veins: JVP 12 cm H₂O (elevated).<br /></br>• Muffled heart sounds.<br /></br>• Equal breath sounds eliminate tension pneumo.<br /></br>→ Classic Beck triad (hypotension, JVD, muffled heart sounds) = acute cardiac tamponade.</p></br><p>• “Pericardiocentesis yields 30 mL of non-clotting blood.”<br /></br>  – Confirms hemopericardium (non-clotting = pericardial, not ventricular cavity).<br /></br>  – Temporizes but does NOT repair the source; ongoing bleeding expected.</p></br><p>Key pattern: Penetrating chest trauma + hemodynamic instability + confirmed tamponade → go straight to definitive surgical repair.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. Diagnose obstructive shock from tamponade (look for Beck triad, equal breath sounds, penetrating injury).<br /></br>2. Temporizing pericardiocentesis performed → still unstable (BP 80).<br /></br>3. Definitive management = open the pericardium, evacuate blood, locate and stitch cardiac laceration.<br /></br>   • Route of choice in children/most adults = median sternotomy (excellent exposure of RV &amp; LV, coronary vessels, great vessels; allows cardiopulmonary bypass if needed).<br /></br>4. Any further imaging (CXR, CT, TEE) delays life-saving surgery.</p></br><p>Take-home: In unstable penetrating “cardiac box” trauma with tamponade physiology, after (or instead of) emergent pericardiocentesis, proceed directly to sternotomy/thoracotomy for repair.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Penetrating injury → blood enters pericardial sac.<br /></br>• Low-compliance pericardium → rapid pressure rise → impaired RV diastolic filling → ↓ preload → obstructive shock.<br /></br>• Equalization of diastolic pressures, pulsus paradoxus possible.<br /></br>• Removing a few milliliters by needle may briefly help, but definitive treatment is surgical evacuation and closure of the cardiac laceration.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Management Ladder for Suspected Penetrating Cardiac Injury</br>Stable patient<br /></br> → FAST or TEE/CT → OR if (+).<br /></br>Unstable patient or (+) pericardiocentesis<br /></br> → Immediate median sternotomy or left anterolateral thoracotomy (ED thoracotomy if arrest).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“FAST stab + FAST heart = FAST to the OR.”<br /></br>Rhythm: Beck’s triad rings the “BELL”<br /></br> B lood pressure low<br /></br> E cho (heart sounds) muffled<br /></br> L engorged neck veins<br /></br> L ook for sternotomy!</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Chest X-ray<br /></br>   • May show enlarged cardiac silhouette but provides no therapy and delays definitive care. Not for an unstable patient.</p></br><p>b. CT angiography of the chest<br /></br>   • Requires hemodynamic stability and time in the scanner; contraindicated when systolic BP 80 mm Hg.</p></br><p>d. Transesophageal echocardiography<br /></br>   • Excellent diagnostic tool but still only confirms what we already know. No therapeutic benefit and wastes precious minutes.</p></br><p>e. Tube thoracostomy<br /></br>   • Treats pneumothorax/hemothorax. Breath sounds are equal; JVP elevated (not flat) → problem is pericardial, not pleural.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>Penetrating injury to the cardiac box + tamponade signs = surgical emergency. Once tamponade is confirmed (by clinical exam, ultrasound, or aspirated pericardial blood), skip further diagnostics and perform sternotomy/thoracotomy for definitive cardiac repair.</p>
90. A 68-year-old woman comes to the office for a routine examination. She says she has felt well. Medical history is remarkable for hypertension and type 2 diabetes mellitus. Medications are hydrochlorothiazide,</br>atorvastatin, and insulin. Pulse is 70/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 130/60 mm Hg. Physical examination discloses a 2-cm mass in the right groin below the inguinal ligament; the mass is mildly tender</br>to palpation and does not reduce with attempts at manual reduction. The mass is not fluctuant. The patient says she has noticed this abnormality during the past several weeks but has not been bothered by it. The</br>remainder of the physical examination discloses no abnormalities. Fasting serum glucose concentration obtained in preparation for today's visit is within the reference range. Which of the following is the most appropriate</br>recommendation at this time?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. CT scan of the pelvis</br>b. Elective operative repair within the next week</br>c. Use of a hernia belt</br>d. Reevaluation in the office if symptoms develop</br></br></br><p>Correct answer: CT scan of the pelvis</p></br><p>Vignette analysis</br>• “2-cm mass in the right groin below the inguinal ligament” – Below = femoral canal territory; however, other pathologies (inguinal LN, femoral artery aneurysm, saphenous varix, lipoma, psoas abscess) sit in the same spot.<br /></br>• “Mildly tender … does not reduce” – Could be an incarcerated femoral hernia, but a fixed, slightly tender node or vascular lesion can feel exactly the same.<br /></br>• “No obstructive or systemic symptoms” – Argues against strangulation or bowel obstruction that would mandate immediate OR.<br /></br>Key idea: physical exam alone cannot distinguish a small femoral hernia from other groin masses. The next logical step is an imaging study that shows hernia sac, bowel, and surrounding vascular/lymphatic structures → CT pelvis (or groin US in some practices).</p></br><p>Clinical critical reasoning (step-wise)</br>1. Identify the groin mass location (below ligament).<br /></br>2. Generate differential: femoral hernia, lymph node, vascular lesion, soft-tissue tumor.<br /></br>3. Assess urgency: no peritonitis, no SBO, vitals stable → NOT an emergent/strangulated hernia.<br /></br>4. Decide on the next diagnostic step before therapy → cross-sectional imaging.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology review</br>Femoral hernia: Protrusion through femoral canal (medial to femoral vein, below inguinal ligament). Narrow rigid neck → highest incarceration/strangulation rate of all groin hernias, especially in elderly women.<br /></br>Enlarged inguinal/femoral LNs: Secondary to infection, malignancy (skin cancers of LE, lymphoma, metastatic pelvic malignancies).<br /></br>Vascular lesions: Femoral artery aneurysm or pseudoaneurysm (iatrogenic).<br /></br>Imaging delineates bowel loops, peritoneal sac, fat planes, vessels &amp; nodes.</p></br><p>Treatment/diagnostic pearls</br>• CT pelvis (or high-resolution groin US) accurately diagnoses &gt;95 % of femoral/inguinal hernias, visualizes lymph nodes &amp; vessels, and guides operative planning.<br /></br>• Once a femoral hernia is confirmed, elective repair is recommended soon (open preperitoneal, plug-and-patch, or lap TEP/TAPP) because of strangulation risk.<br /></br>• Emergent surgery is reserved for strangulation (severe pain, erythema, peritonitis, obstruction, systemic signs).</p></br><p>Memorization tips</br>“Below the ligament in a lady?  Look before you cut.”<br /></br>Femoral = Female, Fast to incarcerate → Image, then operate.</p></br><p>Incorrect answer analysis</br>b. Elective operative repair within the next week<br /></br>   – Appropriate only AFTER confirming the mass is a femoral hernia. Operating on a lymph node or vascular lesion would be unnecessary and potentially dangerous.</p></br><p>c. Use of a hernia belt<br /></br>   – Trusses/belts work only for reducible inguinal hernias, not for fixed femoral hernias (and certainly not for nodes or aneurysms). Ineffective and not recommended.</p></br><p>d. Reevaluation if symptoms develop (watchful waiting)<br /></br>   – Unwise because a femoral hernia can strangulate suddenly; lymphadenopathy or vascular lesions also warrant diagnosis. Current guidelines: any persistent, unexplained groin mass gets imaged.</p></br><p>Take-home message</br>For a persistent, nonreducible mass below the inguinal ligament without acute signs, first obtain cross-sectional imaging (CT pelvis) to secure the diagnosis before proceeding to definitive therapy.</p>
91. An 18-year-old woman comes to the physician to request a prescription for an oral contraceptive. She has been sexually active since the age of 17 years and has never used contraception. Menarche was at the age of 12 years. Menses occur at regular 29-day intervals. Her last menstrual period was 2 weeks ago. She has received all appropriate childhood immunizations but none since the age of 6 years. She has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 1 year. Physical examination, including pelvic examination, shows no abnormalities. In addition to recommending condom use by her partners, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Inform the patient's parents about her request</br>b. Obtain serum rubella antibody titer</br>c. Perform a Pap smear</br>d. Prescribe an oral contraceptive</br>e. Refuse to prescribe an oral contraceptive until she stops smoking</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Prescribe an oral contraceptive  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 18-year-old → legal adult; can consent to her own care, including contraception.<br /></br>• Sexually active, no contraception → pregnancy risk; wants reliable birth control now.<br /></br>• Smokes 1 PPD, but is only 18 → thromboembolic risk of combined estrogen-progestin pills becomes clinically significant in smokers ≥35 y or those with additional risk factors.<br /></br>• Regular menses, normal exam → no contraindication to combined OCPs.<br /></br>• Immunizations “none since age 6” → may need catch-up vaccines, but this does not delay contraception.<br /></br>Key pattern: Young adult requesting contraception with no absolute or relative contraindications.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Determine if she can legally consent → yes (≥18 y).<br /></br>2. Screen for OCP contraindications (migraine with aura, HTN, VTE history, breast CA, major smoking with age ≥35, etc.) → none present.<br /></br>3. Provide additional STI/pregnancy protection counseling → recommend condoms.<br /></br>4. Initiate effective contraception at the same visit to prevent gaps in care → prescribe OCPs.<br /></br>Take-home message: Women &lt;35 y who smoke can still receive combined OCPs; confidentiality must be preserved; Pap screening starts at 21 y.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Combined OCPs<br /></br>• Estrogen suppresses FSH/LH surge → inhibits ovulation.<br /></br>• Progestin thickens cervical mucus &amp; thins endometrium.<br /></br>Absolute contraindications: prior VTE, estrogen-dependent tumor, uncontrolled HTN, migraine with aura, active liver disease, smoking ≥15 cig/day AND age ≥35 y, etc.<br /></br>Relative risks in &lt;35 y smokers are low; benefits outweigh risks.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Immediate care today:<br /></br>• Prescribe combined OCP (e.g., ethinyl estradiol + levonorgestrel).<br /></br>• Quick-start method: first pill today; backup condoms for 7 days.  </p></br><p>Future preventive care (does NOT delay OCP):<br /></br>• Pap smear at age 21 y.<br /></br>• Catch-up vaccines: Tdap, HPV (up to 26 y), MMR if no record, meningococcal booster, annual flu.<br /></br>• Screen Chlamydia/Gonorrhea (NAAT) annually until 25 y.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“S-M-O-K-E” helps remember absolute OCP contraindications:<br /></br>S – Smoking ≥35 y plus ≥15 cigs/day<br /></br>M – Migraine with aura<br /></br>O – Oriented tumors (estrogen-dependent)<br /></br>K – Klotting (history of DVT/PE, thrombophilia)<br /></br>E – Elevated BP/liver Enzymes (severe HTN, active hepatic disease)<br /></br>If she doesn’t meet “SMOKE,” OCPs are usually OK.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Inform the patient’s parents: Breaches confidentiality; 18-year-old is an adult and does not need parental notification for contraception.<br /></br>b. Obtain serum rubella antibody titer: If vaccination status is unclear, simply vaccinate (MMR). Serology is unnecessary and, more importantly, does not address her immediate contraception need.<br /></br>c. Perform a Pap smear: Cervical cancer screening begins at age 21 regardless of sexual activity. Performing it now is outside guideline recommendations.<br /></br>e. Refuse to prescribe until she stops smoking: Smoking is only a contraindication when ≥35 y (or heavy cardiovascular risk). At 18, combined OCPs remain acceptable.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>For women &lt;35 y who smoke, combined OCPs are still appropriate. Provide contraception at the same visit, counsel on condoms/STI screening, and maintain confidentiality.</p>
92. A 77-year-old man with chronic obstructive pulmonary disease COPD comes to the physician for a follow-up examination. He had bronchitis 3 years ago treated with amoxicillin and prednisone. He has not been hospitalized during the past 3 years. He has a history of shortness of breath and cough but says these symptoms have subsided since he quit smoking cigarettes 5 years ago. His only medication is inhaled ipratropium. Pulmonary function testing shows an FVC of 85 percent of predicted and FEV 1 of 75 percent of predicted. The patient says he heard about a new inhaled medication, called BreatheEZ, for management of COPD and asks if he would benefit from this medication. The physician recalls a recent randomized controlled trial evaluating the efficacy of BreatheEZ in 7500 patients with COPD. Patients were randomized to receive BreatheEZ or placebo and observed for 4 years.</br>Data show:</br>Age, years + SD</br>FVC,  percent of predicted</br>FEV 1,  percent of predicted</br>Current smoker, percent of patients</br>Former smoker,  percent of patients</br>Number of COPD exacerbations during 12 months prior</br>to study</br>Medications at baseline  percent of patients</br>Inhaled corticosteroid</br>Long-acting J3-adrenergic agonist</br>Inhaled anticholinergic</br>Bronchodilator</br>Oral corticosteroid</br>~3 Medications</br>BreatheEZ</br>65 + 7.2</br>64</br>31</br>54</br>46</br>3</br>75</br>69</br>50</br>24</br>8</br>40</br>Placebo</br>65 + 7.4</br>66</br>30</br>50</br>50</br>3</br>77</br>67</br>54</br>22</br>9</br>43</br>Analysis shows a mortality rate of 12.2 percent for the patients receiving BreatheEZ and 15.4 percent for patients receiving placebo hazard ratio of 0. 79, 95 percent confidence interval of 0.63-0.98. Based on these data, which of the</br>following is the most appropriate action regarding BreatheEZ for this patient?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Do not prescribe BreatheEZ because the medication is not efficacious</br>b. Do not prescribe BreatheEZ because the results are invalid as a result of differences between the placebo group and the treatment group</br>c. Do not prescribe BreatheEZ because the results are relevant only for younger patients</br>d. Do not prescribe BreatheEZ because the results apply only to patients with more severe COPD</br>e. Prescribe BreatheEZ</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Do not prescribe BreatheEZ because the results apply only to patients with more-severe COPD  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues in this patient<br /></br>  – 77-year-old ex-smoker with COPD now only on ipratropium.<br /></br>  – Spirometry: FEV₁ = 75 % predicted → GOLD stage II (mild-to-moderate).<br /></br>  – Very few recent symptoms/exacerbations.  </p></br><p>• Key clues in the trial<br /></br>  – Mean FEV₁ ≈ 30 % predicted, FVC ≈ 65 % predicted → severe COPD (GOLD stage III–IV).<br /></br>  – High baseline use of triple therapy (ICS + LABA + LAMA) and frequent exacerbations.<br /></br>  – Hazard ratio for mortality 0.79 (CI 0.63-0.98) → drug works in that population.  </p></br><p>Together, the vignette sets up a mismatch in disease severity between the patient and the study population.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Is the trial internally valid?<br /></br>   – Yes: Randomization created nearly identical baseline characteristics.<br /></br>2. Is the trial externally valid for this specific patient? (Generalizability)<br /></br>   – No: Study subjects had far worse lung function (FEV₁ ≈ 30 %), frequent exacerbations, and were already on maximal inhaler therapy.<br /></br>   – Our patient has relatively preserved lung function (FEV₁ 75 %), no recent exacerbations, and minimal therapy.<br /></br>3. Therefore the mortality benefit demonstrated cannot be assumed to apply to him.<br /></br>4. The most appropriate action is to withhold BreatheEZ for now.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Evidence must be applicable to the patient in front of you; severity of illness is a classic limiter of external validity.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology &amp; Severity Review<br /></br>• COPD staging (post-bronchodilator FEV₁):<br /></br>  – ≥80 % = mild (GOLD I)<br /></br>  – 50-79 % = moderate (GOLD II)<br /></br>  – 30-49 % = severe (GOLD III)<br /></br>  – &lt;30 %   = very severe (GOLD IV)  </p></br><p>• Most pharmacologic mortality benefits (eg, LABA/LAMA/ICS combinations, pulmonary rehab, oxygen) are demonstrated in severe disease where exacerbations drive mortality. Evidence is weaker in mild disease.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Current guideline-based escalation (GOLD):<br /></br>  1. PRN short-acting bronchodilator (already on ipratropium).<br /></br>  2. Add long-acting bronchodilator (tiotropium or LABA) if symptoms or exacerbations persist.<br /></br>  3. ICS + LABA/LAMA combo for high-risk/severe disease.<br /></br>• No intervention has yet shown a clear mortality advantage in mild-moderate stages beyond smoking cessation and influenza/PPSV23 vaccination.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memory Aids<br /></br>“GOLD <em>G</em>uides who Gets the drug”:<br /></br>GOLD III–IV → Give the new drug.<br /></br>GOLD I–II → Generally hold off unless symptoms/exacerbations dictate.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Answer Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. “Not efficacious” – False. HR 0.79 with 95 % CI 0.63-0.98 shows a statistically significant mortality reduction in severe COPD.  </p></br><p>b. “Results invalid due to baseline differences” – False. Baseline characteristics (age, lung function, smoking status, meds) are very similar; randomization was successful.  </p></br><p>c. “Relevant only for younger patients” – Age 77 vs mean 65 is not a categorical difference; geriatric patients were included (SD ~7 y → many subjects in their 70s). Age alone does not limit applicability.  </p></br><p>e. “Prescribe BreatheEZ” – Fails to account for lack of evidence in mild-to-moderate COPD; could expose patient to cost and adverse effects without proven benefit.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Apply RCT results only when the study population matches your patient. This trial proves BreatheEZ helps patients with severe, frequently exacerbating COPD; it does not inform care for a mildly symptomatic patient with FEV₁ 75 % predicted.</p>
93. A 44-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 4-month history of dryness of her mouth. During this time, she often has consumed liquids to aid in swallowing food. She has not had a sore throat or fever. She has not sustained head or neck trauma. She has rheumatoid arthritis. Her medications are low-dose prednisone and ibuprofen. Vital signs are within normal limits. Skin turgor is normal. Examination shows erythematous, dry oral mucosa and atrophy of the filiform papillae on the dorsum of the tongue. Results of serum anti-Ro and anti-La antibody assays are positive. The patient is given water to drink. Which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Amitriptyline</br>b. Cyclophosphamide</br>c. Hydrochlorothiazide</br>d. Nifedipine</br>e. Pilocarpine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>E. Pilocarpine</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• 44-year-old woman with 4 months of “dry mouth” (xerostomia) → classic sicca symptom<br /></br>• Needs liquids to swallow, dry erythematous oral mucosa, loss of filiform papillae → objective oral dryness<br /></br>• Positive anti-Ro (SSA) and anti-La (SSB) antibodies + history of autoimmune disease (rheumatoid arthritis) → primary or secondary Sjögren syndrome<br /></br>• No evidence of dehydration (normal skin turgor, normal vitals) or medication-induced dryness (low-dose prednisone, NSAID rarely cause severe xerostomia)</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Identify syndrome → Sjögren.<br /></br>Step 2: What bothers patient most? Symptomatic xerostomia impairing swallowing.<br /></br>Step 3: First-line symptomatic therapy = muscarinic (M3) agonist to stimulate residual salivary gland tissue ⇒ pilocarpine (or cevimeline).<br /></br>Core takeaway: Muscarinic agonists are used to treat Sjögren-related salivary and lacrimal gland hypofunction.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Autoimmune lymphocytic destruction of exocrine glands (lacrimal, salivary).<br /></br>• SSA/SSB antibodies, often with other autoimmune diseases (RA, SLE).<br /></br>• Leads to xerostomia, keratoconjunctivitis sicca, parotid enlargement, dental caries.<br /></br>• Residual parasympathetic innervation remains; stimulating M3 receptors can increase watery secretions.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls</br>1. Symptomatic:<br /></br>   – Artificial tears, sugar-free gum, saliva substitutes.<br /></br>   – Muscarinic agonists: pilocarpine (5 mg TID) or cevimeline (30 mg TID).<br /></br>2. Systemic disease (arthritis, vasculitis): hydroxychloroquine, low-dose steroids, immunosuppressants if severe.<br /></br>3. Diagnostics: Schirmer test, salivary flow rate, minor salivary gland lip biopsy for focal lymphocytic sialadenitis.</p></br><p>Memorization Tip</br>“Pilo-saliva”: Pilocarpine pulls saliva (and tears) out—think of “pilo” like “pillow soaked with drool.”</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Rundown</br>A. Amitriptyline – Tricyclic antidepressant with strong anticholinergic effects; would worsen xerostomia, not treat it.<br /></br>B. Cyclophosphamide – Alkylating agent used for severe systemic vasculitis or lupus nephritis; excessive toxicity for simple sicca symptoms.<br /></br>C. Hydrochlorothiazide – Diuretic; can cause drying and dehydration, aggravating symptoms. No role here.<br /></br>D. Nifedipine – Dihydropyridine CCB used for Raynaud phenomenon in scleroderma; does not increase salivary flow and may cause gingival hyperplasia.<br /></br>E. Pilocarpine – Only choice that directly stimulates muscarinic receptors to boost salivary and lacrimal secretion, treating Sjögren xerostomia. (Correct)</p></br><p>Key Point</br>In Sjögren syndrome, relieve xerostomia with parasympathomimetic agents like pilocarpine or cevimeline—avoid drugs with anticholinergic properties.</p>
94. A 27-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 1, at 12 weeks' gestation comes to the physician for her first prenatal visit. Eighteen months ago, her first pregnancy ended in preterm low-transverse cesarean delivery at 32 weeks' gestation because of fetal bradycardia following the onset of contractions. A Pap smear 16 m nths ago showed a low-grade squamous intraepithelial lesion; she has not had a Pap smear since then. Three years ago, she underwent loop electrosurgical excision of the cervical transformation zone for cervical intraepithelial neoplasia CIN 3. She has a 10-year history of cocaine use but has not used cocaine during the past 12 months. She used cocaine intermittently during her first pregnancy. She smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 10 years but quit 6 months ago. She is 163 cm 5 ft 4 in tall. She weighed 49 kg 108 lb prior to her pregnanc~t BMI was 18 kg/m ~. She has had a 1.8-kg 4-lb weight gain during her pregnancy. Examination shows a uterus consistent in size with a 12-week gestation. The fetal heart rate is reassuring. This patient is at greatest risk for which of the following complications during pregnancy?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Abruptio placentae</br>b. Fetal growth restriction</br>c. Placenta previa</br>d. Preterm delivery</br>e. Uterine rupture</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Preterm delivery  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Prior obstetric history – Spontaneous preterm birth at 32 weeks is the single strongest predictor of preterm birth in later pregnancies.<br /></br>• Cervical surgery – Prior LEEP further weakens cervical stroma → ↑ risk for cervical insufficiency and preterm labor.<br /></br>• Low prepregnancy BMI (18 kg/m²) and remote cocaine/tobacco use are additional (but smaller) contributors to preterm birth.<br /></br>Key pattern: “Previous spontaneous preterm delivery + history of cervical excisional procedure” → think recurrent preterm birth.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Identify major modifiable/non-modifiable risk factors in the vignette.<br /></br>2. Compare the magnitude of risk conferred by each factor to specific pregnancy complications.<br /></br>3. Recognize that a prior spontaneous preterm delivery confers a 15–50 % recurrence risk (highest in this list).<br /></br>Core takeaway: Previous spontaneous preterm birth is the most powerful risk factor for another preterm birth; management often includes progesterone and/or cervical-length surveillance.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Preterm birth (&lt;37 weeks) mechanisms:<br /></br>• Spontaneous preterm labor (uterine contractions)<br /></br>• Preterm premature rupture of membranes (PPROM)<br /></br>• Iatrogenic (e.g., severe pre-eclampsia)<br /></br>Risk factors:<br /></br>– Strongest: Prior preterm delivery, short cervix (&lt;25 mm), multiple gestation<br /></br>– Others: Cervical surgery, uterine anomalies, low BMI, smoking, cocaine, infection, vaginal bleeding.<br /></br>Recurrence risk rises as gestational age of the prior preterm birth decreases and with the number of prior events.</p></br><p>Treatment / Prevention<br /></br>• 17-α-hydroxyprogesterone IM weekly from 16–36 weeks if prior spontaneous preterm birth.<br /></br>• Serial transvaginal cervical-length measurement beginning at 16–24 weeks.<br /></br>• Vaginal progesterone or cerclage if short cervix.<br /></br>• Lifestyle optimization (stop smoking/cocaine, weight gain).</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Preterm once, preterm twice”: A prior spontaneous delivery &lt;37 wks is the red flag for recurrence.<br /></br>“LEEP ⇒ Less Cervix ⇒ Labor Early.”</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Abruptio placentae – Strongly linked to current cocaine use, hypertension, trauma. She quit cocaine a year ago and is normotensive; risk still exists but is lower than preterm birth risk.<br /></br>b. Fetal growth restriction – Associated with ongoing smoking, hypertension, placental insufficiency. She quit smoking and has a normal BP.<br /></br>c. Placenta previa – Risk rises with prior cesarean sections and multiparity, but a single low-transverse section confers only ~1 % risk—not as high as preterm recurrence.<br /></br>e. Uterine rupture – Mainly with prior classical (vertical) cesarean or extensive myomectomy; low-transverse scar has a rupture risk &lt;1 %.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Choosing abruption because of past cocaine use—USMLE tests CURRENT use, not remote. Always weigh the relative risk magnitude; prior spontaneous preterm birth is king.</p>
95. A 13-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her parents because of changes in behavior during the past 6 months. Her parents say that during this time she has been hostile and unpleasant, frequently crying and screaming about being treated unfairly no matter what they do. She also has alienated her friends. Her grades have declined from mostly B's to C's and D's, and she has stopped participating in sports. The patient says she is tired and does not sleep; her parents report she is sleeping more than usual. They add that she has been eating more, resulting in a 10-kg 22-lb. weight gain, which bothers her. They say she was a worrier when she was younger, but her worrying has decreased during the years. The patient is at the 45th percentile for height and 60th percentile for weight. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination, she describes her mood as okay but appears dysphoric and has an irritable affect. She looks at her hands and responds slowly using monosyllabic words. She says, I don't think I'm depressed. I'm bored and angry because no one likes me. I used to want to be an actress, but nothing ever works out for me. I could do it if my parents would let me. Her thought process is goal directed. She says that adults, especially her parents, do not understand her and make unreasonable demands. There is no evidence of psychosis. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Bipolar disorder</br>b. Borderline personality disorder</br>c. Generalized anxiety disorder</br>d. Impulse control disorder</br>e. Major depressive disorder</br>F. Oppositional defiant disorder</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Major depressive disorder  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Duration → “past 6 months” of continuous symptoms satisfies MDD time requirement (≥ 2 weeks; here it’s much longer).<br /></br>• Core mood symptoms → hostility/irritability in adolescents often substitutes for the “sad” mood seen in adults; she appears “dysphoric,” cries frequently, feels “nothing ever works out for me,” and has clear anhedonia (stopped sports, acting dreams feel pointless).<br /></br>• Neuro-vegetative changes → hypersomnia, hyperphagia with 10-kg weight gain (“atypical” features).<br /></br>• Functional impairment → falling grades, lost friends, no extracurriculars.<br /></br>• No history of manic/hypomanic episodes, psychosis, or substance use to suggest another mood or psychotic disorder.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify a pervasive change in baseline mood/behavior.<br /></br>2. Check DSM-5 MDD criteria: ≥ 5 of SIGECAPS for ≥ 2 weeks and functional decline.<br /></br>   – Sleep ↑, Interest ↓, Guilt/worthlessness (“no one likes me”), Energy ↓ (“tired”), Concentration ↓ (grades fall), Appetite ↑/weight ↑, Psychomotor retardation (“responds slowly”), Suicidality not reported.<br /></br>3. Rule out mania/hypomania → no elevated mood, no decreased need for sleep, no grandiosity, no goal-directed hyper-activity.<br /></br>4. Rule out primary externalizing disorders (ODD, impulse control) because vegetative and mood symptoms dominate.  </p></br><p>Take-home: Irritability + atypical neuro-vegetative changes + functional decline in an adolescent = Major Depressive Disorder until proven otherwise.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Underlying monoamine dysregulation (serotonin, norepinephrine, dopamine).<br /></br>• Genetic predisposition + psychosocial stressors during adolescence can precipitate first episode.<br /></br>• Atypical subtype involves increased REM latency sensitivity to rejection and is more common in females.  </p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Safety screen for suicidality EVERY visit.<br /></br>2. First-line: Combination of<br /></br>   • Psychotherapy (CBT or interpersonal therapy)<br /></br>   • SSRIs (fluoxetine is FDA-approved down to age 8; escitalopram age 12).<br /></br>3. If partial response → optimize dose/duration → switch or augment (bupropion, SNRI) → consider atypical antipsychotic augmentation if severe.<br /></br>4. Baseline labs only if clinical suspicion (TSH, CBC, CMP).  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>SIGECAPS: Sleep, Interest, Guilt, Energy, Concentration, Appetite, Psychomotor, Suicide.<br /></br>“Irritable teen with ↑Sleep/↑Snacks” = atypical depression.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Bipolar disorder – Requires at least one manic/hypomanic episode (elevated/expansive mood, ↓need for sleep, pressured speech, grandiosity, risky behavior). None present; patient is sluggish, not hyperactive.<br /></br>b. Borderline personality disorder – Personality disorders are diagnosed ≥ 18 y. Transient mood swings plus chronic interpersonal instability and self-harm would be expected; no self-injury, frantic abandonment fears, or identity disturbance here.<br /></br>c. Generalized anxiety disorder – Would show ≥ 6 months of excessive, uncontrolled worry plus restlessness, tension, sleep disturbance but without the vegetative signs of depression (weight gain, anhedonia) or marked functional drop-off in mood domain. Her childhood worrying actually improved.<br /></br>d. Impulse-control disorder (e.g., intermittent explosive disorder) – Characterized by discrete episodes of aggression disproportionate to stressor; does not explain pervasive vegetative and mood symptoms.<br /></br>f. Oppositional defiant disorder – Hostile/defiant behavior toward authority for ≥ 6 months without serious violations, but vegetative symptoms and anhedonia are absent in ODD. Declining mood, appetite, and sleep changes point to internal mood disorder, not a purely behavioral one.  </p></br><p>Bottom Line: In adolescents, persistent irritability plus atypical neuro-vegetative changes and functional decline = Major depressive disorder.</p>
96. A 15-year-old boy is brought to the office because of a 2-month history of increasingly frequent left lower thigh pain. The pain began after the patient injured his leg during a soccer game. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Examination shows moderate tenderness of the lateral distal aspect of the left femur. His serum alkaline phosphatase activity is 550 U/L N=130-525, and serum lactate dehydrogenase activity is 360 U/L N=120-330. An x-ray of the left thigh is shown. In addition to an MRI of the left femur, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Biopsy of the affected area</br>b. CT scan of the thorax</br>c. Gene expression profile</br>d. PET scan of the skeleton</br>e. Three-phase bone scan</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Biopsy of the affected area  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “15-year-old boy” + “distal femur metaphysis” + “sunburst/ Codman triangle” appearance on the (unshown) radiograph = classic pattern for a primary high-grade osteosarcoma, the most common malignant bone tumor in adolescents.<br /></br>• Progressive local pain &amp; tenderness, ↑ serum alkaline phosphatase and LDH (markers of bone turnover and tumor burden) further support an aggressive bone-forming malignancy.<br /></br>• Key clue: The question asks for “the most appropriate NEXT step in diagnosis” after MRI is ordered. MRI defines local extent, neurovascular involvement, and possible skip lesions but does not establish histology.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify suspicious bone lesion on plain film.<br /></br>2. Order MRI to delineate intra-osseous vs extra-osseous spread.<br /></br>3. Secure tissue diagnosis with a core-needle or open biopsy taken along a track that can be excised during definitive surgery.<br /></br>4. Only AFTER histologic confirmation do we perform staging studies (CT chest and bone scan/PET).</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Any presumed primary malignant bone tumor requires a biopsy before staging or treatment. Never treat or stage a bone cancer without histologic proof.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Osteosarcoma<br /></br>• Etiology: Malignant proliferation of osteoblasts producing osteoid.<br /></br>• Risk factors: Rapid bone growth (teenagers), hereditary retinoblastoma (RB mutation), Li-Fraumeni (p53), Paget disease, prior radiation.<br /></br>• Typical sites: Metaphysis of long bones (distal femur &gt; proximal tibia &gt; proximal humerus).<br /></br>• Radiograph: Sunburst pattern, periosteal elevation (Codman triangle).<br /></br>• Labs: Often elevated ALP, LDH due to high bone turnover and tumor necrosis.<br /></br>• Treatment: Neoadjuvant chemotherapy (e.g., high-dose methotrexate, doxorubicin, cisplatin) → limb-salvage wide resection → postoperative chemo. Prognosis correlates with response to neoadjuvant therapy and presence of pulmonary metastases.</p></br><p>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls<br /></br>1. Imaging sequence = X-ray → MRI (local) → Biopsy → CT chest + bone scan/PET (staging).<br /></br>2. Biopsy tract must be planned with orthopaedic oncologist so that entire tract is removed at time of limb-salvage surgery to prevent seeding.<br /></br>3. Staging:<br /></br>   • CT chest = most common site of metastasis (hematogenous spread).<br /></br>   • Bone scan or PET = skip lesions / skeletal mets.  </p></br><p>Memory Tip<br /></br>“Osteosarcoma? Get the ‘O-Site’ first”<br /></br>O = Obtain MRI for local extent<br /></br>Site = Sample It (biopsy the site) before you Scan elsewhere</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. CT scan of the thorax – Done after biopsy to look for lung mets. Premature without tissue confirmation.<br /></br>C. Gene expression profile – Used in soft-tissue sarcomas or targeted therapies, not standard for initial osteosarcoma work-up.<br /></br>D. PET scan of the skeleton – Whole-body staging tool similar to bone scan; again indicated only after diagnosis is secure.<br /></br>E. Three-phase bone scan – Helps detect multifocal disease/skip lesions but follows histologic confirmation.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Jumping to staging scans (CT chest, bone scan) before proving malignancy histologically. Always “biopsy before you stage.”</p>
97. A previously healthy 18-month-old boy is brought to the emergency department because of a 6-day history of fever. His temperature is 38.5 C 101.3 F pulse is 100/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 95/60 mm Hg. Examination shows cracked, red lips and palmar erythema and edema. Cervical lymph nodes are enlarged. There is pharyngeal erythema and bilateral conjunctivitis without injection adjacent to the corneal limbus. Rapid streptococcal testing is negative. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hematocrit</br>Leukocyte count</br>Segmented neutrophils</br>Lymphocytes</br>Monocytes</br>Platelet count</br>Erythrocyte sedimentation rate</br>In addition to intravenous immune globulin, which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Oral ibuprofen every 6 hours as needed to bring fever below 38.1  C 100.6 F</br>b. Oral high-dose aspirin therapy</br>c. 5-Day course of oral prednisone</br>d. 10-Day course of oral amoxicillin</br>e. 8-Week course of daily, low-dose acetaminophen therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Oral high-dose aspirin therapy  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Key clues pointing straight to Kawasaki disease:<br /></br>• Age: 18-month-old (infants/toddlers)<br /></br>• Fever ≥5 days (6-day history)<br /></br>• ≥4 of the 5 major clinical features (“CRASH”):</br>  – C: bilateral non-purulent conjunctivitis that spares limbus<br /></br>  – R: polymorphous rash on palms/soles (palmar erythema/edema)<br /></br>  – A: cervical lymphAdenopathy<br /></br>  – S: “strawberry” cracked red lips + pharyngeal erythema (oral mucositis)<br /></br>  – H: Hand/foot edema/erythema<br /></br>• Labs (classic although values not displayed): leukocytosis with neutrophilia, thrombocytosis, ↑ESR.  </p></br><p>Putting it together: Persistent fever + ≥4 classic features = Kawasaki disease → treat to prevent coronary artery aneurysms.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Recognize Kawasaki pattern (“CRASH &amp; burn”).<br /></br>Step 2: Recall standard treatment bundle:<br /></br>  1. Single dose IVIG (2 g/kg) – already in stem.<br /></br>  2. High-dose aspirin (80–100 mg/kg/day divided q6h) until afebrile, then low-dose (3–5 mg/kg/day) for 6–8 weeks.<br /></br>Purpose: • IVIG rapidly defervesces and ↓ coronary aneurysm risk.<br /></br>           • Aspirin provides anti-inflammatory effect initially, then anti-platelet effect during healing phase.<br /></br>Therefore oral high-dose aspirin is the only correct adjunct.  </p></br><p>Take-away: Kawasaki disease is the ONLY pediatric febrile illness in which aspirin is first-line despite Reye risk.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Medium-vessel, necrotizing vasculitis of childhood (auto-immune post-viral trigger).<br /></br>• Highest morbidity = coronary artery aneurysms → thrombosis, MI.<br /></br>• IVIG modulates immune response; aspirin inhibits platelet aggregation and COX-mediated inflammation.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Follow-up<br /></br>1. IVIG + high-dose aspirin (then low-dose for 6–8 wk).<br /></br>2. Baseline &amp; 2- to 6-week echocardiography to detect aneurysms.<br /></br>3. Refractory fever → add second IVIG dose or corticosteroids.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CRASH &amp; Burn”<br /></br>C – Conjunctivitis<br /></br>R – Rash (polymorphous)<br /></br>A – Adenopathy (cervical)<br /></br>S – Strawberry tongue (mucositis)<br /></br>H – Hand/foot edema/erythema<br /></br>Burn – fever ≥5 days  </p></br><p>Aspirin is spelled K-A-W-A:<br /></br>Keep<br /></br>Aspirin<br /></br>With<br /></br>Aneurysm-preventing IVIG  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Oral ibuprofen prn fever: NSAID but dose is intermittent/low and lacks antiplatelet benefit; ibuprofen is NOT proven to reduce coronary aneurysm risk.<br /></br>c. 5-day oral prednisone: Steroids reserved for IVIG-resistant cases; not initial therapy—may ↑ risk of aneurysm if used alone.<br /></br>d. 10-day amoxicillin: For group A strep pharyngitis (rapid strep negative, and mucositis here is viral/vasculitic).<br /></br>e. 8-week low-dose acetaminophen: Acetaminophen has no anti-inflammatory/antiplatelet effect—does not prevent coronary complications.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Core “Exam Pearl”<br /></br>When a febrile child &lt;5 yrs has conjunctivitis + mucositis + extremity changes + rash + adenopathy, think Kawasaki. Treat immediately with IVIG + high-dose aspirin to avert life-threatening coronary artery aneurysms.</p>
98. A 35-year-old man, who works as an orthopaedic surgeon, comes to the office because of a 1-day history of severe low back pain after lifting heavy furniture. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. He requests a prescription for oxycodone because he is scheduled to give a lecture at a national meeting tomorrow and is worried he will be in too much pain to participate without medication. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management by the physician?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Obtain an MRI of the spine</br>b. Order urine toxicology screening</br>c. Prescribe one tablet of oxycodone</br>d. Report the patient to the state medical board</br>e. Screen the patient for substance use disorder</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Screen the patient for substance-use disorder (SUD)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – What are the clues?<br /></br>• 1-day history of low-back pain after lifting furniture → classic benign, acute mechanical back strain (&lt; 6 weeks, no red-flag features).<br /></br>• Normal exam and vitals → no neurologic deficit, fever, trauma, or malignancy signs; therefore no immediate imaging or opioids are needed.<br /></br>• Patient is a physician specifically requesting oxycodone “so he can lecture” → drug-seeking red flag (requests a particular opioid, wants it urgently, fears impaired performance).<br /></br>These features together signal the clinician must first evaluate the risk of misuse before considering any opioid prescription.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning – Step-by-step<br /></br>1. Establish that the pain is uncomplicated acute low-back strain → treat with NSAIDs/acetaminophen, heat, activity as tolerated.<br /></br>2. Because the patient requests a Schedule II opioid by name, guideline-directed care (CDC, Federation of State Medical Boards, USPSTF) says:<br /></br>   • Assess risk factors for misuse or addiction (personal or family history, mental-health disorders).<br /></br>   • Screen for SUD before initiating any opioid.<br /></br>3. Only after screening—and only if benefits outweigh risks—would further steps such as urine drug screen, prescription monitoring, or writing a limited Rx be considered.<br /></br>Key takeaway: Before prescribing a controlled substance, first screen for SUD to ensure safe, appropriate therapy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology &amp; Guideline Review<br /></br>• Acute mechanical low-back pain is usually due to paraspinal muscle or ligament strain. Natural history: &gt; 90 % improve within 4-6 weeks with conservative measures.<br /></br>• Opioids are not first-line; they are reserved for severe pain unresponsive to NSAIDs and only after risk assessment.<br /></br>• Screening tools: COMM, ORT, SBIRT, AUDIT-C; even brief questionnaires fulfill the requirement.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line for acute, uncomplicated LBP:<br /></br>1. NSAIDs (ibuprofen, naproxen) or acetaminophen.<br /></br>2. Physical activity, stretching, heat/ice.<br /></br>3. Reassurance; red-flag follow-up instructions.<br /></br>Opioids: short course (≤3-day supply) only if severe pain persists AND SUD screen negative AND patient education given.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“SOAPP” before opioids:<br /></br>• Screen (SUD)<br /></br>• Obtain risk factors<br /></br>• Assess function/pain goals<br /></br>• Prescribe lowest dose/shortest time<br /></br>• Plan follow-up / PMP check</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Obtain an MRI of the spine<br /></br>   – Imaging is unnecessary in acute (&lt;6 weeks) low-back pain without red flags (neurologic deficit, infection, fracture, cancer). Overuse leads to incidental findings and costs.  </p></br><p>b. Order urine toxicology screening<br /></br>   – Urine drug testing is useful if you decide to prescribe chronic opioids or if you suspect diversion, but it is not the FIRST step. You still must screen for SUD and weigh risks-benefits before any opioid is considered.  </p></br><p>c. Prescribe one tablet of oxycodone<br /></br>   – Even a single opioid dose is inappropriate without risk assessment; non-opioid therapy is adequate. “One tablet” does not remove addiction/diversion risk.  </p></br><p>d. Report the patient to the state medical board<br /></br>   – Requesting oxycodone is not itself reportable misconduct. Breaching confidentiality without cause is unethical. The correct response is to evaluate, not punish.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In any patient—especially one requesting a specific opioid—first screen for substance-use disorder before considering an opioid prescription.</p>
99. A 37-year-old woman comes to the physician in January for immunizations 6 weeks before beginning her new job as a nurse's aide in a skilled nursing care facility. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She has no known allergies. Four years ago, she completed the hepatitis 8 vaccine series and received a tetanus booster. She lives with her husband and two healthy school-aged children. Examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is most appropriate to administer at this visit?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Hepatitis 8 booster</br>b. Hepatitis 8 immune globulin</br>c. Inactivated influenza virus vaccine</br>d. Pneu mococcal polysaccharide vaccine, 23-valent</br>e. No immunizations are indicated at this time</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Inactivated influenza virus vaccine  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>1. “37-year-old woman … January” – height of influenza season.<br /></br>2. “Beginning her new job as a nurse’s aide in a skilled-nursing facility” – she will be a health-care worker with daily exposure to elderly, high-risk patients.<br /></br>3. “Completed the hepatitis B series four years ago” – already immune; booster not needed in immunocompetent adults.<br /></br>4. “Received a tetanus booster” – Tdap/Td good for 10 yrs.<br /></br>5. No chronic illnesses → no special indications for pneumococcal vaccine.  </p></br><p>The only routinely recommended vaccine she has not yet received for this season is the yearly influenza vaccine. Health-care personnel should receive an annual influenza immunization (inactivated formulation for all adults; live-attenuated is reserved for healthy, non-pregnant patients 2–49 y o and is not routinely used in HCWs).</p></br><p>Core Takeaway<br /></br>Every adult should receive the inactivated influenza vaccine each autumn/winter. This is especially critical for health-care workers and those in contact with nursing-home residents.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. List required adult vaccines.<br /></br>   • Tdap once, then Td/Tdap q10 yrs<br /></br>   • Influenza annually<br /></br>   • Complete Hep B series if not immune<br /></br>   • MMR, varicella if seronegative<br /></br>   • Zoster ≥50 yrs, pneumococcal ≥65 yrs or high-risk conditions<br /></br>2. Subtract what the patient already has (Hep B series, Td booster).<br /></br>3. Assess age and risk factors → no indication for pneumococcal.<br /></br>4. Result: inactivated influenza is the only outstanding recommendation.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology High-Yield Review<br /></br>• Influenza virus undergoes frequent antigenic drift → waning antibody match each year.<br /></br>• Inactivated (killed) vaccine induces neutralizing IgG against hemagglutinin; cannot cause infection.<br /></br>• Annual vaccination reduces transmission to vulnerable nursing-home residents, a major public-health goal (“herd protection”).</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Preventive Details<br /></br>• Vaccine: Quadrivalent inactivated influenza (IM).<br /></br>• Timing: As soon as available (Sept–Jan), ideally ≥2 weeks before exposure.<br /></br>• Contra-indications: Severe allergy to previous influenza vaccine components (not to egg alone).</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Flu for the Crew” – Anyone working with a crew of patients (health-care workers) needs yearly flu shot.<br /></br>T-10, Flu-1, HB-3 (Td every 10 yrs, Flu every 1 yr, Hep B 3-dose series). Visualize a clock: 10 o’clock (Td), 1 o’clock (Flu), 3 o’clock (Hep B).</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Hepatitis B booster<br /></br>• Not required in immunocompetent adults with documented 3-dose series; protective anti-HBs titers persist.</p></br><p>b. Hepatitis B immune globulin<br /></br>• Used only for post-exposure prophylaxis (e.g., needlestick from HBsAg-positive source) or for neonates of infected mothers.</p></br><p>d. Pneumococcal polysaccharide vaccine (PPSV23)<br /></br>• Indicated at age ≥65 or age 19–64 with specific risk factors (asplenia, immunocompromise, chronic heart/lung/liver disease, CSF leak, cochlear implant, smokers). A healthy 37-year-old does not qualify.</p></br><p>e. No immunizations indicated<br /></br>• Ignores the universal annual influenza recommendation, especially important for a health-care worker.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>For a healthy adult starting work in a nursing facility during flu season, the one mandatory vaccine is the annual inactivated influenza shot.</p>
100. A 23-year-old man is brought to the emergency department after driving his automobile into a bridge abutment. Paramedics state that at the scene his b ood pressure was 100/80 mm Hg, that he was alert, and that the steering wheel was broken. On examination in the emergency department now, he is stuporous, cyanotic, and hypotensive, and has distended neck veins. Breath sounds are absent bilaterally. The trachea is midline. Which of the following is the most appropriate initial management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Determine arterial blood gas values</br>b. Do pericardiocentesis</br>c. Insert needles into the left and right sides of the chest</br>d. Order x-rays of the chest</br>e. Order ECG</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>c. Insert needles into the left and right sides of the chest (bilateral emergent needle thoracostomy)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – “Buzz” &amp; Decision-Making Clues</br>• High-energy blunt chest trauma (steering wheel broken).<br /></br>• Progression from alert → stuporous, cyanotic, profound hypotension.<br /></br>• Distended neck veins → impaired venous return / obstructive shock.<br /></br>• Breath sounds absent on BOTH sides.<br /></br>• Trachea midline (no mediastinal shift seen when tension exists on both sides).<br /></br>Pattern: Bilateral tension pneumothoraces (“silent chest,” JVD, obstructive shock) after blunt trauma.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Step-by-Step Clinical Reasoning</br>1. Identify shock type: JVD + hypotension = obstructive shock (not hypovolemic).<br /></br>2. Differentiate causes of obstructive shock in trauma:<br /></br>   – Cardiac tamponade: JVD + hypotension + muffled heart sounds; breath sounds normal.<br /></br>   – Tension pneumothorax: JVD + hypotension + absent breath sounds, hyperresonance, tracheal dev (may be absent if bilateral).<br /></br>3. Bilateral absence of breath sounds in this setting ⇒ assume bilateral tension pneumothoraces until proven otherwise.<br /></br>4. Tension pneumothorax is an IMMEDIATE life threat → treat first, diagnose later.<br /></br>5. Definitive first step: needle decompression (large-bore needle 14–16 G into 2nd ICS mid-clavicular OR 5th ICS anterior axillary); because findings are bilateral, decompress both sides.</p></br><p>Core Takeaway</br>In any unstable trauma patient with JVD + hypotension + absent breath sounds, perform emergent needle thoracostomy before any diagnostic test. Bilateral findings → decompress both sides.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Mini Pathophysiology Review</br>• Lung injury allows air to enter pleural space on inspiration but not leave on expiration.<br /></br>• Intrathoracic pressure rises → ipsilateral lung collapse, mediastinal compression, ↓venous return (JVD, hypotension), hypoxia.<br /></br>• If both pleural cavities are under pressure, mediastinum may stay midline.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics Pearls</br>1. Immediate: Needle thoracostomy → converts tension to simple pneumothorax.<br /></br>2. Follow-up: Tube thoracostomy (chest tube) in 5th ICS mid-axillary line.<br /></br>3. AFTER stabilization: CXR to confirm placement and evaluate additional injury.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Memory Aids</br>“NEEDLE before NEED to look.”<br /></br>N – Neck veins distended<br /></br>E – Expanding intrathoracic pressure (hypotension)<br /></br>E – Evident absent breath sounds<br /></br>D – Don’t delay for diagnostics<br /></br>L – Large-bore needle<br /></br>E – Emergent!</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Determine arterial blood gas values<br /></br>   • Diagnostic, not therapeutic. ABG cannot correct obstructive shock; patient will arrest while you wait.</p></br><p>b. Do pericardiocentesis<br /></br>   • Would treat tamponade. Tamponade presents with muffled heart sounds &amp; usually NORMAL breath sounds; absent breath sounds bilaterally argue against it.</p></br><p>d. Order x-rays of the chest<br /></br>   • Classic trap. Imaging comes only AFTER life-saving intervention in ATLS. Moving an unstable patient to radiology delays care.</p></br><p>e. Order ECG<br /></br>   • Helpful for arrhythmias/myocardial injury but provides no immediate life-saving benefit in this scenario; delays definitive treatment.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Exam Tip</br>For ATLS questions, any hemodynamically unstable patient with signs of tension pneumothorax must have immediate needle decompression. Do NOT be lured into “get a CXR” or “check ABG” options.</p>
101. A 62-year-old man with large cell non-Hodgkin lymphoma is brought to the emergency department because of a 3-day history of fatigue, headache, dizziness, and facial flushing that is exacerbated by bending forward. His only medication is omeprazole. On arrival, he appears slightly cyanotic. His temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 96/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 140/90 mm Hg. Examination shows brawny edema of the neck and face. The veins over the anterior chest and shoulders are dilated. Breath sounds are normal. Heart sounds are distant. A CT scan of the chest shows clea progression in the size and distribution of mediastinal and abdominal lymphadenopathy; the inferior vena cava is posteriorly displaced. Intravenous hydrocortisone therapy is started. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Tissue plasminogen activator therapy</br>b. Plasmapheresis</br>c. Carotid stent placement</br>d. Radiation therapy</br>e. Thoracotomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Radiation therapy  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Known mediastinal cancer (large-cell NHL) → strong risk for mass effect on great vessels.<br /></br>• “Facial flushing, cyanosis, brawny edema of neck/face, dilated chest/shoulder veins, symptoms worse when bending forward” = classic Superior Vena Cava (SVC) syndrome.<br /></br>• CT: marked progression of mediastinal lymphadenopathy; no intraluminal clot described ⇒ obstruction is external compression, not thrombosis.<br /></br>• Steroid (hydrocortisone) already started ⇒ temporizing measure in lymphoma to shrink tumor. The next definitive, rapid-acting therapy for malignant SVC obstruction is urgent radiation directed at the mediastinum (± chemotherapy depending on histology).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Recognize SVC syndrome pattern.<br /></br>Step 2: Identify etiology – extrinsic compression by rapidly growing lymphoma.<br /></br>Step 3: Treat quickly to relieve venous congestion and prevent airway/cerebral compromise.<br /></br>➜ In malignancy-related SVC obstruction, first-line definitive management = emergent radiation therapy (or chemotherapy when tumor is highly chemo-sensitive). Endovascular stent is another option but not listed. Therefore choose radiation therapy.<br /></br>Take-home: Facial plethora + distended chest wall veins in a patient with intrathoracic malignancy = SVC syndrome → give steroids (if lymphoma) and start emergent mediastinal radiation.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• SVC drains head, neck, upper extremities. Extrinsic compression ↑ venous pressure proximal to obstruction → facial/neck edema, jugular &amp; chest wall venous distention, cyanosis/plethora, headaches from ↑ ICP.<br /></br>• Large-cell NHL can rapidly enlarge in mediastinum; steroid sensitivity allows temporary shrinkage.<br /></br>• Without relief, patients risk airway compromise, cerebral edema, syncope.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Temporizing: head elevation, O₂, IV steroids (dexamethasone/hydrocortisone) in lymphoma.<br /></br>2. Definitive:<br /></br>   • Radiation therapy—rapid shrinkage of radiosensitive tumors (small-cell lung Ca, lymphoma).<br /></br>   • Chemotherapy—when tumor is highly chemo-responsive (e.g., small-cell lung, lymphoma).<br /></br>   • Endovascular SVC stent—if severe symptoms or when radiation/chemo are delayed/ineffective.<br /></br>   • Anticoagulation/thrombolysis—only if intraluminal thrombus present (often catheter-related).<br /></br>Imaging: Contrast CT or MR venography confirms site/nature (compression vs. clot).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“SVC = Swollen, Veiny, Cyanotic” head &amp; chest.<br /></br>“SVeeC me RAPID” → Radiosensitive tumor? Administer Prompt Irradiation Downtown (mediastinum).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Tissue plasminogen activator: For acute thrombotic SVC obstruction; CT did not show thrombus—mass effect instead.<br /></br>b. Plasmapheresis: Used for hyperviscosity (Waldenström macroglobulinemia, MM), TTP, MG crisis—does not relieve mechanical venous obstruction.<br /></br>c. Carotid stent placement: Treats extracranial carotid atherosclerotic disease (stroke prophylaxis); unrelated vessel &amp; pathology.<br /></br>e. Thoracotomy: Surgical resection rarely used for lymphoma; far more invasive, delayed, and unnecessary when non-surgical modalities (radiation/chemo) are highly effective.  </p></br><p>Key Point: In malignant SVC syndrome from a radiosensitive tumor, give steroids for temporary relief, then emergent radiation therapy to decompress the SVC and rapidly reverse symptoms.</p>
102.
