A 42-year-old woman comes to the physician because of increasingly severe right heel pain and swelling since she stepped off balance onto the foot 4 weeks a o; the pain increases with weight-bearing and decreases with rest. She has systemic lupus erythematosus and chronic renal failure. She receives hemodialysis. Her only medication is hydroxychloroquine. Vital signs are within normal limits. The skin over the lower extremities is intact. Dorsalis pedis pulses are 1+ bilaterally. The posterior tuberosity of the right calcaneus is tender to palpation. Motor examination shows no abnormalities. There is pes planus bilaterally. Sensation to light touch is intact over the lower extremities. An x-ray of the right ankle is shown. Serum studies are most likely to show an increased concentration of which of the following in this patient?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Calcitonin</br>b. 1,25-Dihydroxyvitamin D</br>c. 25-Hydroxyvitamin D</br>d. Ionized calcium</br>e. Parathyroid hormone</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Parathyroid hormone (PTH)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 42-year-old woman with chronic renal failure on hemodialysis → kidney cannot 1-α-hydroxylate 25-OH-vitamin D to 1,25-(OH)₂-vitamin D.<br /></br>• ↓ 1,25-(OH)₂-vitamin D and phosphate retention → ↓ serum ionized Ca²⁺.<br /></br>• Resulting hypocalcemia chronically stimulates the parathyroid glands → secondary hyperparathyroidism.<br /></br>• Bone pain after trivial trauma + X-ray (usually shows cortical thinning / sub-periosteal resorption / pathologic fracture) = renal osteodystrophy.</p></br><p>Key pattern: “ESRD + bone pain/fracture” → think secondary hyperparathyroidism → ↑PTH.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-by-Step)<br /></br>1. Identify underlying disease: End-stage renal disease (ESRD) on dialysis.<br /></br>2. Recall biochemical consequences of ESRD:<br /></br>   a. Phosphate retention → hyperphosphatemia.<br /></br>   b. Loss of 1-α-hydroxylase → ↓ 1,25-(OH)₂-vitamin D.<br /></br>   c. Both lead to ↓ serum Ca²⁺.<br /></br>3. Physiologic response: Parathyroids compensate → sustained ↑ PTH (secondary hyperparathyroidism).<br /></br>4. Skeletal effects: High-turnover bone disease (osteitis fibrosa cystica) → bone pain, pathologic fractures (heel).<br /></br>Conclusion: The lab abnormality most consistently elevated is PTH.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Chronic kidney disease → hypocalcemia + hyperphosphatemia → secondary hyperparathyroidism (↑PTH).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Kidneys normally convert 25-OH-vitamin D → 1,25-(OH)₂-vitamin D via 1-α-hydroxylase.<br /></br>• Renal failure → ↓ calcitriol, ↓ intestinal Ca²⁺ absorption, &amp; PO₄³⁻ retention.<br /></br>• Hypocalcemia + hyperphosphatemia → ↑ PTH secretion (secondary hyperparathyroidism).<br /></br>• Chronic ↑ PTH → ↑ osteoclastic activity → sub-periosteal bone resorption, bone cysts, “salt-and-pepper” skull, pathologic fractures = renal osteodystrophy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Labs: ↑ PTH, ↑ PO₄³⁻, ↓/normal Ca²⁺, ↓ 1,25-(OH)₂-vitamin D.<br /></br>• Management: Dietary phosphate restriction, phosphate binders (sevelamer, calcium acetate), vitamin D analogs (calcitriol, alfacalcidol), calcimimetic (cinacalcet), parathyroidectomy if refractory.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Renal ruins the RENAL mnemonic”<br /></br>R – Retains phosphate<br /></br>E – Eliminates 1-α-Enzyme (↓ calcitriol)<br /></br>N – ↓ Number (ionized Ca²⁺)<br /></br>A – Activates (secondary) hyperparathyroidism → ↑PTH<br /></br>L – Lytic bone lesions / fractures</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Calcitonin – Produced by thyroid C-cells; elevated in medullary thyroid cancer, not in CKD.<br /></br>b. 1,25-Dihydroxyvitamin D – LOW (kidneys can’t activate vitamin D).<br /></br>c. 25-Hydroxyvitamin D – Normal or low depending on sun/diet, not elevated; kidneys don’t affect this storage form.<br /></br>d. Ionized calcium – Typically low or low-normal in ESRD due to ↓ calcitriol &amp; ↑ PO₄³⁻; would not be increased.<br /></br>e. Parathyroid hormone – Correct; rises as compensation for hypocalcemia &amp; hyperphosphatemia.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: confusing primary vs secondary hyperparathyroidism. Primary (parathyroid adenoma) gives ↑Ca²⁺ and ↓PO₄³⁻, whereas secondary (renal failure) gives ↓Ca²⁺, ↑PO₄³⁻, and ↑PTH.</p>
103. A 72-year-old woman comes to the physician for a routine follow-up examination. She has type 2 diabetes mellitus, hypertension, chronic kidney disease, and osteoarthritis of the knees. She says that she feels well except for mild swelling of her feet, which has made her shoes feel tighter. Her medications are lisinopril, naproxen, acetaminophen, and metformin. She is 170 cm 5 ft 7 in tall and weighs 81 kg 178 lb; BMI is 28 kg/m 2. She has gained 2.3 kg 5 lb since her last examination 1 month ago. Her temperature is 36.8 C 98.2 F, pulse is 72/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 112/62 mm Hg. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. There is 1+ pitting edema of the ankles. Her hemoglobin A 1c is 6.1  percent, and serum creatinine concentration is 2.1 mg/dl, unchanged from last year. Metformin therapy is discontinued. Which of the following is the most effective measure to improve this patient's renal function?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Add furosemide to the medication regimen</br>b. Add hydrochlorothiazide to the medication regimen</br>c. Discontinue acetaminophen</br>d. Discontinue lisinopril</br>e. Discontinue naproxen</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Discontinue naproxen</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis – what are the key clues?<br /></br>• Chronic kidney disease (CKD): baseline creatinine 2.1 mg/dL<br /></br>• Diabetes &amp; hypertension → diabetic nephropathy most likely etiology<br /></br>• Med list contains an ACE inhibitor (renal-protective) and an NSAID (naproxen)<br /></br>• New ankle edema + 5-lb weight gain over 1 month<br /></br>• No change in creatinine over the past year → still time to preserve remaining renal function</p></br><p>Pattern to recognize for the exam:<br /></br>“CKD + NSAID use + edema/weight gain” → think prostaglandin-dependent afferent arteriolar dilation being blocked → decreased GFR, sodium &amp; water retention, edema. Stop the NSAID.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify potentially reversible nephrotoxic exposure: naproxen.<br /></br>2. Ask: Will removing it improve remaining GFR? Yes—restores afferent vasodilation and eliminates ongoing ischemic injury.<br /></br>3. Consider the other options: they treat symptoms (diuretics) or worsen prognosis (stop ACE) or are irrelevant (acetaminophen).<br /></br>Core takeaway: NSAIDs are a common, often overlooked, cause of prerenal azotemia/CKD progression and edema in elderly diabetics; discontinuation is the single most effective intervention.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Kidney autoregulation:<br /></br>  – Afferent arteriole → dilated by PGE2 &amp; PGI2<br /></br>  – Efferent arteriole → constricted by angiotensin II<br /></br>• NSAIDs block COX → ↓ prostaglandins → constrict afferent arteriole → ↓ renal plasma flow &amp; GFR → acute interstitial nephritis or chronic ischemic injury; also ↑ Na⁺/H₂O retention → edema, ↑ BP.<br /></br>• ACE inhibitors (e.g., lisinopril) dilate the efferent arteriole, mildly ↓ GFR initially but slow diabetic nephropathy long-term by decreasing intraglomerular pressure.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line to slow diabetic CKD: tight glycemic control + ACEi/ARB.<br /></br>Stop all nephrotoxins: NSAIDs, IV contrast, aminoglycosides, amphotericin B, etc.<br /></br>Diuretics (loop/thiazide) can control edema but do NOT halt CKD progression.<br /></br>Acetaminophen is generally renal-safe at therapeutic doses.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“NSAIDs Nix the Afferent” – N for NSAID, A for Afferent constriction.<br /></br>Triple Whammy for AKI: ACE/ARB + diuretic + NSAID → always stop the NSAID first.  </p></br><p>Mnemonic COX blocks the “FlowX”: COX inhibition blocks renal blood “flow”.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Add furosemide<br /></br>   • Would reduce edema but not improve (and may even worsen) renal perfusion or CKD trajectory.  </p></br><p>b. Add hydrochlorothiazide<br /></br>   • Same as furosemide; plus thiazides lose potency when GFR &lt; 30 mL/min.  </p></br><p>c. Discontinue acetaminophen<br /></br>   • Therapeutic acetaminophen is hepatotoxic, not nephrotoxic; removing it offers no renal benefit.  </p></br><p>d. Discontinue lisinopril<br /></br>   • ACE inhibitors slow diabetic CKD progression by reducing intraglomerular pressure; stopping them accelerates decline and sacrifices BP/proteinuria control.  </p></br><p>e. Discontinue naproxen  ← only choice that removes an active nephrotoxin and meaningfully improves renal function.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In diabetics with CKD, ongoing NSAID use is a common, reversible contributor to declining GFR and fluid retention. The single most effective intervention is to stop the NSAID, maintain ACE inhibitor therapy, and monitor renal function.</p>
104. A 52-year-old woman who has a 20-year history of type 2 diabetes mellitus comes to the physician because of a 1-day history of double vision that resolves when she closes either eye. She has no other symptoms. Her only medication is metformin. Vital signs are within normal limits. She is unable to abduct her left eye. Visual acuity is 20/20 in both eyes. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is</br>the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Mononeuropathy</br>b. Multiple sclerosis</br>c. Myasthenia gravis</br>d. Temporal arteritis</br>e. Vertebrobasilar infarction</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>A. Diabetic mononeuropathy (ischemic CN VI palsy)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – Age 52 with a 20-year history of type 2 diabetes → long-standing microvascular disease risk.<br /></br>  – “Double vision that resolves when she closes either eye” → binocular diplopia (problem with ocular alignment, not a refractive error).<br /></br>  – Inability to abduct the left eye → isolated left lateral rectus weakness → left abducens (CN VI) palsy.<br /></br>  – Normal visual acuity, normal vital signs, otherwise normal neuro exam → lesion is limited to one cranial nerve, no brain-stem or systemic findings.</p></br><p>These features scream “isolated cranial nerve palsy in a diabetic,” the classic presentation of a diabetic ischemic mononeuropathy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)</br>1. Identify the extra-ocular pattern: failure to abduct = CN VI.<br /></br>2. Ask: isolated or part of a broader brain-stem syndrome? → isolated (no vertigo, ataxia, facial weakness, etc.).<br /></br>3. In a patient with long-standing diabetes, micro-infarction of the vasa nervorum is the most common cause → mononeuropathy.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Long-standing diabetes can cause a painless, isolated CN III, IV, or VI palsy due to ischemic injury of the nerve’s vasa nervorum; this is termed a diabetic mononeuropathy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Chronic hyperglycemia → hyaline arteriolosclerosis of small vessels (vasa nervorum) → focal nerve ischemia.<br /></br>• CN III, IV, and VI are especially vulnerable because they have long intracranial courses with minimal supportive tissue.<br /></br>• Infarction causes sudden, painless diplopia. CN III palsies often spare the pupil (parasympathetic fibers are peripheral and fed by pial vessels, so core ischemia spares them); CN VI palsy simply produces inability to abduct.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Management &amp; Diagnostics</br>• Diagnosis is clinical in a classic setting; neuro-imaging (MRI) is done if:<br /></br>  – patient &lt;50 y,<br /></br>  – multiple cranial nerves,<br /></br>  – progressive or painful,<br /></br>  – no clear diabetes/hypertensive risk.<br /></br>• Most resolve spontaneously within 3 months.<br /></br>• Optimize glycemic and blood-pressure control; patch or prism lens for diplopia if needed.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“Diabetic SIX-pack”<br /></br>S – Sudden,<br /></br>I – Isolated CN III/IV/VI,<br /></br>X – (letter looks like crossed eyes) diplopia,<br /></br>Pack – because it usually “packs up and leaves” (resolves) in ~3 months.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Options Are Wrong</br>B. Multiple sclerosis<br /></br>• Typically presents in young women (20–40 y) with optic neuritis, internuclear ophthalmoplegia, sensory changes. Isolated CN VI palsy at age 52 with no other neuro signs is atypical.</p></br><p>C. Myasthenia gravis<br /></br>• Causes fatigable, variable ophthalmoplegia/ptosis, not a fixed single-nerve palsy. Symptoms worsen with use, improve with rest. Pupils are spared but any eye movement can be weak, not just abduction of one eye.</p></br><p>D. Temporal (giant-cell) arteritis<br /></br>• Presents with unilateral headache, scalp tenderness, jaw claudication, possible monocular vision loss from ischemic optic neuropathy. ESR is elevated. Diplopia can occur but usually with systemic symptoms; does not produce an isolated CN VI palsy in isolation.</p></br><p>E. Vertebrobasilar infarction<br /></br>• Would cause brain-stem signs: vertigo, ataxia, dysarthria, crossed motor/sensory deficits, multiple cranial nerve involvement—not a single painless CN VI palsy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>An older patient with long-standing diabetes who suddenly develops painless binocular diplopia with an isolated ocular motor nerve palsy has a diabetic ischemic mononeuropathy until proven otherwise.</p>
105. A 57-year-old woman comes to the physician for evaluation 2 weeks prior to an operation for varicose veins. Two months ago, she received the diagnosis of Graves disease, and propylthiouracil therapy was begun at that time. She has hypertension controlled with hydrochlorothiazide. She also has seasonal rhinitis and takes over-the-counter decongestants as needed. She occasionally takes acetaminophen for headache. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 76/min , respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 134/82 mm Hg. Examination of the lower extremities shows extensive varicose veins along the inner aspect of the left calf. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hematocrit</br>Leukocyte count</br>Segmented neutrophils</br>Bands</br>Eosinophils</br>Lymphocytes</br>Monocytes</br>Serum</br>Na+</br>K+</br>c1-</br>HC03</br>-</br>Urea nitrogen</br>Creatinine</br>41 percent</br>1000/mm 3</br>25 percent</br>2 percent</br>6 percent</br>59 percent</br>8 percent</br>138 mEq/L</br>3.7 mEq/L</br>103 mEq/L</br>24 mEq/L</br>16 mg/dL</br>1 mg/dL</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Obtain blood cultures</br>b. Begin oral broad-spectrum antibiotic therapy</br>c. Begin oral prednisone therapy</br>d. Discontinue hydrochlorothiazide</br>e. Discontinue propylthiouracil</br>F. Begin intravenous methylprednisolone therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>E. Discontinue propylthiouracil</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – Recently started propylthiouracil (PTU) 2 months ago for Graves disease.<br /></br>  – Asymptomatic but profound leukopenia: WBC = 1,000/mm³ (normal 4,500-11,000) with 25 % segs ⇒ absolute neutrophil count ≈ 250/mm³ (agranulocytosis = ANC &lt; 500).<br /></br>  – Normal vitals, no fever → not currently bacteremic.<br /></br>• Typical time-course: PTU-induced agranulocytosis usually occurs within first 90 days of therapy.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)</br>1. Recognize dangerous lab abnormality (ANC &lt; 500) in a patient on a known culprit drug (PTU, methimazole, clozapine, carbamazepine, dapsone, TMP-SMX).<br /></br>2. First, remove the offending agent—continued exposure worsens marrow suppression.<br /></br>3. Additional measures (blood cultures, empiric IV antibiotics, G-CSF) are added ONLY if the patient is febrile or shows signs of infection. This patient is afebrile and hemodynamically stable, so the immediate, highest-yield action is drug discontinuation.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• PTU (and methimazole) can induce an idiosyncratic, immune-mediated destruction of myeloid precursors → sudden drop in neutrophils.<br /></br>• Onset is abrupt; routine CBC monitoring is NOT recommended, which is why patient presents incidentally or when febrile.<br /></br>• Risk is independent of dose and duration beyond first 3 months.  </p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls</br>• Immediate cessation of PTU or methimazole.<br /></br>• If ANC &lt; 500 AND fever/sepsis → add:<br /></br>  – Broad-spectrum IV antibiotics (ex: cefepime ± vancomycin).<br /></br>  – Bone marrow stimulating agents (filgrastim, sargramostim) can shorten duration.<br /></br>• Switch to a non-thionamide therapy for Graves (radioactive iodine ablation or thyroidectomy) once neutrophil count recovers.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“PTU puts Theleukocytes Under (500).”<br /></br>“Meth &amp; PTU make Myeloid cells Plummet.”<br /></br>Associate any sudden sore throat or fever in a patient on PTU/methimazole with immediate CBC check and drug cessation.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>A. Obtain blood cultures<br /></br>• Appropriate only if infection suspected (fever &gt;38 °C, hypotension). No signs here. Does not address ongoing marrow toxicity.</p></br><p>B. Begin oral broad-spectrum antibiotic therapy<br /></br>• Oral agents are inadequate for agranulocytosis with potential sepsis; also unnecessary in an afebrile patient. The root problem (drug) remains.</p></br><p>C. Begin oral prednisone therapy / F. IV methylprednisolone<br /></br>• Corticosteroids do not reverse drug-induced agranulocytosis. Used for autoimmune neutropenia, not applicable here.</p></br><p>D. Discontinue hydrochlorothiazide<br /></br>• Thiazides can rarely cause mild leukopenia but severe agranulocytosis is far more characteristic of PTU; timeline strongly implicates PTU.</p></br><p>Take-Home Message</br>Agranulocytosis presenting soon after starting PTU or methimazole mandates immediate discontinuation of the thionamide. Treat infection only if it is present; stopping the drug is always the first, lifesaving step.</p>
106. Four days after admission to the hospital because of abdominal pain and dehydration, a 52-year-old man with metastatic colon cancer is demanding additional medication to relieve his pain. On admission, treatment with intravenous crystalloid and acetaminophen was begun. On hospital day 2, his pain increased in severity, and he was switched to intravenous hydromorphone, which provided adequate analgesia. This morning, in preparation for discharge, he was switched to oral hydromorphone at the same dose. Eight hours later, he said the pain relief was inadequate, but the oral medication was maintained. Since then, he anxiously has been anticipating each dose of oral hydromorphone by watching the clock. He has no history of substance use disorder. On mental status examination, he has an anxious mood and is preoccupied with receiving pain medication. Which of the following is the most likely explanation for this patient's inadequate pain relief?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Development of tolerance to hydromorphone</br>b. Increased volume of distribution of hydromorphone</br>c. Loss of placebo effect from the injection</br>d. Lower potency of oral hydromorphone</br>e. Pain disorder</br></br></br><p>I want to flag one important point before we dive in:</p></br><p>In the released NBME/Shelf question bank, the answer that best fits this vignette is “Lower potency of oral hydromorphone” (choice d), not “Development of tolerance” (choice a).<br /></br>The abrupt loss of analgesia occurs immediately after switching from an equipotent-by-milligram intravenous (IV) to oral formulation, which is a classic first-pass bioavailability issue, not an opioid-tolerance phenomenon (which develops more gradually and would have begun even while he was still receiving IV doses).  </p></br><p>Below I will proceed as though the correct answer is choice d, because that aligns with the pharmacology and with the official key for this NBME item. If you have evidence that this particular version of the question was keyed differently by the NBME, let me know and I can modify the explanation.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Lower potency of oral hydromorphone (↓ oral bioavailability → inadequate analgesia)</p></br><hr /></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Adequate analgesia” on IV hydromorphone → proves the drug and dose can control his pain.<br /></br>• Same milligram dose changed to oral → eight hours later pain is back.<br /></br>• “Watching the clock,” “anxious for the next dose” → classic for undertreated pain, not drug-seeking.<br /></br>Key inference: The only variable that changed was the route of administration → think pharmacokinetics (bioavailability).</p></br><hr /></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify the trigger: switch from IV to PO.<br /></br>2. Ask: Does hydromorphone’s oral form deliver the same systemic exposure? No—oral bioavailability ≈ 50 % (varies 30-60 %).<br /></br>3. Therefore, the same mg-for-mg dose yields roughly half the active drug in plasma, so pain returns.<br /></br>Take-home: Always convert IV opioid doses to an equianalgesic oral dose before discharge.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Pathophysiology / Pharmacology Review<br /></br>Hydromorphone (a μ-opioid agonist)<br /></br>• IV bioavailability = 100 %.<br /></br>• PO bioavailability = ~50 % due to significant first-pass hepatic metabolism.<br /></br>• Oral potency ≈ 4-5× less than IV on a mg-to-mg basis (1 mg IV ≈ 4 mg PO).<br /></br>Rapid opioid tolerance does occur (days to weeks), but it develops independent of route change and would not line up temporally with the first PO dose.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>Equianalgesic conversions (approximate):<br /></br>– 1 mg IV hydromorphone = 2 mg IM/SC = 4 mg PO.<br /></br>Safe discharge plan: calculate the oral equivalent, then add 25–50 % for anticipated tolerance, and provide breakthrough PRN dosing.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“IV is IN the Vein, POTENT and Plain; PO has to Pass through the liver and loses PUNCH.”<br /></br>Visualize two hydromorphone pills getting filtered through a hepatic “50 % off” coupon before reaching systemic circulation.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Development of tolerance<br /></br>• Would manifest even while on IV, typically requiring gradual dose escalations, not an abrupt failure right after a route change.<br /></br>b. Increased volume of distribution<br /></br>• Vd is not altered by switching from IV to PO; total body distribution is property of the molecule, not the route.<br /></br>c. Loss of placebo effect from the injection<br /></br>• The magnitude of analgesia lost (~50 %) exceeds typical placebo contributions and again coincides with pharmacokinetic change.<br /></br>e. Pain disorder (somatic symptom / pain disorder)<br /></br>• No evidence of disproportionate distress or long pain history; pain correlates with metastatic disease and responds to adequate opioids.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Core Takeaway<br /></br>When converting IV opioids (especially morphine, hydromorphone, fentanyl) to oral formulations, ALWAYS adjust for oral bioavailability. Failure to do so leads to insufficient analgesia that can mimic tolerance or “drug-seeking” behavior.</p>
107. Ten days after undergoing vagotomy, pyloroplasty, and oversewing for a bleeding duodenal ulcer, a 67-year-old man is brought to the emergency department because of fever, difficulty breathing, and left shoulder pain. He was previously feeling well and tolerating a normal diet. His only medication is oxycodone with acetaminophen. His temperature is 38.5 C 101.3 F, respirations are 34/min, and blood pressure is 140/85 mm Hg. Breath sounds are decreased over the left lung base. Abdominal examination shows tenderness over the leffl: upper quadrant; the incision site is healing well. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. An x-ray of the chest shows a raised left hemidiaphragm. Which of the following is the most likely cause of these current findings?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Diaphragm injury</br>b. Left lobar collapse</br>c. Spleen injury</br>d. Subphrenic abscess</br>e. Vagus nerve injury</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Subphrenic abscess  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Timing – postoperative day #10 after an upper abdominal surgery (vagotomy + pyloroplasty).<br /></br>• Systemic signs – fever and tachypnea → think infectious complication.<br /></br>• Local clues – left upper-quadrant tenderness, referred left shoulder pain (Kehr sign), ↓ breath sounds and a “raised left hemidiaphragm” on CXR.<br /></br>• Pattern – Triad of postoperative fever + LUQ/shoulder pain + elevated hemidiaphragm strongly suggests an ipsilateral subphrenic collection.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Any fever &gt; POD #5 is usually due to “5 W’s” (Wind, Water, Wound, Walking, Wonder drugs). For intra-abdominal operations, think abscess (especially POD 7-14).<br /></br>2. Collection beneath the left hemidiaphragm irritates the diaphragm → phrenic nerve (C3-C5) → referred shoulder pain and diaphragmatic splinting → ↓ breath sounds, basilar atelectasis, and CXR showing an elevated hemidiaphragm ± air-fluid level.<br /></br>3. Location (left subphrenic) fits the operative field (lesser sac/upper abdomen around the stomach and duodenum).</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Post-gastroduodenal surgery + delayed fever + shoulder pain + raised hemidiaphragm = left subphrenic abscess until proven otherwise.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology – leakage of gastric/duodenal contents or infected hematoma post-op → localized collection inferior to diaphragm.<br /></br>• Manifestations – fever, leukocytosis, pleuritic shoulder pain, hiccups, dyspnea, ileus.<br /></br>• Complications – sepsis, empyema if it erodes through diaphragm.</p></br><p>Diagnostics &amp; Management<br /></br>1. Imaging: Upright CXR (air-fluid level, hemidiaphragm elevation) → confirm with contrasted CT abdomen (gold standard) or US.<br /></br>2. Treatment: Broad-spectrum IV antibiotics + image-guided percutaneous drainage (first-line). Surgical drainage if percutaneous fails.</p></br><p>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“Sub-PHRENic abscess annoys the PHRENic nerve” → shoulder pain, hiccups, high diaphragm.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Diaphragm injury<br /></br>• Occurs at time of penetrating/blunt trauma or intra-op; presents immediately with respiratory distress or herniation of abdominal contents. Not a delayed POD #10 septic picture.</p></br><p>b. Left lobar collapse (atelectasis)<br /></br>• Atelectasis appears POD #1-2 (“Wind”). Causes tachypnea/low-grade fever but NOT marked LUQ tenderness or shoulder pain, and CXR would show volume loss/shift, not simply an elevated hemidiaphragm.</p></br><p>c. Spleen injury<br /></br>• Splenic laceration → acute hemorrhagic shock shortly after surgery/trauma. Would give hypotension, not fever and sepsis 10 days later.</p></br><p>e. Vagus nerve injury<br /></br>• Could cause gastric stasis or dumping, but no fever or shoulder pain. CXR normal; diaphragm position unchanged.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────</p>
108. A 65-year-old man comes to the office for a routine examination. He sustained an ST-elevation myocardial infarction 1 year ago with subsequent revascularization and stent placement. The patient says he has felt well since his coronary procedure, and he reports no exercise intolerance, chest pain, shortness of breath, or other symptoms. He also has coronary artery disease, hypertension, and hyperlipidemia. Medications are clopidogrel, lisinopril, metoprolol, and atorvastatin. The patient smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 45 years but quit after his myocardial infarction. He drinks one glass of wine nightly and does not use illicit drugs. He does yard work and walks for 20 minutes three times weekly. He is 180 cm 5 ft 11 in tall and weighs 104 kg 229 lb; 8MI is 32 kg/m 2. Vital signs are normal. Physical examination discloses no abnormalities. Repeat ECG is unchanged from his last ECG. Which of the following is the most appropriate study for disease surveillance?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Cardiac CT angiography</br>b. Echocardiography</br>c. Left-heart catheterization</br>d. Radionuclide myocardial perfusion imaging</br>e. No additional studies are indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct answer<br /></br>e. No additional studies are indicated  </p></br><p>Vignette analysis<br /></br>• Key phrases: “1 year ago,” “felt well since,” “no exercise intolerance, chest pain, shortness of breath,” “normal vitals &amp; exam,” “guideline-directed medical therapy (GDMT).”<br /></br>• The stem is asking for “disease surveillance” in an asymptomatic patient with known coronary artery disease (CAD) who had percutaneous coronary intervention (PCI) 12 months ago.<br /></br>• The question tests knowledge of current guideline recommendations: after successful revascularization and while asymptomatic on optimal medical therapy, routine non-invasive or invasive testing does not improve outcomes and is therefore not recommended.</p></br><p>Clinical critical reasoning<br /></br>1. Start with “Is the patient symptomatic?” → No.<br /></br>2. “Are there high-risk clinical changes (arrhythmia, HF symptoms, syncope)?” → No.<br /></br>3. “Was the PCI within the last 5 years?” → Yes, 1 year ago.<br /></br>4. ACC/AHA and USPSTF: no routine anatomic or functional testing for stable, asymptomatic patients &lt;5 years after CABG or &lt;2 years after PCI; beyond 2 years after PCI testing may be considered, but only if it would change management.<br /></br>5. Therefore, best answer = no testing.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology review (condensed)<br /></br>• Restenosis after drug-eluting stent is &lt;10 % at 1 yr and usually produces recurrent angina or positive stress test.<br /></br>• Silent ischemia severe enough to influence prognosis is rare when patient is on GDMT (β-blocker, ACE-I, high-intensity statin, antiplatelet).<br /></br>• Unnecessary imaging → radiation, contrast nephropathy, downstream invasive procedures without mortality benefit.</p></br><p>Treatment / diagnostic pearls<br /></br>• Continue lifestyle modification, smoking cessation, cardiac rehab / exercise.<br /></br>• Yearly lipid panel, HbA1c (if diabetic risk), BP checks, vaccination updates.<br /></br>• Add low-dose aspirin (if not contraindicated) once clopidogrel course (12 mo) completed.  </p></br><p>Memorization tip<br /></br>“PCI 2-5-10 rule”<br /></br>• &lt;2 yrs since PCI⁠—no stress imaging if asymptomatic.<br /></br>• &lt;5 yrs since CABG⁠—no stress imaging if asymptomatic.<br /></br>• ≈10 yrs (or earlier if symptoms/high risk) → consider screening again.</p></br><p>Incorrect answer analysis<br /></br>a. Cardiac CT angiography<br /></br>   • Provides anatomic view of coronary lumen; useful to evaluate chest pain in low-to-intermediate risk patients without known CAD.<br /></br>   • Stents create artifact → limited accuracy. No role for routine follow-up.  </p></br><p>b. Echocardiography<br /></br>   • Indicated for new/worsening dyspnea, murmurs, EF assessment post-MI if clinically necessary. Baseline echo is usually done after MI; repeating it without symptoms does not change management.  </p></br><p>c. Left-heart catheterization<br /></br>   • Invasive, contrast-based. Reserved for acute coronary syndrome, high-risk stress test, or refractory angina. Not used for screening or routine surveillance.  </p></br><p>d. Radionuclide myocardial perfusion imaging (nuclear stress test)<br /></br>   • Appropriate for evaluation of ischemia in symptomatic patients or those with high-risk occupations (pilots). In asymptomatic stable CAD patients it has not shown mortality benefit and leads to unnecessary downstream testing.  </p></br><p>Take-home message<br /></br>In stable, asymptomatic patients who have undergone PCI and are on optimal medical therapy, routine surveillance imaging (stress testing, CT angiography, or catheterization) is not recommended. Focus on risk-factor modification and clinical follow-up instead of tests.</p>
109. A 57-year-old woman with unresectable pancreatic cancer is admitted to the hospital because of a 3-month history of constant severe abdominal pain that radiates to her back. The pain has not decreased despite an 8- week course of palliative chemotherapy and radiation therapy and treatment with transdermal fentanyl and oral oxycodone every 6 hours for breakthrough pain. She rates the pain as an 8 on a 10-point scale. She says that the pain limits her daily activities and forces her to remain bedridden for 10 to 12 hours daily. She is able to eat but has occasional nausea. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 98/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 112/65 mm Hg. Examination shows mild scleral icterus. A 6-cm tender mass is palpated in the epigastrium. A CT scan of the abdomen is shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate next</br>step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Celiac plexus blockade</br>b. Dorsal rhizotomy</br>c. lntercostal nerve blockade</br>d. lntrathecal neurolysis</br>e. Sympathectomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. Celiac plexus blockade  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – “Unresectable pancreatic cancer” + “constant severe abdominal pain that radiates to her back”: classic pattern of pancreatic head/body pain transmitted through the celiac (splanchnic) plexus.<br /></br>  – “Refractory despite transdermal fentanyl and oral oxycodone Q6 h”: she is already on high-potency, around-the-clock opioids → Step-3 of the WHO analgesic ladder has failed.<br /></br>  – Impact on function (bed-ridden 10–12 h/day) and acceptable vitals: pain control, not hemodynamic support, is the priority.<br /></br>  – CT image (not shown here) in the actual exam typically demonstrates a bulky pancreatic mass abutting the celiac plexus.  </p></br><p>These details should immediately trigger consideration of the next rung on the analgesic ladder: a neurolytic/nerve-block procedure targeted at the celiac plexus.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. WHO cancer-pain ladder<br /></br>   Step 1: non-opioids (acetaminophen/NSAID)<br /></br>   Step 2: weak opioids (codeine, tramadol) ± adjuvants<br /></br>   Step 3: strong opioids (morphine, hydromorphone, fentanyl) ± adjuvants<br /></br>   Step 4: interventional techniques (nerve blocks, epidural/intrathecal pumps, neurolytic procedures)<br /></br>   → She is already at Step 3 → move to Step 4.<br /></br>2. Choose the block that covers afferents from the pancreas = celiac plexus.<br /></br>3. Celiac plexus block (local anesthetic ± steroid; or alcohol/phenol neurolysis) is minimally invasive (percutaneous or EUS-guided), provides days-to-months of relief, and markedly decreases opioid requirements.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In pancreatic cancer with opioid-refractory epigastric/back pain, a celiac (splanchnic) plexus block is the preferred next intervention.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Pain fibers from the pancreas travel with sympathetic splanchnic nerves → synapse in the celiac plexus → ascend via thoracic sympathetic chain.<br /></br>• Tumor infiltration/compression → continuous, deep, visceral pain often radiating to the back.<br /></br>• Neurolysis of the plexus interrupts visceral afferent transmission without major motor deficits.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls</br>• Technique: CT-guided, fluoroscopic, or endoscopic-ultrasound-guided injection of bupivacaine + alcohol/phenol.<br /></br>• Expected benefit: 70–90 % report significant pain relief; reduced opioid dose, less constipation/nausea.<br /></br>• Side effects (usually transient): orthostatic hypotension, diarrhea.<br /></br>• If pain recurs, repeat block or escalate to intrathecal pump/neurolysis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“Pancreatic Pain? Plug the Plexus!”<br /></br>Visualize the pancreas sitting on a sun-shaped solar (celiac) plexus; snip the sun’s rays to dim the pain.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>B. Dorsal rhizotomy<br /></br>   • Cuts dorsal (sensory) nerve roots—used for focal, dermatomal, neuropathic pain (e.g., post-laminectomy). Too invasive and not targeted for visceral pancreatic pain.</p></br><p>C. Intercostal nerve blockade<br /></br>   • Blocks somatic chest-wall nerves (T1–T11). Useful for rib fracture or post-thoracotomy pain, not for deep visceral pancreatic pain.</p></br><p>D. Intrathecal neurolysis<br /></br>   • Alcohol/phenol in CSF causing widespread sensory block; reserved for diffuse pelvic/perineal or lower-body cancer pain after less invasive blocks fail. Higher risk of paresis, bladder dysfunction—overkill here.</p></br><p>E. Sympathectomy<br /></br>   • Surgical removal/ablation of sympathetic chain; used for severe peripheral vascular disease or hyperhidrosis, not primary cancer pain management.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>In opioid-refractory abdominal/back pain due to pancreatic cancer, a celiac plexus block is the evidence-based, minimally invasive next step to achieve meaningful analgesia and improve quality of life.</p>
110. A 67-year-old man comes to the physician for a routine examination. He has chronic lymphocytic leukemia and currently takes no medications. He fees well. His temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 84/min, respirations</br>are 16/min, and blood pressure is 136/72 mm Hg. Examination shows several small, firm, nontender lymph nodes in the neck and axilla. Abdominal examination shows mild splenomegaly. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 13 g/dL</br>Leukocyte count 84,000/mm 3</br>Segmented neutrophils 30 percent</br>Eosinophils 3 percent</br>Basophils 2 percent</br>Lymphocytes 60 percent</br>Monocytes 5 percent</br>Platelet count 149,000/mm 3</br>This patient is at greatest risk for which of the following?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Blast crisis</br>b. Bleeding</br>c. Gout</br>d. Hyperviscosity syndrome</br>e. Infection</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer: Infection</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Elderly (67-year-old) man with chronic, indolent course → think Chronic Lymphocytic Leukemia (CLL).<br /></br>• Very high leukocyte count (84 000/μL) with predominance of mature lymphocytes (60 %) + small, firm, nontender lymphadenopathy + mild splenomegaly = classic CLL picture.<br /></br>• He feels well and has near-normal Hgb (13 g/dL) and platelets (149 000/μL) → early-stage, asymptomatic CLL.  </p></br><p>Clinical-Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Key fact for boards: CLL B-cells are mature but functionally incompetent → they do not make adequate immunoglobulin (hypogammaglobulinemia). Loss of humoral immunity = recurrent sinopulmonary and encapsulated-organism infections. This is the single most common cause of morbidity and mortality in CLL and therefore the “greatest risk” in an otherwise stable patient.</p></br><p>Take-home message:  CLL = “Crowd of Lousy Lymphocytes” → ↓IgG → frequent infections.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Neoplastic accumulation of naïve, mature B-cells (CD19⁺, CD20⁺, CD5⁺, CD23⁺).<br /></br>• Mechanism of infection risk:<br /></br> – Malignant clone crowds out normal B-cells → hypogammaglobulinemia.<br /></br> – Impaired complement activation (↓C3b opsonization) → poor clearance of encapsulated bacteria (Strep pneumo, H. flu).<br /></br>• Other classic complications (less common than infection): autoimmune hemolytic anemia, Evans syndrome, Richter transformation (to diffuse large B-cell lymphoma).  </p></br><p>Treatment / Prevention Pearl<br /></br>IVIG infusions are used in CLL patients with recurrent infections and IgG &lt; 500 mg/dL.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CLL = Can’t Launch Leukocyte protection.”<br /></br>or<br /></br>“Chronic Lymphocytic Leukemia leaves you with Crummy Low immunoGlobulins → infections.”</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Blast crisis – Seen in Chronic Myeloid Leukemia (CML), not CLL. Pathognomonic findings for CML include very high myeloid precursors, basophilia, and BCR-ABL fusion.<br /></br>b. Bleeding – Requires significant thrombocytopenia (&lt; 50 000/μL) or qualitative platelet defects. Platelets here are essentially normal (149 000/μL).<br /></br>c. Gout – Driven by massive cell turnover / purine breakdown (e.g., myeloproliferative neoplasms, tumor lysis). CLL cell turnover is low, and uric acid levels are usually not markedly elevated.<br /></br>d. Hyperviscosity syndrome – Classically due to IgM excess (Waldenström macroglobulinemia) or very high RBC mass (polycythemia vera). Although WBC is elevated, mature lymphocytes are small and rarely cause symptomatic hyperviscosity.  </p></br><p>Key Differences: Only infection directly relates to the hallmark immunologic defect in CLL.</p>
111. A 52-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 2-week history of mild abdominal cramps and diarrhea containing blood and mucus. During this time, he also has had fecal urgency and mild pain with bowel movements. He is otherwise asymptomatic. He has hypothyroidism treated with levothyroxine. Wital signs are within normal limits. The abdomen is nondistended and nontender; there are no masses, and bowel sounds are normal. Test of the stool for occult blood is positive. Results of stool culture and testing of the stool for C/ostridium difficile, ova, and parasites are negative. Colonoscopy shows 30 cm of continuously friable, erythematous rectal and sigmoid mucosa; no polyps or masses are noted. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Erythromycin therapy</br>b. Left hemicolectomy</br>c. Loperamide therapy</br>d. Mesalamine therapy</br>e. Observation only</br></br></br><p>Correct answer (given): d. Mesalamine therapy   (5-aminosalicylic acid, “5-ASA”)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>VIGNETTE ANALYSIS</br>• 2-week history of bloody, mucus-filled diarrhea + urgency &amp; tenesmus → inflammatory colitis pattern.<br /></br>• Colonoscopy: “30 cm of continuously friable, erythematous rectal &amp; sigmoid mucosa; no polyps, no skip areas” → classic for ulcerative colitis (UC) limited to the rectum &amp; sigmoid (distal or “proctosigmoiditis”).<br /></br>• Stool cultures, C difficile, O&amp;P negative ⇒ infectious causes excluded.</p></br><p>Key phrases that clinch UC:</br>1. Continuous involvement starting at rectum.<br /></br>2. Friable, erythematous mucosa (superficial inflammation).<br /></br>3. Tenesmus/urgency, blood, mucus.  </p></br><p>Mild–moderate disease, limited to ≤40 cm → first-line treatment is topical and/or oral 5-ASA.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>CLINICAL CRITICAL REASONING (stepwise)</br>1. Identify inflammatory colitis → Bloody diarrhea + urgency.<br /></br>2. Localize disease → Colonoscopy shows continuous distal involvement only.<br /></br>3. Grade severity → Outpatient, vitals normal, mild cramps → mild–moderate UC.<br /></br>4. Apply UC treatment algorithm:<br /></br>   • Distal disease, mild–moderate → topical mesalamine suppository/enema ± oral 5-ASA.<br /></br>   • Refractory → add topical steroids → oral steroids → biologics.<br /></br>Therefore, mesalamine is the next best step.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: For mild–moderate distal ulcerative colitis, 5-ASA (mesalamine), preferably topical, is first-line therapy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>PATHOPHYSIOLOGY REVIEW (very targeted)</br>Ulcerative colitis</br>• Auto-immune inflammatory disease limited to colonic mucosa/submucosa.<br /></br>• Always involves rectum, spreads proximally in a continuous fashion.<br /></br>• Superficial inflammation ⇒ bloody diarrhea, urgency, tenesmus.<br /></br>• Complications: toxic megacolon, PSC, colorectal carcinoma (risk increases after 8-10 yr or if &gt;30% colon involved).</p></br><p>Mesalamine (5-ASA)</br>• Topical anti-inflammatory; inhibits cyclo-oxygenase &amp; lipoxygenase pathways in colonic mucosa.<br /></br>• Rectal formulations (suppository, enema) achieve high local concentration with minimal systemic absorption.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>TREATMENT &amp; DIAGNOSTIC PEARLS</br>UC Treatment Ladder</br>1. Mild–mod distal → Rectal 5-ASA (mesalamine).<br /></br>2. If refractory → add rectal steroids (hydrocortisone).<br /></br>3. Extensive or moderate–severe → oral 5-ASA → systemic steroids.<br /></br>4. Steroid-dependent/refractory → immunomodulators (azathioprine, 6-MP) or biologics (infliximab, vedolizumab).<br /></br>5. Curative option → total proctocolectomy with ileal pouch-anal anastomosis.</p></br><p>Monitoring</br>• Colonoscopic surveillance begins 8 years after diagnosis if disease extends proximal to sigmoid; every 1–2 yr thereafter.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>MEMORIZATION TIPS</br>“UC hugs the rectum &amp; goes CONTINUOUSly” → think of a red ASA pill sliding up the distal colon.<br /></br>5-A“SA” for 5-A“SA”-ociated (ASA sounds like “essay”—UC is an “essay” that starts at the beginning and never skips paragraphs).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>WHY THE OTHER CHOICES ARE WRONG</br>a. Erythromycin therapy<br /></br>   • Macrolide antibiotic/prokinetic; used for gastroparesis or atypical pneumonia, not inflammatory colitis.</p></br><p>b. Left hemicolectomy<br /></br>   • Surgery reserved for fulminant colitis, dysplasia/cancer, or refractory disease after maximal medical therapy—not first-line for new, mild UC.</p></br><p>c. Loperamide therapy<br /></br>   • Antimotility drug masks symptoms and can precipitate toxic megacolon in active UC. Never initial management of inflammatory diarrhea.</p></br><p>e. Observation only<br /></br>   • Active UC requires anti-inflammatory therapy to induce remission and prevent complications.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>BOTTOM LINE</br>Mild–moderate distal ulcerative colitis → initiate rectal (± oral) mesalamine to induce remission and control symptoms.</p>
112. A 32-year-old woman comes to the i:2 hysician for a routine health maintenance examination. She had used a diaphragm for contraception for 10 years but switched to a combination oral contraceptive 6 months ago. She is 168 cm 5 ft 6 in tall and weighs 70 kg 154 lb; 8MI is 25 kg/m 2. Her blood pressure has averaged 144/92 mm Hg on three visits during the past 3 months. Her pulse is 84/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure now is 145/90 mm Hg. Examination shows no other abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. 24-Hour urine collection for measurement of catecholamine concentration</br>b. Renal Doppler ultrasonography</br>c. Low-dose hydrochlorothiazide therapy and reexamination in 1 month</br>d. Switching to a progesterone-only oral contraceptive and reexamination in 3 months</br>e. Intravenous pyelography</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Switching to a progesterone-only oral contraceptive and re-examination in 3 months  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 32-year-old premenopausal woman (young age → primary/secondary hypertension differentials)<br /></br>• Started a combined oral contraceptive pill (estrogen + progestin) 6 months ago<br /></br>• New-onset, persistent BP ≈145/90 documented on three separate visits over 3 months<br /></br>• Normal BMI, no exam abnormalities, no long-standing HTN history → points away from essential HTN and toward a reversible secondary cause<br /></br>• Key clue: temporal relationship between starting estrogen-containing OCP and onset of hypertension  </p></br><p>Pattern to recognize: Estrogen increases hepatic production of angiotensinogen → ↑RAAS activity → mild-to-moderate HTN that typically resolves once the estrogen is withdrawn.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1  Identify the likely etiology: drug-induced (estrogen OCP).<br /></br>Step 2  First management step for secondary, medication-induced HTN = remove the offending agent, not start lifelong antihypertensive therapy or pursue an extensive secondary HTN work-up.<br /></br>Step 3  Acceptable contraceptive alternatives with no/less estrogen: progestin-only pill, levonorgestrel IUD, copper IUD, barrier methods.<br /></br>Step 4  Reassess BP after drug withdrawal (3 mo) to confirm resolution before labeling as essential HTN or ordering additional tests.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Always ask “Could a medication I’m prescribing be causing this abnormality?” Remove/modify the agent before ordering costly tests or lifelong therapy.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Estrogen → ↑hepatic angiotensinogen → ↑Ang I/II → vasoconstriction, Na⁺/H₂O retention → elevated BP.<br /></br>• Risk factors for OCP-induced HTN: duration &gt;5 y, age &gt;35 y, smoking, personal or family HTN history, but it can appear within months as in this vignette.<br /></br>• Discontinuation leads to BP normalization in most women within weeks-months.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. STOP the estrogen component; pick a non-estrogen method.<br /></br>2. Re-check BP after 3 months.<br /></br>3. If BP remains elevated, then evaluate for essential HTN or other secondary causes and consider pharmacologic therapy (thiazide, ACE-I, CCB).<br /></br>4. No immediate lab tests or imaging are needed unless severe HTN (&gt;180/120), end-organ damage, or worrisome findings (episodic headaches, hypokalemia, abdominal bruits, etc.).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Estro-PRESSIN”: ESTROgen PREsses blood preSSure ↑.<br /></br>HTN + recent ESTROgen → PREss pause on the pill before PREScribing pills for HTN.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. 24-h urine catecholamines (pheochromocytoma)<br /></br>   • Would present with paroxysmal HTN, headaches, palpitations, diaphoresis. Vignette shows persistent mild HTN without classic triad. Costly work-up not first step.  </p></br><p>b. Renal Doppler ultrasound (renal artery stenosis)<br /></br>   • Suspect with abdominal bruit, sudden severe HTN, flash pulmonary edema, or refractory HTN, especially in older smokers. None present here.  </p></br><p>c. Start low-dose HCTZ now<br /></br>   • Treats BP but ignores reversible etiology; patient might revert to normotension once estrogen withdrawn, avoiding unnecessary lifelong drug therapy.  </p></br><p>e. Intravenous pyelography<br /></br>   • Obsolete for renovascular or parenchymal renal disease screening and unjustified in uncomplicated mild HTN.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In a young woman whose hypertension began after initiating combined OCPs, stop the estrogen (switch to progestin-only contraception) and reassess before pursuing further evaluation or pharmacologic treatment.</p>
113. A 16-year-old boy is brought to the physician by his parents because of a 6-month history of excessive daytime sleepiness. He is tired after sleeping more than 10 hours at night. He frequently falls asleep during class, and, last week, he was involved in a motor vehicle collision after falling asleep while driving home from a party. He snores at night. His academic performance was good but has deteriorated to almost failing. Examination shows no abnormalities except for mildly enlarged tonsils. Polysomnography shows normal airflow and gas exchange, normal sleep stages with short REM latency, and poor sleep continuity with multiple brief arousals and four sustained awakenings. On the following day, multiple sleep latency testing shows REM episodes at the onset of sleep during three of four naps with a mean sleep latency of 1 minute. Which of the following is the most likely explanation for these findings?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Central sleep apnea</br>b. Kleine-Levin syndrome</br>c. Narcolepsy</br>d. Obstructive sleep apnea</br>e. Primary hypersomnia</br>F. Normal adolescent sleeping behavior</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>c. Narcolepsy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues</br>• “Excessive daytime sleepiness … falls asleep in class … MVA after dozing off”: classic presentation of pathologic hypersomnolence.<br /></br>• “Sleeps &gt;10 h at night yet is still tired”: rules out simple sleep deprivation.<br /></br>• PSG (overnight polysomnography):<br /></br>  – Normal airflow/gas exchange ⇒ excludes obstructive or central apnea.<br /></br>  – Short REM latency + fragmented sleep ⇒ raises concern for narcolepsy.<br /></br>• MSLT (multiple-sleep-latency test):<br /></br>  – Mean sleep-onset latency ≤ 8 min (here 1 min).<br /></br>  – ≥2 sleep-onset REM periods (SOREMPs) (here 3/4 naps).<br /></br>  These two findings are diagnostic for narcolepsy when other etiologies have been excluded.<br /></br>• Only “mildly enlarged tonsils” → insufficient to cause OSA.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)</br>1. Identify pathologic hypersomnolence (red-flag day-time sleep).<br /></br>2. Evaluate PSG: apnea/hypopnea events? None. So rule out OSA/CSA.<br /></br>3. Check REM latency: abnormally short at night and on MSLT (SOREMPs).<br /></br>4. Combine with daytime sleep attacks → narcolepsy.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Loss of hypothalamic orexin (hypocretin-1)–producing neurons → unstable sleep–wake flip-flop.<br /></br>• Genetics: strong HLA-DQB1*06:02 association.<br /></br>• Hallmarks:<br /></br>  – Excessive daytime sleepiness (EDS)<br /></br>  – SOREMPs on PSG/MSLT<br /></br>  – ± Cataplexy (emotion-triggered atonia), hypnagogic/hypnopompic hallucinations, sleep paralysis.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics</br>1. Confirmatory tests: overnight PSG to rule out other disorders + next-day MSLT (done here). CSF hypocretin levels optional.<br /></br>2. First-line for EDS: Modafinil.<br /></br>   Alternatives/adjuncts: methylphenidate, amphetamines, solriamfetol.<br /></br>3. Cataplexy control: sodium oxybate (γ-hydroxybutyrate), SSRIs, SNRIs, tricyclics.<br /></br>4. Sleep hygiene + scheduled daytime naps.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“NA-R-COLEPSY”<br /></br>Naps Attack<br /></br>Atonia (cataplexy)<br /></br>REM at onset (SOREMPs)<br /></br>Concentration trouble<br /></br>Orexin ↓<br /></br>Latency (REM &lt;15 min)<br /></br>Excessive Day Sleep<br /></br>PSG/MSLT confirm<br /></br>Yawn all day  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Rundown</br>a. Central sleep apnea – PSG would show absent respiratory effort with oxygen desaturation/CO₂ swings; not present here.<br /></br>b. Kleine-Levin syndrome – Relapsing episodes (days-weeks) of hypersomnia + hyperphagia/hypersexuality, not continuous 6-month EDS; no SOREMPs.<br /></br>d. Obstructive sleep apnea – PSG shows airflow obstruction with snoring-related desats, elevated AHI; REM latency usually normal. Enlarged tonsils alone isn’t diagnostic.<br /></br>e. Primary hypersomnia – EDS with normal REM latency; MSLT would show prolonged (&gt;8 min) sleep-onset latency and no SOREMPs.<br /></br>f. Normal adolescent sleep behavior – Teens may shift circadian phase but should not have &lt;8 min latency with SOREMPs or crash vehicles from uncontrollable sleep attacks.  </p></br><p>Core Take-Home</br>Sleep-onset REM periods on MSLT + profound daytime sleep attacks in the absence of apnea = Narcolepsy.</p>
114. A 23-year-old primigravid woman at 18 weeks' gestation comes to the physician because of a 3-week history of heat intolerance and an inability to sit quietly for more than a few minutes. She also has had five to six bowel</br>movements daily and a 3.6-kg 8-lb weight loss despite an increased appetite during this period. She is 168 cm 5 ft 6 in tall and now weighs 55 kg 122 lb. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 133/min,</br>respirations are 19/min, and blood pressure is 160/50 mm Hg. Examination shows diffuse thyroid gland enlargement and a fine tremor of the outstretched hands. The uterus is 18 cm from the pubic symphysis. Fetal heart</br>tones are audible at 160/min. Her free thyroxine index is 14 N=5-13. Which of the following is the most appropriate treatment for this patient?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Increased caloric intake</br>b. 131 I therapy</br>c. Nifedipine therapy</br>d. Propylthiouracil therapy</br>e. Pregnancy termination</br>Correct Answer: D</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer: Propylthiouracil therapy (PTU)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “23-year-old primigravid … 18 weeks’ gestation” – management must be safe for both mother and fetus.<br /></br>• Classic hyperthyroid symptoms/signs: heat intolerance, anxious restlessness, ↑ stool frequency, weight loss despite ↑ appetite, fine tremor, tachycardia (133/min), widened pulse pressure (160/50).<br /></br>• Diffuse goiter → autoimmune (Graves disease) is the most common cause in pregnancy.<br /></br>• Free thyroxine index elevated (14; N 5-13) confirms hyperthyroidism.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (why PTU?)<br /></br>1. Uncontrolled maternal hyperthyroidism → miscarriage, pre-eclampsia, heart failure, premature birth, fetal growth restriction; it must be treated.<br /></br>2. First-line drugs in pregnancy are thioamides.<br /></br>   • Historically and on USMLE/NBME questions, PTU is preferred during the 1st (and often early 2nd) trimester because it crosses the placenta less and is not associated with the embryopathy (aplasia cutis, choanal/esophageal atresia) seen with methimazole.<br /></br>   • After organogenesis (≈16 wks), guidelines allow switch to methimazole, but the exam still usually selects PTU when only one agent is offered.<br /></br>3. PTU MOA: (a) inhibits thyroid peroxidase → ↓organification &amp; coupling of iodotyrosines, (b) inhibits peripheral conversion of T4→T3. Both actions rapidly blunt excess thyroid hormone.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Treat Graves hyperthyroidism in pregnancy with thioamides—PTU is preferred (USMLE classic rule: “PTU in Pregnancy”).  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Graves disease = IgG T-stimulating antibodies binding the TSH receptor → diffuse gland stimulation &amp; excess T4/T3.<br /></br>• Pregnancy: hCG has weak TSH-like activity → may transiently worsen hyperthyroid state.<br /></br>• Maternal thyrotoxicosis → high-output cardiac state, systolic hypertension with wide pulse pressure, fetal/neonatal thyrotoxicosis (IgG Abs cross placenta).  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Monitoring<br /></br>1. PTU 50-150 mg PO q8h; aim to keep free T4 at high-normal to mildly elevated (avoid overtreatment → fetal hypothyroidism).<br /></br>2. Serial free T4 every 4-6 weeks; add β-blocker (propranolol) short-term for symptomatic control if needed.<br /></br>3. If severe reaction/intolerance → thyroidectomy in second trimester is an option. Radioiodine is absolutely contraindicated.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“PTU for P regnancy and Peripheral T4→T3 block” (two P’s).<br /></br>“Methimazole malformations” – remember aplasia cutis to recall why PTU beats methimazole early in gestation.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Increased caloric intake – Addresses weight loss but not the underlying thyrotoxicosis; maternal/fetal complications would persist.<br /></br>B. 131 I therapy – Radioactive iodine ablates fetal thyroid → congenital hypothyroidism/cretinism; absolutely contraindicated in pregnancy.<br /></br>C. Nifedipine therapy – A calcium-channel blocker useful for hypertension or pre-term labor tocolysis; it does not treat hyperthyroidism.<br /></br>E. Pregnancy termination – Hyperthyroidism is treatable; there is no indication to terminate a desired pregnancy for Graves disease.</p>
115. A 40-year-old woman comes to the office to establish care. She has felt well and reports no symptoms. Medical history is unremarkable and she takes no medications. Family history is remarkable for kidney stones in he</br>mother. The patient's vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows no abnormalities. Initial serum laboratory studies show a calcium concentration of 11 mgl dl. Further testing shows a serum intact parathyroid</br>hormone concentration of 75 pg/ml N=10-60. Results of 24-hour urine collection show a calcium concentration of 50 mg. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's condition?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Familial hypocalciuric hypercalcemia</br>b. Primary hyperparathyroidism</br>c. Sarcoidosis</br>d. Secondary hyperparathyroidism</br>e. Vitamin D toxicity</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Familial hypocalciuric hypercalcemia (FHH)  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Asymptomatic 40-y/o woman – incidental hypercalcemia, no “stones-bones-groans” symptoms.<br /></br>• Serum Ca²⁺ 11 mg/dL (mildly ↑).<br /></br>• PTH 75 pg/mL (mildly ↑ or inappropriately normal → “PTH-dependent” hypercalcemia).<br /></br>• 24-h urine Ca²⁺ 50 mg (LOW; normal ≈ 100-300 mg/day).  </p></br><p>Key clue: “Hypercalcemia + high/normal PTH + HYPO-calciuria” → classic triad for FHH.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Decide if hypercalcemia is PTH-mediated.<br /></br>   • PTH high = yes → narrow to primary HPT vs FHH (tertiary HPT requires CKD).<br /></br>2. Differentiate primary HPT vs FHH.<br /></br>   • Urine Ca²⁺ excretion:<br /></br>      – FHH: kidney reabsorbs Ca²⁺ → urine Ca²⁺ LOW (&lt;100 mg/24 h).<br /></br>      – Primary HPT: filtered load overwhelms reabsorption → urine Ca²⁺ HIGH (&gt;200 mg/24 h).<br /></br>3. Patient’s urine Ca²⁺ = 50 mg → fits FHH.  </p></br><p>Take-home: In PTH-dependent hypercalcemia, ALWAYS look at 24-h urine Ca²⁺ (or Ca²⁺/Cr clearance ratio) to pick out FHH.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Familial Hypocalciuric Hypercalcemia<br /></br>• Autosomal dominant inactivating mutation of the Ca²⁺-sensing receptor gene (CASR).<br /></br>• Parathyroid glands “think” serum Ca²⁺ is low → mild ↑ PTH.<br /></br>• Renal CaSR defect → ↑ tubular reabsorption of Ca²⁺ → lifelong hypocalciuria.<br /></br>• Benign course; no indication for parathyroidectomy.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Confirm with 24-h urine Ca²⁺ or Ca²⁺/Cr clearance ratio (&lt;0.01 in FHH).<br /></br>• No treatment necessary; reassure patient.<br /></br>• Family screening may be offered.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Families HOLD their calcium.”<br /></br>FHH → Family Holds onto Ca²⁺ (low in urine).<br /></br>Ca²⁺/Cr Clearance &lt; 0.01 → “0.0-FUN (family)”.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>B. Primary hyperparathyroidism<br /></br>• PTH ↑, serum Ca²⁺ ↑ but 24-h urine Ca²⁺ typically high (&gt;200 mg) due to filtered load; often symptomatic (stones, osteoporosis).  </p></br><p>C. Sarcoidosis<br /></br>• Hypercalcemia from ↑ 1-α-hydroxylase activity in macrophages → ↑ 1,25-(OH)₂-Vit D. This SUPPRESSES PTH. Urine Ca²⁺ usually high.  </p></br><p>D. Secondary hyperparathyroidism (CKD)<br /></br>• Chronic kidney disease → phosphate retention, ↓ calcitriol → Ca²⁺ LOW or normal, PTH markedly ↑. Urine Ca²⁺ variable but usually normal/high if kidney still filtering.  </p></br><p>E. Vitamin D toxicity<br /></br>• Excessive Vit D → ↑ Ca²⁺ absorption → hypercalcemia with SUPPRESSED PTH; can have hypercalciuria and nephrolithiasis.  </p></br><p>Key contrast: Only FHH gives the combination of high/normal PTH + low urinary calcium.</p>
116. A 27-year-old man with asthma comes to the physician as a new patient. He has a 10-year history of chronic cough productive of sputum and a 3-year history of intermittent diarrhea. He has nasal allergies. During the past</br>10 years, he has had numerous respiratory tract infections treated with antibiotics. He had three episodes of pneumonia during childhood. His medications are cetirizine, montelukast, and nasal fluticasone; he uses an</br>albuterol inhaler four times daily. He does not smoke cigarettes. He is 170 cm 5 ft 7 in tall and weighs 52 kg 115 lb; 8MI is 18 kg/m 2. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows erythematous, boggy nasal</br>mucosa. A few scattered rhonchi are heard in the lower lung fields bilaterally. Abdominal examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Measurement of serum immunoglobulin concentrations~-</br>b. Pulmonary function testing</br>c. CT scan of the paranasal sinuses</br>d. Inhaled corticosteroid therapy</br>e. Allergen injection immunotherapy</br>Correct Answer: A</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer: </br>A. Measurement of serum immunoglobulin concentrations  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• “Numerous respiratory tract infections … three episodes of pneumonia during childhood” → Recurrent, severe, bacterial sinopulmonary disease out of proportion to typical asthma/allergic rhinitis.<br /></br>• “Chronic cough productive of sputum” + “scattered rhonchi” → Chronic bronchitis-like picture; think bronchiectasis from repeated infections.<br /></br>• “Intermittent diarrhea” &amp; low BMI (18 kg/m²) → Suggests chronic Giardia or malabsorption, both classic in antibody deficiency.<br /></br>• Age 27, onset in childhood/adolescence → Fits common variable immunodeficiency (CVID) or another hypogammaglobulinemia.  </p></br><p>The constellation of recurrent encapsulated-organism infections, chronic upper-airway disease, and Giardia-associated diarrhea should immediately prompt evaluation for an antibody production defect.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Why A is right)</br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify pattern: recurrent bacterial respiratory infections + chronic sinonasal disease + Giardia diarrhea.<br /></br>2. Most common adult primary immunodeficiency causing this pattern = CVID (low IgG ± low IgA/IgM).<br /></br>3. First diagnostic test for suspected humoral immunodeficiency = quantitative serum immunoglobulins (IgG, IgA, IgM).<br /></br>Core takeaway: Recurrent sinopulmonary infections + Giardia diarrhea in an adult → check immunoglobulin levels to diagnose hypogammaglobulinemia.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>Common Variable Immunodeficiency<br /></br>• Defect: impaired B-cell differentiation → low IgG ± low IgA/IgM.<br /></br>• Etiology: sporadic &gt; familial; presents after age 2, often in teens/20s.<br /></br>• Clinical: recurrent sinopulmonary bacterial infections, chronic GI infections (Giardia), autoimmune disease, ↑ risk lymphoma, bronchiectasis.<br /></br>• Treatment: lifelong IVIG replacement, aggressive infection control, routine vaccinations with non-live vaccines.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls</br>1. Diagnostic work-up begins with quantitative IgG, IgA, IgM.<br /></br>2. If low, confirm with vaccine antibody titers and flow cytometry, then start IVIG.<br /></br>3. High-resolution CT chest often obtained later to evaluate bronchiectasis but is not first step.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“CVID = Chronic sinopulmonary, Very low Igs, Intestinal Diarrhea.”<br /></br>Visual: Picture a “C-VID” tape of repeated videos (recurrent infections) with lungs, sinuses, and GI tract on loop.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>B. Pulmonary function testing<br /></br>• Helpful for asthma severity but does not explain recurrent bacterial infections and chronic diarrhea. Would not alter immediate management of suspected immunodeficiency.</p></br><p>C. CT scan of the paranasal sinuses<br /></br>• Would document sinus disease but not the underlying cause. Imaging after identifying &amp; treating root problem.</p></br><p>D. Inhaled corticosteroid therapy<br /></br>• He probably needs one for asthma (overusing albuterol), but this addresses airway inflammation, not the repeated infections/diarrhea. Missing the bigger, systemic issue.</p></br><p>E. Allergen injection immunotherapy<br /></br>• For allergic rhinitis; contraindicated/ineffective if patient has significant antibody deficiency (they cannot make protective IgG to allergen). Must diagnose immunodeficiency first.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Attributing every cough to “uncontrolled asthma.” Recurrent pneumonia + Giardia is a red flag for humoral immunodeficiency; always check immunoglobulins.</p>
117. A study is conducted to assess the accuracy of a new rapid test to detect streptococcal pharyngitis. A total of 200 patients with sore throat undergo rapid testing; these results are compared with the results of subsequent throat cultures for group A beta-hemolytic streptococcus. The results show:</br>                culture      </br>                45   10   55</br>rapid test      5   140   145</br>total           50  150 200</br>Which of the following best represents the prevalence of streptococcal pharyngitis within this population?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. 10/150 = 6.7 percent</br>b. 45/150 = 30 percent</br>c. 45/200 = 22.5 percent</br>d. 50/200 = 25 percent</br>e. 55/200 = 27.5 percent</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. 50/200  =  25 %</p></br><p>───────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Subsequent throat cultures” = gold-standard test.<br /></br>• Prevalence is the proportion of the study population that truly has the disease (culture-positive), irrespective of the index (rapid) test result.<br /></br>• 50 culture-positive patients out of 200 total participants.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning – Step-by-Step<br /></br>1. Identify the total number of true disease cases → Culture-positive column total = 50.<br /></br>2. Identify the study (denominator) size → 200 subjects.<br /></br>3. Compute prevalence = cases ÷ population = 50/200 = 0.25 = 25 %.<br /></br>Take-away: Prevalence depends ONLY on the gold-standard outcome, not on rapid-test performance.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>Group A β-hemolytic Streptococcus (Strep pyogenes) causes acute bacterial pharyngitis. Prevalence in primary-care settings varies seasonally (≈15–30 % in children during winter). Knowing local prevalence helps determine post-test probability when interpreting rapid antigen tests.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls<br /></br>• Gold standard: throat culture (24–48 h).<br /></br>• Rapid antigen detection test: high specificity, moderate sensitivity; negative tests in high-risk patients often need culture back-up.<br /></br>• First-line therapy for confirmed infection: 10-day oral penicillin V or single-dose benzathine penicillin G; amoxicillin often used in kids for palatability.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Pre-valence is Pre-test proportion.”<br /></br>– Look at the disease column total BEFORE considering the test.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. 10/150 = 6.7 % → False-positive rate (culture-negative but rapid-positive ÷ all culture-negative).<br /></br>b. 45/150 = 30 % → Positive predictive value (true positives ÷ all rapid-positives)… depends on both disease and test.<br /></br>c. 45/200 = 22.5 % → Portion of the entire sample that is true-positive by BOTH tests; ignores false negatives.<br /></br>e. 55/200 = 27.5 % → All rapid-positive results ÷ total; this is the proportion who tested positive, not necessarily the true prevalence.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Mixing up prevalence (uses gold standard) with predictive values (use index test results).</p>
118. A 77-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 4-year history of a lesion over her forearm that has increased gradually in size. She works outdoors in her garden 3 days weekly and does not wear sunscreen,</br>and she is concerned the lesion could be cancer. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. A photograph of the lesion is shown. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of</br>the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Observation only</br>b. Topical 5 percent fluorouracil therapy</br>c. Topical triamcinolone therapy</br>d. Punch biopsy of the lesion</br>e. Wide elliptical excision of the lesion</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Observation only  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Patient profile: 77-year-old woman (older age group → common benign “barnacles of aging”).<br /></br>• Lesion characteristics: “4-year history,” “gradually increased in size,” photo (not provided to you) on forearm. Classic board pictures for this scenario show a sharply demarcated, waxy, stuck-on, tan-to-dark-brown verrucous plaque.<br /></br>• Absence of red flags: no pain, bleeding, rapid change, itching, ulceration, surrounding erythema, palpable lymph nodes, or systemic symptoms.<br /></br>These clues strongly point to seborrheic keratosis (SK), a completely benign epidermal proliferation.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Identify pattern: Elderly + “stuck-on,” slowly enlarging lesion = SK.<br /></br>Step 2 – Rule out malignancy: Lacks asymmetric borders, variegated color, ulceration, scale, or actinic “sand-paper” texture.<br /></br>Step 3 – Management: SKs require no therapy unless symptomatic or for cosmetic reasons → reassurance/observation.<br /></br>Take-home message: In a classic SK without worrisome features, the safest, cheapest, and most correct test answer is “do nothing.”  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Seborrheic keratosis<br /></br>• Etiology: Benign clonal proliferation of immature keratinocytes; increased incidence with age and genetic predisposition (FGFR3, PIK3CA mutations).<br /></br>• Clinical: Sharply demarcated velvety or verrucous plaque that appears “stuck-on.” Colors range light tan to black. Most common on trunk, face, extremities.<br /></br>• Significance: Purely cosmetic. Sudden explosive onset of multiple SKs (Leser–Trélat sign) can signal an internal malignancy (most often GI adenocarcinoma), but that is not the case here.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• No treatment if asymptomatic.<br /></br>• Removal (cryotherapy, curettage) only for itching, irritation (e.g., rubbing against clothing), or cosmetic reasons.<br /></br>• No lab work or biopsy required with classic appearance.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Stuck-on barnacle in a Senior” → Seborrheic Keratosis needs no Surgery.<br /></br>Visualization: Picture ship barnacles stuck on the hull of an old boat = SKs stuck on aging skin.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Topical 5% fluorouracil therapy<br /></br>—Used for actinic keratoses (premalignant rough, erythematous, sand-paper keratotic papules) or superficial BCC. SKs are smooth/waxy, not rough, and lack scale.  </p></br><p>c. Topical triamcinolone therapy<br /></br>—Corticosteroids treat inflammatory dermatoses (eczema, psoriasis). SK is not inflammatory; steroid cream would have no effect.  </p></br><p>d. Punch biopsy of the lesion<br /></br>—Indicated when diagnosis is uncertain or lesion has atypical worrisome features (ABCDE for melanoma, ulceration, rapid change). Classic SK findings → biopsy unnecessary.  </p></br><p>e. Wide elliptical excision of the lesion<br /></br>—Appropriate for invasive malignancies (e.g., melanoma, squamous cell carcinoma) requiring margins. This benign lesion does not.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfalls<br /></br>• Mistaking an SK for melanoma/SCC: Remember “waxy/verrucous &amp; stuck-on” with long, slow history = SK.<br /></br>• Over-treating benign lesions: Board questions reward least invasive, most cost-effective management.  </p></br><p>Core concept: Recognize the benign, classic appearance of seborrheic keratosis and reassure rather than intervene.</p>
119. A 25-year-old man comes to the physician 2 hours after the sudden onset of sharp right buttock pain that radiates to the back of his knee. The pain worsens when he sits for a prolonged period or drives. He is able to walk.</br>Use of over-the-counter ibuprofen has provided mild relief. He has no history of serious illness and takes no other medications. On examination, ipsilateral and contralateral straight-leg raise testing is positive. Muscle</br>strength is normal throughout. Sensation is intact throughout. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Application of cold compresses</br>b. Recommendation of bed rest for 1 week</br>c. Recommendation of normal activities as tolerated</br>d. Traction</br>e. Use of a lumbar brace</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c.
