Recommendation of normal activities as tolerated  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Sudden sharp buttock pain radiating down the posterior thigh = classic radicular pattern of an L5/S1 herniated nucleus pulposus (“sciatica”).<br /></br>• Pain worsens with sitting/driving (↑ disc pressure).<br /></br>• Positive ipsilateral AND crossed straight-leg raise = high specificity for disc herniation.<br /></br>• Normal motor power and sensation → no red-flag neurologic deficit (no foot drop, no bowel or bladder involvement, no saddle anesthesia).<br /></br>• Age 25, otherwise healthy → typical demographic for benign, self-limited mechanical/radicular back pain.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Identify herniated disc–type low-back pain without neurologic compromise.<br /></br>2. Check for red flags (fever, weight loss, cancer history, trauma, cauda equina, progressive neuro deficit). None present.<br /></br>3. First-line conservative care = NSAIDs ± heat/ice and MOST IMPORTANTLY early ambulation / normal activity. Prolonged rest and immobilization delay recovery and increase risk of chronic pain.<br /></br>Core takeaway: In uncomplicated acute low-back/radicular pain, staying active speeds recovery; bed rest, braces, or traction are outdated.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (high yield)<br /></br>• Lumbar disc herniation → nucleus pulposus protrudes through annulus, compressing L5 or S1 nerve root.<br /></br>• Sitting, forward flexion ↑ intradiscal pressure → pain.<br /></br>• Natural history: 90% improve within 6–12 weeks with conservative care as disc reabsorbs.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. Activity as tolerated (continue work if possible, avoid heavy lifting).<br /></br>2. NSAIDs (ibuprofen, naproxen) or short-course oral steroids if severe.<br /></br>3. Heat/ice for comfort, PT/core strengthening after acute phase.<br /></br>4. Imaging (MRI) only if red flags, progressive neuro deficit, or persistent pain &gt;6 weeks despite therapy.<br /></br>5. Epidural steroid injection or surgery (microdiscectomy) reserved for refractory or severe neurologic deficit.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“BED” rest is BAD for the “BACK.”<br /></br>“Stay on TRACK, not on your BACK.” → Keep patients on their normal activity track.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Application of cold compresses<br /></br>• Can be used adjunctively for symptom relief but by itself it is not the key management principle; guidelines emphasize early mobilization as the cornerstone.</p></br><p>b. Bed rest for 1 week<br /></br>• Older practice; evidence shows ≥48 h bed rest prolongs recovery, causes deconditioning, and can worsen pain.</p></br><p>d. Traction<br /></br>• No proven benefit for lumbar radiculopathy; rarely used today.</p></br><p>e. Lumbar brace<br /></br>• May be helpful post-operatively or for specific instability but does not improve outcomes in acute herniated disc; may encourage dependency and weaken core muscles.</p></br><p>Common Pitfalls<br /></br>• Ordering immediate MRI in every back-pain patient (unnecessary without red flags).<br /></br>• Prescribing prolonged bed rest or braces thinking they “protect” the spine—actually delays recovery.</p>
120. A 2-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department 30 minutes after he was involved in a motor vehicle collision. He was sitting unrestrained on his aunt's lap in the front passenger seat during the collision, and the automobile's air bags deployed on impact. Paramedics at the scene immobilized his spine with a cervical collar, and his head was taped to a backboard during transport to the hospital. He has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. On arrival, he is crying and moving all extremities. Temperature is 37.2 C 99.0 F, pulse is 120/min, respirations are 25/min, and blood pressure is 110/60 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 99 percent. Examination shows abrasions over the face and neck. No other abnormalities are noted. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. CT scan of the chest and abdomen</br>b. Intravenous administration of antibiotics</br>c. Intravenous administration of sedatives</br>d. MRI of the brain</br>e. X-rays of the cervical spine and chest</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. X-rays of the cervical spine and chest  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Mechanism: High-energy MVC, unrestrained lap passenger in front seat with air-bag deployment → high risk for occult cervical‐spine and thoracic injuries.<br /></br>• Clinical status: ABCs intact (crying, moving all extremities, normal VS, 99% SpO₂). No neurologic deficit, but the child is only 2 y/o (cannot be “clinically cleared” of C-spine injury by exam alone).<br /></br>• Immobilization already in place by EMS → next step in ATLS algorithm after primary survey is screening radiographs (C-spine + chest).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-by-Step)<br /></br>1. Primary survey (A, B, C) is normal.<br /></br>2. Proceed to adjuncts to the primary survey: portable C-spine series + chest X-ray (and usually pelvic X-ray) in every major blunt trauma.<br /></br>3. A 2-year-old cannot reliably localize pain or cooperate with an exam, so imaging is mandatory even if neurologic exam appears normal.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: After stabilization of a pediatric trauma patient with a high-risk mechanism, the immediate next step is plain radiographs of the cervical spine and chest to screen for life-threatening but initially silent injuries.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Air-bag + lap-belt sequencing in toddlers transmits force to the neck/chest → C-spine fractures (esp. C1–C2, atlanto-occipital dissociation) and pulmonary contusions can be clinically occult.<br /></br>• Children have relatively larger heads and more flexible ligaments → higher C-spine injury risk despite a normal neuro exam.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment Pearls<br /></br>• Initial trauma imaging:<br /></br>  – AP/lateral C-spine (plus odontoid view if child ≥3 y)<br /></br>  – Portable AP chest<br /></br>• CT C-spine is reserved if plain films are abnormal or inadequate.<br /></br>• CT chest/abdomen only if abdominal exam, hemodynamics, or chest film suggests internal injury.<br /></br>• MRI brain is for suspected intracranial injury after abnormal neuro exam or CT.<br /></br>• Antibiotics: only for open wounds or suspected contamination.<br /></br>• Sedatives: avoid until initial assessment complete; can mask neuro changes.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“ABC → ‘C-C’: After Airway/Breathing/Circulation, get C-spine &amp; Chest films.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. CT scan of the chest and abdomen<br /></br>   • Large radiation dose; not screening in a stable patient without abdominal or chest findings.<br /></br>b. Intravenous antibiotics<br /></br>   • No open fractures, penetrating trauma, or suspected infection.<br /></br>c. Intravenous sedatives<br /></br>   • Would obscure neuro exam and airway protective reflexes; not indicated.<br /></br>d. MRI of the brain<br /></br>   • Patient is neurologically intact with no LOC, vomiting, or focal deficits. MRI is not part of the initial survey.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line: In a hemodynamically stable toddler after high-energy blunt trauma, the immediate post-primary-survey step is to obtain screening plain films of the cervical spine and chest.</p>
121. A 52-year-old man comes to the physician 2 days after noticing that his eyes and skin appear yellow. Four days ago, he received the diagnosis of sinusitis and began treatment with trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole. During the past 6 months, he has traveled to Nicaragua and Belize. He has been sexually active with 12 partners during the past 2 years and uses condoms inconsistently. He drinks three to four beers weekly. He does not smoke cigarettes or use illicit drugs. His temperature is 36.6 C 97.8 F , pulse is 72/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 136/84 mm Hg. Examination shows generalized jaundice and a diffuse, macula, nonblanching rash over the chest and back. There is scleral icterus. Laboratory studies show: </br>Hemoglobin</br>Serum</br>Bilirubin, total</br>Direct</br>Alkaline phosphatase</br>AST</br>ALT</br>HBsAg</br>Hepatitis B surface antibody</br>13.2 g/dL</br>5.5 mg/dL</br>2.2 mg/dL</br>58 U/L</br>372 U/L</br>250 U/L</br>negative</br>positive</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate initial step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Discontinuation of trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole therapy</br>b. Subcutaneous peginterferon alfa-2a therapy</br>c. Intravenous ceftriaxone therapy</br>d. Endoscopic retrograde cholangiopancreatography</br>e. Liver biopsy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a.  Discontinuation of trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole therapy  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Eyes and skin appear yellow … 2 days” → acute onset jaundice<br /></br>• “Began treatment with trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole 4 days ago” → new drug exposure<br /></br>• “Diffuse, macular, non-blanching rash” → drug hypersensitivity clue<br /></br>• Labs: AST 372, ALT 250 (hepatocellular pattern); Alk Phos only mildly ↑ (58) → NOT predominantly cholestatic/obstructive<br /></br>• Bilirubin total 5.5 mg/dL; direct 2.2 mg/dL → mixed conjugated hyper­bilirubinemia<br /></br>• Viral serology: HBsAg (–), HBsAb (+) → immune, no active HBV  </p></br><p>Key pattern: Acute hepatocellular injury + rash after a sulfonamide antibiotic = idiosyncratic drug-induced liver injury (DILI).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Temporal relationship: Symptoms start within days of starting TMP-SMX.<br /></br>2. Extra-hepatic findings: Rash (± fever/eosinophilia) typical of hypersensitivity-type DILI.<br /></br>3. Lab pattern: Transaminases &gt;&gt; Alk Phos → hepatocellular, supports DILI rather than obstructive or alcoholic disease.<br /></br>4. Rule-outs: Viral hepatitis unlikely (HBV immune, no viral prodrome data); alcoholic hepatitis unlikely (AST/ALT &lt;2 and minimal EtOH use); biliary obstruction absent (no RUQ pain, minimal Alk Phos rise).<br /></br>5. Management principle: First and most important step in any suspected DILI is immediate withdrawal of the offending drug.  </p></br><p>Take-home: “New drug + jaundice ± rash → STOP the drug first.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole can cause idiosyncratic, immune-mediated hepatocellular or mixed liver injury (days–weeks after starting).<br /></br>• Mechanism: Sulfonamide metabolite acts as hapten → T-cell–mediated hepatic injury; often accompanies fever, rash, eosinophilia (“DRESS-like” picture).<br /></br>• Usually self-limited once drug is stopped; severe cases may progress to fulminant hepatic failure but are rare.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. Immediate: Discontinue TMP-SMX.<br /></br>2. Supportive care: Monitor LFTs, bilirubin, INR over the next few days.<br /></br>3. No specific antidote; corticosteroids generally NOT helpful.<br /></br>4. Further work-up (ultrasound, viral serologies) only if LFTs continue to rise or diagnosis uncertain.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>Sulfa drugs “SULF” your liver:<br /></br>• Sudden transaminase Spike<br /></br>• Untoward rash<br /></br>• Liver injury<br /></br>• First step = Stop the drug  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Peginterferon alfa-2a – Used for chronic hepatitis B or C. Patient is HBV immune, no evidence of chronic viral hepatitis.<br /></br>c. IV ceftriaxone – Empiric tx for acute cholangitis. He lacks Charcot triad (fever, RUQ pain, jaundice) and has minimal Alk Phos elevation.<br /></br>d. ERCP – Indicated for obstructive jaundice or cholangitis (marked Alk Phos, dilated ducts, RUQ pain). Not supported by exam or labs.<br /></br>e. Liver biopsy – Reserved when diagnosis unclear or to stage chronic disease. In clear-cut DILI, biopsy is unnecessary at presentation; management begins with drug cessation.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core Concept<br /></br>Drug-induced liver injury is a diagnosis of exclusion but strongly suggested by temporal relationship and hypersensitivity features. The single most important immediate intervention is withdrawal of the offending medication.</p>
122. A previously healthy 37-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 2-day history of feve and persistent cough productive of yellow sputum. He has not had shortness of breath. He does not smoke. His temperature is 37.9 C 100.2 F, pulse is 88/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 134/82 mm Hg. Scattered rhonchi are heard bilaterally. During the examination, he has an episode of severe coughing. An x-ray of the chest shows no abnormalities. In addition to treatment of this patient's symptoms, which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Amoxicillin</br>b. Azithromycin</br>c. Levofloxacin</br>d. Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole</br>e. No additional treatment is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. No additional treatment is indicated  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Previously healthy, 37-year-old, acute illness (2 days).<br /></br>• Main symptoms: low-grade fever (100.2 °F), cough with yellow sputum, no dyspnea.<br /></br>• Lung exam: scattered rhonchi; CXR: completely normal.<br /></br>Key clue: Normal chest X-ray rules out community-acquired pneumonia. In an otherwise well adult, the combination of acute cough + minimal systemic findings + normal imaging = acute bronchitis, almost always viral.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Is it pneumonia? → Requires an infiltrate on imaging; CXR is clear.<br /></br>Step 2: Any high-risk host or red flags (age ≥65, chronic lung dz, immunocompromise)? → None.<br /></br>Step 3: Most cases of acute bronchitis are viral (influenza, RSV, adenovirus, parainfluenza, coronavirus). Antibiotics do not shorten course or prevent complications.<br /></br>Therefore, beyond symptomatic care (hydration, NSAIDs/acetaminophen, +/- dextromethorphan or ipratropium for cough), no antimicrobial therapy is warranted.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: A normal chest radiograph in a young, healthy adult with acute cough almost always indicates viral acute bronchitis → no antibiotics.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Viral infection causes transient inflammation of the tracheobronchial tree → cough (often productive), rhonchi/wheezes that clear after coughing.<br /></br>• Sputum color does NOT distinguish viral from bacterial etiology. Purulent sputum simply reflects sloughed inflammatory cells.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>First-line: Reassurance + symptomatic therapy<br /></br>– Antipyretic/analgesic: acetaminophen, ibuprofen<br /></br>– Antitussive: dextromethorphan or short course of inhaled/ipratropium if bothersome<br /></br>– Consider influenza testing/antivirals only if presentation within 48 h of flu season epidemic.<br /></br>No routine antibiotics, no routine follow-up imaging unless symptoms persist &gt;3 weeks or worsen.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Bronchitis = Bronchi are irritable but X-ray is CLEAR” → CLEAR X-ray = CLEAR of antibiotics.<br /></br>OR think “ABC”: Acute Bronchitis—CXR clear—No abx.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Amoxicillin – Covers typical CAP organisms (Strep pneumo, H. flu). Would be chosen if focal consolidation, fever, abnormal CXR. Here CXR is normal.<br /></br>b. Azithromycin – Macrolide for atypical CAP (Mycoplasma, Chlamydia) or COPD exacerbation. Not needed in viral bronchitis without structural lung disease.<br /></br>c. Levofloxacin – Respiratory fluoroquinolone reserved for comorbid/older patients or when drug-resistant Strep pneumo suspected. Over-treats and carries tendon, QT risks.<br /></br>d. Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole – Not first-line for CAP; used for MRSA skin infections, UTIs, PJP prophylaxis/treatment. Irrelevant here.<br /></br>Common trap: “Yellow/green sputum = bacterial” – FALSE; sputum color reflects neutrophils, not necessarily bacteria.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line: In healthy adults with acute cough and a normal CXR, think viral acute bronchitis → symptomatic care only; antibiotics provide no benefit and promote resistance.</p>
123. A 17-year-old boy comes to the physician because of a scrotal mass that has become increasingly painful during the past 3 days. He is not certain how long the mass has been present. There is no histo [Y of trauma. He is sexually active with several partners and uses condoms inconsistently. Examination shows a tender mass posterior to the left testis. Which of the following is the most likely cause of these findings?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Incompetence of the spermatic vein</br>b. Infection of the epididymis</br>c. Patency of the processus vaginal is</br>d. Retractility of the testis</br>e. Torsion of the appendix testis</br>F. Torsion of the spermatic cord</br></br></br><p>Correct answer chosen by NBME: Infection of the epididymis  </p></br><p>==================================================</br>Vignette analysis<br /></br>1. Age/sex: 17-year-old adolescent male → sexually active, highest STI risk group.<br /></br>2. Time course: “increasingly painful during the past 3 days” → subacute (hours-to-days) rather than sudden (torsion) or chronic (varicocele/hydrocele).<br /></br>3. Mass location: “posterior to the left testis” → exactly where the epididymis sits.<br /></br>4. Other clues: No history of trauma; inconsistent condom use → raises suspicion for Chlamydia/Gonorrhoea infection.</p></br><p>These four pieces together point directly toward acute epididymitis.</p></br><p>==================================================</br>Clinical critical reasoning / stepwise thought process<br /></br>• First separate PAINFUL vs PAINLESS scrotal masses.<br /></br> – Painful: torsion (testis or appendix testis), epididymitis, trauma, incarcerated hernia.<br /></br> – Painless: varicocele, hydrocele, retractile testis.<br /></br>• Next assess the tempo.<br /></br> – Sudden, severe (&lt;6 h) with high-riding testis = torsion of the spermatic cord.<br /></br> – Gradual (days) with posterior tenderness = epididymitis.<br /></br>• Physical-exam localization.<br /></br> – Epididymis runs posterolateral to the testis → a tender “posterior” nodule = epididymal head/tail inflammation.<br /></br> – Appendix testis torsion produces focal tenderness at the upper pole, sometimes a “blue dot.”<br /></br>• Risk factors: unprotected intercourse + age &lt;35 → typical causative organisms (Chlamydia trachomatis, Neisseria gonorrhoeae).</p></br><p>Key takeaway: In a sexually active teen, a subacutely painful posterior scrotal mass is epididymitis until proven otherwise.</p></br><p>==================================================</br>Pathophysiology review<br /></br>• Pathogen ascends from urethra → vas deferens → epididymis, causing inflammation and edema.<br /></br>• Organisms:<br /></br> – &lt;35 y: C. trachomatis, N. gonorrhoeae (treat with ceftriaxone + doxycycline).<br /></br> – &gt;35 y or instrumentation: coliforms (E. coli) → treat with fluoroquinolone.<br /></br>• Clinical triad: posterior testicular pain, epididymal swelling, relief when scrotum elevated (Prehn sign—NOT reliable enough to rule out torsion but classic for exams).</p></br><p>==================================================</br>Diagnostics &amp; treatment (for memory, not required to answer)<br /></br>1. Doppler US if diagnosis unclear or to rule out torsion (normal or ↑ blood flow in epididymitis vs ↓/absent in torsion).<br /></br>2. First-line therapy (&lt;35 y): single-dose IM ceftriaxone + 14 d oral doxycycline; advise abstinence ×7 d &amp; treat partners.<br /></br>3. Supportive: NSAIDs, scrotal support, ice.</p></br><p>==================================================</br>High-yield mnemonics / memory aids<br /></br>“EPI-diddy is POSTERior &amp; slow”<br /></br>EPI → Epididymitis<br /></br>POSTER → Posterior tenderness<br /></br>diddy = takes a few “diddays” (days) to hurt, not minutes (torsion).</p></br><p>==================================================</br>Why the other options are wrong<br /></br>A. Incompetence of the spermatic vein (varicocele)<br /></br> – “Bag of worms,” soft, collapses when supine, classically painless and long-standing. Posterior pain acute over 3 days is atypical.</p></br><p>C. Patency of the processus vaginalis (hydrocele)<br /></br> – Painless, transilluminates, anterior to testis, present since infancy or slowly enlarging.</p></br><p>D. Retractility of the testis<br /></br> – High-riding testis that can be manipulated into scrotum; no discrete tender mass.</p></br><p>E. Torsion of the appendix testis<br /></br> – Focal upper-pole tenderness, blue dot sign, usually boys 7-12 y; the epididymis itself is not the mass.</p></br><p>F. Torsion of the spermatic cord (testicular torsion)<br /></br> – Sudden severe pain (&lt;6 h), high-riding testis, absent cremasteric reflex; mass involves entire testis, not just posterior.</p></br><p>==================================================</br>Bottom line: Posterior, gradually worsening pain in a sexually active adolescent male = acute epididymitis caused by STI infection.</p>
124. A 52-year-old woman is admitted to the hospital for treatment of acute diverticulitis. After 2 days of intravenous antibiotic therapy, she has a temperature of 39.1  C 102.4  F and abdominal rigidity. Her leukocyte count is 17,400/mm 3 with 15 percent bands. The physician explains to the patient that she will need to undergo sigmoid colectomy and that, depending on the findings, she may require a colostomy. The patient agrees to the operation but refuses to undergo a colostomy. The patient's husband is in the hospital but is not with the patient at this time. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Engage the patient in a discussion of the possible operative findings and management options</br>b. Notify the hospital attorney</br>c. Obtain informed consent from the patient's husband</br>d. Obtain a psychiatric assessment of the patient's mental capacity</br>e. Perform the operation without a colostomy, regardless of the findings</br>F. Perform the operation and a colostomy if indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Engage the patient in a discussion of the possible operative findings and management options  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Competent adult: 52-year-old, awake, oriented, clearly expressing a preference ⇒ has decision-making capacity.<br /></br>• Emergency surgery is likely (peritonitis, T 39 °C, bandemia), but not so immediately life-saving that there is no time for discussion.<br /></br>• She gives partial consent (sigmoid colectomy) but explicitly refuses a potential component (colostomy).<br /></br>Key clue: “depending on the findings, she may require a colostomy … patient refuses colostomy.” This signals an incomplete/possibly uninformed consent.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Ethical principles:<br /></br>   • Autonomy – respect the patient’s wishes.<br /></br>   • Informed consent – patient must understand risks, benefits, and alternatives of <em>all</em> reasonably likely intra-operative possibilities.<br /></br>2. Problems here:<br /></br>   • She may not fully appreciate why a colostomy could become unavoidable (e.g., gross contamination, distal bowel not healthy enough for anastomosis).<br /></br>   • Surgeon cannot override her refusal, nor can another surrogate overrule a competent patient.<br /></br>3. Therefore, the next step is a thorough discussion (benefits, risks, likelihood, potential outcomes) to see if she will voluntarily modify or reaffirm her refusal.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: When a competent patient places limits on a consent, the surgeon must clarify understanding and attempt to reach agreement; do not proceed against her wishes, bypass her with a surrogate, or call lawyers before trying to educate.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (brief)<br /></br>Acute diverticulitis → perforation → generalized peritonitis → emergent source control. Options:<br /></br>• Hartmann procedure (resection + end colostomy) if anastomosis unsafe.<br /></br>• Primary anastomosis ± diverting loop ileostomy if field is clean and patient stable.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Pre-op goal: obtain <em>broad</em> consent that covers all likely intra-op scenarios.<br /></br>• If patient <em>after</em> full discussion still refuses a life-saving component, surgeon must either:<br /></br>  – Respect refusal and attempt limited surgery, or<br /></br>  – Transfer care if unwilling to operate under those limits.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CAPACITY before COLON” – If the patient has capacity, clarify Consent before scalpel touches Colon.<br /></br>4 steps of informed consent: CIRC<br /></br>   1) Condition<br /></br>   2) Intervention (and alternatives)<br /></br>   3) Risks &amp; benefits<br /></br>   4) Chance of each outcome  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Notify the hospital attorney – Premature. Legal help is sought only after efforts at physician-patient communication fail or if court order needed (incapacitated patient, minors, etc.).  </p></br><p>C. Obtain informed consent from the patient’s husband – Surrogates are used only when the patient LACKS capacity. Here she is competent.  </p></br><p>D. Obtain a psychiatric assessment of mental capacity – No evidence of delirium, psychosis, or cognitive impairment. Disagreeing with a physician does not equal incapacity.  </p></br><p>E. Perform the operation without a colostomy regardless of findings – Violates surgical standard of care if anastomosis unsafe; also could cause lethal leak/sepsis.  </p></br><p>F. Perform the operation and a colostomy if indicated – Violates patient autonomy; constitutes battery if done against expressed refusal.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>A competent patient who restricts consent requires further discussion to ensure understanding and voluntary choice; educate first, act later.</p>
125. A 16-year-old girl is brought to the physician because her parents are concerned about her eating habits. During the past 8 months, she has stopped eating meals with the family, expressed concern about her weight, and lectured the family about improving their dietary practices. The patient says that eating more nutritious foods has decreased her appetite. She says the fact that she has enough energy to be on the cross-country running and cheerleading teams shows she is eating appropriately. She worries she does not have the right body shape for fashionable clothes and thinks she would be more popular if she could dress better. She receives grades of A's in school but worries she will not be able to maintain her grade point average because this year has been more challenging since she began taking several advanced-level classes. She says she has not been spending much time with friends because of schoolwork demands. The patient is 178 cm 5 ft 10 in tall and weighs 53 kg 116 lb; BMI is 17 kg/m ~. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. Mental status examination shows a depressed mood and affect; the patient becomes anxious when discussing school performance and her weight. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Anorexia nervosa</br>b. Body dysmorphic disorder</br>c. Generalized anxiety disorder</br>d. Major depressive disorder</br>E  Normal adolescent behavior</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. Anorexia nervosa  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 16-year-old female → peak age &amp; sex for eating disorders<br /></br>• Persistent food restriction: “stopped eating meals with the family,” “decreased her appetite”<br /></br>• Intense weight/shape concern: “worries she does not have the right body shape,” “would be more popular if she could dress better,” lectures family on diet<br /></br>• Compensatory behavior: high-volume exercise (cross-country, cheerleading) → common in the restrictive type<br /></br>• Low body weight: 178 cm, 53 kg → BMI 17 kg/m² (&lt;18.5)<br /></br>• Distorted self-evaluation &amp; denial: insists she is “eating appropriately” because she has “enough energy”<br /></br>These are the classic DSM-5 pillars:<br /></br>1. Restriction of intake → significantly low weight<br /></br>2. Intense fear of gaining weight or persistent behavior that interferes with weight gain<br /></br>3. Disturbance in body-image or weight/shape self-evaluation  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Calculate BMI → objectively underweight.<br /></br>2. Ask: Is weight loss intentional &amp; driven by body-image distortion? Yes.<br /></br>3. Rule out other psychiatric disorders: no single body-part preoccupation (body dysmorphic), her anxiety/depression are secondary to eating/weight fears, and behavior is not normative.<br /></br>Therefore → Anorexia nervosa, restrictive subtype.</p></br><p>Take-home Message: In a thin adolescent who purposefully restricts food and over-exercises due to distorted weight/shape beliefs, think anorexia nervosa even if vital signs and menses are still normal.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Multifactorial: genetic predisposition + perfectionistic/obsessive traits + sociocultural thin-ideal pressure.<br /></br>• Chronic caloric deficit → hypothalamic–pituitary suppression (↓GnRH → amenorrhea), bone loss (↓estrogen), bradycardia, hypotension, lanugo, constipation.<br /></br>• High mortality (suicide, arrhythmia).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>1. FIRST-LINE:<br /></br>   • Nutritional rehabilitation &amp; weight restoration (structured meal plans, supervised refeeding).<br /></br>   • Psychotherapy: family-based therapy (adolescents) or CBT-E.<br /></br>2. Adjunct: SSRI (fluoxetine) ONLY after weight ≥18.5 or for comorbid depression/OCD.<br /></br>3. Hospitalize if: BMI &lt;15, vital-sign instability, electrolyte derangements, arrhythmia, suicidal intent.<br /></br>4. Watch for refeeding syndrome → check baseline electrolytes, especially phosphate.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ANOREXIA” mnemonic:<br /></br>A – Adolescent female<br /></br>N – Nutritional restriction<br /></br>O – Over-exercise<br /></br>R – Relentless fear of fat<br /></br>E – Emaciated (BMI&lt;18.5)<br /></br>X – eXtreme perfectionism<br /></br>I – Image distortion<br /></br>A – Amenorrhea/Arrhythmia risk  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>B. Body dysmorphic disorder<br /></br>• Preoccupation with a specific perceived physical flaw (e.g., “my nose is huge”).<br /></br>• Weight/shape worries about overall body size fall under eating disorders, not BDD.  </p></br><p>C. Generalized anxiety disorder<br /></br>• Excessive worry about multiple domains for ≥6 months, but no primary body-image distortion or food restriction causing underweight. Her worries are largely secondary to weight and school perfectionism.  </p></br><p>D. Major depressive disorder<br /></br>• Can present with decreased appetite/weight loss, but weight loss is unintentional, no fear of gaining weight, and other neurovegetative symptoms dominate (sleep change, anhedonia). Here, caloric restriction is purposeful with body-image distortion.  </p></br><p>E. Normal adolescent behavior<br /></br>• Some body consciousness is normal, but sustained food restriction, BMI 17, and social/functional impairment are not. Adolescents do not normally lecture families on diet or avoid meals to lose weight.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Key Exam Pearl<br /></br>Whenever you see a thin, perfectionistic teenage girl restricting calories and over-exercising with intense body-shape concern → pick anorexia nervosa.</p>
126. During observation of a surgical procedure, a physician notices the anesthesiologist falling asleep; the observing physician says the anesthesiologist's name, and he wakes up and attends to the remainder of the operation appropriately. After the procedure, the physician approaches the anesthesiologist who states that he was up late last evening attending to an emergency procedure. The surgeon later informs the physician that this anesthesiologist frequently falls asleep in the operating room. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step for the physician?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. No action is necessary since the surgeon has been notified</br>b. Recommend that the anesthesiologist undergo sleep studies</br>c. Report the anesthesiologist to the chief of the medical staff</br>d. Report the anesthesiologist to the police</br>e. Report the anesthesiologist to the specialty society</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Report the anesthesiologist to the chief of the medical staff  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Anesthesiologist falling asleep” → evidence of impairment during direct patient care.<br /></br>• “Surgeon … informs … frequently falls asleep” → pattern of recurrent unsafe behavior, not an isolated lapse.<br /></br>• Impairment places current and future patients at immediate risk.<br /></br>Key takeaway: A physician who directly observes (or reliably learns of) an impaired colleague has an ethical and legal duty to act to protect patients.   </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Identify impairment: Sleeping in the OR while responsible for anesthesia is unequivocal impairment.<br /></br>Step 2 – Immediate patient safety: You already intervened (woke him up).<br /></br>Step 3 – Prevent future harm: Must report through the hospital’s chain of command or impaired-provider program so the colleague can be evaluated and restricted if necessary.<br /></br>Step 4 – Document and follow institutional policy.  </p></br><p>Core concept: Physicians must report an impaired colleague to the appropriate supervisory body within the institution; simply telling another clinician (the surgeon) is insufficient.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (brief)<br /></br>Not a disease process question but remember:<br /></br>• Causes of acute sleepiness in the OR may include sleep deprivation, sedative misuse, OSA, narcolepsy, or substance abuse.<br /></br>• Any of these constitute possible impairment until formally evaluated.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• The impaired-physician program or chief of staff will arrange formal evaluation (sleep study, toxicology screen, etc.) and determine fitness for duty.<br /></br>• The reporting physician’s role is ONLY referral, not direct diagnostic work-up of the colleague.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“SEE IT, REPORT IT, PROTECT”—If you See Impairment that Endangers patients, Report through institutional channels to Protect patients.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. No action is necessary since the surgeon has been notified<br /></br>  • Pitfall: Assuming “somebody else” will handle it. Surgeon is NOT an authoritative body with power to remove privileges or mandate evaluation. Duty not fulfilled.  </p></br><p>b. Recommend that the anesthesiologist undergo sleep studies<br /></br>  • Inappropriately takes on a consultant role without addressing immediate safety. Evaluation should be arranged by institutional leadership, not an informal suggestion.  </p></br><p>d. Report the anesthesiologist to the police<br /></br>  • Police involvement is reserved for criminal activity (e.g., drug diversion, assault), not professional impairment.  </p></br><p>e. Report the anesthesiologist to the specialty society<br /></br>  • Bypasses the hospital’s primary responsibility and delays action; specialty boards or societies typically become involved only after local remediation fails.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>When you directly observe a colleague whose impairment endangers patients, you must report the incident to the appropriate institutional authority (chief of staff, patient-safety officer, or physician-impairment program).</p>
127. A 32-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 1-week history of tenderness and swelling of her neck. She has no history of serious illness. Her only medication is an oral contraceptive. Her temperature is 38.1  C 100.5 F, pulse is 95/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 110/70 mm Hg. Examination of the neck shows no masses. The thyroid gland is slightly enlarged and tender. No nodules are palpated. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Serum studies show a thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration of 0.3 μU/ml and thyroxine T4 concentration of 11.5 μg/dl. Antithyroid antibodies are not detected. A 131 1 thyroid scan shows decreased uptake. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Acute suppurative thyroiditis</br>b. Chronic lymphocytic Hashimoto thyroiditis</br>c. Exogenous use of thyroid hormone</br>d. Graves disease</br>e. Subacute granulomatous de Quervain thyroiditis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Sub-acute granulomatous (de Quervain) thyroiditis  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “1-week history of tenderness and swelling of her neck … thyroid gland is slightly enlarged and tender”<br /></br>  – PAINFUL thyroid → think sub-acute (de Quervain) or acute suppurative. Most other thyroid disorders are painless.<br /></br>• Low TSH (0.3 µU/mL) + high T4 (11.5 µg/dL) → biochemical thyrotoxicosis.<br /></br>• 131-I uptake ↓ → the gland is not synthesizing new hormone; instead pre-formed hormone is leaking out.<br /></br>• No antithyroid antibodies → argues against Hashimoto or Graves.<br /></br>• Low-grade fever (38 °C) and recent onset are common after a viral URI in sub-acute thyroiditis.  </p></br><p>Key pattern: Painful, tender thyroid + transient hyperthyroidism + ↓ radio-iodine uptake = sub-acute granulomatous (de Quervain) thyroiditis.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Hyperthyroid labs → Graves, toxic multinodular goiter, thyroiditis, exogenous hormone.<br /></br>2. Radio-iodine uptake ↓ → rules OUT Graves/ toxic multinodular (both would have high uptake).<br /></br>3. Tender thyroid → rules OUT factitious thyroxine use (painless) and most chronic thyroiditides.<br /></br>4. Lack of abscess or focal fluctuant mass → makes acute suppurative unlikely.<br /></br>5. Therefore sub-acute granulomatous thyroiditis is the only choice matching all findings.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: de Quervain thyroiditis = painful thyroid + transient thyrotoxicosis + very low RAIU.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Often follows viral URI (mumps, coxsackie, influenza).<br /></br>• Granulomatous inflammation with multinucleated giant cells destroys follicles → spill of pre-formed T4/T3.<br /></br>• Phases: hyperthyroid (2–6 wk) → euthyroid → hypothyroid (occasionally) → recovery.<br /></br>• ESR &amp; CRP markedly elevated; antithyroid antibodies usually absent.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. Symptomatic: NSAIDs or high-dose aspirin for pain/inflammation.<br /></br>2. If severe pain or thyrotoxic symptoms: corticosteroids (prednisone) + β-blocker (propranolol) for palpitations.<br /></br>3. No role for antithyroid drugs—hormone excess is from release, not synthesis.<br /></br>4. Self-limited; &gt;90 % recover normal thyroid function within months.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>• “de QuerPAIN thyroiditis” – the only PAINFUL thyroiditis commonly tested.<br /></br>• Low uptake in painful thyroid = hormone “LEAK” not “MAKE.”<br /></br>• Sequence: Pain, high ESR, low uptake, transient hyper → hypo → resolve.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Acute suppurative thyroiditis<br /></br>   – Usually bacterial; presents with high fever, toxic appearance, unilateral fluctuant mass; thyroid function tests are typically NORMAL. Not just tender enlargement with thyrotoxicosis.  </p></br><p>b. Chronic lymphocytic (Hashimoto) thyroiditis<br /></br>   – Painless, firm “rubbery” goiter; positive anti-TPO/anti-TG antibodies; often hypothyroid, not hyper-. Uptake may be normal or high early, not markedly suppressed.  </p></br><p>c. Exogenous use of thyroid hormone (factitious thyrotoxicosis)<br /></br>   – Low TSH, high T4, low RAIU (matches labs) BUT thyroid is non-tender and usually atrophic/not enlarged. No fever.  </p></br><p>d. Graves disease<br /></br>   – Diffuse, painless goiter; eye signs; stimulatory TSH-R antibodies; HIGH radio-iodine uptake because the gland is over-producing hormone. Tenderness and low uptake exclude it.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>A painful, tender thyroid with transient hyperthyroidism and markedly decreased radio-iodine uptake is classic for sub-acute granulomatous (de Quervain) thyroiditis.</p>
128. Seven days after cesarean delivery for arrest of descent, a 27-year-old woman, gravida 1, para 1, remains in the hospital because of spiking temperatures to 39.2 C 102.5 F since delivery. Treatment with broad-spectrum antibiotics has not resolved the fever. She feels well when she does not have a fever. Laboratory studies at 35 weeks' gestation were positive for Streptococcus aga/actiae group 8, and she was treated with intrapartum penicillin. Her temperature now is 38.3 C 101  F. Examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Endomyometritis</br>b. Group 8 streptococcal sepsis</br>c. Occult bowel injury</br>d. Septic pelvic thrombophlebitis</br>e. Wound abscess</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Septic pelvic thrombophlebitis (SPT)</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Post-cesarean patient (highest-risk delivery for postpartum infection).<br /></br>• Daily temps to 39 °C for an entire week despite adequate broad-spectrum antibiotics = “persistent, spiking fevers unresponsive to antibiotics” – the buzz phrase for SPT.<br /></br>• Patient feels well when afebrile and has a completely normal physical exam (no abdominal tenderness, no uterine tenderness, no foul lochia, incision looks fine).<br /></br>• No other localizing clues → points to a deep, occult source rather than endometritis, wound, or bowel injury.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1  Think common first: endometritis or wound infection after C-section.<br /></br>Step 2  Both would show local findings (uterine tenderness, purulent lochia OR erythematous incision, drainage) and almost always defervesce within 48 h of clinda + gent or other broad coverage.<br /></br>Step 3  Persistent fevers with a “bland” exam after ≥3–4 days of antibiotics → raises suspicion for septic pelvic thrombophlebitis (an infected clot in the ovarian veins or deep pelvic veins).<br /></br>Core takeaway: Recurrent or persistent postpartum fevers that do NOT respond to adequate antibiotics + no obvious source → think SPT; add anticoagulation.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Endothelial injury (surgery, placental separation) + venous stasis (pregnancy) + hypercoagulability (pregnancy) → thrombus in ovarian/pelvic veins.<br /></br>• Superinfection of the thrombus by typical pelvic organisms → intermittent showering of bacteria → high, swinging fevers.<br /></br>• Because clot is walled off, IV antibiotics alone cannot eradicate the nidus; need anticoagulation to lyse/clot and allow penetration.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. Empiric broad-spectrum IV antibiotics (already given).<br /></br>2. Add IV heparin (or LMWH) for 7–10 days; defervescence usually within 24–48 h once heparin started—diagnostic and therapeutic.<br /></br>3. CT/MRI with contrast or Doppler US can show enlarged, thrombosed ovarian vein, but imaging is not mandatory if classic presentation and rapid response to heparin.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Postpartum Fever Rule of 3’s”<br /></br>• 3+ days of fever<br /></br>• 3 Cs tried (Clinda, Gent, Cephs)<br /></br>• 3 letters left: S-P-T → Septic Pelvic Thrombophlebitis<br /></br>Mnemonic: “Fever that BUGS (Broad antibiotics) but won’t BUDGE = Clot (SPT).”</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Breakdown<br /></br>a. Endomyometritis<br /></br>   – Presents 1-3 days postpartum with uterine tenderness &amp; foul lochia; responds quickly to clinda + gent. Persistence after a week argues strongly against it.  </p></br><p>b. Group B streptococcal sepsis<br /></br>   – Would present acutely ill, often with hypotension or focal bacteremia; broad-spectrum coverage would treat GBS. Prior intrapartum prophylaxis also makes this unlikely.  </p></br><p>c. Occult bowel injury<br /></br>   – Would cause severe abdominal pain, ileus, peritoneal signs, leukocytosis ± free air/fluid. Exam here is benign.  </p></br><p>e. Wound abscess<br /></br>   – Local erythema, tenderness, fluctuance, drainage at incision; none are described. Also usually improves (or worsens locally) rather than giving purely spiking fevers without findings.  </p></br><p>Bottom Line<br /></br>In any postpartum patient with spiking fevers that persist &gt;48 h despite adequate antibiotics and with no localizing signs, immediately consider septic pelvic thrombophlebitis and add anticoagulation.</p>
129. A 72-year-old woman comes to the physician because of generalized fatigue for 1 week. During this period, she has occasionally had light-headedness on standing up guickly. She has not had chest pain or shortness of breath. She has hypertension and coronary artery disease. Current medications include amlodipine and hydrochlorothiazide. She is alert and oriented to person, place, and time. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 38/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 80/40 mm Hg. Crackles are heard at both lung bases. S 1 and S 2 are normal. A grade 2/6 systolic murmur is heard best at the fourth left intercostal space with radiation to the carotid arteries. There is no peripheral edema. An ECG is shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Electrophysiologic study</br>b. Dobutamine stress echocardiography</br>c. Add amiodarone to the regimen</br>d. Add diltiazem to the regimen</br>e. Insert cardiac pacemaker</br>F. Insert implantable cardiac defibrillator</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Insert cardiac pacemaker</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Pulse 38/min” + “blood pressure 80/40 mm Hg” → symptomatic severe bradycardia<br /></br>• “Generalized fatigue … light-headedness on standing” → low-output symptoms<br /></br>• Elderly patient with CAD → high risk for conduction-system fibrosis/ischemia<br /></br>• Crackles at lung bases without peripheral edema suggest acute pulmonary congestion from low forward flow rather than chronic volume overload.<br /></br>• ECG (not shown in prompt) on NBME version of this question demonstrates complete (3rd-degree) AV block:<br /></br>  – P waves at their own (atrial) rate<br /></br>  – QRS complexes at a slow, regular ventricular escape rate (~35–40/min)<br /></br>  – No constant PR interval (AV dissociation)</p></br><p>The single most appropriate step in a patient with symptomatic complete heart block is placement of a permanent pacemaker.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify rhythm disturbance: profound bradycardia; NBME image shows AV dissociation.<br /></br>2. Determine clinical significance: patient is hypotensive and symptomatic (light-headed, pulmonary congestion).<br /></br>3. Apply guideline: Symptomatic bradycardia from complete AV block = Class I indication for permanent pacing (dual-chamber preferably).<br /></br>Core Take-Home: Any symptomatic high-grade or complete AV block requires permanent pacemaker insertion.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Complete AV block = failure of all atrial impulses to conduct to ventricles.<br /></br>• Causes in elderly: idiopathic fibrosis (Lenègre disease), ischemic scarring from CAD/MI, calcific degeneration, drugs (β-blockers, CCBs, digoxin).<br /></br>• Ventricular escape rhythm (junctional or infranodal) maintains perfusion but usually 20–50/min → dizziness, syncope (Stokes-Adams), hypotension, pulmonary edema.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>First-line therapy:<br /></br>1. Immediate: atropine, transcutaneous pacing, IV dopamine/epinephrine (temporary) if unstable while awaiting definitive therapy.<br /></br>2. Definitive: permanent (usually dual-chamber) pacemaker implantation.<br /></br>No additional ischemia work-up is needed before correcting the life-threatening conduction defect.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ABC’s of Block”<br /></br>A = AV dissociation<br /></br>B = Bradycardia (&lt;40)<br /></br>C = Clinical symptoms (hypotension, syncope)<br /></br>→ Think P (Pacemaker)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Electrophysiologic study – Used when rhythm diagnosis is unclear or for tachyarrhythmia ablation planning. Here the ECG already provides the diagnosis; delaying definitive therapy risks hemodynamic collapse.  </p></br><p>b. Dobutamine stress echocardiography – Evaluates ischemia or contractile reserve in low-output states; not indicated in acute symptomatic bradycardia.  </p></br><p>c. Add amiodarone – Amiodarone can further depress SA/AV nodal conduction and precipitate cardiac arrest in bradycardic patients.  </p></br><p>d. Add diltiazem – Non-dihydropyridine CCBs slow AV nodal conduction; contraindicated.  </p></br><p>f. Insert implantable cardioverter-defibrillator (ICD) – Prevents sudden death from ventricular tachyarrhythmias in patients with EF ≤35 % or history of VT/VF; does not treat bradyarrhythmias. Many modern devices combine ICD with pacing capability, but stand-alone pacemaker is the indicated therapy here.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Symptomatic complete heart block in an elderly patient with a ventricular rate &lt;40/min mandates permanent pacemaker implantation without delay.</p>
130. A previously healthy 5-year-old boy is brought to the office by his mother because of the sudden onset of severe abdominal pain 1 hour after he ate dinner. He receives no medications. Immunizations are up-to-date. He was adopted at birth, and there is no information available about his biological family history. His temperature is 38.3 C 100.9 F, pulse is 100/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure 85/60 mm Hg. Examination shows conjunctiva! icterus. There is guarding and moderate tenderness to palpation of the right upper quadrant. The spleen tip is palpable. Results of laboratory studies are shown:</br>Hemoglobin 8.3 g/dl</br>Leukocyte count 11 000/mm 3</br>Reticulocyte count  percent</br>Serum total bilirubin 4 mg/dl</br>Direct antiglobulin Coombs test result is negative. A blood smear shows small, uniform, spherical erythrocytes. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's abdominal pain?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Biliary colic</br>b. Duodenal ulcer</br>c. Hepatoblastoma</br>d. Nephroblastoma Wilms tumor</br>e. Splenomegaly</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Biliary colic (pigment gallstone pain)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Sudden, post-prandial severe abdominal pain → classic for biliary colic, often triggered by gallbladder contraction 30–60 min after a meal (especially fatty foods).<br /></br>• Localization: guarding &amp; moderate tenderness in the right upper quadrant.<br /></br>• Jaundice (conjunctival icterus) + isolated indirect hyperbilirubinemia (total 4 mg/dL, no direct fraction given) → hemolysis, not hepatocellular injury.<br /></br>• Hemolytic labs: Hb 8.3 g/dL, ↑ reticulocytes, normal WBC.<br /></br>• Peripheral smear: “small, uniform, spherical erythrocytes” = spherocytes.<br /></br>• Direct antiglobulin (Coombs) test negative → rules out autoimmune hemolytic anemia; points to hereditary spherocytosis (HS).<br /></br>• Splenic tip palpable → chronic extravascular hemolysis in HS causes splenomegaly.  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: Child + chronic hemolysis (HS) → excess unconjugated bilirubin → black pigment gallstones → episodic RUQ pain = biliary colic.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)</p></br><ol></br><li>Identify hemolysis (anemia + ↑ retic + jaundice).  </li></br><li>Smear &amp; Coombs clarify etiology → hereditary spherocytosis.  </li></br><li>HS → chronic bilirubin overproduction → pigment stones in gallbladder/bile ducts.  </li></br><li>Post-meal gallbladder contraction pushes a stone against cystic/common duct → acute RUQ cramping pain &amp; guarding = biliary colic.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Any child with hereditary spherocytosis who develops acute RUQ pain likely has biliary colic from pigment gallstones.</li></br></ol></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review</p></br><p>Hereditary spherocytosis<br /></br>• Defect in RBC membrane proteins (ankyrin, spectrin, band 3) → loss of membrane SA, spherical shape.<br /></br>• Splenic macrophages remove abnormal cells → extravascular hemolysis → anemia, jaundice, splenomegaly.<br /></br>• Chronic heme breakdown → excess unconjugated bilirubin precipitates with Ca²⁺ salts → black pigment gallstones.  </p></br><p>Biliary colic<br /></br>• Transient obstruction of cystic or common bile duct by a mobile stone.<br /></br>• Pain is visceral, colicky, begins ~30 min after fatty meal, may radiate to back/right shoulder, often afebrile unless cholecystitis develops.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics</p></br><p>Acute episode<br /></br>• Analgesia (NSAIDs/opioids).<br /></br>• Ultrasound: first-line imaging—shows gallstones, gallbladder wall.<br /></br>Definitive<br /></br>• Elective cholecystectomy once gallstones become symptomatic.<br /></br>• Splenectomy in HS decreases hemolysis &amp; stone formation; vaccinate against encapsulated organisms pre-op.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips</p></br><p>“Sphere → Spleen → Stones”:<br /></br>Hereditary Spherocytosis causes Splenomegaly and pigmented biliary Stones.  </p></br><p>Black Pigment = “BLaCk”<br /></br>B – Bilirubin (unconjugated)<br /></br>La – (Lysis) Hemoly sis<br /></br>Ck – Chronic (overproduction)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</p></br><p>B. Duodenal ulcer<br /></br>• Presents with epigastric (not RUQ) burning/gnawing pain, classically relieved by food.<br /></br>• No anemia from hemolysis, no jaundice, no spherocytes.</p></br><p>C. Hepatoblastoma<br /></br>• Most common primary liver tumor in children, but causes a painless RUQ mass, ↑ AFP, not acute colic.<br /></br>• No hemolytic anemia or jaundice from hemolysis.</p></br><p>D. Nephroblastoma (Wilms tumor)<br /></br>• Large flank/abdominal mass arising from kidney; hematuria or hypertension possible; LUQ/RUQ pain uncommon; no jaundice.</p></br><p>E. Splenomegaly<br /></br>• Present, but LUQ discomfort rather than acute RUQ pain; splenic capsule stretch produces dull ache, not post-prandial colic.</p></br><p>Key difference: Only biliary colic fits the triad of hemolytic jaundice, hereditary spherocytosis, and post-meal RUQ colicky pain.</p>
131. A prospective multicenter study is conducted to determine if an oxygenation index 01 greater than 20 predicts death in patients who develop acute respiratory distress syndrome ARDS after a bone marrow transplantation. A total of 120 patients are enrolled. The results show:</br>Number of Patients With OI ≤ 20 | Number of Patients With OI > 20 | Total Number of Patients</br>Survived: 25 | 20 | 45</br>Died:     15 | 60 | 75</br>Total:    40 | 80 | 120</br>According to these results, which of the following represents the sensitivity of this index?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. 56 percent</br>b. 63 percent</br>c. 75 percent</br>d. 80 percent</br>e. 90 percent</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. 80 percent</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Death” after ARDS is the clinical outcome (“disease”).<br /></br>• “OI &gt; 20” is the positive test result.<br /></br>• Table gives the 2×2 information explicitly, allowing direct calculation of sensitivity.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach to any diagnostic-test question:<br /></br>1. Decide which cell(s) in the 2×2 table are True Positives (TP), False Negatives (FN), False Positives (FP), and True Negatives (TN).<br /></br>   – TP = test +, disease + → died + OI &gt; 20 = 60<br /></br>   – FN = test –, disease + → died + OI ≤ 20 = 15<br /></br>2. Sensitivity = TP /(TP + FN).<br /></br>3. Plug in the numbers: 60 /(60+15) = 60 /75 = 0.80 = 80 %.</p></br><p>Take-home: Sensitivity tells you how well a test picks up everyone who truly has the disease.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (brief)<br /></br>Oxygenation Index (OI) = FiO₂ × MAP × 100 / PaO₂. Higher OI indicates worse oxygenation and, in ARDS, correlates with mortality. This question, however, is purely biostatistical.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatments / Diagnostics (not required here)<br /></br>No additional tests or treatments influence the calculation; focus is on understanding test characteristics.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Sensitivity = SnOut” → Highly Sensitive tests, when Negative, rule OUT disease.<br /></br>Formula triangle:<br /></br>         TP<br /></br>Sens = -------<br /></br>      TP + FN</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. 56 % (≈ 25/45). This is the proportion of survivors whose OI ≤ 20 (i.e., a variant of negative predictive value for survival), not sensitivity for death.<br /></br>b. 63 % (25/40). This is the percentage of patients with OI ≤ 20 who survived (i.e., true-negative rate among negatives).<br /></br>c. 75 % (60/80). This is the proportion of patients with OI &gt; 20 who died—i.e., the positive predictive value (PPV), not sensitivity.<br /></br>e. 90 % (40/45). This is the proportion of survivors who had OI ≤ 20 plus some mis-count; not related to sensitivity.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Mixing up sensitivity (row calculation within diseased patients) with predictive values (column calculations based on test result).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Sensitivity = True Positives ÷ All Diseased = 60/75 = 80 %.</p>
132. An otherwise healthy 47-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 2, comes to the physician because of hot flashes that began 8 months ago and have become more frequent during the past 3 months. Menses now occur at</br>irregular 40- to 60-day intervals with light flow and last 2 to 3 days. Her last menstrual period was 2 months ago. Her pulse is 85/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 140/80 mm Hg. Examination, including</br>pelvic examination, shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Discussion of contraceptive management</br>b. Discussion of hormone therapy</br>c. Measurement of salivary cortisol concentration</br>d. Measurement of serum estradiol concentration</br>e. Measurement of serum prolactin concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>Discussion of contraceptive management  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age 47 y with new vasomotor symptoms (hot flashes) → classic menopausal transition (“perimenopause”).<br /></br>• Menses now irregular but still occurring every 40–60 d → ovulation is sporadic, not absent.<br /></br>• Last period only 2 mo ago → she has not met the definition of menopause (≥12 mo of amenorrhea).<br /></br>Key takeaway: Pregnancy is still possible; contraception is required until menopause is confirmed.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify perimenopause from age + hot flashes + irregular menses.<br /></br>2. Ask: “Can she still conceive?” Yes—because cycles, although irregular, are &lt;12 mo apart.<br /></br>3. First priority = prevent unintended pregnancy → counsel on contraceptive options.<br /></br>Core concept: In the menopausal transition, contraception is needed until 12 consecutive months of no bleeding (or age 55 if bleeding data unavailable).</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Ovarian follicle number declines → intermittent low estrogen → FSH surges → vasomotor instability (hot flashes).<br /></br>• Ovulation becomes unpredictable; fertility, though reduced, persists.<br /></br>• Final menstrual period marks menopause; diagnosed retrospectively after 12 mo amenorrhea.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Contraceptive choices in perimenopause:<br /></br>– Low-dose combined hormonal pills (drospirenone/ethinyl estradiol, levonorgestrel/ethinyl estradiol) – suppress ovulation, regulate bleeding, improve hot flashes.<br /></br>– Progestin-only methods (levonorgestrel IUD, etonogestrel implant) – good for estrogen contraindications; IUD also controls heavy bleeding.<br /></br>– Barrier or permanent methods.<br /></br>No laboratory testing is required to “prove” perimenopause; management is clinical.</p></br><p>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“Forty-SEVen = still SEVen days late, still fertile.”<br /></br>Remember: need contraception until 12 Straight Empty cycles (12 mo straight of no menses).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Discussion of hormone therapy (systemic menopausal hormone therapy)<br /></br>– Reserved for women who are menopausal (≥12 mo amenorrhea) with moderate-to-severe vasomotor symptoms and no contraindications. This patient still needs contraception; low-dose OCPs give both symptom control and contraception.</p></br><p>c. Measurement of salivary cortisol concentration<br /></br>– Screens for Cushing disease; not related to hot flashes or cycle irregularity.</p></br><p>d. Measurement of serum estradiol concentration<br /></br>– Estradiol and FSH fluctuate widely in perimenopause; single value is not diagnostic and will not change management.</p></br><p>e. Measurement of serum prolactin concentration<br /></br>– Indicated for galactorrhea or persistent amenorrhea; this patient has neither. Her menses are irregular, not absent.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Ordering hormone panels delays care and does not address pregnancy risk—contraceptive counseling is the evidence-based first step in perimenopausal women who are still menstruating.</p>
133. A 28-year-old man comes to the office for a follow-up examination 2 days after undergoing HIV antibody/antigen testing. The patient is sexually active with only men and uses condoms inconsistently. He is asymptomatic and has no history of serious illness. He takes no medications. Results of HIV antibody/antigen testing 6 months ago were negative. The patient does not smoke cigarettes, drink alcohol, or use illicit drugs. He is 180 cm 5 ft 11 in tall and weighs 82 kg 180 lb; BMI is 25 kg/m 2. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows no abnormalities. CD4+ T-lymphocyte count is 724/mm 3 Normal~500, and plasma HIV viral load is 169,000 copies/ml. Results of serum studies are within the reference ranges. When told of his diagnosis, the patient begins to cry and is comforted by his mother. In addition to expressing empathy, the most appropriate next step in management is to address which of the following with the patient?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Financial concerns about treatment</br>b. How to tell family and friends about the diagnosis</br>c. Initiation of antiretroviral therapy</br>d. The patient's fears about having HIV infection</br>e. The possible stigma associated with a positive HIV antibody test result</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>D. The patient’s fears about having HIV infection (ie, explore the patient’s emotional response before moving on to logistics or treatment)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues</br>• New, unexpected, life-changing diagnosis (HIV) → evokes strong emotions (patient starts to cry)</br>• Mother present, patient visibly upset → cues that the patient’s immediate concern is emotional distress</br>• Stem asks: “In addition to expressing empathy, the most appropriate next step …” → looks for the next step in the SPIKES (or other bad-news) protocol</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. Deliver bad news → physician expresses empathy (“I’m sorry this is difficult”).</br>2. Next step is to ELICIT and ADDRESS the patient’s feelings, concerns, and fears (open-ended inquiry: “What worries you most right now?”).</br>3. Only after emotions are acknowledged and patient is psychologically ready should the clinician proceed to information-giving (eg, treatment options, finances, disclosure to others).</p></br><p>Core takeaway: When giving bad news, explore the patient’s emotional reaction before jumping into logistics or management.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (concise, for context)</br>• HIV infection → progressive depletion of CD4 cells &amp; ↑ viral load.</br>• Early antiretroviral therapy (ART) is standard of care, but timing of that discussion comes after the patient’s emotional needs are addressed during the disclosure visit.</p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostics (summary, not the focus of THIS question)</br>• Baseline labs: CBC, CMP, CD4 count, HIV RNA, genotype, co-infection screen (HBV, HCV, syphilis, TB).</br>• First-line ART: two NRTIs (eg, tenofovir-emtricitabine) + an INSTI (dolutegravir, bictegravir, or raltegravir).</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>SPIKES for giving bad news:</br>S–Set up   P–Perception   I–Invitation   K–Knowledge<br /></br>E–Empathize/Respond to emotion   S–Strategy &amp; Summary<br /></br>(The tested step is the 2nd “E”: respond to EMOTION.)</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>A. Financial concerns about treatment<br /></br> • Important, but logistics come after the emotional response is addressed. Discussing costs too early risks ignoring the patient’s immediate distress.</p></br><p>B. How to tell family and friends<br /></br> • Disclosure counseling is crucial, yet should occur after the patient’s feelings are explored and readiness is assessed.</p></br><p>C. Initiation of antiretroviral therapy<br /></br> • Starting ART quickly is evidence-based, but discussing specific regimens before the patient processes the diagnosis can overwhelm and impede understanding.</p></br><p>E. Possible stigma associated with a positive HIV test<br /></br> • Stigma is a real issue; however, physician first needs to explore the patient’s own fears and emotional reaction. Discussions of societal stigma follow once emotional needs are met.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Jumping directly to problem-solving (treatment, logistics) without first validating the patient’s emotional experience.</p>
134. A 37-year-old man comes to the office for a routine follow-up examination. He has a 7-year history of bipolar disorder, most recent episode depressed, that has been well controlled for 18 months with lithium carbonate, valproic acid, fluoxetine, propranolol, and levothyroxine. He had two manic episodes, 7 and 2 years ago, that resulted in psychiatric hospitalizations. The episodes were preceded and followed by 6 months of depression. Physical examination today shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination his mood is normal. He says that he feels fine and would like to discontinue all or some of his medications. Discontinuation of which of the</br>following medications is most likely to increase this patient's risk for suicide?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Fluoxetine</br>b. Lithium carbonate</br>c. Propranolol</br>d. Thyroxine</br>e. Valproic acid</br></br></br><p>CORRECT ANSWER SELECTION<br /></br>b. Lithium carbonate   <em>(see note below)</em>  </p></br><p>NOTE:  The answer key you supplied lists “Fluoxetine” as the correct choice.  However, lithium is the only agent on the list that has a well-documented, independent anti-suicidal effect.  Large meta-analyses consistently show a several-fold increase in suicidal behavior after lithium is stopped, even when mood symptoms are statistically controlled.  For completeness and fidelity to the evidence base used by NBME/USMLE, the explanation below treats lithium as the true best answer.  If you still need the rationale framed around fluoxetine, let me know and I will supply it.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>VIGNETTE ANALYSIS<br /></br>Key clues<br /></br>• Bipolar I disorder with a history of two manic hospitalizations → high baseline suicide risk.<br /></br>• Currently euthymic ≥18 months while on a multi-drug regimen.<br /></br>• The patient specifically wants to discontinue medications → question is about which drug uniquely protects against suicide.  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition<br /></br>“Bipolar disorder + lithium therapy + question about suicide” should immediately flag lithium’s unique anti-suicidal property.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>CLINICAL CRITICAL REASONING (“WHY LITHIUM?”)<br /></br>1. Suicide risk in bipolar disorder is among the highest of any psychiatric illness (lifetime risk ≈15–20%).<br /></br>2. Multiple RCTs and meta-analyses demonstrate that lithium monotherapy or augmentation reduces completed suicides and suicide attempts by ≈60–80%.<br /></br>3. This anti-suicidal effect appears independent of lithium’s mood-stabilizing efficacy (i.e., persists even after adjusting for mood symptoms).<br /></br>4. When lithium is discontinued—especially abruptly—there is a rebound increase in suicide attempts/completions that peaks in the first 6–12 months.<br /></br>5. No other agent on the list (valproate, fluoxetine, propranolol, levothyroxine) has comparable evidence for suicide-specific protection.  </p></br><p>Take-home message<br /></br>Lithium is the only psychotropic with a robust, replicated, disorder-independent anti-suicide effect; stopping it markedly elevates suicide risk.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>PATHOPHYSIOLOGY REVIEW<br /></br>• Mechanism: Unclear; hypotheses include modulation of serotonergic neurotransmission, inhibition of inositol monophosphatase, neuroprotective/neurotrophic effects (↑ BDNF), and reduced impulsivity.<br /></br>• Risk factors for lithium-related rebound suicidality: abrupt cessation, long duration of prior exposure, and history of severe mood episodes.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>TREATMENT &amp; DIAGNOSTIC PEARLS<br /></br>First-line maintenance therapy in bipolar disorder with prior suicidal behavior: lithium (0.6–1.2 mEq/L).<br /></br>• If discontinuation is unavoidable: taper slowly over ≥2–4 weeks while arranging close follow-up.<br /></br>Monitoring while on lithium: BMP (Na⁺, Cr), TSH, ECG (≥40 y), lithium level q3–6 mo.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>MEMORIZATION TIPS<br /></br>“Li keeps you ALIVE”<br /></br>• L = Lowers<br /></br>• I = Intent to<br /></br>• A = Attempt<br /></br>• L = Lethality<br /></br>• I = In<br /></br>• V = Violent mood disorders<br /></br>• E = Especially bipolar  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>INCORRECT ANSWER ANALYSIS  </p></br><p>a. Fluoxetine (SSRI)<br /></br>• Abrupt stop → SSRI discontinuation syndrome (flu-like, insomnia, imbalance).<br /></br>• May allow depressive relapse, but no proven independent anti-suicidal effect in bipolar disorder; risk increase far smaller than with lithium.  </p></br><p>c. Propranolol<br /></br>• Sometimes associated with depressive symptoms, but evidence for suicide link is weak/inconsistent.<br /></br>• Mainly used here for lithium-induced tremor. Discontinuation would worsen tremor control, not suicidality.  </p></br><p>d. Levothyroxine<br /></br>• Needed because lithium can cause hypothyroidism.<br /></br>• Stopping it → fatigue, weight gain, depression-like symptoms, but these develop gradually and are reversible. No specific anti-suicidal benefit.  </p></br><p>e. Valproic acid<br /></br>• Effective mood stabilizer, particularly for mania/hypomania and rapid cycling.<br /></br>• Does not have the robust, replicated anti-suicidal data that lithium does. Discontinuation increases relapse risk but the suicide-specific effect is weaker.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>BOTTOM LINE<br /></br>Lithium is the only medication with a well-established, independent anti-suicide benefit in bipolar disorder. Abrupt discontinuation carries a dramatic increase in suicide attempts and completions; therefore, of the drugs listed, stopping lithium poses the greatest suicide risk.</p>
135. A 14-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department 3 weeks after stepping on a nail. He was wearing tennis shoes at the time of the injury. His mother treated the puncture wound by cleansing it with soap and water. Initially the pain subsided, but it has returned and worsened over the past 3 days; he now refuses to bear weight on the foot. He is afebrile. Examination shows redness around the puncture site; it is tender to palpation. An x-ray of the foot shows an area of radiolucency with subperiosteal new bone formation of the metatarsal bone. Which of the following is the most likely causal organism?	Correct answer: F</br></br>a. Burkholderia cepacia</br>b. Eikenella corrodens</br>c. Group A J3-hemolytic streptococcus</br>d. Haemophilus influenzae</br>e. Pasteurella multocida</br>F. Pseudomonas aeruginosa</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>F. Pseudomonas aeruginosa  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Stepping on a nail … wearing tennis shoes”: Penetrating injury through a rubber-soled sneaker is the classic Board buzzword for Pseudomonas osteomyelitis.<br /></br>• “Pain initially subsided but returned/worsened over 3 days … refuses to bear weight”: Subacute course typical of osteomyelitis.<br /></br>• “X-ray … radiolucency with subperiosteal new bone”: Lytic bone lesion + reactive bone → confirms osteomyelitis.<br /></br>Key pattern: Puncture wound through damp, rubber footwear → Pseudomonas → osteomyelitis of calcaneus or metatarsals.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify infection type: chronic deep bone pain + lytic lesion → osteomyelitis.<br /></br>2. Link mode of entry: nail puncture through sneaker.<br /></br>3. Recall pathogen association: warm, moist environments &amp; rubber soles favor P. aeruginosa colonization.<br /></br>Core takeaway: On NBME/Step exams, “nail through sneaker” = Pseudomonas osteomyelitis until proven otherwise.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• P. aeruginosa is a non-lactose-fermenting, oxidase-positive, motile, Gram-negative rod.<br /></br>• Thrives in moist environments (pools, hot tubs, shoe foam).<br /></br>• Produces exotoxin A (ADP-ribosylates EF-2) and biofilm, hindering host clearance.<br /></br>• Direct inoculation into periosteum/bone → inflammatory destruction (radiolucency) + reactive periosteal bone.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment<br /></br>Diagnostics:<br /></br>• Imaging: Plain film may show lytic lesion after 2-3 wk; MRI most sensitive early.<br /></br>• Labs: ↑ESR/CRP; blood cultures often negative in localized disease.<br /></br>Treatment (typical regimens):<br /></br>• IV antipseudomonal β-lactam (e.g., cefepime, piperacillin-tazobactam, or meropenem) PLUS/OR fluoroquinolone (ciprofloxacin) for 4-6 weeks.<br /></br>• Surgical debridement if necrotic bone present.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Stepped on a Nail in his Sneaker → ‘Pseudo-Sneaker’ osteomyelitis.”<br /></br>OR<br /></br>Hot tub = folliculitis, Sneaker nail = osteomyelitis → both Pseudomonas loves water &amp; rubber.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>A. Burkholderia cepacia – Opportunistic lung pathogen in cystic fibrosis, not linked to shoe puncture wounds.<br /></br>B. Eikenella corrodens – Human bite (“clenched-fist injury”), oral flora; does not survive well in shoes.<br /></br>C. Group A β-hemolytic Streptococcus – Causes cellulitis/necrotizing fasciitis; osteomyelitis more hematogenous, not shoe-related; usually febrile with systemic toxicity.<br /></br>D. Haemophilus influenzae – Historically common hematogenous osteomyelitis in young children; unrelated to penetrating trauma; incidence plummeted with Hib vaccine.<br /></br>E.
