Pasteurella multocida – Animal (cat/dog) bites or scratches; Gram-negative coccobacillus producing rapid cellulitis. No association with nails or sneakers.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: choosing S. aureus (not listed here) because it is the most frequent osteomyelitis organism overall; however, test writers emphasize unique epidemiologic clues—here, the rubber-sole clue mandates Pseudomonas.</p>
136. A 42-year-old primigravid woman at 34 weeks' gestation is brought to the emergency department because of a 4-hour history of decreased fetal movement. Pregnancy has been complicated by gestational diabetes</br>treated with insulin. She has no history of serious illness or other pregnancy complications. She is 160 cm 5 ft 3 in tall. She weighed 82 kg 180 lb prior to her pregnancy; BMI was 32 kg/m 2. She has had an 18-kg 40-</br>lb weight gain during her pregnancy. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 70/min, and blood pressure is 130/70 mm Hg. The cervix is not dilated. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Her</br>fingerstick blood glucose concentration is 110 mg/dl. A fetal heart tracing is shown. This pattern is unchanged during the next hour. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Bed rest in the left lateral decubitus position</br>b. Induction of labor</br>c. Intravenous insulin drip</br>d. Amnioinfusion</br>e. Cesarean delivery</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>a. Bed rest in the left lateral decubitus position</p></br><p>────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• 34-week primigravida with decreased fetal movement → triggers an assessment of fetal well-being (non-stress test/continuous fetal heart rate tracing).<br /></br>• “Pattern is unchanged during the next hour” – i.e., the tracing remains non-reassuring (Category II) but not frankly ominous (no recurrent late decels with absent variability or prolonged bradycardia).<br /></br>• No maternal fever, bleeding, ruptured membranes, or labor. Cervix closed → not an intrapartum situation yet.<br /></br>• Key clue for the correct answer: first step for a non-reassuring antepartum tracing is intra-uterine resuscitation measures (maternal repositioning, oxygen, IV fluids), not immediate delivery.</p></br><p>Pattern recognition: A flat or variable heart rate pattern in a patient who is supine is often attributable to aortocaval (IVC) compression → ↓ uterine blood flow → transient fetal hypoxia. Simply turning the mother onto her left side relieves compression, augments venous return, and usually corrects the tracing.</p></br><p>────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise thought process:</br>1. Identify suspicious tracing (Category II) but no Category III features.<br /></br>2. Ask: “Can we correct this with conservative measures before resorting to delivery?”<br /></br>3. First-line intra-uterine resuscitation = maternal left lateral decubitus (± O2, IV bolus, stop oxytocin).<br /></br>4. Re-evaluate tracing; if it normalizes → expectant management; if still abnormal → proceed to biophysical profile, contraction stress test, or delivery depending on severity.</p></br><p>Take-home concept: Always attempt reversible, low-risk interventions (maternal repositioning) before committing a pre-term fetus to induction or cesarean for a Category II tracing.</p></br><p>────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Supine maternal position compresses the gravid uterus onto the IVC and aorta → ↓ maternal venous return &amp; cardiac output → ↓ uterine perfusion → ↓ fetal oxygen.<br /></br>• Fetal hypoxemia → decreased variability, potential variable or late decelerations, ↓ fetal movement (“sleepy baby”).<br /></br>• Left lateral position displaces uterus off the vessels, restoring flow.</p></br><p>────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls</br>Intra-uterine resuscitation hierarchy (memorize “LOFT”):</br>L – Left (or right) lateral maternal positioning<br /></br>O – Oxygen (10 L face mask)<br /></br>F – Fluids (IV bolus)<br /></br>T – Turn off uterotonic drugs (oxytocin) + Tocolysis if tachysystole</p></br><p>Only if these fail do you move to:</br>• Biophysical profile / contraction stress test<br /></br>• Delivery (induction vs cesarean) based on gestational age, cervical exam, and severity of tracing.</p></br><p>────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“LEFT saves the baby” – when the tracing looks off, put mom on her LEFT side first.<br /></br>Category II = “Try to Fix”; Category III = “Time to Deliver.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>b. Induction of labor<br /></br>   • Cervix closed, fetus 34 weeks (pre-term), tracing not Category III. Induction exposes neonate to prematurity risks without first attempting reversible measures.</p></br><p>c. Intravenous insulin drip<br /></br>   • Maternal glucose is 110 mg/dL (normal). Glycemic issue is not causing the fetal tracing abnormality.</p></br><p>d. Amnioinfusion<br /></br>   • Used for recurrent variable decelerations from umbilical cord compression during ACTIVE labor after membranes rupture. Not indicated antepartum with intact membranes.</p></br><p>e. Cesarean delivery<br /></br>   • Reserved for persistent Category III tracings unresponsive to intra-uterine resuscitation or other emergent obstetric indications (placental abruption, cord prolapse, etc.). Premature at 34 weeks; tracing not that severe.</p></br><p>────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>For a non-reassuring but not ominous fetal heart tracing outside of labor, the very first intervention is maternal repositioning—bed rest in the left lateral decubitus position—to relieve aortocaval compression and improve uteroplacental perfusion.</p>
137. A previously healthy 62-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 2-day history of severe right orbital headache. She takes no medications. She does not smoke cigarettes or drink alcohol. Her blood pressure</br>is 170/90 mm Hg. Examination shows a supple neck. Visual acuity is 20/40 in the left eye and 20/60 in the right eye. The cup:disc ratio is 7:10 in the left eye and 9:10 in the right eye. Neurologic examination shows no</br>other focal findings. Her erythrocyte sedimentation rate is 15 mm/h. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Acute glaucoma</br>b. Cluster headache</br>c. Migraine</br>d. Pseudotumor cerebri</br>e. Temporal arteritis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Acute glaucoma  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Severe unilateral, retro-orbital (“right orbital”) pain that came on suddenly (2-day history) → classic way an acute elevation in intra-ocular pressure is described on exams.<br /></br>• Visual loss: visual acuity worse in the painful eye (20/60 vs 20/40) = vision threatened.<br /></br>• Dramatically enlarged cup-to-disc ratio (normal ≤0.3; here 0.9 on the affected side, 0.7 contralaterally) → pathognomonic for glaucomatous optic-nerve cupping.<br /></br>• Age ≥60 yrs is a major glaucoma risk factor.<br /></br>• Normal ESR (15 mm/h) argues strongly against temporal/giant-cell arteritis, the main alternative cause of new-onset headache with visual complaints in this age group.<br /></br>Key pattern: older patient + acute unilateral ocular/temporal pain + decreased vision + optic-nerve cupping = acute (angle-closure or acutely decompensated) glaucoma.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Localize pain to the eye, not the head/brain (no meningeal signs, neuro exam normal).<br /></br>2. Fundoscopy shows cupping rather than swelling → disease is inside the eye, not intracranial.<br /></br>3. Cupping = elevated intra-ocular pressure damaging the optic nerve → glaucoma.<br /></br>4. Time course is abrupt &amp; symptomatic → “acute glaucoma” (most commonly acute angle-closure although some texts lump an acutely symptomatic open-angle exacerbation under same term).  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Any sudden, unilateral, painful vision loss accompanied by a very high cup-to-disc ratio is glaucoma until proven otherwise and is an ophthalmologic emergency.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Angle-closure glaucoma: Sudden closure of anterior-chamber angle → precipitous rise in IOP. Triggers: dim light (pupil dilation), sympathomimetics, anticholinergics.<br /></br>• Open-angle glaucoma: Chronic impaired aqueous outflow through trabecular meshwork; can become symptomatic once optic damage is advanced.<br /></br>• Elevated IOP → optic-nerve axonal loss → excavation (“cupping”) of optic disc.  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Emergent ↓IOP while awaiting definitive laser/surgical therapy:<br /></br>‒ Topical β-blocker (timolol)<br /></br>‒ Topical α-agonist (apraclonidine)<br /></br>‒ Topical prostaglandin (latanoprost)<br /></br>‒ IV or oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitor (acetazolamide)<br /></br>‒ Miotic (pilocarpine) once IOP begins to fall (opens the angle)<br /></br>‒ If angle-closure: IV mannitol possible; definitive peripheral laser iridotomy.<br /></br>Confirmatory test: Tonometry showing elevated IOP; gonioscopy distinguishes open vs closed angle.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Red, Rock-Hard eye that’s Really painful” = acute angle-closure glaucoma (4 R’s).<br /></br>Cup-to-disc ratio: think of a coffee cup; when the “cup” is larger than one-third of the “saucer,” glaucoma is brewing.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Cluster headache – Severe periorbital pain but typically in middle-aged men, lasts 15 min–3 h, occurs in clusters; exam between attacks is normal (no cupping). Often with lacrimation/rhinorrhea, Horner sign.<br /></br>c. Migraine – Unilateral throbbing headache ± aura, photophobia; ocular fundus is normal; usually history of recurrent episodes since younger age.<br /></br>d. Pseudotumor cerebri (idiopathic intracranial hypertension) – Young obese woman with diffuse headache, pulsatile tinnitus, diplopia (CN VI palsy). Fundoscopy shows papilledema (blurred disc margins), not cupping.<br /></br>e. Temporal (giant-cell) arteritis – Age ≥50 with temporal headache, jaw claudication, polymyalgia rheumatica; ESR typically markedly elevated (&gt;50 mm/h). Fundoscopy may reveal ischemic optic neuropathy—not cupping.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Students often jump to temporal arteritis whenever an elderly patient has a new headache and vision change. Always check ESR/CRP and fundoscopic description: cupping → glaucoma; pale swollen disc → arteritic ischemic optic neuropathy.</p>
138. A 27-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a progressive rash and easy bruising during the past 2 weeks. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She enjoys horseback riding and first</br>noticed the rash on her legs when she took off her riding boots. She initially believed it was caused by the long time spent horseback riding that day. Her pulse is 62/min, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is</br>113/77 mm Hg. Examination shows a blood-filled blister on the right buccal mucosa. There is a petechial rash over both shins and dorsum of the feet. Laboratory studies show a platelet count of 9000/mm 3. Prothrombin time</br>is 12 sec INR=1.1 and partial thromboplastin time is 29 sec. Results of thyroid, liver, and renal function tests are within the reference ranges. A blood smear shows decreased platelets. Which of the following is the most</br>appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Observation only</br>b. Cyclosporine therapy</br>c. Prednisone therapy</br>d. Platelet transfusion</br>e. Splenectomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Observation only  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Isolated thrombocytopenia – platelet count 9 000/mm³ with a normal hemoglobin, WBC, PT/INR, and aPTT ⇒ rules-out bone-marrow failure, DIC, TTP/HUS, liver disease, or vit K antagonist use.<br /></br>• Mucocutaneous bleeding (petechiae on the legs, single blood-filled blister on buccal mucosa) with an otherwise normal physical examination ⇒ classic for immune thrombocytopenic purpura (ITP).<br /></br>• Time course 2 weeks in a previously healthy young woman ⇒ acute ITP. (Acute ITP in children is exceedingly common; in adults it is less common but still behaves similarly.)<br /></br>Key pattern: isolated thrombocytopenia + mucocutaneous bleeding + normal coagulation profile = ITP.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Identify emergency features → No life-threatening hemorrhage (no GI bleed, hematuria, intracranial bleed, or heavy epistaxis).<br /></br>2. Determine severity → Skin findings + a single small oral lesion are considered mild bleeding.<br /></br>3. Decide on intervention threshold → For acute ITP, therapy is reserved for (a) clinically significant bleeding or (b) very low platelets with a high risk of bleeding that outweighs treatment toxicity.<br /></br>   • Multiple society guidelines accept observation as first-line in otherwise healthy patients who have only mild skin/oral bleeding, even with platelets &lt;10 000/mm³, because 60-80 % will experience spontaneous remission within weeks.<br /></br>   • Treatment (steroids, IVIG) is escalated only if bleeding worsens or the count continues to fall.<br /></br>Take-away:  In uncomplicated, mild‐bleeding acute ITP, watchful waiting is appropriate despite severe thrombocytopenia.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (ITP)<br /></br>• Autoantibodies (IgG) against platelet membrane glycoproteins (IIb/IIIa → most common).<br /></br>• Antibody-coated platelets are destroyed in the spleen → isolated thrombocytopenia.<br /></br>• Often triggered by viral infection, vaccinations, pregnancy, or idiopathic.<br /></br>• Two clinical phenotypes:<br /></br>  – Acute ITP (usually children/young adults) → abrupt onset, often post-viral, high spontaneous remission.<br /></br>  – Chronic ITP (more common in middle-aged women) → relapsing/remitting, low spontaneous remission.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Initial evaluation: CBC + smear, HIV/HCV screen (secondary causes), ± bone marrow only if atypical.<br /></br>Management ladder (adults &amp; children, acute ITP)<br /></br>1. Observation:<br /></br>   • Platelets &gt;10 000 and ONLY skin ± mild oral lesions; no high-risk comorbidities.<br /></br>2. Corticosteroids (prednisone 1 mg/kg/day) or IVIG:<br /></br>   • Worsening mucosal bleeding, platelet &lt;10 000 and high bleeding risk, or if patient needs a procedure.<br /></br>3. Rituximab, thrombopoietin receptor agonists, or splenectomy:<br /></br>   • Chronic / refractory ITP.<br /></br>4. Platelet transfusion:<br /></br>   • Life-threatening hemorrhage or prior to urgent surgery; transfused platelets are quickly destroyed but give temporary support.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ITP = Isolated Thrombocytopenia Problem”<br /></br>• Kids = Keep watching (observation)<br /></br>• Adults = Act if Actively bleeding or Always &lt;10k for &gt;2–3 days<br /></br>Mnemonic “WASH”:<br /></br>Watch → Steroids → Anti-CD20 (rituximab) → splenectomy/Stimulators (TPO agonists)  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Cyclosporine therapy – Calcineurin inhibitor used in aplastic anemia or severe autoimmune diseases after failure of steroids/IVIG. Not first-line in ITP.<br /></br>c. Prednisone therapy – Standard first intervention for ITP when bleeding is more than mild or platelet &lt;10–20 k with high-risk features. In this vignette the bleeding is minimal; guidelines allow observation first.<br /></br>d. Platelet transfusion – Indicated only for life-threatening bleeding or pre-procedure. Transfused platelets would be rapidly destroyed by autoantibodies in ITP, offering little benefit for mild mucocutaneous bleeding.<br /></br>e. Splenectomy – Considered in chronic, refractory ITP (≥6–12 months, steroid-dependent or relapsing). Not appropriate in an acute episode that is likely to resolve.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall:  Reflexively treating every platelet count &lt;30 000 with steroids. In ITP, treatment decisions balance bleeding risk against medication toxicity; many acute cases, particularly in young patients with only skin findings, will self-resolve without pharmacotherapy.</p>
139. A 69-year-old man comes to the clinic for a routine examination. He says he feels well. He has hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, and osteoarthritis. His medications are lisinopril, hydrochlorothiazide, and lovastatin. He smoked two packs of cigarettes daily for 30 years but quit 8 years ago. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate screening test to decrease</br>this patient's risk for a cardiovascular event?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Abdominal ultrasonograph</br>b. Determination of ankle brachia! indices</br>c. ECG</br>d. Echocardiography</br>e. Exercise stress test</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. One-time abdominal ultrasonography to screen for an abdominal aortic aneurysm (AAA)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – “What are they really testing?”</p></br><p>• 69-year-old man → meets the USPSTF age cut-off (65-75 yr males)<br /></br>• Smoked 2 packs/day × 30 yr; quit 8 yr ago → “ever-smoker,” the key trigger word for AAA screening<br /></br>• Completely asymptomatic physical exam → they are asking about a population-based preventive screen, not a diagnostic test for symptoms  </p></br><p>The only USPSTF grade-A cardiovascular screen that applies to these exact demographics is a one-time abdominal ultrasound for AAA.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)</p></br><ol></br><li>Identify risk category: male 65-75 + any lifetime cigarette use → qualifies for AAA screen.  </li></br><li>Ask: Which screening test has proven mortality benefit in this group? → Abdominal U/S (detects silent AAA, allows elective repair before rupture).  </li></br><li>Rule out tempting but non-evidence-based screens (ECG, stress test, etc.) that do NOT lower mortality in asymptomatic patients.</li></br></ol></br><p>Core Takeaway: The ONLY cardiovascular screening test with grade-A evidence in asymptomatic older male smokers is one-time abdominal ultrasound for AAA.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (Quick &amp; High-Yield)</p></br><p>• AAA = degeneration of aortic wall (elastin loss, inflammation) → progressive dilation; rupture → massive retroperitoneal hemorrhage, &gt;80 % mortality.<br /></br>• Major risk factors: age ≥60, male sex, white race, smoking (strongest modifiable), hypertension, atherosclerosis.<br /></br>• Detectable by simple, non-invasive ultrasound long before rupture; prophylactic repair when diameter ≥5.5 cm or rapid expansion lowers mortality.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Management / Diagnostic Pearls</p></br><ol></br><li></br><p>Screening<br /></br>   • USPSTF: One-time abdominal U/S men 65-75 who have ever smoked (Grade A).<br /></br>   • Optional in never-smokers; not recommended routinely in women.</p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Follow-up once detected<br /></br>   • 3.0–3.9 cm → U/S q3 y<br /></br>   • 4.0–4.9 cm → q12 mo<br /></br>   • 5.0–5.4 cm → q6 mo<br /></br>   • ≥5.5 cm, growth &gt;0.5 cm/6 mo, or symptomatic → elective surgical repair (open or EVAR).</p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Medical optimization: aggressive smoking cessation, statin, BP control.</p></br></li></br></ol></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</p></br><p>“65 + 1 puff = 1 scan.”<br /></br>• Age 65–75 + history of ≥1 cigarette in lifetime → 1 ultrasound.</p></br><p>Think “AAA = ‘Age, Atherosclerosis, and tar (smoking).’ ”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</p></br><p>B. Ankle-brachial index<br /></br>• Insufficient evidence for screening asymptomatic adults; no proven mortality benefit.</p></br><p>C. Resting ECG<br /></br>• USPSTF gives an “I” (insufficient) for primary screening; routine ECG does not reduce cardiovascular events in asymptomatic patients.</p></br><p>D. Echocardiography<br /></br>• Used to evaluate known murmurs, HF, valvular or structural disease—not recommended for general cardiovascular screening.</p></br><p>E. Exercise stress test<br /></br>• Reserved for symptoms (chest pain, dyspnea) or very high-risk occupations; routine use in asymptomatic adults leads to false positives and unnecessary interventions.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Equating “high cardiovascular risk factors” with indication for stress testing; guidelines require symptoms or specific occupational risks, not just age + risk factors.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Key Exam Message</p></br><p>For the NBME &amp; Step 2 CK:<br /></br>• Memorize the USPSTF grade-A/B screening list.<br /></br>• Men 65-75 with any smoking history? → Think immediately: one-time abdominal ultrasound for AAA.</p>
140. A previously healthy 37-year-old man comes to the office because of a 4-week history of intermittent difficulty swallowing solids and liquids. The patient describes a sensation of something lodged in the center of his chest and says that the sensation is often painful. He has not had any other symptoms. He takes no medications. Examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Esophageal carcinoma</br>b. Esophageal spasm</br>c. Gastroesophageal reflux disease</br>d. Hiatal hernia</br>e. Pharyngoesophageal Zenker diverticulum</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>B. Esophageal spasm (classically “diffuse esophageal spasm,” DES)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• “Intermittent difficulty swallowing solids AND liquids” → Motility disorder (both bolus types affected simultaneously).<br /></br>• “Sensation of something lodged in the center of his chest … often painful” → Substernal chest pain + dysphagia = think DES.<br /></br>• Age 37, only 4-week history, otherwise healthy → Too young and too short a time-course for malignancy; no constitutional symptoms.</p></br><p>Pattern: Brief, episodic chest pain + simultaneous dysphagia to solids &amp; liquids = esophageal motility problem, most commonly diffuse esophageal spasm.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)</br>1. Decide obstruction vs motility:<br /></br>   • Structural obstruction (e.g., carcinoma, Schatzki ring) → dysphagia starts with solids, later liquids.<br /></br>   • Motility disorder → dysphagia to both from the outset.</p></br><ol></br><li>Identify the specific motility disorder:<br /></br>   • DES: Intermittent, non-progressive, chest pain that can mimic angina.<br /></br>   • Achalasia: Long-standing, usually years, regurgitation, weight loss, bird-beak LES on imaging (not in this vignette).</li></br></ol></br><p>Core takeaway: “Simultaneous dysphagia to solids &amp; liquids + substernal chest pain in a young adult = diffuse esophageal spasm.”</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• DES = uncoordinated, high-amplitude, non-peristaltic contractions of the esophageal body.<br /></br>• Etiology: unknown; thought to involve abnormal enteric nerve nitric oxide signaling.<br /></br>• Manometry: repetitive, simultaneous contractions with normal LES relaxation.<br /></br>• Barium swallow: corkscrew or rosary-bead esophagus.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls</br>Diagnosis</br>• Best initial test: Barium esophagram → corkscrew pattern.<br /></br>• Gold standard: Esophageal manometry.</p></br><p>Treatment</br>• Acute symptoms: Sublingual nitroglycerin or rapid-acting nifedipine.<br /></br>• Chronic: Long-acting nitrates, calcium-channel blockers (diltiazem), TCAs.<br /></br>• Refractory: Endoscopic botulinum toxin injection or peroral endoscopic myotomy (POEM).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“Spasm = Simultaneous, Sudden, Solids &amp; liquids.”<br /></br>Visualize a corkscrew twisting every time the patient swallows—matching the barium swallow.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>A. Esophageal carcinoma<br /></br>   • Progressive dysphagia (solids → liquids), older age, weight loss, anemia; not intermittent and not to liquids from the start.</p></br><p>C. Gastroesophageal reflux disease<br /></br>   • Heartburn, sour taste, positional symptoms; dysphagia is late and usually to solids; pain is burning/retrosternal, not “lodged” sensation.</p></br><p>D. Hiatal hernia<br /></br>   • Often asymptomatic or presents like GERD; does not typically cause painful dysphagia to liquids.</p></br><p>E. Pharyngoesophageal (Zenker) diverticulum<br /></br>   • Oropharyngeal dysphagia (difficulty initiating swallow), regurgitation of undigested food, halitosis, coughing/aspiration; chest discomfort less common.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>Simultaneous dysphagia to solids and liquids with episodic chest pain in a young, otherwise healthy patient = diffuse esophageal spasm.</p>
141. An 8-year-old girl is brought to the physician because of a 2-week history of vaginal discharge and itching. She has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. Vital signs are within normal limits. Pelvic examination shows erythema of the perineum and thin, white vaginal discharge. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Wet mount preparation of the discharge shows epithelial cells and a small number of leukocytes. Addition of KOH preparation to the discharge produces no odor. Which of the following is the most likely explanation for these findings?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Candidiasis</br>b. Foreign body in the vagina</br>c. Herpes simplex</br>d. Nonspecific vulvovaginitis</br>e. Trichomoniasis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Nonspecific vulvovaginitis  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Patient profile: 8-year-old PREPUBERTAL girl (key)<br /></br>• Symptoms: 2 wk vaginal discharge + itching; no systemic illness<br /></br>• Exam: mild erythema, THIN white discharge (not curdy, not foul-smelling)<br /></br>• Wet mount: only epithelial cells + few leukocytes (essentially a “normal” smear)<br /></br>• KOH “whiff” test: NO fishy odor  </p></br><p>Together these findings point to the most common cause of vulvovaginal complaints in prepubertal girls—nonspecific (irritant or bacterial) vulvovaginitis—rather than an infectious vaginitis seen in adolescents/adults.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Ask, “Is she estrogenized?”  Prepubertal = thin epithelium, alkaline pH → easily irritated.<br /></br>2. Look for characteristic discharge clues:<br /></br>   • Thick clumpy (Candida)? No.<br /></br>   • Green frothy + many PMNs (Trichomonas)? No.<br /></br>   • Foul fishy with clue cells (BV/foreign body)? No.<br /></br>3. Microscopy essentially normal → favors nonspecific irritation/overgrowth of mixed fecal/skin flora.<br /></br>Core takeaway: In young girls, MOST vulvovaginal complaints are nonspecific and related to hygiene/irritation, not classic STIs or Candida.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Prepubertal low estrogen →<br /></br>  – Thin, poorly keratinized vulvar/vaginal epithelium<br /></br>  – Minimal lactobacilli → pH becomes alkaline (≈6-7)<br /></br>  – ∴ Easily colonized by mixed enteric/skin bacteria and irritated by soaps, tight clothing, wiping back-to-front, etc.<br /></br>• Results in mild erythema, pruritus, watery/white/yellow discharge; microscopy nondiagnostic.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Management (FYI for Step 2)<br /></br>1. Reassurance + education:<br /></br>   – Proper wiping, cotton underwear, avoid bubble baths/irritants, keep area dry.<br /></br>2. Sitz baths; topical petrolatum or 1% hydrocortisone prn itching.<br /></br>3. Culture &amp; targeted antibiotics only if refractory or severe.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Little Girls = Little Estrogen = Little Clues”<br /></br>(Low estrogen → no classic ‘clue cells,’ ‘trichomonads,’ or ‘yeast buds’—think nonspecific!)  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Candidiasis<br /></br>   • Thick, cottage-cheese discharge, intense pruritus.<br /></br>   • Wet mount (KOH) shows BUDDING YEAST/PSEUDOHYPHAE. Absent here.  </p></br><p>b. Foreign body in vagina<br /></br>   • Malodorous, often BLOODY discharge; sometimes spotting on underwear; may see greenish/brown discharge.<br /></br>   • Foul odor typically present (whiff even without KOH). Not present.  </p></br><p>c. Herpes simplex<br /></br>   • Painful vesicles/ulcers, dysuria. Discharge not primary feature; microscopy shows multinucleated giant cells on Tzanck smear. None seen.  </p></br><p>e. Trichomoniasis<br /></br>   • Profuse, GREEN, FROTHY discharge, STRAWBERRY cervix, many PMNs; motile flagellated organisms on wet mount; fishy odor with KOH often present (mixed BV-like amines). Not seen in vignette.  </p></br><p>Key differentiator: only nonspecific vulvovaginitis fits “thin white discharge + minimal cells + no odor” in a prepubertal child.</p>
142. Four days after undergoing open reduction and internal fixation of an intertrochanteric fracture of the right hip sustained in a fall, a 72-year-old woman has the sudden onset of fever and rigors. A central venous catheter had been placed preoperatively in the ri ht internal ju ular vein for vascular access. She received perioperative cefazolin for 24 hours. Her temperature is 39.2 C 102.6 F, pulse is 104/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 80/50 mm Hg. Examination shows a well-healing incision. There are no localizing signs of infection. Which of the following is most likely to have prevented these findings?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Preoperative chest physiotherapy</br>b. Appropriate barrier precautions during catheter insertion</br>c. Continued cefazolin prophylaxis after 24 hours</br>d. Perioperative J3-adrenergic blocking agent therapy</br>e. Epidural anesthesia</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>b. Appropriate barrier precautions during catheter insertion (full sterile gown, gloves, cap, mask, large sterile drape)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Post-op day 4: nosocomial infections (catheter, pneumonia, UTI, wound) enter the differential.<br /></br>• High fever + rigors + hypotension (septic shock picture) but</br>  – Surgical wound “well-healing”<br /></br>  – “No localizing signs of infection” (no cough, dysuria, cellulitis, etc.)<br /></br>• Key device: central venous catheter placed pre-op and still in situ → most likely source is catheter-related bloodstream infection (CRBSI).</p></br><p>Central line sepsis is classically rapid-onset fever/rigors in an otherwise uncomplicated post-op patient.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1  Identify likely source → central line.<br /></br>Step 2  Ask, “What single intervention best prevents CRBSI?”<br /></br>Step 3  Evidence-based answer → maximal sterile barrier precautions at time of insertion (cap, mask, sterile gown, gloves, full-body drape, chlorhexidine prep).  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Meticulous sterile technique at insertion is the most effective preventive strategy for CLABSI.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Skin flora (Staph aureus, coagulase-negative staph) migrate along the catheter tract → colonize the line → bacteremia.<br /></br>• Risk increases with: multiple lumens, femoral site, duration in place, breaks in sterile field during insertion.<br /></br>• Barrier precautions block inoculation at the moment of insertion, the critical step in colonization.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls</br>• Remove the suspect line and send the catheter tip for culture; obtain peripheral blood cultures; start empiric IV vancomycin + gram-negative coverage while awaiting cultures.<br /></br>• Other preventive measures: chlorhexidine skin prep, avoid femoral site, hand hygiene, daily assessment of line necessity, antimicrobial/impregnated catheters in high-risk environments.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“5 S’s for a Safe Central Line”:<br /></br>1. Scrub hands<br /></br>2. Skin prep with chlorhexidine<br /></br>3. Sterile gown &amp; gloves<br /></br>4. Surgical cap &amp; mask<br /></br>5. Sheet (full-body drape)</p></br><p>If any “S” is missing → ↑ CLABSI risk.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Preoperative chest physiotherapy<br /></br>   • Lowers post-op atelectasis/pneumonia, not bloodstream infections.</p></br><p>c. Continued cefazolin prophylaxis after 24 h<br /></br>   • Prolonged antibiotics do NOT reduce catheter infections; instead they promote resistance &amp; C. difficile. Guidelines limit peri-op prophylaxis to ≤24 h for clean-orthopedic surgery.</p></br><p>d. Perioperative β-adrenergic blockade<br /></br>   • Used to blunt peri-op tachycardia/ischemia in selected cardiac-risk patients; no impact on infectious complications.</p></br><p>e. Epidural anesthesia<br /></br>   • Decreases post-op pain, ileus, and maybe DVT/PE; does not prevent bacteremia from a central line.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Take-Home Message</br>In a post-operative patient with sudden fever/rigors and no localizing findings, always think “central line sepsis.” The single best preventive measure is maximal sterile barrier precautions at the time of catheter insertion.</p>
143. A 17-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her mother because she is concerned that her daughter has never had a menstrual period. The mother reports that her own menarche occurred at the age of 13 years. The patient has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She is in the 11th grade, receives grades of A's and B's, and sings in the school choir. She has never been sexua ly active. She is 173 cm 5 ft 8 in tall a nd wei hs 66 kg 145 lb; BMI is 22 k /m ri.. Her blood pressure is 100/60 mm Hg. Breast development is sexual maturity ratin SMR stage 5, and pubic hair development is SMR stage 2. Examination of the external genitalia shows no abnormalities. Insertion of a speculum into the vagina is difficult. The cervix cannot be visualized or palpated. The uterus is not palpable. Pelvic ultrasonography and MRI of the pelvis confirm the absence of a uterus and ovaries. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Karyotype analysis</br>b. MRI of the brain</br>c. Intravenous pyelography</br>d. Laparoscopy</br>e. Gonadectomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. Karyotype analysis  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>1. Primary amenorrhea – no menses by age ≥ 15 yr.<br /></br>2. Secondary‐sex characteristics: breasts Tanner 5 → estrogen exposure is adequate.<br /></br>3. Sparse pubic hair (Tanner 2) → diminished androgen effect.<br /></br>4. Pelvic exam / imaging: blind‐ending vagina, no cervix, no uterus, no ovaries visualized.  </p></br><p>Putting the clues together:</br>• “Breasts present + uterus absent” narrows differential to two classic entities:<br /></br>  – Müllerian agenesis (MRKH, 46,XX, normal ovaries → normal pubic hair)<br /></br>  – Complete androgen insensitivity syndrome (CAIS, 46,XY, intra-abdominal testes, minimal pubic hair)  </p></br><p>Sparse pubic hair and non-visualized gonads strongly favor CAIS. To distinguish definitively between 46,XX and 46,XY, the next diagnostic step is karyotype (± serum testosterone).  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (stepwise)<br /></br>1. Confirm uterus present?  → No → differential = MRKH vs CAIS.<br /></br>2. Evaluate androgen effect (pubic/axillary hair). → Decreased → think CAIS.<br /></br>3. Order karyotype. If 46,XY and testosterone in male range → CAIS. If 46,XX → MRKH.  </p></br><p>Core Take-away<br /></br>In primary amenorrhea with absent uterus, karyotyping is required to separate CAIS (46,XY) from Müllerian agenesis (46,XX).  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• CAIS: Mutation in androgen-receptor gene → tissues cannot respond to testosterone or DHT.<br /></br>  – Testes secrete AMH → Müllerian structures regress (no uterus, cervix, upper vagina).<br /></br>  – Testosterone can’t masculinize external genitalia; aromatized estrogens → normal breast development.<br /></br>  – Androgen-dependent hair minimal.<br /></br>• MRKH: Failure of Müllerian duct development (uterus/cervix/upper vagina absent) but ovaries and androgen response are normal → normal pubic/axillary hair.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Follow-up<br /></br>• CAIS: Gonadectomy AFTER completion of puberty (late teens) to allow endogenous estrogen-driven development yet remove malignancy risk (seminoma, dysgerminoma).<br /></br>• Vaginal lengthening procedure if sexual activity desired.<br /></br>• Psychological support, genetic counseling.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Breasts but no uterus” = think “M&amp;M Candy”:<br /></br>M – MRKH (XX, Much hair)<br /></br>M – Male karyotype (XY) = minimal hair (CAIS) → order Karyotype  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Explanations<br /></br>B. MRI of the brain – For hypothalamic/pituitary causes (e.g., prolactinoma) where uterus would be present and breasts usually absent; not indicated when uterus is absent.<br /></br>C. Intravenous pyelography – Used to screen renal anomalies in MRKH, but MRKH not yet confirmed; karyotype is first.<br /></br>D. Laparoscopy – Direct visualization adds little after high-resolution MRI confirmed absent uterus/ovaries.<br /></br>E. Gonadectomy – Necessary in CAIS but only after confirmation of 46,XY and after puberty; doing it now without diagnosis risks unnecessary surgery if patient is 46,XX.</p>
144. A 2-week-old male newborn is brought to the emergency department by his mother because of a 2-day history of sweating while feeding and decreased formula intake. He has not had fever, vomiting, diarrhea, or cough. He was born at term following an uncomplicated pregnancy and delivery. On arrival, he appears cyanotic and is in moderate respiratory distress. He is at the 30th percentile for length and 20th percentile for weight. Temperature is 37.2 C 99.0 F, pulse is 160/min, respirations are 80/min, and blood pressure is 80/50 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 80 percent. A rade 4/6, systolic ejection murmur is heard best at the middle and upper left sternal borders. Chest x-ray is shown. In addition to a displaced aorta, this patient most likely has which of the following sets of cardiac anomalies?	Correct answer: F</br></br>a. Atrial septal defect ASD and left ventricular outflow tract obstruction</br>b. ASD and right ventricular outflow tract obstruction</br>c. Patent ductus arteriosus PDA and left ventricular outflow tract obstruction</br>d. PDA and right ventricular outflow tract obstruction</br>e. Ventricular septal defect VSD and left ventricular outflow tract obstruction</br>F. VSD and right ventricular outflow tract obstruction</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>F. Ventricular septal defect (VSD) and right-ventricular outflow-tract obstruction  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age &amp; timing – Cyanosis beginning in the 2nd week of life (not immediately at birth) suggests a duct-dependent cyanotic lesion whose presentation is unmasked as the ductus arteriosus closes.<br /></br>• Symptoms – Sweating with feeds, tachypnea (RR 80/min), poor weight gain → classic heart‐failure signs in infants.<br /></br>• Physical exam – Grade 4/6 harsh systolic ejection murmur along the mid-to-upper LSB = murmur of RV outflow obstruction (pulmonic stenosis) or flow across an overriding aorta.<br /></br>• CXR (not shown) – “Displaced aorta” is USMLE code for an overriding aorta. A classic “boot-shaped” heart silhouette would further point to RV hypertrophy.<br /></br>Key diagnostic phrase: displaced/overriding aorta + cyanotic infant + harsh systolic murmur = think Tetralogy of Fallot (TOF).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Tetralogy of Fallot includes four defects:<br /></br>1. Overriding (displaced) aorta<br /></br>2. Large VSD<br /></br>3. Right-ventricular outflow-tract (RVOT) obstruction (usually infundibular/pulmonic stenosis)<br /></br>4. Right-ventricular hypertrophy (creates the “boot” on CXR)  </p></br><p>The question already gives #1 (overriding aorta). The paired lesions you still need to name are #2 (VSD) and #3 (RVOT obstruction) → choice F.  </p></br><p>Take-home: When you see “overriding aorta” in a cyanotic neonate, immediately recall the other two essential lesions of TOF: VSD + RVOT obstruction.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Anterocephalad displacement of the infundibular septum during embryogenesis → mal-aligned VSD + narrowed RVOT (pulmonary stenosis).<br /></br>• RV faces high resistance → hypertrophies; blood preferentially shunts R→L across the VSD into the overriding aorta → systemic desaturation (cyanosis).<br /></br>• Cyanosis worsens as ductus closes and with anything that decreases SVR or increases PVR (crying, feeding).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Immediate/ED:<br /></br>• Prostaglandin E1 infusion to keep ductus arteriosus open (improves pulmonary flow).<br /></br>• Supplemental O₂, knee-chest positioning, morphine for “tet” spells.<br /></br>Definitive:<br /></br>• Surgical repair (VSD patch &amp; relieve RVOT obstruction) usually within first few months.<br /></br>Diagnostics:<br /></br>• Echo confirms anatomy and severity.<br /></br>• ECG → right-axis deviation, RVH.<br /></br>Mnemonic: PROVe your TOF<br /></br>P – Pulmonic stenosis (RVOT obstruction)<br /></br>R – Right-ventricular hypertrophy<br /></br>O – Overriding aorta<br /></br>V – VSD  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>1. “Boot-shaped heart kicks you into PROVe” → remember the 4 lesions.<br /></br>2. OverRiding Aorta Overlaps Right and Left ventricles (O-R = OverRight) to cue that blood from Right Ventricle enters the aorta.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. ASD + LVOT obstruction – Would cause left-sided obstructive physiology (e.g., critical aortic stenosis) with pulmonary over-circulation, not cyanosis relieved by ductus. Murmur would be at the right upper sternal border with a weak femoral pulse.<br /></br>B. ASD + RVOT obstruction – ASD alone rarely causes severe neonatal cyanosis; shunt direction is usually L→R early.<br /></br>C. PDA + LVOT obstruction – Seen in critical coarctation or hypoplastic left-heart; patients present with shock when duct closes, not isolated cyanosis with harsh pulmonic murmur.<br /></br>D. PDA + RVOT obstruction – Would require ductal flow INTO pulmonary artery for survival; murmur of continuous PDA would be heard at left infraclavicular area— not described.<br /></br>E. VSD + LVOT obstruction – Describes conditions like subaortic stenosis; cyanosis uncommon because shunt is L→R; you would hear a harsh systolic murmur at the right upper sternal border.  </p></br><p>Only choice F pairs the two lesions that, together with an overriding aorta, define Tetralogy of Fallot and explain this infant’s presentation.</p>
145. A 14-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her parents because of an 8-month history of shaking of her hands that occurs when she tries to do things that require steadiness, such as writing, threading a needle, or doing art work. Her teachers have reported that her handwriting is sometimes difficult to read. The shaking is worse when the patient is stressed or tired, but she says she is not bothered by it. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Her father also has mild shaking of the hands. The patient is 15 cm 5 ft 2 in tall and wei hs 43 kg 95 lb; BM I is 17 kg/m 2. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination of the eyes shows full ocular movements and mild end-gaze nystagmus. There is a fast tremor of the hands when the patient holds her arms outstretched. When the patient draws spirals and straight lines, the tremor causes waviness in the figures drawn. There is no dysmetria on finger-nose and heel-shin testing. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. DNA screening for spinocerebellar atrophy</br>b. Electromyography and nerve conduction studies</br>c. Measurement of serum ceruloplasmin concentration</br>d. MRI of the brain</br>e. No further diagnostic studies are indicated~-</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. No further diagnostic studies are indicated</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Action/postural tremor: “shaking … when she tries to do things that require steadiness” and “when the patient holds her arms outstretched.”<br /></br>• Fine-motor impairment: illegible handwriting, wavy spirals/lines.<br /></br>• Situational variability: worse with fatigue or stress – classic for essential (benign familial) tremor.<br /></br>• Positive family history: father has the same tremor → autosomal-dominant pattern.<br /></br>• Absence of other cerebellar signs: normal finger-nose, heel-shin; only very mild end-gaze nystagmus (can be normal).<br /></br>• Normal vitals, growth parameters, and no systemic findings.</p></br><p>Together these data fit the textbook picture of essential tremor (ET). ET is a clinical diagnosis; once classic features are present and the neurologic exam is otherwise normal, no additional work-up is necessary.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify tremor type (resting vs postural/action vs intention).<br /></br>2. Look for “red flags” (ataxia, rigidity, dystonia, KF rings, neuropathy, systemic disease). None are present.<br /></br>3. Check family history → supports ET.<br /></br>4. Conclusion: classic, isolated, familial postural tremor → diagnose clinically → no tests.</p></br><p>Take-home: Essential tremor = isolated action tremor ± head/voice tremor, AD inheritance, exacerbated by stress, improved by β-blocker/alcohol, and diagnosed clinically.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Autosomal dominant mutation (most linked to LINGO1 or HTRA2 loci).<br /></br>• Circuitry: Abnormal oscillatory activity in cerebello-thalamo-cortical loop; thought to involve impaired GABAergic modulation.<br /></br>• Onset: Bimodal (teen years or after age 40).<br /></br>• Course: Slowly progressive amplitude; frequency usually 6–10 Hz.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment Snapshot (for step 2 CK)<br /></br>1st line: Propranolol (non-selective β-blocker) OR primidone (barbiturate).<br /></br>2nd line/adjunct: Topiramate, gabapentin, benzodiazepines.<br /></br>Refractory: High-frequency thalamic DBS or focused ultrasound.</p></br><p>(Alcohol transiently improves symptoms but is not recommended as therapy.)</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>• “BATS” for essential tremor Rx:<br /></br>  B – Beta-blocker (propranolol)<br /></br>  A – Alcohol improves<br /></br>  T – Topiramate/primidone second line<br /></br>  S – Surgical DBS if severe<br /></br>• Differentiation aid:<br /></br>  Resting tremor = “Pill-rolling” Parkinson’s (occurs at REST)<br /></br>  Intentional tremor = “I” for cerebellar Intentional<br /></br>  Stress-exacerbated Action tremor with family history = Essential</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. DNA screening for spinocerebellar ataxia<br /></br>   • Would be considered if progressive gait ataxia, dysarthria, dysmetria, or oculomotor abnormalities were present. None of those appear here.</p></br><p>b. Electromyography &amp; nerve-conduction studies<br /></br>   • Useful for peripheral neuropathy, myopathy, or neuromuscular junction disorders (e.g., ALS, myasthenia). ET is a central tremor; EMG/NCS would be normal.</p></br><p>c. Serum ceruloplasmin (Wilson disease)<br /></br>   • Wilson presents with wing-beating or dystonic tremor plus hepatic, psychiatric, or ocular (Kayser-Fleischer rings) findings; inheritance is autosomal recessive (family history often negative). None of these features are in the vignette.</p></br><p>d. MRI brain<br /></br>   • Indicated when structural cerebellar/brainstem lesions are suspected (e.g., tumors, MS, stroke). Normal neuro exam except tremor argues against a focal lesion.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>A classic, isolated, familial action tremor in a healthy adolescent = essential tremor. It is diagnosed clinically; ordering additional studies only increases cost without adding diagnostic value.</p>
146. An 11-year-old girl is brought to the office by her mother because of a 1-year history of behavioral issues at school. The mother reports her daughter has become more isolated and her grades have fallen from A's and B's to</br>C's. The mother is also concerned about her daughter's appearance. She has dyed her hair green and has an asymmetric haircut. The patient has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. When her mothe</br>leaves the room, the patient says she is tired of everyone at school. She feels that she is in the wrong body. She also hates her name and would prefer using a gender-neutral one. She states she has felt this way her</br>entire life but now feels that she has to say something. She has tried to talk with her parents about her feelings, but they dismiss her when she brings up her concerns. The patient says the few friends she has have become</br>different and interested in things she does not really like. She is also upset about her body changes and wants them to stop. Physical examination discloses no abnormalities. On mental status examination, she is fully</br>oriented and exhibits no deficits. This patient is at greatest risk for which of the following disorders?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Antisocial personality disorder</br>b. Body dysmorphic disorder</br>c. Dissociative identity disorder</br>d. Major depressive disorder</br>e. Schizoaffective disorder</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Major depressive disorder  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Feels she is in the wrong body … hates her name … wants body changes to stop”  → classic language for gender dysphoria in a pre-/early-pubertal child.<br /></br>• Social withdrawal (“more isolated”), falling grades, change in appearance, conflict with parents → psychosocial stressors that commonly accompany gender dysphoria.<br /></br>• No psychosis, intact orientation, normal physical exam.  </p></br><p>Pattern: Gender-non-conforming youth + social rejection = very high prevalence of internalizing disorders (depression, anxiety, suicidality). Recognizing that link is what the NBME is testing.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1  Identify primary condition: childhood gender dysphoria.<br /></br>Step 2  Recall epidemiologic data: &gt;50 % of transgender or gender-diverse adolescents meet criteria for major depressive disorder (MDD) and/or have suicidal ideation; rates far exceed those for personality disorders or psychotic disorders.<br /></br>Step 3  Match to answer list → pick MDD.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: The most common and clinically important psychiatric comorbidity in children/adolescents with gender dysphoria is major depressive disorder (and suicidality).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-yield)<br /></br>Gender dysphoria<br /></br>• Etiology: multifactorial (biologic, psychosocial).<br /></br>• Key feature: marked incongruence between experienced gender and assigned sex lasting ≥6 mo, causing distress/impairment.<br /></br>• Consequences: chronic minority stress, peer victimization, parental rejection → ↑ hypothalamic–pituitary–adrenal activation, serotonin dysregulation → internalizing disorders, especially depression.  </p></br><p>Major depressive disorder in youth<br /></br>• ≥2 weeks of depressed/irritable mood or anhedonia + ≥4 vegetative/cognitive sx.<br /></br>• Risk factors: chronic stress, bullying, lack of family support, gender dysphoria, LGBTQ status.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Management Pearls<br /></br>1. First-line for gender dysphoria = multidisciplinary gender-affirming care (therapy, possible puberty blockers such as GnRH agonists, eventual hormone therapy).<br /></br>2. Screen at every visit for depression and suicidality (PHQ-A, direct questioning).<br /></br>3. Treat MDD with CBT +/- SSRI (fluoxetine is FDA-approved for children ≥8 y).<br /></br>4. Family counseling to increase acceptance; school advocacy to reduce bullying.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Dysphoria → Depression”: both start with “Dys/Dep”; remember the two travel together.<br /></br>“Green hair, grades down, gender doubts → think ‘blue mood.’ ”  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Antisocial personality disorder<br /></br>• Requires pervasive disregard for others’ rights &amp; criminality after age 18 with evidence of conduct disorder before 15 (aggression, theft, deceit). Not present here.  </p></br><p>b. Body dysmorphic disorder<br /></br>• Preoccupation with imagined physical defect (eg, nose too big). This patient’s distress is about gender incongruence, not a specific body part defect.  </p></br><p>c. Dissociative identity disorder<br /></br>• Two or more distinct personalities plus amnesia. No episodes of dissociation or memory gaps described.  </p></br><p>e. Schizoaffective disorder<br /></br>• Psychosis + mood episodes, with ≥2 weeks of delusions/hallucinations in the absence of mood symptoms. Patient has no psychotic features.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: assuming externalizing problems (eg, antisocial traits) are most likely because of behavioral changes; in gender-dysphoric youth, the danger is internalizing illness—depression and suicidality.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Take-Home Message<br /></br>Adolescents with gender dysphoria have a markedly elevated risk of major depressive disorder and suicide. Proactive screening and timely treatment are essential.</p>
147. A 13-year-old boy is brought to the office by his father because the father is concerned that his son seems to have a small penis. The patient has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Vaccinations are up-to-date. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows a small amount of long, downy pubic hair at the base of the penis. The scrotum is pink and enlarged; the penis is 3 cm in length. Which of the</br>following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Measurement of serum luteinizing hormone concentration</br>b. Measurement of serum testosterone concentration</br>c. Testosterone therapy</br>d. Ultrasonography of the scrotum</br>e. Reassurance that the patient's sexual development is normal~-</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Reassurance that the patient’s sexual development is normal  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age 13 y: Normal window for the onset of male puberty is 9–14 y.<br /></br>• Genital / pubic-hair description = Tanner stage 2:<br /></br>  – “Small amount of long, downy hair at the base of the penis” = PH2<br /></br>  – “Scrotum is pink and enlarged; penis is 3 cm” = G2 (testicular/scrotal enlargement precedes penile growth).<br /></br>• No systemic illness, normal vitals: no red flags for chronic disease or endocrinopathy.  </p></br><p>Together, these findings show the boy is at the very start of puberty—exactly where he should be for his age.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process<br /></br>1. Ask: Is this delayed puberty?<br /></br>   • Delayed puberty in boys = no testicular enlargement (testis &lt;4 mL or length &lt;2.5 cm) by age 14 or &gt;5 y between Tanner stage 2 and 5.<br /></br>   • This patient is only 13 and already at Tanner 2. → NOT delayed.<br /></br>2. Are there signs of disorder (gynecomastia, tall stature with eunuchoid habitus, chronic illness, anosmia)?  None.<br /></br>3. Therefore no laboratory or imaging work-up is indicated.<br /></br>4. Provide reassurance and counseling to patient and father.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Pubertal development that falls within the normal age range and Tanner staging does not require hormonal testing; reassurance is the correct “do nothing” answer choice.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Puberty onset is triggered by pulsatile GnRH → ↑LH/FSH → ↑testosterone → secondary sexual characteristics.<br /></br>• Testicular (scrotal) enlargement is the first sign (Tanner G2) and typically precedes penile growth by ~1 y.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Indications for labs (LH, FSH, testosterone), imaging, or testosterone therapy:<br /></br>  – No puberty by 14 y, or stalled progression (&gt;5 y without reaching Tanner 5)<br /></br>  – Suspicion for primary hypogonadism (Klinefelter), secondary hypogonadism (Kallmann, pituitary), chronic disease, or testicular pathology.<br /></br>• In a healthy 13-year-old at Tanner 2 these criteria are not met.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“14-4-5 Rule” for boys:<br /></br>– No testes ≥4 mL by 14 y → work-up.<br /></br>– &gt;5 y to finish puberty → work-up.<br /></br>If the boy beats the 14-4-5 rule → reassure.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Measurement of serum LH concentration<br /></br>b. Measurement of serum testosterone concentration<br /></br>– Both tests are reserved for suspected delayed puberty or hypogonadism. Normal-age Tanner 2 boys do not need them; would lead to unnecessary anxiety/cost.  </p></br><p>c. Testosterone therapy<br /></br>– Exogenous testosterone is used only for confirmed hypogonadism or constitutional delay in an older adolescent. Giving it now could prematurely close epiphyses and stunt final height.  </p></br><p>d. Ultrasonography of the scrotum<br /></br>– Indicated for testicular masses, torsion, or ambiguous genitalia—not for normal pubertal development.  </p></br><p>Summary<br /></br>A 13-year-old who has just started Tanner stage 2 is squarely within the normal pubertal timeline. No investigations are warranted; provide reassurance to both patient and parent.</p>
148. A 57-year-old woman comes to the emergency department 1 hour after the onset of left-sided chest pain and shortness of breath. The pain is exacerbated by inspiration. She has no history of exertional chest pain or shortness of breath. She has received estrogen and progestin replacement therapy since menopause 7 years ago. Her temperature is 38 C 100.4  F, pulse is 105/min and regular, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 120/80 mm Hg. A rub and decreased breath sounds are heard over the left posterior hemithorax. Heart sounds are normal. Examination of the lower extremities shows no abnormalities. Arterial blood gas analysis on room air shows:</br>pH .48</br>Pco~ ~8 mm Hg</br>Po 2 65 mm Hg</br>An x-ray of the chest shows blunting of the left costophrenic angle and a wedge-shaped opacity in the left lower lobe. An ECG shows right heart strain. Venous duplex ultrasonography of the lower extremities shows no abnormalities. Ventilation-perfusion lung scans show a perfusion defect in the left lower lobe and are consistent with a high probability of pulmonary embolus. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Venography of the lower extremities</br>b. Spiral CT scan of the chest</br>c. Lung biopsy</br>d. Pulmonary angiography</br>e. Thoracentesis</br>F. No additional studies are indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>F. No additional studies are indicated  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Sudden‐onset pleuritic chest pain + dyspnea → classic presentation of pulmonary embolism (PE).<br /></br>• Risk factor: 7 years of estrogen-progestin hormone replacement therapy → ↑ hypercoagulability.<br /></br>• Physical exam: pleural friction rub + diminished breath sounds posteriorly = pulmonary infarction/effusion.<br /></br>• ABG: pH 7.48, PCO₂ 28 mm Hg (respiratory alkalosis from tachypnea) + PaO₂ 65 mm Hg (hypoxemia) = typical gas pattern in PE.<br /></br>• CXR: “wedge-shaped opacity” (Hampton hump) + blunted costophrenic angle = pulmonary infarct with small effusion.<br /></br>• ECG: right-heart strain (S₁Q₃T₃, right axis, or RV strain) supports PE.<br /></br>• V/Q scan: high-probability perfusion defect in same lobe as CXR finding.<br /></br>• Duplex leg ultrasound: negative—does not rule out PE (sensitivity &lt; 100%).  </p></br><p>Key pattern: classic risk factor + classic symptoms/signs + high-probability V/Q scan → diagnosis of PE is already secured.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Pre-test probability is high (age, estrogen therapy, acute pleuritic pain, tachycardia).<br /></br>Step 2: Non-invasive imaging (V/Q) shows a high-probability defect that anatomically matches the chest radiograph.<br /></br>Step 3: Guidelines (ACCP / ACC) – If clinical probability is high AND V/Q scan is high-probability, the positive predictive value approaches 85–90 %; proceed directly to treatment.<br /></br>Therefore, the most appropriate next step in diagnosis is no further testing; move on to anticoagulation (heparin → warfarin/DOAC) or, if contraindicated, IVC filter.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In PE work-up, a high-probability V/Q scan concordant with high clinical suspicion is considered diagnostic—do not delay therapy for additional confirmatory tests.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• PE = thrombus (usually from deep leg/pelvic veins) embolizes to pulmonary arterial tree → ventilation–perfusion mismatch (V &gt; Q) → hypoxemia and reflex hyperventilation (resp alkalosis).<br /></br>• Pulmonary infarction (≈10 %) occurs when small peripheral arteries are occluded; causes pleuritic pain, hemoptysis, Hampton hump on CXR, and small effusion (blunted angle).</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>First-line therapy (initiate immediately):<br /></br>1. Anticoagulation – unfractionated heparin IV or low-molecular-weight heparin (enoxaparin) → bridge to warfarin (INR 2–3) or continue LMWH plus DOAC (e.g., apixaban, rivaroxaban).<br /></br>2. Thrombolysis (alteplase) or surgical embolectomy for hemodynamic compromise/massive PE.<br /></br>3. IVC filter if anticoagulation is contraindicated or fails.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Hampton hump + Hormones (estrogen) = High-probability PE → Heparin, no more Hunting for tests.”<br /></br>V/Q decision rule: High–high = treat, Low–low = stop, Mismatch = CT/angiography.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Venography of the lower extremities – Invasive, now rarely used. A negative duplex US already lowers probability of DVT, and PE diagnosis has been made; venography would not change management.<br /></br>b. Spiral CT (CT-PA) – Indicated when V/Q scan is nondiagnostic or when CXR is normal but clinical suspicion persists. Not necessary with high-probability V/Q in a high-risk patient.<br /></br>c. Lung biopsy – Never used to diagnose PE; too invasive. Would be considered for interstitial lung disease or malignancy.<br /></br>d. Pulmonary angiography – Gold standard but invasive, with contrast risks. Reserved for indeterminate non-invasive studies when establishing diagnosis will change management; unnecessary here.<br /></br>e. Thoracentesis – Might relieve large symptomatic effusions or diagnose pleural disease, but here effusion is small, explained by PE, and will resolve with anticoagulation; no diagnostic value.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfalls<br /></br>• Ordering additional imaging despite a diagnostic V/Q scan delays therapy and exposes the patient to unnecessary risk.<br /></br>• Believing a negative leg ultrasound rules out PE—40–50 % of PE patients have no sonographic DVT.</p></br><p>Bottom line: High clinical suspicion + high-probability V/Q scan = diagnostic PE. Treat immediately; no further diagnostic tests are needed.</p>
149. An 80-year-old woman, who resides in a nursing care facility, is brought to the office by a nurse because of a 1-month history of moderate lower abdominal pain and constant low-volume urinary incontinence that now requires use of an absorbent pad. She also has had frequent urinary tract infections during the past year. She has hypertension, type 2 diabetes mellitus, and dementia. Her medications are insulin, labetalol, and donepezil. She appears frail and sits in a wheelchair. She is 160 cm 5 ft 3 in tall and weighs 51 kg 113 lb ; BMI is 20 kg/m 2. Vital signs are within normal limits. Abdominal examination shows a soft, nontender midline mass below the umbilicus. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's urinary incontinence?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Medication adverse effect</br>b. Neurogenic bladder</br>c. Stress incontinence</br>d. Urethral hypermobility</br>e. Uterine prolapse</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Neurogenic bladder (diabetic autonomic neuropathy → overflow incontinence)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Constant low-volume urinary incontinence” → classic wording for overflow incontinence (dribbling, continuous leakage).<br /></br>• “Soft, nontender midline mass below the umbilicus” → palpably distended bladder.<br /></br>• “Frequent UTIs during the past year” → stasis of urine from incomplete emptying.<br /></br>• Long-standing diabetes mellitus (and advanced age) → strong risk factor for autonomic neuropathy of the detrusor.<br /></br>These clues collectively point to impaired detrusor contractility with retention → overflow.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify the incontinence pattern: continuous dribble + high post-void residual (suggested by the suprapubic mass) = overflow.<br /></br>2. Determine the mechanism: either bladder outlet obstruction or neurogenic bladder.<br /></br>3. Physical exam shows no pelvic organ prolapse; there is no BPH–like obstruction in women.<br /></br>4. Diabetes mellitus is the quintessential cause of neurogenic (atonic) bladder → detrusor underactivity.<br /></br>5. Therefore the most likely cause = neurogenic bladder secondary to diabetic autonomic neuropathy.</p></br><p>Key takeaway: In an elderly diabetic with dribbling and a distended suprapubic mass, always think “overflow incontinence from neurogenic bladder.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Diabetic autonomic neuropathy damages parasympathetic innervation to the detrusor muscle.<br /></br>• Result: impaired detrusor contraction → large residual volumes, bladder over-distention, high intravesical pressure, and continuous leakage.<br /></br>• Chronic stasis predisposes to recurrent UTIs.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls<br /></br>Diagnostics<br /></br>• Post-void residual (PVR) ultrasound/catheterization &gt; 200 mL confirms overflow.<br /></br>• Urodynamic testing can confirm impaired detrusor activity.</p></br><p>Treatment<br /></br>1. Immediate: clean intermittent catheterization (or indwelling catheter if necessary).<br /></br>2. Long-term:<br /></br>   – Scheduled catheterization program.<br /></br>   – Cholinergic agonist (bethanechol) may help but limited efficacy.<br /></br>   – Tight glycemic control to slow neuropathic progression.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Diabetic Detrusor is Dead”: Diabetes → Dead (atonic) detrusor → Distended bladder → Dribbling. Visualize an overfilled water balloon slowly leaking.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Medication adverse effect<br /></br>• Donepezil (cholinesterase inhibitor) actually increases detrusor contractility (can cause urge, not retention).<br /></br>• Labetalol and insulin do not cause urinary retention. No anticholinergic or α-agonist listed.</p></br><p>c. Stress incontinence<br /></br>• Requires leakage with ↑ intra-abdominal pressure (cough, laugh). No such triggers described; also no palpable bladder.</p></br><p>d. Urethral hypermobility (an anatomic cause of stress incontinence)<br /></br>• Same reasoning as above; examination usually shows cystocele or positive Q-tip test, not a suprapubic mass.</p></br><p>e. Uterine prolapse<br /></br>• Presents with pelvic pressure, vaginal bulge, and sometimes obstructive voiding, but you would see/prolapse on pelvic exam; mass would be perineal/vaginal, not suprapubic bladder distention.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Continuous dribbling + suprapubic fullness in an elderly diabetic woman = overflow incontinence from neurogenic bladder.</p>
150. An 18-year-old man is brought to the physician by his parents because of a 1-week history of increasingly severe pain, swelling, and locking sensation in his right knee. Last week, his knee was twisted and bent as he was pinned during a wrestling match. He was unable to finish the match. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Vital signs are within normal limits. The skin is intact. Examination of the right knee shows 2+ effusion. Ran e of motion of the left knee is full. Range of motion of the right knee in extension is limited by pain. McMurray testing of the right knee produces pain over the medial joint line. Lachman and abduction and adduction stress tests are negative bilaterally. Dorsalis pedis pulses are 2+ bilaterally. Sensation to light touch is intact over the lower extremities. X-rays of the right knee show no abnormalities. An MRI of the medial aspect of the right knee is shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Arthroscopy and operative repair of the knee</br>b. Aspiration and manipulation of the knee</br>c. Physical therapy and knee immobilization</br>d. Physical therapy and use of crutches</br>e. Rest and ibuprofen therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Arthroscopy and operative repair of the knee  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Twisted and bent … pinned during a wrestling match” → classic non-contact twisting injury, foot planted.<br /></br>• “Increasing pain, swelling, and locking sensation” → mechanical block, highly suggestive of a meniscal tear.<br /></br>• “McMurray testing … pain over the medial joint line” → localizes lesion to medial meniscus.<br /></br>• Lachman, varus, valgus tests normal → no ACL, LCL, or MCL instability.<br /></br>• X-ray normal → rules out fracture; MRI (image supplied) confirms medial meniscal tear (often bucket-handle type in young patients).  </p></br><p>Key pattern: Young athlete + twisting injury + joint-line tenderness + positive McMurray + locking = meniscal tear that usually requires arthroscopic repair.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Identify pathology: medial meniscal tear (mechanical symptoms).<br /></br>2. Age &amp; activity: 18-year-old, high functional demands → repair favored.<br /></br>3. Symptom severity: locking = true mechanical block, unlikely to resolve with rest or PT.<br /></br>4. Imaging: MRI confirms tear, no bony injury.<br /></br>5. Management hierarchy:<br /></br>   • Acute, symptomatic, reparable tear (especially peripheral or bucket-handle) → early arthroscopy with repair or partial meniscectomy.<br /></br>   • Non-operative care is reserved for small, degenerative, or minimally symptomatic tears, typically in older patients.  </p></br><p>Take-home: Persistent locking after an acute meniscal tear in a young patient is a surgical disease.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Menisci distribute load &amp; provide stability.<br /></br>• Medial meniscus is firmly attached to MCL &amp; joint capsule → less mobile, more often torn.<br /></br>• Twisting on a planted foot → shear force → longitudinal or bucket-handle tear.<br /></br>• Displaced fragment can flip into intercondylar notch, producing painful “locking.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line for symptomatic young patient:<br /></br>• Arthroscopic reduction of displaced fragment + meniscal repair (inside-out, outside-in, or all-inside techniques) if in vascular (red-red or red-white) zone.<br /></br>• Partial meniscectomy if tear in avascular white-white zone or irreparable.<br /></br>Post-op: protected weight-bearing, early ROM, quad strengthening.  </p></br><p>Diagnostics to know:<br /></br>• McMurray, Thessaly tests – bedside screening.<br /></br>• MRI – gold standard non-invasive test (T2 shows fluid in tear).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Lock = Scope.”<br /></br>If the knee “locks” after a twist, think meniscal tear → arthroSCOPE.<br /></br>Medial Meniscus = “MC (most common) injury” because it’s tethered to the MCL.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Aspiration and manipulation<br /></br>• Used for hemarthrosis relief or post-operative adhesions, not for a structural tear causing locking. Won’t fix displaced meniscal fragment.  </p></br><p>C. Physical therapy and knee immobilization<br /></br>• May help collateral-ligament sprain or mild meniscal degeneration, but immobilization worsens stiffness and does not resolve mechanical block.  </p></br><p>D. Physical therapy and use of crutches<br /></br>• Appropriate for partial ligament tears or small, stable meniscal tears without locking. Persistent mechanical symptoms predict failure of conservative therapy.  </p></br><p>E. Rest and ibuprofen therapy<br /></br>• Analgesia alone is sufficient for minor sprains or degenerative/atraumatic meniscal tears in older adults. Ineffective for acute displaced tear with locking in a young athlete.  </p></br><p>Common test trap: students choose conservative treatment because X-ray is normal; remember that soft-tissue injuries rely on exam + MRI, and locking mandates operative intervention.</p>
151. A 22-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 3-day history of a sore throat and difficulty opening his jaw. He has no history of serious illness, and he takes no medications. He is allergic to penicillin. His temperature is 38.4  C 101  F, pulse is 120/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 100/70 mm Hg. There is cervical lymphadenopathy bilaterally. Examination of the throat shows deviation of the uvula to the right, edema of the soft palate on the left and bilateral pharyngeal erythema with exudates. After drainage of the abscess, which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Amoxicillin</br>b. Cephalexin</br>c. Clindamycin</br>d. Erythromycin</br>e. Metronidazole</br>F. Tetracycline</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Clindamycin  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Severe sore throat,” “difficulty opening his jaw (trismus),” “uvula deviated to the right,” and “soft-palate edema” → classic constellation for a peritonsillar abscess (quinsy).<br /></br>• Fever (38.4 °C) and tachycardia support an acute infectious process.<br /></br>• Key modifier: “Allergic to penicillin.” This immediately rules out any β-lactam that risks cross-reactivity.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Identify diagnosis → peritonsillar abscess (complication of tonsillitis).<br /></br>2. Definitive management → incision &amp; drainage (performed) PLUS systemic antibiotics that cover:<br /></br>   • Streptococcus pyogenes (GAS)<br /></br>   • Oral anaerobes (Prevotella, Fusobacterium, Peptostreptococcus)<br /></br>3. Antibiotic selection in penicillin-allergic patient → clindamycin is first-line because it:<br /></br>   • Has excellent activity against GAS and most oral anaerobes.<br /></br>   • Has no β-lactam structure → safe in true penicillin allergy.<br /></br>Take-home: After drainage of a peritonsillar abscess, give clindamycin if the patient cannot receive penicillins.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• Tonsillar crypt infection (usually GAS) spreads through the soft palate → localized pus collection between tonsil &amp; pharyngeal muscles.<br /></br>• Trismus results from irritation of the pterygoid muscles; uvular deviation is caused by mass effect.<br /></br>• Potential complications: airway obstruction, parapharyngeal or retropharyngeal spread, Lemierre syndrome (IJ-vein thrombosis).  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line IV/PO regimens (no penicillin allergy):<br /></br>• Ampicillin-sulbactam IV or Amoxicillin-clavulanate PO.  </p></br><p>Penicillin allergy:<br /></br>• Clindamycin IV/PO.  </p></br><p>Additional measures:<br /></br>• Ensure airway stability.<br /></br>• Analgesia and hydration.<br /></br>• Tonsillectomy only for recurrent abscesses or obstructive events.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Quinsy?
