Think ‘Clinda’ when Penicillin can’t.”<br /></br>OR<br /></br>Abscess ABC:<br /></br>A – Aspirate / incision &amp; drainage<br /></br>B – Bugs = GAS + anaerobes<br /></br>C – Clindamycin if Can’t use penicillin  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Amoxicillin – β-lactam; contraindicated in true penicillin allergy; would cover GAS but not adequate anaerobic coverage unless combined with clavulanate.<br /></br>b. Cephalexin – 1st-gen cephalosporin; ~2% cross-reactivity, still avoided in true anaphylactic penicillin allergy; weak anaerobe coverage.<br /></br>d. Erythromycin – Covers GAS but poor anaerobic coverage &amp; increasing resistance; not first-line for deep oral abscesses.<br /></br>e. Metronidazole – Excellent anaerobe coverage but NO activity against Streptococcus pyogenes; would require combination with another agent.<br /></br>f. Tetracycline – Variable GAS activity, poor anaerobe coverage, and not recommended for acute suppurative pharyngeal infections.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Core “Shelf/Step-2” Takeaway<br /></br>Peritonsillar abscess = Drain + broad aerobic/anaerobic coverage.<br /></br>• Amp-sulbactam/Amox-clav if no allergy.<br /></br>• Clindamycin if penicillin allergic.</p>
152. A 16-year-old boy who was diagnosed with HIV infection 1 year ago comes for a follow-up examination. He has had no opportunistic infections. Immunizations are up-to-date. His CD4+ T-lymphocyte count is 550/mm 3 Normal~S00, and plasma HIV viral load is less than 1000 copies/ml, a hundredfold decrease since he began triple antiretroviral therapy 1 year ago. He and his girlfriend are sexually active and use condoms consistently. His plans for the summer include traveling to Africa for 2 weeks on a medical missionary trip, buying a cat, joining a swim team, and obtaining a part-time job in a day-care center which involves caring for toilet-trained toddlers. Avoidance of which of the following activities is most likely to minimize his chances of opportunistic infection?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Cleaning the cat litter</br>b. Joining the swim team</br>c. Sexual activity</br>d. Traveling to Africa</br>e. Working in a day-care center</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Cleaning the cat litter</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Known HIV-positive adolescent on effective triple ART → CD4 count 550 / mm³ (essentially normal immunity; no history of AIDS-defining illness)<br /></br>• Plans: cat ownership (cleaning litter), swimming, condom-protected intercourse, short trip to Africa, working only with toilet-trained toddlers.<br /></br>• Question stem: “Which activity should he avoid to MINIMIZE chances of opportunistic infection?” → test writer wants the ONE activity that still carries a unique pathogen exposure even in a patient whose CD4 count is &gt;500.</p></br><p>Key clue: “buying a cat” + “cleaning the cat litter.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Determine current immune status: CD4 &gt;500 ⇒ essentially the same spectrum of infections as general population, BUT certain zoonotic exposures still pose risk.<br /></br>2. Screen each proposed activity for a pathogen that can be acquired uniquely or more severely in HIV patients.<br /></br>3. Cat feces → Toxoplasma gondii oocysts. Toxo encephalitis is an AIDS-defining illness and can still reactivate later if immunity wanes. Avoidance (or at least not cleaning litter) is standard CDC advice for all HIV patients, regardless of CD4.<br /></br>4. Other activities (swimming, condom-protected sex, day-care work with toilet-trained kids, brief Africa travel with recommended vaccines) do not introduce a high-risk, unique opportunistic pathogen at his current immune level.</p></br><p>Take-home: Cat litter = Toxoplasma exposure → unique, preventable risk for HIV patients.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Toxoplasma gondii life cycle: Felids are definitive hosts, shedding oocysts in feces → Oocysts sporulate (24–48 h) → ingestion (litter handling) → latent tissue cysts in muscle &amp; CNS.<br /></br>• Immunocompetent host: asymptomatic or mild mono-like illness.<br /></br>• Immunosuppressed host/CD4 &lt;100: reactivation ⇒ multiple ring-enhancing lesions in brain, encephalitis, chorioretinitis.<br /></br>• Prevention in HIV:<br /></br>  – Avoid raw/undercooked meat<br /></br>  – Wash fruits/vegetables<br /></br>  – Wear gloves / delegate litter box cleaning; change litter daily before sporulation<br /></br>  – Prophylactic TMP-SMX when CD4 &lt;100 and IgG + </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Management &amp; Prevention Pearls<br /></br>• ART to keep CD4 high is most important overall.<br /></br>• Vaccines/PPX at CD4 &gt;200: no additional OI prophylaxis needed except standard precautions (cat litter, raw meat).<br /></br>• Travel: give routine and region-specific vaccines (yellow-fever if CD4 &gt;200, Hep A/B, typhoid, update MMR).<br /></br>• Day-care: CMV risk highest when changing diapers; toilet-trained kids lower risk. Good hand hygiene suffices.<br /></br>• Swimming: No specific OI risk; avoid if chronic skin breakdown (Pseudomonas folliculitis) but not an absolute issue.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Cat = Tox Catastrophe”: HIV patients must “Pass the scooper” – never scoop poop.<br /></br>CD4 ladder:<br /></br>• &gt;500 = basically normal (except Toxo precautions)<br /></br>• &lt;200 = PCP, Histoplasma<br /></br>• &lt;100 = Toxo, Cryptococcus<br /></br>• &lt;50  = MAC, CMV retinitis</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>b. Joining the swim team<br /></br>• Public pools do not confer significant OI risk at CD4 &gt; 500. Pseudomonas or Giardia risk is similar to general population; good showering practices suffice.</p></br><p>c. Sexual activity (with consistent condom use)<br /></br>• Condoms markedly reduce HIV transmission to partner and protect him from other STIs. Opportunistic infections (e.g., HSV, HPV) would not qualify as “opportunistic” in this context and are not unique to HIV patients with CD4 &gt; 500.</p></br><p>d. Traveling to Africa<br /></br>• With CD4 &gt;200, routine travel vaccines and malaria prophylaxis render the trip safe. Most travel-related infections (malaria, typhoid) are not AIDS-defining OIs and affect immunocompetent travelers as well.</p></br><p>e. Working in a day-care center (toilet-trained toddlers)<br /></br>• Primary concern would be CMV, but risk plummets once children are toilet-trained and diapers are avoided. Standard hand hygiene is adequate; no guideline advises against this occupation at his immune level.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Even well-controlled HIV patients should avoid handling cat litter to prevent acquisition of Toxoplasma gondii, an opportunistic pathogen that can reactivate if future immunosuppression occurs.</p>
153. A 42-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 1, at 12 weeks' gestation comes to the physician for her first prenatal visit. She conceived a dichorionic-diamniotic twin gestation after in vitro fertilization. She has no history of serious illness, and her only medication is a prenatal vitamin. Two years ago, her first pregnancy ended in cesarean delivery at 39 weeks' gestation because of breech presentation. She is 165 cm 5 ft 5 in tall. She weighed 68 kg 150 lb prior to her pregnancy; BMI was 25 kg/m 2. She has had a 2-kg 4-lb weight gain during her pregnancy. Her blood pressure is 110/70 mm Hg. Examination shows a uterus consistent in size with a 12-week gestation. This patient is at greatest risk for which of the following pregnancy complications?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Fetal aneuploidy</br>b. Preterm labor</br>c. Twin-twin transfusion syndrome</br>d. Umbilical cord entanglement</br>e. Uterine rupture</br>F. Vanishing twin syndrome</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer: b. Preterm labor  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key phrases: “dichorionic-diamniotic twin gestation,” “in vitro fertilization,” normal BMI, prior uncomplicated cesarean.<br /></br>• The only feature that meaningfully alters pregnancy risk is the presence of twins (two placentas, two sacs), not the prior C-section (because uterine rupture is mostly a concern only if she attempts a trial of labor).<br /></br>• DC-DA twins rule out monochorionic &amp; monoamniotic complications (TTTS, cord entanglement).  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Identify the highest-frequency complication of twin gestation.<br /></br>   • Prematurity (delivery &lt;37 wk) occurs in ~55–60 % of twin pregnancies; the mean gestational age at delivery is ≈35 wk.  </p></br><p>Step 2 – Compare the incidences of the other listed complications in DC-DA twins:<br /></br>   • Vanishing twin – largely a first-trimester event detected on early ultrasound; incidence ~15-20 % but occurs before the “twin pregnancy” has actually declared itself. After 12 wk the risk falls sharply.<br /></br>   • Aneuploidy – risk rises with maternal age (42 y) but absolute risk for any single fetus is still ≈1–2 %; when multiplied by two fetuses it is ≈2–4 %, far lower than 55 % for preterm delivery.<br /></br>   • Twin-twin transfusion – requires monochorionic placenta (not present).<br /></br>   • Umbilical-cord entanglement – requires monoamniotic sac (not present).<br /></br>   • Uterine rupture – risk with classical or low-transverse scar attempting VBAC ≈0.5–1 %; much lower than prematurity, and this patient has not committed to VBAC.  </p></br><h2>Take-home: The most common and therefore “greatest-risk” outcome of any multifetal gestation—even uncomplicated DC-DA twins—is preterm labor/delivery.</h2></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Twin gestation → uterine over-distension + higher placental mass → increased prostaglandin production &amp; cervical insufficiency → earlier myometrial activation and cervical ripening → spontaneous or indicated preterm birth.  </p></br><p>Risk factors that compound prematurity in twins<br /></br>• Assisted reproductive technology (IVF)<br /></br>• Maternal age &gt;35 y<br /></br>• Low or high BMI, smoking, short inter-pregnancy interval (not present here)  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Management Overview<br /></br>1. Early anatomy scan &amp; serial growth ultrasounds q4 wk after 20 wk.<br /></br>2. Cervical length screening (TVUS) 16–24 wk.<br /></br>3. Consider progesterone or cerclage ONLY if short cervix (&lt;25 mm) or prior PTB.<br /></br>4. Educate regarding preterm labor symptoms; administer antenatal corticosteroids &amp; magnesium neuro-protection if labor &lt;34 wk.  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Twins come TWO early.” – Two babies, TWO-thirds chance of prematurity.<br /></br>TTTS &amp; cord Tangling require “Shared real estate” (mono-chorion or mono-amnion).  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>A. Fetal aneuploidy – Maternal age gives each fetus ~1–2 % risk of trisomy; still &lt;5 % overall.<br /></br>C. Twin-twin transfusion syndrome – Needs a single shared chorion; DC-DA twins have separate placentas.<br /></br>D. Umbilical cord entanglement – Only in monoamniotic twins (one sac).<br /></br>E. Uterine rupture – Requires labor stress on a scar; elective repeat C-section or intact LUS scar keeps risk very low (~0.5 %).<br /></br>F. Vanishing twin – Early first-trimester phenomenon, incidence drops once cardiac activity persists past 10–12 wk; less common than later prematurity.  </p></br><p>Core concept: In any multiple gestation, think prematurity first; chorionicity/amnionicity then determines the rarer twin-specific complications.</p>
154. On arrival to the postanesthesia care unit 15 minutes after undergoing fourth and fifth metacarpophalangeal arthroplasty, a 32-year-old woman with rheumatoid arthritis has a fingerstick blood glucose concentration of 350 mg/dl. During induction of general anesthesia, endotracheal intubation was complicated by arthritic changes to the cervical spine, and dexamethasone was administered to decrease airway edema. She received lactated Ringer solution during the 60-minute procedure; intraoperative blood loss was minimal. Medications are prednisone and methotrexate. She is awake. Vital signs are within normal limits. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. Neurologic examination shows no focal findings. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's hyperglycemia?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Dexamethasone administration during induction of anesthesia</br>b. Methotrexate-induced pancreatic insufficiency</br>c. New-onset diabetes mellitus</br>d. Prednisone-induced adrenal insufficiency</br>e. Normal perioperative stress response</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>A. Dexamethasone administration during induction of anesthesia</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• “Finger-stick glucose 350 mg/dL 15 min after surgery” – strikingly high and very acute rise.<br /></br>• “Dexamethasone was administered to decrease airway edema” – single IV stress-dose of a very potent glucocorticoid.<br /></br>• Long-term meds: prednisone (lower potency) and methotrexate (DMARD).<br /></br>• Surgery time only 60 min, minimal blood loss, vitals stable → no large catecholamine surge.<br /></br>Key pattern: abrupt, marked hyperglycemia shortly after IV glucocorticoid.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1 – Recognize the precipitator: IV dexamethasone (≈25× glucocorticoid strength of cortisol) causes a rapid spike in serum glucose by (1) stimulating hepatic gluconeogenesis, (2) inducing peripheral insulin resistance, and (3) impairing glucose uptake.</p></br><p>Step 2 – Rule out alternatives:</br>• Normal surgical stress usually elevates glucose to 120-200, not 350, especially after a brief, low-stress case.<br /></br>• Chronic prednisone would have raised baseline glucose, not cause a new abrupt jump.<br /></br>• Methotrexate pancreatitis is rare, subacute, and produces abdominal pain.<br /></br>• Adrenal insufficiency causes HYPO-glycemia.<br /></br>• New-onset DM does not appear within minutes of an anesthetic.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: A single peri-operative dose of a potent systemic glucocorticoid can cause marked, transient hyperglycemia.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>Glucocorticoids (cortisol, prednisone, dexamethasone):</br>• ↑ Transcription of key gluconeogenic enzymes (PEP-CK, G6Pase).<br /></br>• ↓ Peripheral glucose uptake (antagonizes insulin action).<br /></br>• Net effect – hepatic glucose output rises, skeletal muscle and adipose uptake falls → serum glucose spikes within 15-30 min and can persist 6-12 h with dexamethasone (long t½, high potency).</p></br><p>Potencies (anti-inflammatory equivalent to 5 mg prednisone):</br>• Hydrocortisone 20 mg<br /></br>• Prednisone 5 mg<br /></br>• Dexamethasone 0.75 mg ← very potent</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls</br>• Usually no therapy needed for a one-time dose; monitor and treat with sliding-scale insulin if &gt;180-200 mg/dL to reduce infection risk and promote wound healing.<br /></br>• Anticipate steroid-induced hyperglycemia in any patient receiving ≥1 dose of IV dexamethasone, methylprednisolone, or high-dose prednisone.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“Steroid = Sugar Spike” – any systemic steroid can “spike” glucose quickly.<br /></br>Potency ladder: Hydrocortisone &lt; Prednisone &lt; Methylprednisolone &lt; Dexamethasone (“HiPPeMD”).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>B. Methotrexate-induced pancreatic insufficiency – MTX rarely causes acute pancreatitis; would present with abdominal pain, ↑ lipase, not immediate postoperative hyperglycemia.</p></br><p>C. New-onset diabetes mellitus – DM develops over weeks–months; glucose would not surge abruptly minutes after anesthesia.</p></br><p>D. Prednisone-induced adrenal insufficiency – Chronic exogenous steroids suppress the HPA axis; acute deficiency occurs if steroids are stopped, causing hypotension &amp; HYPO-glycemia, not hyperglycemia.</p></br><p>E. Normal perioperative stress response – Stress hyperglycemia exists, but levels &gt;300 mg/dL this soon after a short, minor surgery are atypical without exogenous steroids.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>A single intra-operative dose of the high-potency glucocorticoid dexamethasone is the most plausible and well-recognized cause of the patient’s marked, immediate postoperative hyperglycemia.</p>
155. Three days after an operation for aortic stenosis, a 67-year-old white man is being evaluated by the surgical team. A medical student takes one of the two patient information charts hanging outside the room. While reading the chart, the student realizes that the ethnicity listed on the chart does not match the ethnicity of the patient. Verification of the name on the chart shows that the student has taken the chart for the other patient in the double room. Both charts are labeled room 456-1, instead of the chart for the window bed being labeled 456-2. Individual members of the team laugh at the mix-up, and a resident states that the charts are often misnumbered due to patients shifting room position. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step by the senior member of the team?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Emphasize during new intern orientation that chart information is often incorrect</br>b. Report the incident to the quality assurance committee for further analysis</br>c. Verbally reprimand the medical student for reading the incorrect chart</br>d. Verbally reprimand the members of the team who laughed</br>e. Verbally reprimand the nurse who mislabeled the chart</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Report the incident to the quality-assurance committee for further analysis  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues:<br /></br>  – Two different patients share “456-1” on their charts → labeling/ID system failure, not an isolated human slip.<br /></br>  – “Charts are often misnumbered due to patients shifting room position” → a recurrent, known problem → system-level issue.<br /></br>  – The event was caught before harm occurred → a “near miss,” which accrediting bodies (e.g., The Joint Commission) require to be reported and analyzed.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. When a patient-identification error or near miss is discovered, the priority is to prevent future harm by fixing the underlying system.<br /></br>2. Best practice = initiate a formal quality-improvement (QI) process (incident report → quality-assurance committee → root-cause analysis → corrective action).<br /></br>3. Blaming individuals or relying on ad-hoc reminders does not reliably prevent recurrence.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Near misses must be reported to the institutional QI mechanism so the system failure can be analyzed and corrected.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (conceptual)<br /></br>While no biologic pathophysiology is involved, the “pathophysiology” of medical error follows the Swiss-cheese model: multiple small defenses must all fail for harm to reach the patient. Mislabeling charts removes an essential defense (correct patient identification).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostic &amp; Management Pearls<br /></br>• Sentinel event / near miss → Incident report → Root-Cause Analysis (RCA).<br /></br>• RCA typically asks “What happened? Why? How can we prevent it?” and generates system fixes (bar-code ID bands, electronic MAR, double-check protocols, etc.).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“B for ‘Bring it to the Board’ (quality Board/committee).”<br /></br>Any event that COULD have harmed a patient but didn’t = near miss → B = Report to QA Board.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Emphasize during intern orientation that chart info is often incorrect<br /></br>   • Education alone is weak; knowing an error exists ≠ fixing it. Fails to address root cause.  </p></br><p>c. Verbally reprimand the medical student<br /></br>   • Student actually helped identify the problem; punishing reporters discourages error reporting (opposite of a safety culture).  </p></br><p>d. Verbally reprimand the team members who laughed<br /></br>   • Addresses professionalism but not the systemic labeling flaw; doesn’t prevent future misidentification.  </p></br><p>e. Verbally reprimand the nurse who mislabeled the chart<br /></br>   • Focuses on a single person; ignores the recurring nature (“often misnumbered”) indicating a system problem. Blame-and-train is low-yield for patient safety.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>A recurrent chart-labeling error is a system failure and near miss. The correct response is to report it through the hospital’s quality-assurance mechanism for root-cause analysis and system-level corrective action.</p>
156. A 42-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 1-month history of intermittent mild pain in her right lower abdomen. She has not had fever, chills, changes in bowel habits, or urinary symptoms. Three years ago, she underwent bilateral prophylactic mastectomy because her maternal grandmother, mother, and two older sisters have a history of breast cancer. The patient has no history of serious illness, and her only medication is a calcium supplement containing vitamin D. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 80/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 116/68 mm Hg. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. The abdomen is soft and nondistended, and the liver is not enlarged. Examination of the right lower quadrant shows tenderness to deep palpation; there are no signs of peritonitis. On pelvic examination, a mass is palpated in the right adnexal region; no other abnormalities are noted. Pelvic ultrasonography shows an 8 x 6-cm complex, multiseptate, right ovarian mass; there is no ascites. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Fine-needle biopsy of the mass</br>b. Operative exploration</br>c. PET scan</br>d. Serum carcinoembryonic antigen CEA assay</br>e. Testing for BRCA mutations</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>B. Operative exploration</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Complex, multiseptate ovarian mass (8 × 6 cm) → worrisome for malignancy (solid components, septations, &gt;10 cm or rapidly enlarging would raise even more concern).<br /></br>• Right‐lower-quadrant tenderness but no peritonitis → mass is confined, no evidence of rupture.<br /></br>• Age 42 with strong family history of breast cancer and prior prophylactic mastectomy → very high probability of BRCA1/2 mutation, which markedly elevates epithelial ovarian carcinoma risk.<br /></br>These clues combine to point toward a potentially malignant ovarian neoplasm that must be managed as such.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Suspicious adnexal mass on imaging = treat as ovarian cancer until proven otherwise.<br /></br>2. Core principle: NEVER stick a needle into a potentially malignant ovarian mass pre-op because spillage seeds tumor cells throughout the peritoneal cavity, upstaging disease and worsening prognosis.<br /></br>3. Therefore, the correct diagnostic (and therapeutic) next step is operative exploration with removal of the mass intact and full surgical staging (total abdominal hysterectomy, bilateral salpingo-oophorectomy, peritoneal washings, omentectomy, and lymph node sampling, depending on intra-op findings).</p></br><p>Take-home: Suspected ovarian cancer → exploratory laparotomy/laparoscopy with en-bloc removal; avoid percutaneous biopsy.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• BRCA1/2 germline mutations impair homologous DNA repair → accumulation of DNA damage → high-grade serous carcinoma of the ovary/fallopian tube.<br /></br>• Epithelial ovarian cancer spreads transcoelomically (shedding into peritoneum); rupture or biopsy can disseminate tumor cells.<br /></br>• Clinical presentation is nonspecific (bloating, pelvic pain, GI discomfort) → imaging features help raise suspicion.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Diagnostic/operative steps:<br /></br>1. Exploratory laparotomy or laparoscopy.<br /></br>2. Surgical staging: TAH-BSO, omentectomy, peritoneal biopsies/washings ± pelvic/para-aortic lymphadenectomy.<br /></br>3. Histopathology confirms type and stage.<br /></br>4. Post-op carboplatin + paclitaxel for most epithelial cancers.<br /></br>5. Consider PARP inhibitors (olaparib, niraparib) for BRCA-positive disease.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Don’t FNA an Ova” → Fine-needle aspiration of an ovarian mass suspected for malignancy is contraindicated.<br /></br>“Capture the Cancer Intact” → remove whole, then stage.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Fine-needle biopsy of the mass – Contraindicated for potential ovarian carcinoma due to peritoneal seeding; would upstage disease from Stage I to at least Stage IC.<br /></br>C. PET scan – Can evaluate metastasis, but would not replace the need for surgical staging and does not avoid risk of rupture. Imaging cannot provide definitive tissue diagnosis.<br /></br>D. Serum carcinoembryonic antigen (CEA) – Marker for colorectal and mucinous tumors; CA-125 is more typical for epithelial ovarian cancer, but even CA-125 would not alter initial management because surgery is still required.<br /></br>E. Testing for BRCA mutations – Appropriate for risk assessment and family counseling, but it does nothing to diagnose the current adnexal mass. Management of this mass cannot wait for genetic results.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Believing that all masses should be biopsied first. Ovarian masses are the major exception—biopsy is harmful if malignancy is suspected.</p></br><p>Bottom Line<br /></br>Suspected malignant ovarian tumor = proceed directly to operative exploration and staging; avoid percutaneous biopsy.</p>
157. A 28-year-old man comes to the emergency department because of a 1-week history of increasingly severe right lower abdominal pain. During this time, he also has had fever, nausea, and vomiting. He has not had chills. He has no history of serious illness or operative procedures and takes no medications. Temperature is 38.3 C 101.0 F, pulse is 100/min, and blood pressure is 120/60 mm Hg. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. On abdominal examination, a 6 x 8-cm, tender mass is palpated in the right lower quadrant. There is no overlying erythema or induration. CT scan of the abdomen and pelvis shows a 6 x 8-cm fluid collection with an enhancing rim in the right lower quadrant. In addition to intravenous broad-spectrum antibiotic therapy, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Aspiration of the fluid</br>b. Colonoscopy</br>c. Exploratory laparotomy</br>d. Percutaneous drainage</br>e. Observation only</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d.  Percutaneous drainage</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “1-week history … fever, nausea, vomiting” + RLQ pain → classic presentation of complicated appendicitis.<br /></br>• “6 × 8-cm tender mass” palpable in RLQ → walled-off inflammatory phlegmon/abscess.<br /></br>• CT: “fluid collection with an enhancing rim” → confirms an abscess; size ≥3 cm is clinically significant.<br /></br>• Hemodynamically stable, no generalized peritonitis → non-operative (percutaneous) source control is preferred.</p></br><p>Key pattern: Perforated appendix that has localized → appendiceal abscess &gt;3 cm → antibiotics + image-guided drainage now, interval appendectomy later.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)</br>1. Identify that this is not simple acute appendicitis (symptoms &gt;48 h + palpable mass + CT abscess).<br /></br>2. Decide goal: control infection but avoid immediate laparotomy because the inflamed, adherent tissues make surgery high-risk.<br /></br>3. Evidence-based management:<br /></br>   • Broad-spectrum IV antibiotics.<br /></br>   • Image-guided percutaneous drainage when abscess ≥3 cm or patient symptomatic.<br /></br>   • Elective interval appendectomy ~6–8 weeks later.</p></br><p>Take-home concept: In a stable patient with a mature peri-appendiceal abscess, percutaneous drainage is preferred over immediate surgery.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• A perforated appendix can be contained by the omentum and adjacent bowel loops, forming a localized abscess.<br /></br>• Ongoing bacterial proliferation raises intraluminal pressure → pain, fever, leukocytosis.<br /></br>• Early surgery through inflamed tissues risks bowel injury, fistula, and difficult closure; drainage converts it to a controlled infection.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line (stable, localized abscess ≥3 cm)<br /></br>1. IV broad coverage (e.g., piperacillin-tazobactam or ceftriaxone + metronidazole).<br /></br>2. CT- or US-guided catheter drainage.<br /></br>3. Clinical and radiologic reassessment; remove drain when output minimal and patient afebrile.<br /></br>4. Interval laparoscopic appendectomy weeks later to prevent recurrence.</p></br><p>If unstable or diffuse peritonitis → emergent exploratory surgery + appendectomy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“AB-CD for Abscess”<br /></br>A – Antibiotics<br /></br>B – Big (&gt;3 cm)<br /></br>C – Catheter<br /></br>D – Drainage<br /></br>If it’s big and walled-off, AB-CD it!</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Aspiration of the fluid<br /></br>• Simple needle aspiration without leaving a drain is inadequate for &gt;3 cm abscess—rapid re-accumulation and persistent sepsis risk.</p></br><p>b. Colonoscopy<br /></br>• Contra-indicated acutely (perforation risk). Used later to rule out cecal malignancy in older patients, not in this 28-year-old with clear CT abscess.</p></br><p>c. Exploratory laparotomy<br /></br>• Indicated only for generalized peritonitis or unstable patient. In a contained abscess, surgery now increases morbidity (bowel injury, fistula, wound infection).</p></br><p>e. Observation only<br /></br>• Abscess &gt;3 cm plus systemic symptoms mandates active source control; observation alone risks enlargement, rupture, or sepsis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Localized appendiceal abscess in a stable patient = IV antibiotics + CT-guided percutaneous drainage now, interval appendectomy later.</p>
158. A 77-year-old woman comes to the emergency department 1 hour after the sudden onset of pain, numbness, and stiffness of her right forearm and hand. She has hypertension and atrial fibrillation. She had a myocardial infarction 2 years ago. Her medications are metoprolol, lisinopril, and warfarin. Her temperature is 36.8 C 98.2 F, pulse is 60/min and irregularly irregular, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 140/90 mm Hg. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. The right forearm and hand are pale and cool with a capillary refill time of 8 seconds. Pulses are absent in the distal aspect of the right upper extremity. The right axillary pulse and pulses in the left upper extremity are within normal limits. An ECG shows atrial fibrillation with a ventricular rate of 72/min. There are no acute changes. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 12.3 g/dL</br>Hematocrit G7 percent</br>Leukocyte count 10,500/mmB</br>Platelet count 354,000/mm 3</br>Prothrombin time 14 sec INR=1.5</br>Partial thromboplastin time 27 sec</br>She is scheduled for embolectomy in 90 minutes. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Addition of adenosine to the medication regimen</br>b. Addition of heparin to the medication regimen ---~</br>c. Addition of sodium nitroprusside to the medication regimen</br>d. Application of a heating pad to the right upper extremity</br>e. Discontinuation of metoprolol therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct answer: b. Addition of heparin to the medication regimen  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Sudden onset of pain, numbness, and stiffness” + “pale and cool” limb, prolonged capillary refill, and “absent distal pulses” = the classic 6 P’s of acute arterial occlusion (Pain, Pallor, Pulselessness, Poikilothermia, Paresthesia, Paralysis).<br /></br>• A-fib → high risk of peripheral arterial embolus.<br /></br>• INR 1.5 (&lt;2.0) ⇒ warfarin sub-therapeutic; existing clot can propagate.<br /></br>• Embolectomy already planned (definitive revascularization).  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Recognize acute limb ischemia (ALI).<br /></br>Step 2: Identify embolic source (A-fib) + inadequate anticoagulation.<br /></br>Step 3: Immediate priorities in ALI:<br /></br>   1. Systemic anticoagulation with IV unfractionated heparin ASAP.<br /></br>   2. Vascular surgery / interventional radiology for revascularization.<br /></br>Heparin prevents clot propagation and formation of new thrombi while waiting for the OR → limb-saving.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In any suspected ALI, start IV heparin immediately once diagnosis is made, even before imaging or surgery.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• A-fib → stasis in left atrial appendage → mural thrombus → embolization to brachial artery → acute cessation of distal flow.<br /></br>• Within hours, ischemic cascade causes nerve and muscle necrosis; time = tissue. Heparin does NOT lyse the clot (that’s surgery/TPA) but buys time by halting new thrombus and microthrombi.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. Bolus IV unfractionated heparin (80 U/kg) then infusion (18 U/kg/h) → target aPTT 2-3× control.<br /></br>2. Emergent vascular imaging (CTA) if diagnosis uncertain; here, clinical exam is classic, so go directly to OR.<br /></br>3. Alternatives when surgery not feasible: catheter-directed thrombolysis (tPA) for viable limb (&lt;14 days).<br /></br>4. Long-term: strict therapeutic anticoagulation (warfarin with INR 2-3 or DOAC) + A-fib rate/rhythm control.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ALI = HeParin in a Hurry”:<br /></br>H – Heparin<br /></br>P – Prevent Propagation<br /></br>Hurry – Do it immediately while the patient Hurries to the OR.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Adenosine – transiently blocks AV node; used for PSVT, not for limb ischemia. Does nothing for an arterial embolus.<br /></br>c. Sodium nitroprusside – potent arterial/venous vasodilator for hypertensive emergencies; would not restore flow beyond an occluded artery and risks hypotension.<br /></br>d. Heating pad – increases metabolic demand in ischemic tissue → accelerates necrosis; contraindicated.<br /></br>e. Discontinuing metoprolol – would not treat the acute occlusion and could worsen rate control in A-fib.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Confusing thrombolytics with anticoagulation. Thrombolytics dissolve an existing clot but carry high bleeding risk; systemic anticoagulation with heparin is the universally accepted first step in ALI unless contraindicated.</p>
159. A 45-year-old woman comes to the physician requesting a second opinion of a recent diagnosis of multiple sclerosis MS. She has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 25 years and asks if cigarette smoking contributed</br>to her current condition. The physician finds a recently published study evaluating the relationship between cigarette smoking and MS. A total of 100,000 women between the ages of 18 and 40 years were polled about their</br>medical and social histories over a 10-year period. Data show that 205 participants reported developing MS. The rates of cigarette smoking in participants with MS were compared with those of 615 age- and</br>location-matched participants without MS. Which of the following best describes this study design?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Case series</br>b. Case-control</br>c. Cross-sectional</br>d. Historical cohort</br>e. Prospective cohort</br></br></br><p>⚠️  Important note before the full explanation<br /></br>Based on standard epidemiologic definitions, the study described in the vignette is a <strong>case-control study</strong>, not a case series.<br /></br>• The investigators first identified “cases” (the 205 women who developed MS) and then selected “controls” (615 age- and location-matched women without MS) to compare a prior exposure (cigarette smoking).<br /></br>• A case series, in contrast, simply describes characteristics of a group of patients who all have the same condition; it has no comparison group and does not estimate an exposure–disease association.</p></br><p>If your answer key truly lists “Case series” as correct, it is almost certainly a typographical or formatting error (e.g., the correct answer might have been choice “B”, which on your copy was mislabeled). Everything that follows assumes the correct design is <strong>case-control</strong>.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Case-control study  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues<br /></br>1. “205 participants reported developing MS” → investigators started by finding people with the outcome (MS).<br /></br>2. “Rates of cigarette smoking … were compared with those of 615 age- and location-matched participants without MS” → explicit mention of a <em>control</em> group without the outcome but otherwise similar.<br /></br>3. Exposure (smoking) was assessed retrospectively; the 10-year polling period is simply where the information came from.  </p></br><p>These elements are the classic pattern for a case-control study:<br /></br>• Identify cases → select controls → look back to measure past exposure.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning / Take-Home Point<br /></br>Case-control studies are the design of choice when:<br /></br>• The outcome is rare (only 205/100,000 developed MS).<br /></br>• You need to study multiple potential exposures.<br /></br>Key exam mantra: “Start with disease, look back for the exposure.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield Tie-In)<br /></br>Multiple sclerosis is an autoimmune demyelinating disease of the CNS with genetic susceptibility (HLA-DRB1) and environmental triggers (vitamin D deficiency, EBV, smoking). Smoking is an established modifiable risk factor that accelerates conversion from clinically isolated syndrome to definite MS and worsens progression.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Study Design Refresher – Rapid Comparison<br /></br>• Case-control: Outcome known ➜ compare prior exposure(s). Gives OR.<br /></br>• Cohort (prospective or retrospective): Exposure known ➜ follow for outcome. Gives RR.<br /></br>• Cross-sectional: Snapshot at one point in time; measures prevalence.<br /></br>• Case series: Descriptive; no comparison group; cannot estimate association.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Mnemonic<br /></br>“CASE-control = start with the CASE.”<br /></br>Visualize a file cabinet labeled “MS cases.” You pull out those files first, then grab matching blank folders (controls) from the drawer next to it, and finally flip back in time to see who smoked.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Case series<br /></br>• Would merely list clinical features of the 205 women with MS. No control group, no exposure comparison.</p></br><p>c. Cross-sectional<br /></br>• Would measure exposure and disease at the same moment (prevalence). Here, disease status determined first, and exposure assessed retrospectively.</p></br><p>d. Historical (retrospective) cohort<br /></br>• Would start with smoking status documented in the past and then look forward in old records to see who developed MS. This study started with outcome, not exposure.</p></br><p>e. Prospective cohort<br /></br>• Would enroll smokers and nonsmokers now and watch prospectively for new MS cases. Again, sequence is reversed in the vignette.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>The design is a <strong>case-control study</strong>: begin with MS cases, choose matched controls, and look backward to compare smoking exposure.</p>
160. A previously healthy 57-year-old man is admitted to the hospital for evaluation of a 7-hour history of palpitations. He takes no medications. He drinks six beers daily. He does not smoke. He is 168 cm 5 ft 6 in tall and weighs 70 kg 155 lb; BMI is 25 kg/m 2. His temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 110/min and irregularly irregular, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 126/70 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 97 percent. Examination shows no jugular venous distention. Cardiac examination shows no murmurs, rubs, or gallops. There is no pedal edema. Test of the stool for occult blood is negative. An ECG shows atrial fibrillation at a ventricular rate of 114/min with no ischemic changes. The physician decides to administer oral digoxin. Which of the following is the most appropriate way to record an order on this patient's chart?	Correct answer: F</br></br>a. Digoxin 125 μg PO qd</br>b. Digoxin 125 μg PO daily</br>c. Digoxin .125 mg PO daily</br>d. Digoxin 125 mcg PO qd</br>e. Digoxin 125.0 mcg PO daily</br>F. Digoxin 0.125 mg PO daily</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>F. Digoxin 0.125 mg PO daily  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Digoxin” – a narrow-therapeutic-index drug; small dosing errors can be lethal.<br /></br>• Dose required for rate control in atrial fibrillation is &lt;1 mg (most commonly 0.125 mg or 0.25 mg).<br /></br>• Question asks for “the most appropriate way to record an order,” i.e., medication-safety / prescribing-notation question, not a pharmacology-mechanism question.  </p></br><p>Key testing clue: the stem does NOT ask “what drug?” but “how to record the order.” The test writers want you to apply Joint Commission &amp; ISMP safe-prescribing rules (look for leading zeros, avoid trailing zeros &amp; dangerous abbreviations such as µg, qd, etc.).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Why F is safest)<br /></br>1. Leading zero: For doses &lt;1 mg, always precede the decimal with a “0” to prevent a ten-fold overdose (.125 mg → 125 mg).<br /></br>2. No trailing zero: Never write “125.0 mcg” (trailing “0” can be misread as 1250 mcg).<br /></br>3. Units: write “mg” or “mcg,” never the Greek “µ” (can be mistaken for “m”) and avoid “μg.”<br /></br>4. Frequency: spell out “daily,” never “qd” (can be misread as “qid”).<br /></br>Choice F satisfies every rule: “0.125 mg PO daily.”  </p></br><p>Core takeaway:<br /></br>“Always lead, never trail, and spell it out.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (very concise)<br /></br>• A-fib with rapid ventricular response; digoxin is a rate-control option (positive inotrope, vagotonic AV-node slowing).<br /></br>• Therapeutic window: 0.8–2 ng/mL. Small dosing errors → toxicity (nausea, vision changes, arrhythmias). Hence meticulous prescribing notation is mandatory.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls (for context)<br /></br>• First-line rate control: β-blocker or non-DHP CCB; digoxin useful in hypotension or HFrEF.<br /></br>• Digoxin usual maintenance: 0.125 mg or 0.25 mg PO daily (renal dose adjust).<br /></br>• Monitor: serum creatinine, digoxin level if toxicity suspected, K⁺ &amp; Mg²⁺.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>Mnemonic: “L ea D, S p el l”<br /></br>L – Leading zero for &lt;1 unit<br /></br>D – Drop trailing zeros<br /></br>S – Spell out units &amp; frequency (no µ, no qd)  </p></br><p>Visual: Picture a “0” leading a decimal across a finish line while a larger “.0” trails behind and falls off a cliff—remind yourself “Lead, never trail.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Digoxin 125 μg PO qd<br /></br>• Uses Greek “μ” (unsafe), abbreviation “qd” (unsafe). Could be misread as mg → 1000-fold error.  </p></br><p>b. Digoxin 125 μg PO daily<br /></br>• Still uses “μ”; also 125 μg = 0.125 mg, but without leading zero the magnitude is less obvious; safer to stay in mg for digoxin.  </p></br><p>c. Digoxin .125 mg PO daily<br /></br>• Missing leading zero. “.125” can be read as 125 mg → fatal.  </p></br><p>d. Digoxin 125 mcg PO qd<br /></br>• “mcg” is acceptable, but “qd” is not.  </p></br><p>e. Digoxin 125.0 mcg PO daily<br /></br>• Trailing zero after decimal → can be misread as 1250 mcg.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom line: On NBME/USMLE, the safest prescription for any dose &lt;1 unit is “0.x (unit)”, avoid trailing zeros, avoid dangerous abbreviations.</p>
161. A 37-year-old woman comes to the emergency department 12 hours after the onset of severe epigastric pain that radiates to her back. Two weeks ago, abdominal ultrasonography showed gallstones, and she began a low-fat diet. She has no other history of serious illness. On arrival, her temperature is 37.9 C 100.2  F, pulse is 130/min, respirations are 28/min and shallow, and blood pressure is 95/60 mm Hg. Abdominal examination shows distention and ecchymoses over the periumbilical area. There is diffuse severe tenderness of all quadrants. Bowel sounds are absent. Serum studies show a total bilirubin concentration of 1 mg/dl, amylase activity of 1400 U/L, and lipase activity of 950 U/L N=14-280. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. 24-Hour urine collection for measurement of amylase activity</br>b. CT scan of the abdomen with contrast</br>c. MRI of the abdomen</br>d. Endoscopic retrograde cholangiopancreatography</br>e. Paracentesis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. CT scan of the abdomen with IV contrast</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Severe epigastric pain radiating to the back,” serum amylase 1400 U/L and lipase 950 U/L (&gt;3× upper limit) → classic for acute pancreatitis.<br /></br>• Periumbilical ecchymosis (Cullen sign) + hypotension, tachycardia, fever, absent bowel sounds → suggests severe/necrotizing pancreatitis with possible retroperitoneal hemorrhage.<br /></br>• Goal now is NOT to establish the diagnosis (already met by pain + enzymes) but to determine severity and detect complications (necrosis, hemorrhage, pseudocyst, abscess) to guide management.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process<br /></br>1. Diagnostic criteria met: pain + enzyme elevation → no further biochemical testing required.<br /></br>2. Hemodynamic instability + peritoneal signs + Cullen sign = high-risk/severe disease.<br /></br>3. Imaging of choice to stage severity, detect necrosis/hemorrhage, and direct possible intervention is a contrast-enhanced CT of the abdomen.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Once pancreatitis is clinically and biochemically diagnosed, the “next step in diagnosis” in a patient with severe features is contrast CT to evaluate complications.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield points)<br /></br>• Gallstones (most common cause in U.S.) → transient obstruction of ampulla → premature pancreatic enzyme activation → autodigestion.<br /></br>• Severe inflammation may lead to:<br /></br>  – Hemorrhage → Cullen (periumbilical) &amp; Grey-Turner (flank) signs.<br /></br>  – Pancreatic necrosis, abscess, pseudocyst, ARDS, hypocalcemia, shock.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Management &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Initial supportive care: Aggressive IV fluids (LR), pain control, NPO, oxygen.<br /></br>Imaging:<br /></br>• CT abdomen with IV contrast (best test ≥48 h from onset or immediately in severe cases) reveals extent of necrosis, hemorrhage, peripancreatic fluid.<br /></br>• Ultrasound later to look for gallstones once patient stabilizes.<br /></br>ERCP only if there is cholangitis or persistent biliary obstruction.<br /></br>Antibiotics only for infected necrosis; otherwise not indicated.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Quick Memory Tips<br /></br>“PANCREAS 2-3”:<br /></br>P-ain radiating to back<br /></br>A-mylase/Lipase &gt;3× nl<br /></br>N-ecrosis feared → Need Contrast CT<br /></br>C-ullen &amp; Grey-Turner = hemorrhage<br /></br>R-inger’s lactate = fluid of choice<br /></br>E-RC P only if obstructed<br /></br>A-ntibiotics only if infected necrosis<br /></br>S-everity scores (Ranson, BISAP)  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. 24-hour urine amylase: Rarely used; adds no information once serum enzymes are diagnostic.<br /></br>c. MRI abdomen: Good for biliary/pancreatic duct detail but CT is faster, cheaper, and preferred first-line for acute complications.<br /></br>d. ERCP: Invasive; reserved for gallstone pancreatitis with cholangitis or dilated CBD/obstruction, not for initial evaluation.<br /></br>e. Paracentesis: Appropriate for ascites or suspected SBP, not for assessing acute pancreatitis complications in retroperitoneum.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Severe gallstone pancreatitis with signs of hemorrhage demands immediate contrast-enhanced CT to stage disease and look for necrosis or fluid collections that will guide further management.</p>
162. A 72-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 3-month history of progressive shortness of breath with exertion and nonproductive cough. She has systemic sclerosis scleroderma treated with lisinopril and prednisone. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 90/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 130/80 mm Hg. Examination shows tight facial skin and telangiectasias over the cheeks. There is thick, taut skin over the fingers. Bilateral basilar crackles are heard. On cardiac examination, a right ventricular heave is palpated at the left sternal border where a grade 2/6, systolic ejection murmur is also heard best. A fixed, split S 2 and an S 3 gallop are heard. An ECG is shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Aortic stenosis</br>b. Cor pulmonale</br>c. Left ventricular hypertrophy</br>d. Pulmonary edema</br>e. Ventricular septal defect</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Cor pulmonale  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Systemic sclerosis (scleroderma)” → well-known risk factor for pulmonary arterial hypertension (PAH) and interstitial lung disease.<br /></br>• “3-month history of progressive dyspnea + non-productive cough” → pulmonary process, not cardiogenic fluid overload.<br /></br>• “Bilateral basilar crackles” + “right-ventricular heave, grade 2/6 systolic ejection murmur at LSB, fixed split S2” → classic constellation of pulmonary hypertension with resultant RV pressure overload.<br /></br>    – Systolic ejection murmur at LUSB = flow murmur across a normal pulmonic valve due to high velocity flow from elevated PA pressure (NOT aortic stenosis, which is at RUSB and radiates to carotids).<br /></br>    – Fixed, split S2 = delayed closure of the pulmonic valve from high PA pressure.<br /></br>• “S3 gallop” palpable on the right side of the sternum → RV volume overload/failure.<br /></br>• ECG (not shown) in PAH/cor pulmonale usually demonstrates right-axis deviation, P pulmonale (peaked P waves in II), or RV strain pattern (R/S &gt;1 in V1, T-wave inversion in V1–V3).  </p></br><p>Putting these together → isolated right-sided heart failure due to pulmonary hypertension = cor pulmonale.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify systemic sclerosis → think lung complications (interstitial fibrosis &amp; PAH).<br /></br>2. Dyspnea + basilar crackles → pulmonary pathology present.<br /></br>3. Physical exam: RV heave + pulmonic flow murmur + fixed split S2 → elevated pulmonary artery pressure.<br /></br>4. S3 on right → RV failure.<br /></br>5. No left-sided findings (no carotid radiation, no displaced PMI) → rules against left-sided valvular disease.<br /></br>⇒ Diagnosis = cor pulmonale (right-sided heart failure 2° pulmonary disease).  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Pulmonary hypertension from connective-tissue disorders can progress to cor pulmonale, producing a flow murmur, fixed split S2, and signs of RV failure.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Systemic sclerosis → vascular endothelial dysfunction → intimal proliferation &amp; fibrosis in pulmonary arterioles → ↑ pulmonary vascular resistance → pulmonary arterial hypertension → RV concentric hypertrophy → eventual dilatation &amp; failure (cor pulmonale).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment (High-yield)<br /></br>Diagnostics<br /></br>• Echocardiogram: estimates PA systolic pressure, RV hypertrophy/dysfunction.<br /></br>• Right-heart cath (gold standard) confirms mPAP ≥25 mmHg with normal PCWP (&lt;15 mmHg).<br /></br>• PFTs: ↓ DLCO, often restrictive pattern if concomitant interstitial lung disease.  </p></br><p>Treatment<br /></br>1. Oxygen &amp; diuretics for symptom relief of right HF (e.g., furosemide).<br /></br>2. PAH-specific therapy if WHO group 1 features: endothelin-receptor antagonist (bosentan/ambrisentan), PDE-5 inhibitor (sildenafil), or prostacyclin analog (epoprostenol, treprostinil).<br /></br>3. Treat underlying scleroderma lung disease (immunosuppressants like mycophenolate for ILD component).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CREST causes PRESSed pulm vessels”<br /></br>CREST/limited cutaneous scleroderma → Pulmonary vascular RESistance ↑ → RV PRESSure overload → cor Pulmonale.  </p></br><p>Think “Fixed split S2 = P2 lagging” → Delayed P2 in Pulmonary hypertension.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Answers Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Aortic stenosis<br /></br>• Murmur is harsh crescendo-decrescendo at right 2nd intercostal space, radiates to carotids, associated with pulsus parvus et tardus. None of those exam features are present; split S2 would be paradoxical (not fixed).  </p></br><p>c. Left ventricular hypertrophy<br /></br>• Produces displaced, sustained PMI, S4, apical heave, NOT a right sternal border heave or fixed split S2. Dyspnea would stem from diastolic heart failure, not pulmonary vascular disease.  </p></br><p>d. Pulmonary edema<br /></br>• Would cause bibasilar crackles PLUS pink frothy sputum, orthopnea, bilateral infiltrates on CXR, and a left-sided S3 gallop from LV failure. No RV heave or fixed split S2.  </p></br><p>e. Ventricular septal defect<br /></br>• Classically a holosystolic (not ejection) murmur at LSB, often with palpable thrill; fixed split S2 only with large L→R shunts in childhood, not acquired in a 72-year-old.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In a patient with systemic sclerosis who develops progressive exertional dyspnea, a pulmonic flow murmur, fixed split S2, and right-sided S3, think pulmonary arterial hypertension leading to cor pulmonale.</p>
163. A 16-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her mother because she has not yet had a menstrual period. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Her immunizations are up-to-date. She does not appear to be in acute distress. She is at the 75th percentile for height, 80th percentile for weight, and 55th percentile for BMI. Breast and pubic hair development are sexual maturity rating stage 1. Pelvic examination shows no abnormalities. Serum studies show a thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration of 2.4 μU/ml, follicle-stimulating hormone concentration of 76 mlU/mL, and prolactin concentration of 13 ng/ml. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Karyotype analysis</br>b. Measurement of serum estradiol concentration</br>c. MRI of the brain</br>d. 12-Day course of medroxyprogesterone therapy</br>e. EEG</br>F. No further evaluation is indicated at this time</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. Karyotype analysis  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 16-year-old with primary amenorrhea (no menses by age 15).<br /></br>• Sexual-maturity rating (SMR) stage 1 breasts &amp; pubic hair → no estrogen effect.<br /></br>• FSH = 76 mIU/mL (markedly elevated) → hyper-gonadotropic hypogonadism (gonads failing, pituitary working).<br /></br>• Normal TSH &amp; prolactin → rules out thyroid disease and prolactinoma.<br /></br>Key pattern: “Primary amenorrhea + absent secondary sexual characteristics + ↑FSH” → think gonadal dysgenesis (e.g., Turner 45,X or 46,XY Swyer) → next diagnostic step = karyotype.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Determine if secondary sexual characteristics are present.<br /></br>   – Absent → problem is before/at gonad.<br /></br>2. Check gonadotropins.<br /></br>   – High FSH/LH → ovaries not responding (primary ovarian failure).<br /></br>3. Primary ovarian failure before puberty = gonadal dysgenesis; must know chromosomal etiology → order karyotype.</p></br><p>Take-home concept: In primary amenorrhea, elevated FSH points to ovarian dysgenesis, and the very next move is chromosome analysis to identify Turner or other sex-chromosome abnormalities.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Turner syndrome (45,X) is the most common cause of streak gonads → no estradiol → no breast development; feedback loss → ↑FSH/LH.<br /></br>• 46,XY complete gonadal dysgenesis (Swyer) presents similarly but with risk of gonadoblastoma.<br /></br>Finding the karyotype guides counseling, screening (e.g., aortic coarctation in Turner), and management (possible prophylactic gonadectomy in 46,XY).</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment Highlights<br /></br>• Karyotype: differentiates 45,X, mosaicism, 46,XY, or 46,XX with gene mutations.<br /></br>• Further work-up then tailored: Echo, renal US in Turner; prophylactic bilateral gonadectomy in 46,XY.<br /></br>• Hormone replacement (estrogen ± progesterone) for secondary sex characteristics and bone health once diagnosis confirmed.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“High FSH at FIRST visit?  FIRST test = chromosomes.”<br /></br>(“High F-S-H → Find the Sex (chromosome) Hap.”)</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>B. Serum estradiol – Would be low, but we already know that from absent breasts &amp; high FSH; adds no new decisive information.<br /></br>C. MRI brain – Used when FSH/LH are low/normal (hypogonadotropic hypogonadism). Here they are high.<br /></br>D. Medroxyprogesterone withdrawal test – Requires prior estrogen priming (breast development, endometrial buildup). Not useful in an estrogen-deficient patient.<br /></br>E. EEG – No relevance; amenorrhea is not a seizure disorder.<br /></br>F. No further evaluation – Incorrect; unexplained primary amenorrhea with lab abnormalities mandates work-up.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Primary amenorrhea + no pubertal development + ↑FSH → suspect gonadal dysgenesis; order a karyotype to determine the exact chromosomal abnormality and plan management.</p>
164. In a recent study of Americans, the following data were presented:</br>Group: Mortality Rate From Firearms per 100,000</br>Native – 2.2</br>African – 1.2</br>Caucasian – 0.8</br>Asian – 0.1</br>The authors concluded that, in the USA, a greater number of Native Americans died of injuries sustained from firearms than did Caucasian Americans. Which of the following is the most accurate interpretation of the</br>authors' conclusion?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Correct</br>b. Incorrect, because of the cohort effect</br>c. Incorrect, because the conclusion is based on rates</br>d. Incorrect, because the conclusion is not adjusted for socioeconomic factors</br>e. Incorrect, because the conclusion is not adjusted for urban/rural differences</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c.  Incorrect, because the authors drew a conclusion about absolute numbers even though only rates were provided.</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key data: firearm-mortality rates per 100 000 – Native 2.2, African 1.2, Caucasian 0.8, Asian 0.1.<br /></br>• Phrase to scrutinize: “a greater number of Native Americans died … than did Caucasian Americans.”<br /></br>• Flag: The table gives rates, not counts. Without knowing the size of each population, you cannot infer total deaths.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Epi version)<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Identify what the table measures → incidence (deaths/100 000).<br /></br>2. Identify what the authors claim → absolute numbers.<br /></br>3. Ask: Do we have the denominator sizes? → No.<br /></br>4. Conclusion → Claim is unsupported; the higher rate in Natives does not guarantee a higher total count because Caucasians make up a much larger share of the U.S. population (~60% vs ~1.3%).<br /></br>Core takeaway: Never convert rates to absolute numbers without population denominators.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (contextual)<br /></br>Not required here, but remember: Rates help compare risk; absolute numbers describe public-health burden.</p></br><p>Diagnostic / Statistical Tests Review<br /></br>To support the authors’ claim correctly, they would need:<br /></br>• Population size of each group (U.S. Census data).<br /></br>• Then calculate absolute deaths = rate × population / 100 000.<br /></br>Example:<br /></br>‒ Native Americans ~3 million → 2.2/100 000 ≈ 66 deaths.<br /></br>‒ Caucasians ~200 million → 0.8/100 000 ≈ 1 600 deaths.<br /></br>Thus, the burden is actually far higher in Caucasians.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“R for Rate = Risk per unit; N for Number = Needs the denominator.”<br /></br>Visualize two bars: a skinny but very tall bar (rate) vs a wide but short bar (population); product of both gives the area (absolute deaths).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. “Correct” – Wrong. Higher rate ≠ higher count when the population sizes differ dramatically.<br /></br>b. “Incorrect because of the cohort effect” – Cohort effect refers to differences between birth cohorts over time; irrelevant here.<br /></br>d. “Not adjusted for socioeconomic factors” – Could influence rates but doesn’t explain the logical mismatch between rates and counts.<br /></br>e. “Not adjusted for urban/rural differences” – Similar to (d); adjustment issues don’t justify equating rate with number.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Students often equate “highest rate” with “most cases.” Always check whether the question is asking about risk (rate) or burden (absolute number).</p>
165. An 8-year-old girl with attention-deficit/hyperactivity disorder is brought to the office by her mother for a well-child examination. The mother says that her daughter has been generally healthy and has been doing well in school since beginning long-acting methylphenidate therapy 2 years ago. The mother is concerned about her daughter having to take the medication for an extended period of time. Medical history is otherwise unremarkable and she takes no other medications. The patient is at the 25th percentile for height and weight. Vital signs are normal. Physical examination discloses no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate response to the mother regarding this patient's need for medication?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Her current medication regimen will likely continue to benefit her into adulthood</br>b. She will likely need increased doses of the medication during adulthood</br>c. She will likely need to switch to a different medication as she approaches adulthood</br>d. She will outgrow the need for medication during late adolescence</br></br></br><p>Correct answer<br /></br>A. “Her current medication regimen will likely continue to benefit her into adulthood.”</p></br><p>──────────────────────────</br>Vignette analysis</br>• 8-year-old girl with well-documented ADHD → diagnosis is established.<br /></br>• Has taken long-acting methylphenidate for 2 years with good academic and behavioral response → confirms efficacy and tolerability.<br /></br>• Mother’s question: “Will she have to stay on medicine for a long time?” → test is really about the natural history of ADHD and long-term pharmacotherapy.</p></br><p>Key clue</br>• ADHD commonly persists into adolescence and adulthood (~50-70 % of cases); pharmacologic treatment therefore often remains beneficial beyond childhood.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────</br>Clinical critical reasoning</br>Step 1  Determine if ADHD symptoms abate with age.<br /></br> – Most children do NOT “outgrow” ADHD; many retain either the full syndrome or clinically significant residual symptoms.</p></br><p>Step 2  Decide if current medication class remains first-line long-term.<br /></br> – Stimulants (methylphenidate, amphetamines) remain first-line for both adolescents and adults.<br /></br> – No evidence that patients must switch classes or routinely escalate to high doses because of tolerance.</p></br><p>Take-home message: ADHD often continues into adulthood, and the same stimulant that works in childhood will usually remain beneficial long term.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology review (high-yield)</br>• Core deficit: impaired regulation of dopaminergic/noradrenergic signaling in prefrontal cortex → inattention, impulsivity, hyperactivity.<br /></br>• Persistence: Brain maturation lags ≈3–5 years; structural and functional differences persist into adulthood, explaining chronic course in many patients.</p></br><p>Risk factors for persistence</br>• Combined-type ADHD, ≥1 psychiatric comorbidity, family history, more severe baseline symptoms.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; monitoring pearls</br>First-line in children AND adults<br /></br>• Long-acting stimulants: methylphenidate (Concerta, Ritalin LA), lisdexamfetamine, mixed amphetamine salts.  </p></br><p>Dosing changes over time<br /></br>• Adjust to body weight and symptom control; no systematic “tolerance” requiring large dose escalations.<br /></br>• Monitor growth (height/weight), BP, HR, sleep, and appetite at every visit.</p></br><p>When to consider switching<br /></br>• Lack of efficacy, intolerable side effects, or coexisting substance-use disorder (then use non-stimulants: atomoxetine, guanfacine ER, bupropion).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────</br>Memory aid</br>“ADHD likes company” → It often STAYS with you:<br /></br> S  – Stimulants stay first-line<br /></br> T  – Treatment continues<br /></br> A  – Adult persistence common<br /></br> Y  – You usually don’t need to raise the dose drastically</p></br><p>──────────────────────────</br>Why the other choices are wrong</br>B. “She will likely need increased doses of the medication during adulthood.”<br /></br>• Routine upward titration for tolerance is uncommon; dose is individualized, sometimes even reduced as demands change.</p></br><p>C. “She will likely need to switch to a different medication as she approaches adulthood.”<br /></br>• Stimulants remain first-line through adulthood; switching is only needed for side-effects or inadequate response, not simply for age.</p></br><p>D. “She will outgrow the need for medication during late adolescence.”<br /></br>• Approximately half continue to meet full ADHD criteria and many more have residual symptoms. Empiric discontinuation often leads to relapse.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────</br>Exam-level takeaway</br>ADHD frequently persists into adulthood; the same stimulant that is effective in childhood will usually continue to be effective, so reassure parents that long-term therapy is often necessary and beneficial.</p>
166. A 68-year-old man comes to the emergency department because of a 1-week history of severe low back pain that worsens with movement. He also has a 2-month history of fatigue and decreased appetite resulting in a 7- kg 15-lb weight loss. He has not had fever, night sweats, bowel incontinence, urinary retention, or weakness. He has hypertension treated with hydrochlorothiazide. He smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 30 years but quit 20 years ago. He does not drink alcohol or use illicit drugs. He is 178 cm 5 ft 10 in tall and weighs 77 kg 170 lb; BMI is 24 kg/m 2. Vital signs are within normal limits. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 99 percent. There is tenderness to palpation at L2-4. Muscle strength is 5/5 in the lower extremities. Deep tendon reflexes are normal at the knees and ankles; there is no clonus. Rectal tone is norma l. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Results of laboratory studies are shown: Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in evaluation?</br>Hemoglobin: 11.2 g/dL</br>Serum</br>Urea nitrogen: 54 mg/dL</br>Creatinine: 2.1 mg/dL</br>Protein, total: 7.8 g/dL</br>Albumin: 3.2 g/dL</br>Alkaline phosphatase: 67 U/L	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. CT scan of the chest</br>b. MRI of the spine</br>c. Renal ultrasonography</br>d. Serum and urine protein electrophoresis</br>e. Serum iron and ferritin concentrations</br>F. Serum parathyroid hormone concentration</br>g. Serum prostate-specific antigen concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Serum and urine protein electrophoresis (SPEP + UPEP)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues</br>• “68-year-old man” → malignancy &amp; myeloma age group.<br /></br>• “Severe low-back pain” + point tenderness at L2-L4 → vertebral bone disease (fracture, lytic lesion, or metastasis).<br /></br>• Constitutional symptoms: 2-month fatigue, ↓appetite, 7-kg weight loss → cancer red flag.<br /></br>• Laboratory triad suggestive of myeloma:</br>  – Normocytic anemia: Hgb 11.2 g/dL.<br /></br>  – Renal insufficiency: BUN 54, Cr 2.1 mg/dL.<br /></br>  – “Globulin gap”: Total protein 7.8 – Albumin 3.2 = 4.6 g/dL (elevated).<br /></br>• Normal alkaline phosphatase (67 U/L) → points away from osteoblastic metastases; typical of purely lytic lesions such as multiple myeloma.<br /></br>Pattern: “CRAB” (hyperCalcemia, Renal failure, Anemia, Bone pain) → classic screening step is SPEP/UPEP to look for an M-protein.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-by-Step)</br>1. Back pain + weight loss → think malignancy.</br>2. Decide between metastatic solid tumor vs plasma-cell dyscrasia.</br>3. Clues favoring myeloma over metastatic carcinoma:</br>   – Elevated globulin gap without marked ↑ALP.<br /></br>   – Renal failure out of proportion to degree of dehydration/HTN.<br /></br>   – Age ≥65.<br /></br>4. First diagnostic test when myeloma is suspected = SPEP ± UPEP (high-yield USMLE fact).</p></br><p>Core takeaway: For an older adult with anemia, renal dysfunction, bone pain, and an elevated globulin gap, always screen for multiple myeloma with serum AND urine protein electrophoresis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Brief Pathophysiology Review</br>• Multiple myeloma = clonal proliferation of plasma cells producing monoclonal immunoglobulin (M-protein) or light chains.</br>• CRAB manifestations:</br>  C – hyperCalcemia from osteoclast-activating factors.<br /></br>  R – Renal damage from light-chain cast nephropathy.<br /></br>  A – Anemia from marrow infiltration and renal EPO deficiency.<br /></br>  B – Bone pain/lytic lesions → vertebral compression fractures; ALP usually normal because lesions are purely osteolytic (no osteoblastic response).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostics &amp; Management Snapshot</br>1. Screening: SPEP, UPEP, serum free-light-chain assay.<br /></br>2. Confirmatory: Bone-marrow biopsy ≥10 % clonal plasma cells, skeletal survey or whole-body low-dose CT.<br /></br>3. Initial treatment (symptomatic MM):<br /></br>   • Proteasome inhibitor (e.g., bortezomib) + immunomodulator (lenalidomide) + steroid.<br /></br>   • Bisphosphonate (zoledronic acid) for bone disease.<br /></br>   • Autologous stem-cell transplant in eligible patients.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>CRAB = Calcium, Renal, Anemia, Bone pain → think Myeloma, order SPEP + UPEP.<br /></br>“ALP stays Low in Lytic myeLoma” – helps distinguish from osteoblastic metastases (prostate, breast) where ALP is high.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Options Are Incorrect</br>a. CT scan of the chest<br /></br>   • Would evaluate for lung cancer metastasis, but lung mets usually raise ALP and do NOT cause globulin gap or renal failure by light-chains. First screen for myeloma with simple blood/urine studies.</p></br><p>b. MRI of the spine<br /></br>   • Excellent for detecting spinal lesions or cord compression; however, you must establish the underlying diagnosis first. No neurologic deficits are present, so urgent MRI is not required yet.</p></br><p>c. Renal ultrasonography<br /></br>   • Useful for obstructive uropathy. Creatinine elevation here is more likely intrinsic (light-chain nephropathy). Ultrasound would not detect monoclonal protein disease.</p></br><p>e. Serum iron and ferritin concentrations<br /></br>   • Would evaluate iron-deficiency vs anemia of chronic disease; neither explains bone pain and renal failure with elevated globulin gap.</p></br><p>f. Serum parathyroid hormone (PTH) concentration<br /></br>   • Hyperparathyroidism causes bone pain and renal stones but not elevated globulin gap, anemia, or lytic vertebral lesions with normal ALP.</p></br><p>g. Serum prostate-specific antigen (PSA) concentration<br /></br>   • Prostate cancer metastases are osteoblastic → ↑ALP, not purely lytic, and do not cause elevated globulin gap or renal insufficiency from light chains.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>In an older patient with the CRAB constellation, the single best next step is serum and urine protein electrophoresis to look for a monoclonal spike—screening for multiple myeloma.</p>
167. Four days after undergoing uncomplicated left total hip replacement, a hospitalized 75-year-old man has a 1-day history of progressive confusion. He began ambulating on postoperative day 1 and previously had been alert, fully oriented, and cognitively intact. He has been receiving hydrocodone for pain as needed; he has requested one dose during the past 24 hours. He has hypertension well controlled with atenolol. He has no other history of serious illness and takes no other medications. On admission, the patient said he did not drink alcohol; today, his brother says that the patient drinks six to eight 12-oz beers daily. The patient's temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 90/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 130/80 mm Hg. Physical examination shows lateral gaze palsy. The surgical incision is clean, dry, and nonerythematous. Neurologic examination shows ataxia. On mental status examination, he is alert but not oriented to person, place, or time. He identifies only one of three objects after six attempts. He recalls zero of three objects after 5 minutes. Results of a complete blood count, measurement of serum electrolyte and glucose concentrations, and liver and renal function tests are within the reference ranges. Urine toxicology screening is negative. An ECG shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Administration of lorazepam</br>b. Administration of vitamin B 1 thiamine</br>c. Blood and urine cultures</br>d. CT scan of the head</br>e. Discontinuation of atenolol</br>F. Discontinuation of hydrocodone</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Administration of vitamin B1 (thiamine)</p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━</br>Vignette Analysis – “Red-flag” clues</br>1. “Progressive confusion,” “not oriented,” poor immediate &amp; short-term recall → acute encephalopathy.</br>2. “Lateral gaze palsy” (ophthalmoplegia) + “ataxia” → brain-stem/cerebellar involvement.</br>3. Brother reveals chronic alcohol use (6–8 beers/day) that the patient denied → high suspicion for nutritional deficiency.</br>4. Post-operative day 4 and labs (electrolytes, glucose, CBC, LFTs, renal) all normal → rules out metabolic, infectious, and medication-induced causes.</br>5. Classic triad: confusion + ophthalmoplegia + ataxia = Wernicke encephalopathy.</p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)</br>Step 1. Recognize the triad ⟶ think Wernicke.<br /></br>Step 2. Identify precipitant: peri-operative stress, poor intake, IV fluids with glucose will further deplete thiamine stores.<br /></br>Step 3. Immediate priority = replace thiamine IV.
