Neurologic damage is rapidly reversible early, permanent later (Korsakoff).<br /></br>Core takeaway: In any confused alcoholic (or malnourished) patient with eye findings and/or ataxia, give IV thiamine first, even before glucose.</p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Thiamine (vit B1) is a co-factor for pyruvate dehydrogenase, α-ketoglutarate dehydrogenase, transketolase.<br /></br>• Deficiency ⟶ impaired cerebral glucose utilization, local lactic acidosis, petechial hemorrhages in mammillary bodies, thalamus, periaqueductal gray.<br /></br>• Precipitants: chronic alcohol use, malnutrition, hyperemesis, bariatric surgery, dialysis, refeeding with glucose.</p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━</br>Treatment/Diagnostics</br>First-line: IV thiamine 100 mg immediately, then daily; ALWAYS precede any IV glucose.<br /></br>If hypoglycemia is suspected, give thiamine, then D50.<br /></br>No imaging or cultures are required to start treatment—delay risks irreversible damage.<br /></br>Monitor for improvement in eye movements &amp; mentation within hours-days; gait recovers last.  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━</br>Mnemonics / Memory Aids</br>“COAT” for Wernicke:<br /></br>C – Confusion<br /></br>O – Ophthalmoplegia<br /></br>A – Ataxia<br /></br>T – Thiamine (treat first!)</p></br><p>“Give the B1 before the D (dextrose).”</p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Lorazepam – Appropriate for alcohol withdrawal (tremors, agitation, tachycardia, HTN, hallucinations, seizures). This patient lacks autonomic hyperactivity and has focal neuro signs = Wernicke, not withdrawal.<br /></br>c. Blood/urine cultures – No fever, leukocytosis, wound infection signs; low likelihood of sepsis. Cultures delay definitive therapy.<br /></br>d. Head CT – Could rule out stroke/bleed, but classic Wernicke triad + normal vitals/labs makes CT a low-yield first step; treatment should not be delayed.<br /></br>e. Discontinue atenolol – Beta-blocker does not explain acute triad; vitals stable.<br /></br>f. Discontinue hydrocodone – Only one dose in 24 h; opioid intoxication presents with miosis &amp; hypoventilation, not ophthalmoplegia &amp; ataxia.</p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━</br>Bottom Line</br>Post-op confused alcoholic with ophthalmoplegia + ataxia = Wernicke encephalopathy. Give IV thiamine immediately—never wait for labs or imaging, and always before glucose.</p>
168. A 42-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 1-day history of increasingly severe pain of his right knee associated with redness and swelling for the past 6 hours. He has had two episodes of pain in his right great toe over the past 8 months and missed 2 weeks of work in construction during each episode. He has been otherwise healthy. Examination of the right knee shows marked tenderness, swelling, and erythema. Laboratory findings are within the reference range except for a serum uric acid concentration of 7.6 mg/dl. Examination of synovial fluid aspirate shows uric acid crystals. Which of the following is the most appropriate initial pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Allopurinol</br>b. Celecoxib</br>c. lndomethacin</br>d. Probenecid</br>e. Sulfinpyrazone</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>Allopurinol  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Recurrent attacks: two flares in the great toe during the last 8 months + a third, current flare of the knee.<br /></br>• Functional impairment: each prior attack cost him 2 weeks of work → meets the “≥2 attacks/yr or significant disability” threshold for starting chronic urate-lowering therapy (ULT).<br /></br>• Confirmed diagnosis: synovial fluid shows monosodium urate crystals; serum uric acid is elevated (7.6 mg/dL).<br /></br>Key takeaway: After ≥2 disabling gout attacks, the guidelines (ACR/ACP) recommend initiating long-term ULT, not just treating the flare. The first-line agent for lifelong ULT is a xanthine-oxidase inhibitor (allopurinol or febuxostat); allopurinol is preferred.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Verify that this is crystal-proven gout (it is).<br /></br>2. Decide whether to treat an acute flare or to institute chronic prophylaxis.<br /></br>   – Acute therapy is necessary now, but the question explicitly asks for “initial pharmacotherapy” after the diagnosis has been established in a patient with recurrent, disabling disease.<br /></br>3. Guideline trigger for ULT: ≥2 attacks/yr, tophi, CKD ≥ stage 2, uric-acid nephrolithiasis, or serum uric acid &gt; 9 mg/dL. The patient satisfies the first criterion.<br /></br>4. Choose the appropriate first-line ULT: allopurinol (start 100 mg daily and titrate).  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (very targeted)<br /></br>• Purines → xanthine → uric acid via xanthine oxidase. Allopurinol (a structural analog of hypoxanthine) competitively inhibits xanthine oxidase, lowering production of uric acid.<br /></br>• Reducing the total urate pool diminishes crystal formation, preventing future attacks and allowing existing tophi to regress.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Allopurinol is started concurrently with an anti-inflammatory flare prophylaxis (low-dose colchicine or NSAID) for the first 3–6 months to blunt the risk of paradoxical flare when serum urate begins to fall.<br /></br>• Target serum urate &lt; 6 mg/dL (&lt; 5 mg/dL if severe). Titrate the dose every 2–5 weeks based on urate levels.<br /></br>• Renal dosing: reduce initial dose if eGFR &lt; 60 mL/min/1.73 m².<br /></br>• Screen for HLA-B*58:01 in Han Chinese, Thai, or Korean patients with CKD before prescribing (risk of severe cutaneous reaction).  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“2 Strikes → Lower the Spikes.”<br /></br>• ≥2 gout strikes (attacks) in a year = start urate-lowering therapy.<br /></br>“AlloPURinol PURges PURines” – inhibits PURine-to-urate conversion.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Celecoxib – COX-2 selective NSAID useful for acute pain when traditional NSAIDs are contraindicated (e.g., peptic ulcer, anticoagulation). Treats a flare but does NOT prevent future attacks; does not meet the long-term management goal.<br /></br>c. Indomethacin – Classic first-line NSAID for an acute gout flare. Again, suppresses current inflammation only; falls short of the prophylactic requirement prompted by recurrent disability.<br /></br>d. Probenecid – Uricosuric (blocks URAT1 in the proximal tubule) useful in under-excretors with normal renal function and no history of nephrolithiasis. It increases urinary uric acid, risking stones and is second-line to allopurinol as initial ULT.<br /></br>e. Sulfinpyrazone – Another uricosuric with the same limitations as probenecid; also rarely used because of marrow suppression and GI toxicity.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Students often reflexively reach for indomethacin whenever they see “painful, swollen joint + urate crystals.” Always pause to see if the stem is asking about acute management (pain control) or chronic/preventive management. The trigger words “recurrent,” “disabling,” or “≥2 attacks” push you toward urate-lowering therapy—most commonly allopurinol.</p>
169. A 14-month-old girl is brought to the physician by her father because of a 3-week history of cough. One month ago, she had the onset of nasal congestion and runny nose. Two days later, she was noted to have a cough. The nasal congestion and runny nose resolved spontaneously after 7 days; the cough has not improved. Her father describes the cough as a dry, smoker's cough. The patient has had similar symptoms twice durin the past 6 months. Each episode resolved spontaneously after 2 weeks. At the age of 3 months, she had bronchiolitis. She has eczema treated with topical hydrocortisone. She was born at term following an uncomplicated pregnancy and spontaneous vaginal delivery. She receives no other medications. Her mother has a childhood history of asthma, and her father has seasonal allergies. The patient attends day care 5 days weekly. She coughs intermittently in the examination room. She is at the 20th percentile for length and 10th percentile for weight. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 72/min, respirations are 22/min, and blood pressure is 103/72 mm Hg. The lungs are clear to auscultation. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Asthma</br>b. Chronic bronchitis</br>c. Cystic fibrosis</br>d. Hypersensitivity pneumonitis</br>e. lgA immunodeficiency</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Asthma  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age: 14-month-old. Asthma symptoms often begin in infancy/early childhood.<br /></br>• Preceding URI: Cough started 2 days after a “cold” and persists after the URI resolved → classic viral trigger for asthma.<br /></br>• Recurrent, self-limited episodes: 3 episodes in 6 mo, each lasting ≈2 wk, compatible with intermittent asthma.<br /></br>• Atopic background: Personal eczema + mother with asthma + father with allergic rhinitis = strong atopic diathesis.<br /></br>• Cough description: Dry, “smoker’s” cough; cough-variant asthma frequently presents with an isolated, non-productive cough, sometimes without audible wheeze.<br /></br>• Normal lung exam between episodes: Asthma patients are often completely normal when not actively bronchospastic.  </p></br><p>Key pattern: Infant/child with atopy + recurrent cough after viral URIs + normal inter-episode exam → think cough-variant asthma.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify that the acute URI has already resolved (no fever, congestion gone).<br /></br>2. Persistent cough &gt;2 wk with prior similar episodes suggests a reactive airway process, not infection.<br /></br>3. Personal and family atopy make asthma vastly more likely than other causes in this age group.<br /></br>4. Clear lungs today rules out pneumonia or chronic suppurative disease but fits asthma in its quiescent phase.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In toddlers, isolated recurrent “post-viral” cough in an atopic child is most commonly cough-variant asthma.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Asthma = chronic, reversible airway inflammation → bronchial hyper-responsiveness.<br /></br>• Viral URIs, allergens, cold air, exercise trigger IgE-mediated mast-cell activation → bronchoconstriction &amp; mucus production → cough, wheeze.<br /></br>• Infants may manifest only cough because small airways generate minimal wheeze audible on exam.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls<br /></br>Diagnosis<br /></br>• Often clinical in this age: episodic symptoms + atopy + response to bronchodilator trial.<br /></br>• If uncertain: bedside albuterol or nebulized short-acting β2-agonist; improvement in cough supports diagnosis.<br /></br>Treatment (intermittent asthma)<br /></br>1. Rescue: Inhaled albuterol PRN.<br /></br>2. Controller: If frequent episodes or nighttime symptoms → add low-dose inhaled corticosteroid (eg, budesonide, fluticasone).<br /></br>3. Parent education: Avoid triggers, give inhaler with spacer/ mask.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>ATOPIC TRIAD = “EAA” → Eczema, Allergies, Asthma.<br /></br>Cough-variant asthma mnemonic: “C-O-U-G-H”<br /></br>C = Cold (viral) trigger<br /></br>O = Occurs Off and on<br /></br>U = URI precedes it<br /></br>G = Goes away with albuterol<br /></br>H = History of atopy  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Chronic bronchitis<br /></br>• Diagnostic criterion: productive cough ≥3 mo each of 2 consecutive years; rare in infants; requires chronic irritant (smoke) exposure.  </p></br><p>c. Cystic Fibrosis<br /></br>• Recurrent bacterial (not purely viral) infections, productive cough, failure to thrive, malabsorption; would expect weight &lt;5th %, steatorrhea, crackles. Sweat test diagnostic.  </p></br><p>d. Hypersensitivity pneumonitis<br /></br>• Presents with acute dyspnea, fever, diffuse crackles hours after antigen (moldy hay, birds); interstitial CXR changes; not episodic isolated cough in toddler.  </p></br><p>e. IgA immunodeficiency<br /></br>• Recurrent sinopulmonary &amp; GI infections (Giardia) beginning in late infancy; bacterial infections, otitis media; no atopic background alone; would not resolve spontaneously in 2 wk cycles.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line:<br /></br>In a toddler with eczema and atopic family history, recurrent post-viral dry cough with normal lung exam strongly indicates cough-variant asthma.</p>
170. A previously healthy 24-year-old nurse is brought to the emergency department because of weakness of the lower extremities for 24 hours. She takes no medications. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 100/min, and blood pressure is 112/78 mm Hg. Muscle strength is 4/5 in the upper and lower extremities. Laboratory studies show:</br>Serum</br>Na⁺: 134 mEq/L</br>Cl⁻: 94 mEq/L</br>K⁺: 2.4 mEq/L</br>HCO₃⁻: 34 mEq/L</br>Mg²⁺: 2.4 mEq/L</br>Urea nitrogen: 14 mg/dL</br>Glucose: 118 mg/dL</br>Urine</br>Na⁺: 46 mEq/L</br>K⁺: 64 mEq/L</br>Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's hypokalemia?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Aldosterone-secreting tumor</br>b. Hypomagnesemia</br>c. Juxtaglomerular cell hyperplasia</br>d. Surreptitious diuretic use</br>e. Surreptitious laxative use</br></br></br><p>⚠️  Note to user:  The reasoning below shows that “surreptitious diuretic use” (choice d) fits the laboratory profile and clinical presentation far better than “aldosterone-secreting tumor.”  If “aldosterone-secreting tumor” was supplied to you as the keyed answer, it is almost certainly a typo or keying error on the original source.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Correct answer (best supported by the vignette)</br>d. Surreptitious diuretic use</br>────────────────────────────────────────</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Young health-care worker (nurse) ⟶ profession with easy access to medications; raises suspicion for covert/self-administration.<br /></br>• Weakness + K⁺ 2.4 mEq/L ⟶ severe hypokalemia (classic for loop/thiazide diuretics).<br /></br>• HCO₃⁻ 34 mEq/L &amp; Cl⁻ 94 mEq/L ⟶ metabolic alkalosis with hypochloremia (“contraction alkalosis”).<br /></br>• Urine Na⁺ 46 mEq/L &amp; Urine K⁺ 64 mEq/L ⟶ kidneys are actively wasting both Na⁺ and K⁺.<br /></br>   – Vomiting/NG suction would give low U-Cl (&lt; 20).<br /></br>   – Diuretics or mineralocorticoid excess give high U-Cl (&gt; 20).<br /></br>• BP 112/78 (normal) ⟶ rules strongly against mineralocorticoid excess (Conn syndrome or renin-secreting tumor), which almost always produces hypertension.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)</br>1. Metabolic alkalosis?  Check urine chloride.<br /></br>   – High (&gt; 20) ⇒ diuretics vs mineralocorticoid excess.<br /></br>2. Evaluate volume status/BP.<br /></br>   – Normotensive/possibly slightly low-normal BP ⇒ diuretic use.<br /></br>   – Hypertensive &amp; expanded ECF ⇒ primary/secondary hyperaldosteronism.<br /></br>3. Contextual clue:  health professional access.<br /></br>4. Therefore surreptitious diuretic use is the most likely cause.</p></br><p>Core “take-home” concept</br>• Metabolic alkalosis + hypokalemia + high urine Cl⁻:<br /></br>   – Hypertension present → think mineralocorticoid excess.<br /></br>   – Hypertension absent → think diuretics (especially in a patient who could be abusing them).</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>Loop &amp; thiazide diuretics block Na⁺ reabsorption in the TAL or DCT → ↑ distal Na⁺ delivery → ↑ K⁺ &amp; H⁺ secretion in the collecting duct (via aldosterone-sensitive Na⁺ channel) → hypokalemic metabolic alkalosis.  Volume contraction maintains high aldosterone and stimulates renin, but overall BP may be normal or low because the diuretic effect outstrips Na⁺ retention.</p></br><p>Key Diagnostic Tests</br>Urinanalysis:</br>• High urine Na⁺ &amp; Cl⁻ despite metabolic alkalosis strongly suggest recent diuretic ingestion.<br /></br>• A urine diuretic screen (HPLC) confirms the diagnosis.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Rundown</br>a. Aldosterone-secreting tumor (Conn syndrome)<br /></br>   – Expect HTN, suppressed renin, metabolic alkalosis, low-normal serum Na⁺.  Normotensive patient makes this unlikely.</p></br><p>b. Hypomagnesemia<br /></br>   – Serum Mg²⁺ here is normal (2.4 mg/dL). Mg depletion causes refractory hypokalemia but would not give metabolic alkalosis with high U-Na⁺/Cl⁻ pattern.</p></br><p>c. Juxtaglomerular cell hyperplasia (renin-secreting tumor, “secondary hyperaldo”)<br /></br>   – Produces severe HTN with high renin and high aldosterone. BP does not fit.</p></br><p>e. Surreptitious laxative use<br /></br>   – Causes GI K⁺ loss → hypokalemia + non-anion-gap metabolic acidosis (bicarbonate loss in stool), NOT alkalosis.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“Low Cl⁻, Low BP, Lots of pee salt = LooP/Thiazide diuretics”<br /></br>Think: “Diuretic ⇒ Dilute urine &amp; Dropped pressure.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom line: Normal BP + metabolic alkalosis + high U-Cl in a young nurse = covert loop/thiazide diuretic use, not primary aldosteronism.</p>
171. A 47-year-old man comes to the office because of a 6-month history of a nonpainful, nonitchy lesion over his left forearm. Three years ago, he underwent a renal transplant for chronic renal failure secondary to lgA</br>nephropathy. His only medication is tacrolimus. Vital signs are within normal limits. A photograph of the lesion is shown. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely</br>diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Actinic keratosis</br>b. Contact dermatitis</br>c. Lichen plan us</br>d. Molluscum contagiosum</br>e. Squamous cell carcinoma</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>e. Squamous cell carcinoma (SCC)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues</br>• 47-year-old man with a chronic immunosuppressed state (renal transplant, tacrolimus).<br /></br>  – Transplant recipients have a 65- to 250-fold increased risk of cutaneous SCC due to reduced immune surveillance and long-term UV exposure.<br /></br>• 6-month history of a slowly growing, non-pruritic, non-tender lesion.<br /></br>• Location: dorsal forearm (sun-exposed site).<br /></br>• Photograph (not shown here) is expected to reveal a hyperkeratotic, scaly, indurated papule/nodule ± central ulceration—classic for SCC.<br /></br>Pattern: “Sun-exposed region + immunosuppressed + keratotic/scaly/ulcerated papule” → think SCC.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)</br>1. Is it inflammatory (itchy, acute)?  → No (rules out contact dermatitis, lichen planus).<br /></br>2. Is it viral &amp; umbilicated?  → No (rules out molluscum contagiosum).<br /></br>3. Is it premalignant (actinic keratosis) or frankly malignant (SCC)?<br /></br>   • 6-month growth and nodule formation suggest invasion beyond the epidermis → SCC.<br /></br>   • Immunosuppression skews toward SCC &gt; BCC.<br /></br>Take-home: Transplant patients = high-yield cohort for aggressive cutaneous SCC.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• UV-induced DNA damage (pyrimidine dimers) + impaired p53 repair → dysplasia of keratinocytes in the epidermis → invasion into dermis = SCC.<br /></br>• Risk factors: cumulative sun exposure, fair skin, chronic scars/burns, arsenic, and—most tested—immunosuppression (organ transplant, HIV, systemic steroids).<br /></br>• Presentation: firm, hyperkeratotic or ulcerated papule/plaque; may bleed when scraped.<br /></br>• Metastatic risk: low overall (≈5%), but markedly higher in transplant patients or lesions on lip/ear.</p></br><p>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment</br>• Full-thickness punch or excisional biopsy confirms diagnosis (look for invasive cords of atypical keratinocytes with keratin “pearls”).<br /></br>• Treatment:<br /></br>  – Low-risk lesions: standard excision (4-6 mm margins) or ED&amp;C (electrodesiccation &amp; curettage).<br /></br>  – High-risk lesions (size &gt;2 cm, high-risk site, immunosuppressed): Mohs micrographic surgery.<br /></br>  – Adjuvant: topical 5-FU, imiquimod, or radiation for non-surgical candidates.<br /></br>• Prevention: sun protection, routine dermatologic surveillance in transplant recipients.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“SCC = Sun, Scars, Cyclosporine/Tacrolimus (immunosuppression).”<br /></br>“Transplant = Trans-plant turns into Trans-planted tumor (SCC).”</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Actinic keratosis (AK)<br /></br>   • Premalignant; feels like “sandpaper,” &lt;1 cm macule/papule. Usually multiple. In transplant patients AK progress rapidly to SCC, but AKs are thinner, not nodular/ulcerated.  </p></br><p>b. Contact dermatitis<br /></br>   • Acute/ subacute itchy erythematous plaques with vesicles or lichenification; requires allergen exposure, pruritus—absent here.  </p></br><p>c. Lichen planus<br /></br>   • Pruritic, purple, polygonal, planar papules/plaques often on wrists/ankles; Wickham striae. Pruritus key, immune-mediated, no link to immunosuppression.  </p></br><p>d. Molluscum contagiosum<br /></br>   • Umbilicated, flesh-colored papules caused by poxvirus, common in HIV/children. Lesions are small, multiple, waxy—not scaly indurated plaques.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall</br>Students often pick actinic keratosis because of “sun-exposed scaly lesion.” Remember:<br /></br>• AK = thin, superficial, feels rough.<br /></br>• SCC = thick, indurated, may ulcerate/bleed and is more common/aggressive in immunosuppressed patients.</p></br><p>Core Takeaway</br>In an immunosuppressed (transplant) patient, any persistent hyperkeratotic lesion on sun-exposed skin should be presumed to be invasive squamous cell carcinoma until proven otherwise.</p>
172. rA. 62-year-old man with chronic obstructive pulmonary disease comes to the physician because of a 1-month history of leg swelling and progressive shortness of breath. He has not had fever or cough. He smoked one and one-half packs of cigarettes daily for 45 years but quit 1 year ago. His medications are theophylline and albuterol and ipratropium inhalers. His temperature is 36.7 C 98 F, pulse is 96/min, respirations are 22/min, and blood pressure is 150/76 mm Hg. Examination shows jugular venous distention 10 cm above the sternal angle. Breath sounds are markedly decreased throughout all lung fields. Cardiac examination shows decreased heart sounds with no murmurs, gallops, or rubs. The liver edge is palpated 3 cm below the right costal margin. There is 2+ pitting edema to the knees. His FEV 1 is 1.05 L 40 percent of predicted. Arterial blood gas analysis on room air shows:</br>pH: 7.42</br>PCO₂: 36 mm Hg</br>PO₂: 45 mm Hg</br>HCO₃⁻: 30 mEq/L</br>An x-ray of the chest shows hyperinflated lungs with apical bullae. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Polysomnography</br>b. Home oxygen therapy</br>c. Mechanical ventilation</br>d. Bullectomy</br>e. Lung volume reduction operation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Home oxygen therapy</p></br><hr /></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Long-standing COPD (FEV1 40 % predicted, hyperinflated lungs with apical bullae).<br /></br>• Progressive dyspnea, JVD, hepatomegaly and bilateral pitting edema → right-sided heart failure (cor pulmonale) from chronic hypoxic pulmonary vasoconstriction.<br /></br>• Arterial blood gas on room air: PaO2 45 mm Hg (severely low), PaCO2 36 mm Hg (near normal), HCO3- 30 mEq/L (renal compensation for chronic intermittent hypercapnia).<br /></br>• Key guideline trigger: PaO2 ≤ 55 mm Hg OR SaO2 ≤ 88 % at rest, or PaO2 55-59 mm Hg with cor pulmonale/polycythaemia → INDICATION for long-term supplemental oxygen.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify chronic hypoxemia (PaO2 45).<br /></br>2. Recognize end-organ sequelae (peripheral edema, JVD, enlarged liver = cor pulmonale).<br /></br>3. Apply evidence-based COPD management guidelines: long-term (&gt;15 h/day) ambulatory oxygen improves survival, quality of life, exercise tolerance and decreases pulmonary-artery pressure.<br /></br>Take-home message: In COPD, the ONLY therapy proven to prolong survival (besides smoking cessation) is continuous home oxygen in patients with severe resting hypoxemia.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Chronic alveolar destruction → V/Q mismatch → persistent hypoxemia → reflex pulmonary vasoconstriction → pulmonary HTN → right-sided heart failure (cor pulmonale). Renal compensation retains HCO3- for prior CO2 retention, explaining the elevated bicarbonate with near-normal PCO2.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>First-line:<br /></br>• Home oxygen ≥ 15 h/day; titrate to keep SaO2 ≥ 90 % (usually 1-3 L/min via nasal cannula).<br /></br>Adjuncts: inhaled bronchodilators (already on), vaccinations, pulmonary rehab, consideration of long-acting anticholinergics / LABA-ICS.<br /></br>Second-line / Advanced: lung volume reduction surgery or transplant in highly selected patients; bullectomy only for giant bullae causing compression.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“O2 ≤ 55 keeps you alive.”<br /></br>“55–59 needs a sign” → evidence of cor pulmonale or polycythaemia.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Polysomnography – Used to diagnose obstructive sleep apnea (snoring, daytime somnolence). Does not address persistent daytime PaO2 45 mm Hg; delaying oxygen would risk worsening cor pulmonale.<br /></br>c. Mechanical ventilation – Indicated for acute COPD exacerbation with severe acidosis or respiratory fatigue (PaCO2 markedly elevated, pH &lt; 7.25). Patient is stable, alert, and normocapnic.<br /></br>d. Bullectomy – Consider only when a single or few giant bullae (&gt; 30 % hemithorax) compress functional lung; chest X-ray here shows diffuse hyperinflation, not isolated massive bullae.<br /></br>e. Lung volume reduction surgery – Appropriate for selected emphysema patients with upper-lobe–predominant disease, FEV1 20-45 % and PaCO2 &lt; 60/PaO2 &gt; 45; survival benefit is far less than that of oxygen and requires optimization of medical therapy first.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Forgetting that resting hypoxemia itself is the trigger for oxygen therapy; additional tests or surgical referrals come only after this life-prolonging treatment is instituted.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Key Takeaway<br /></br>For COPD patients, chronic hypoxemia (PaO2 ≤ 55 mm Hg, or ≤ 59 mm Hg with cor pulmonale) mandates immediate initiation of long-term home oxygen—one of the few interventions proven to extend survival.</p>
173. A 21-year-old man is brought to the emergency department because of a 2-hour history of muscle spasms of the face and neck; his eyes appear to be rolling up inside his head. He began treatment with haloperidol 24 hours ago for paranoid ideation. His temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 96/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 130/82 mm Hg. He is fully oriented and cognitively intact, but he is adamant that he has been poisoned. Which of the following is most appropriate to add to his medication regimen?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Amitriptyline</br>b. Benztropine</br>c. Chlorpromazine</br>d. Flumazenil</br>e. Naloxone</br>F. Nifedipine</br>g. Physostigmine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Benztropine  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key time clue: “began treatment with haloperidol 24 hours ago.”<br /></br>• Key symptoms: “muscle spasms of the face and neck,” “eyes rolling up inside his head” (oculogyric crisis).<br /></br>• Normal vital signs, intact cognition → rules out malignant hyperthermia/NMS or delirium.<br /></br>Pattern: A young patient who has just started a high-potency typical antipsychotic and within hours develops sustained involuntary contractions = ACUTE DYSTONIA (the first extrapyramidal symptom to appear).</p></br><hr /></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process<br /></br>1. Identify the drug exposure: haloperidol (strong D2 blockade).<br /></br>2. Recall EPS timeline mnemonic “A DAPT”:<br /></br>   • A – Acute Dystonia (hours-days)<br /></br>   • A – Akathisia (days-weeks)<br /></br>   • P – Parkinsonism (weeks-months)<br /></br>   • T – Tardive dyskinesia (months-years)<br /></br>3. Match presentation: hours-day 1 + spasms → Acute dystonia.<br /></br>4. Acute dystonia mechanism = dopamine blockade → excess cholinergic tone in nigrostriatal pathway.<br /></br>5. Anticholinergic or antihistamine with strong anticholinergic property rapidly relieves symptoms. Benztropine (centrally acting antimuscarinic) is first-line.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Acute dystonia from antipsychotics is treated emergently with a centrally acting anticholinergic such as benztropine or IV/IM diphenhydramine.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Haloperidol blocks D2 receptors in mesolimbic (desired antipsychotic effect) and nigrostriatal tracts (undesired).<br /></br>• Nigrostriatal dopamine normally inhibits acetylcholine interneurons.<br /></br>• Blockade → ↓DA → relative ↑ACh → sustained firing of motor neurons → dystonic contractions (torticollis, trismus, oculogyric crisis).<br /></br>• Antimuscarinics re-balance by decreasing ACh activity.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics Review<br /></br>Acute dystonia management:<br /></br>1. Stop or lower offending antipsychotic dose (if possible).<br /></br>2. Give IM/IV benztropine 1–2 mg or diphenhydramine 25–50 mg; relief usually within minutes.<br /></br>3. Prevent recurrence: continue scheduled benztropine for several days or switch to lower-potency or atypical antipsychotic if psychosis control allows.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>• “Benztropine Beats (the) Bent neck” – visualize a bent neck snapping straight after benztropine.<br /></br>• EPS timeline “A DAPT” – you must ADAPT to antipsychotic side effects in chronological order.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Amitriptyline – TCA used for depression/neuropathic pain; strong anticholinergic but not utilized acutely IM/IV and has cardiotoxicity risk. Not standard, slower onset.<br /></br>c. Chlorpromazine – another typical antipsychotic; would worsen D2 blockade and dystonia.<br /></br>d. Flumazenil – competitive antagonist at GABA-A benzodiazepine site; used only for benzo overdose presenting with respiratory depression/coma.<br /></br>e. Naloxone – opioid antagonist for opioid toxicity (miosis, respiratory depression).<br /></br>f. Nifedipine – dihydropyridine CCB for hypertension, Raynaud, tocolysis; no role in EPS.<br /></br>g. Physostigmine – acetylcholinesterase inhibitor used for severe anticholinergic poisoning; would raise ACh and potentially exacerbate dystonia.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: Confusing physostigmine (↑ACh) with benztropine (↓ACh). Remember: EPS = too much cholinergic tone, so you need an ANTIcholinergic agent.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Key Takeaway<br /></br>Within hours to a day of starting a high-potency antipsychotic, new-onset focal muscle spasms (acute dystonia) should prompt immediate treatment with a centrally acting anticholinergic such as benztropine.</p>
174. A 60-year-old man with stage 4 chronic kidney disease comes to the office for a follow-up examination. He also has type 2 diabetes mellitus and hypertension. Medications are insulin, lisinopril, furosemide, amlodipine, and aspirin. Vital signs are temperature 37.0 C 98.6 F, pulse 80/min, respirations 15/min, and blood pressure 145/85 mm Hg. Cardiopulmona [Y examination discloses no abnormalities. There is 1+ lower extremity edema. Laboratory studies show a serum intact parathyroid hormone concentration of 258 pg/ml N=10-60. Measurement of additional serum laboratory studies is most likely to show which of the following?</br>Phosphorus Calcium Calcitriol	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Decreased decreased decreased</br>b. Decreased increased decreased</br>c. Decreased increased increased</br>d. Increased decreased decreased</br>e. Increased decreased increased</br>F. Increased increased increased</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Phosphorus ↑ / Calcium ↓ / Calcitriol (1,25-OH₂-vitamin D) ↓  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Stage 4 CKD (eGFR 15-29 mL/min) → classically produces secondary hyperparathyroidism.<br /></br>• PTH = 258 pg/mL (markedly ↑, normal 10-60) confirms secondary HPT.<br /></br>• Clues excluding other causes: no lithium, no neck surgery, elderly diabetic with long-standing CKD.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: CKD → phosphate excretion fails → serum PO₄³⁻ rises.<br /></br>Step 2: Phosphate binds ionized Ca²⁺ &amp; precipitates in tissues → serum Ca²⁺ falls.<br /></br>Step 3: Diseased kidneys cannot convert 25-OH-vitamin D to 1,25-OH₂-vitamin D (calcitriol) because 1-α-hydroxylase activity is reduced → intestinal Ca²⁺ absorption falls further.<br /></br>Step 4: Low Ca²⁺ + high phosphate + low calcitriol strongly stimulate parathyroid glands → ↑ PTH (secondary).  </p></br><p>Take-home: CKD = ↑ PO₄³⁻, ↓ Ca²⁺, ↓ calcitriol → secondary hyperparathyroidism.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Etiology: Nephron loss →<br /></br>• ↓ GFR → PO₄³⁻ retention.<br /></br>• ↓ 1-α-hydroxylase → less calcitriol.<br /></br>Resulting mineral changes stimulate diffuse parathyroid hyperplasia. Chronic PTH causes renal osteodystrophy (bone pain, fractures).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. Dietary phosphate restriction.<br /></br>2. Non-calcium phosphate binders (sevelamer, lanthanum).<br /></br>3. Active vitamin D analogs (calcitriol, doxercalciferol) or calcimimetics (cinacalcet) if PTH remains high.<br /></br>Lab monitoring: PO₄³⁻, Ca²⁺, 25-OH-D, PTH every 3-6 mo in stage 4-5 CKD.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CKD = 3 D’s”<br /></br>• Dead nephrons → Phosphate Deposition (↑ PO₄³⁻)<br /></br>• Deficient vitamin D activation (↓ calcitriol)<br /></br>• Depleted serum Ca²⁺ (↓ Ca²⁺)  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. ↓ PO₄³⁻ / ↓ Ca²⁺ / ↓ calcitriol – Low phosphate would occur with primary hyperparathyroidism or over-treatment with phosphate binders, not untreated CKD.<br /></br>b. ↓ PO₄³⁻ / ↑ Ca²⁺ / ↓ calcitriol – Pattern of primary hyperparathyroidism (↑ Ca²⁺, ↓ PO₄³⁻) or ectopic PTHrP. CKD cannot raise Ca²⁺ because calcitriol is low.<br /></br>c. ↓ PO₄³⁻ / ↑ Ca²⁺ / ↑ calcitriol – Seen with excess vitamin D ingestion. Not CKD (kidneys can’t synthesize calcitriol).<br /></br>e. ↑ PO₄³⁻ / ↓ Ca²⁺ / ↑ calcitriol – Phosphate and calcitriol move in opposite directions; if calcitriol were high, Ca²⁺ would rise as well.<br /></br>f. ↑ PO₄³⁻ / ↑ Ca²⁺ / ↑ calcitriol – Hypervitaminosis D or granulomatous disease (sarcoid). Not consistent with CKD.  </p></br><p>Key difference: Only choice d simultaneously shows high phosphate plus low Ca²⁺ and low calcitriol, the classic triad of CKD-induced secondary hyperparathyroidism.</p>
175. A 7-year-old boy is brough to the office by his mother for an initial well-child visit to establish care. The patient's family recently emigrated from Eastern Europe. The mother is concerned about her son's behavior at home and at school. He has been easily distracted, has difficulty completing his chores at home, has been fidgety during classes, and often disrupts others by talking out of turn. His mother says that as an infant he underwent surgical correction of a cleft palate and a heart defect, but she does not have his medical records with her today. The patient is at the 5th percentile for height and weight and the 10th percentile for BMI. Examination shows an elongated face with narrow palpebral fissures, low-set ears, a broad nasal bridge, micrognathia, downward turned mouth, and small teeth. Results of which of the following laboratory studies are most likely to be abnormal in this patient?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Erythrocyte sedimentation rate</br>b. Hematocrit</br>c. Leukocyte count</br>d. Serum calcium concentration</br>e. Serum creatinine concentration</br>F. Serum thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Serum calcium concentration (↓)</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Past surgical repair of cleft palate + congenital heart defect → classic “velocardiofacial”/DiGeorge spectrum.<br /></br>• Dysmorphic facies: long face, narrow palpebral fissures, low-set ears, broad nasal bridge, micrognathia, down-turned mouth, small teeth → typical 22q11.2 deletion features.<br /></br>• Behavioral problems/ADHD, short stature → neurocognitive &amp; growth issues often seen in this syndrome.<br /></br>• Key anatomic clue: 22q11 deletion → failure of 3rd &amp; 4th pharyngeal pouch development → absent/hypoplastic parathyroids → hypocalcemia.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Clinical-Critical Reasoning (stepwise)<br /></br>1. Recognize triad: Cleft palate + Conotruncal cardiac defect + Characteristic facies.<br /></br>2. Associate with 22q11 deletion (DiGeorge/velocardiofacial).<br /></br>3. Recall pathophysiology: absent parathyroids → ↓PTH → ↓serum Ca²⁺.<br /></br>4. Therefore the lab most likely to be abnormal is serum calcium (low).</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Any child with cleft palate + conotruncal heart disease + dysmorphic facies warrants evaluation for 22q11 deletion; expect hypocalcemia due to parathyroid aplasia.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>22q11.2 microdeletion → failure of neural-crest derived 3rd (inferior parathyroids, thymus) &amp; 4th (superior parathyroids) pharyngeal pouches.<br /></br>Consequences:<br /></br>• Parathyroid aplasia → ↓PTH → hypocalcemic tetany, seizures, muscle cramps.<br /></br>• Thymic hypoplasia → T-cell deficiency (↓CD3⁺ count, recurrent viral/fungal infections).<br /></br>• Conotruncal cardiac defects (truncus arteriosus, Tetralogy, interrupted aortic arch, VSD).<br /></br>• Cleft palate/velopharyngeal insufficiency → feeding, speech issues.<br /></br>• Characteristic facies &amp; cognitive/behavioral problems, including ADHD.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls<br /></br>• Labs: ↓Ca²⁺, sometimes ↓Mg²⁺, ↑PO₄³⁻, ↓/inappropriately normal PTH; normal overall leukocyte count but ↓T-cell subsets.<br /></br>• Imaging: CXR may show absent thymic shadow.<br /></br>• Genetic test: FISH or microarray for 22q11.2 deletion.<br /></br>• Management:<br /></br>  – IV or oral calcium + calcitriol (1,25-OH vitamin D) for hypocalcemia.<br /></br>  – Immunology referral; live vaccines only if adequate T-cell numbers.<br /></br>  – Early intervention for speech, learning, behavioral issues.<br /></br>  – Surgical correction of cardiac/palatal defects as needed.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>Mnemonic “CATCH-22”:<br /></br>C – Cardiac (conotruncal)<br /></br>A – Abnormal facies<br /></br>T – Thymic aplasia<br /></br>C – Cleft palate<br /></br>H – Hypocalcemia<br /></br>22 – 22q11 deletion</p></br><p>Visual: Picture a “catch-22” trap set in the 3rd &amp; 4th pouches, snipping off parathyroids &amp; thymus→ low Ca²⁺ &amp; low T-cells.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. ESR – Elevated in inflammation/autoimmune disease; no evidence of systemic inflammation here.<br /></br>b. Hematocrit – Anemia could cause fatigue/inattention but not linked to cleft palate + congenital heart disease + dysmorphic facies.<br /></br>c. Leukocyte count – Total WBC usually normal; immune defect is specific to T-cells (qualitative), not quantitative leukopenia.<br /></br>e. Serum creatinine – Renal disease not suggested; his small size is constitutional/chronic, not nephropathic.<br /></br>f. TSH – Thyroid dysgenesis presents with hypotonia, macroglossia, prolonged jaundice in infancy; not associated with 22q11 deletion.</p></br><p>Key differentiator: Only hypocalcemia directly stems from the embryologic defect described.</p>
176. A 4-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department 30 minutes after the sudden onset of shortness of breath and wheezing at a birthday party. Witnesses report that he was acting normally until he was noted to be in distress; they did not see him choke on anything. He has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. His temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 100/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 90/60 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 95 percent. Examination shows decreased breath sounds over the left hemithorax. lnspiratory and expiratory chest x-rays are shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Asthma</br>b. Foreign body aspiration</br>c. Laryngotracheobronch itis croup</br>d. Pneumothorax</br>e. Rib fracture</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Foreign body aspiration  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Sudden onset” of respiratory distress in an otherwise healthy child at a party → classic trigger for inhaling a peanut, candy, small toy.<br /></br>• Unilateral physical finding: “decreased breath sounds over the left hemithorax.” Asthma, croup, and most viral illnesses cause diffuse or upper-airway findings, not focal.<br /></br>• Vital signs essentially normal (no fever, only mild tachypnea) → argues against infectious or inflammatory process.<br /></br>• Paired inspiratory–expiratory films: this combination is ordered specifically when an aspirated object is suspected to look for air-trapping. On expiration the affected side remains hyperlucent (trapped air) while the normal side becomes denser.  </p></br><p>Key pattern: Sudden focal wheeze or decreased breath sounds + normal/near-normal pulse-ox + inspiratory/expiratory film = foreign body.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Acute focal symptom → think obstruction (not reactive airway, not infection).<br /></br>2. Unilateral exam finding → narrows to pneumothorax vs foreign body.<br /></br>3. Child was not running around or injured → pneumothorax/rib fracture less likely.<br /></br>4. Chest x-ray (inspiratory/expiratory) is tailored for detecting ball-valve air-trapping typical of bronchial obstruction.<br /></br>5. Put together: acute obstruction in bronchus = foreign body aspiration.  </p></br><p>Take-home concept: In pediatrics, unilateral decreased breath sounds after an abrupt event = aspirated object until proven otherwise; confirm with expiratory film and remove via rigid bronchoscopy.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Object lodges in mainstem or segmental bronchus (right side slightly more common, but either lung possible).<br /></br>• “Ball-valve” mechanism: air enters on inspiration (negative intrathoracic pressure pulls obstruction away), but on expiration the object occludes the lumen → air-trapping, hyperinflation, mediastinal shift toward the contralateral lung on expiration film.<br /></br>• If complete obstruction: atelectasis rather than hyperinflation may be seen.<br /></br>• Complications when diagnosis delayed: post-obstructive pneumonia, bronchiectasis.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnosis &amp; Treatment<br /></br>1. Imaging: Inspiratory/expiratory or lateral decubitus film; CT if doubt persists.<br /></br>2. Definitive therapy: Rigid bronchoscopy (allows visualization + removal).<br /></br>3. Follow-up: Repeat bronchoscopy or imaging if symptoms persist to rule out residual fragments.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ONE-Sided, ONE-Second, ONE-Peanut”<br /></br>• ONE-sided findings (unilateral)<br /></br>• Starts in ONE second (sudden)<br /></br>• Usually ONE aspirated object (peanut, toy)  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Asthma<br /></br>• Presents with bilateral diffuse wheezing; no unilateral absent breath sounds.<br /></br>• No trigger (exercise, allergen), no history, and x-ray usually normal or shows hyperinflation bilaterally, not focal air-trapping.  </p></br><p>c. Laryngotracheobronchitis (Croup)<br /></br>• Age fits, but classic prodrome of URI, barking cough, inspiratory stridor (upper-airway noise), and fever.<br /></br>• Neck x-ray shows steeple sign, not unilateral lung changes.  </p></br><p>d. Pneumothorax<br /></br>• Would show absent lung markings and a visible pleural line on both inspiratory and expiratory films; the lung would collapse rather than remain over-inflated.<br /></br>• Often associated with trauma or underlying lung disease; breath sounds usually hyperresonant on percussion rather than simply decreased.  </p></br><p>e. Rib fracture<br /></br>• Requires trauma history; exam shows localized pain, crepitus, and normal lung parenchyma on x-ray (unless complicated by pneumothorax).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>A healthy child who suddenly develops unilateral wheezing or decreased breath sounds → think foreign body. Confirm with expiratory imaging and treat with rigid bronchoscopy.</p>
177. A hospitalized 59-year-old man has undergone three unsuccessful attempts at endoscopic sclerotherapy of esophageal varices during the past 4 days. On admission, he had a 1-week history of recurrent upper gastrointestinal bleeding. He has necrotizing pancreatitis, splenic vein thrombosis, type 2 diabetes mellitus, and hypertension. His medications are insulin, hydrochlorothiazide, lisinopril, and pancrelipase. He has a history of alcohol use disorder but has not consumed alcohol during the past 8 years. He has no other history of psychiatric illness. He is an inmate in a federal prison and has 18 years remaining on his sentence. He asks that no further efforts to control the bleeding be undertaken. His brother, who has not visited the patient in 6 years, says this is the patient's way of committing suicide; the brother insists that efforts to extend the patient's life be continued. The patient appears cachectic but alert. His pulse is 104/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 88/68 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on 4 Umin of oxygen by nasal cannula shows an oxygen saturation of 94 percent. Physical examination shows scattered ecchymoses and petechiae over the upper extremities. On mental status examination, he provides an accurate account of his current condition and the probable outcome, including death, if he continues to bleed. He says he is down but then says he means tired. He reports no suicidal ideation. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Abide by the patient's request</br>b. Attempt to obtain emergency legal guardianship of the patient for medical decision-making</br>c. Consult the prison authorities</br>d. Continue treatment in accordance with the brother's wishes</br>e. Perform complete neurocognitive testing</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A.  Abide by the patient’s request  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>•  “Provides an accurate account of his current condition and the probable outcome, including death, if he continues to bleed” → Demonstrates understanding and appreciation of consequences.<br /></br>•  “He is alert … reports no suicidal ideation” → Clear consciousness; no evidence that the wish to stop treatment is an attempt at self-harm.<br /></br>•  No evidence of psychosis, delirium, intoxication, or overt depression severe enough to impair judgment.<br /></br>•  Desire to halt further treatment is consistent with his current suffering and prognosis (continues to bleed after three failed endoscopic attempts and is now hypotensive).  </p></br><p>These details fulfill the four legal/ethical elements of decision-making capacity (communicates a choice, understands information, appreciates consequences, and reasons about options). Therefore, his autonomous refusal must be honored.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify the decision: Continue aggressive variceal therapy vs. comfort-focused care.<br /></br>2. Does the patient have decision-making capacity?<br /></br>   • Choice = clear (“no further efforts”)<br /></br>   • Understanding = can repeat facts and prognosis<br /></br>   • Appreciation = knows he may die<br /></br>   • Reasoning = request is consistent with values (fatigued, poor quality of life)<br /></br>3. Any coercion or suicidal intent? None.<br /></br>4. If capacity intact, patient’s autonomy overrides family, staff, or prison officials.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: A competent adult has the absolute right to accept or refuse any medical treatment, even if that decision may result in death.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (brief)<br /></br>• Necrotizing pancreatitis → splenic vein thrombosis → left-sided portal hypertension → gastric/esophageal varices.<br /></br>• Recurrent bleeding despite multiple endoscopic sessions → high mortality; next options normally include Sengstaken-Blakemore tube, TIPS, or surgery—each invasive with significant risk.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>Standard escalation (for exam purposes) after failed endoscopic therapy:<br /></br>1. IV octreotide + antibiotics<br /></br>2. Balloon tamponade or TIPS (transjugular intrahepatic portosystemic shunt)<br /></br>3. Surgical shunt or splenectomy for isolated splenic vein thrombosis<br /></br>However, all require the patient’s consent. Capacity → patient can refuse.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CURR” for capacity:<br /></br>C – Communicates a consistent choice<br /></br>U – Understands the information<br /></br>R – Reasons logically<br /></br>R – Recognizes consequences<br /></br>If all CURR boxes are checked, follow the patient’s wishes.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Obtain emergency guardianship<br /></br>• Only pursued when the patient lacks capacity or there is no readily available surrogate. This patient is competent.  </p></br><p>C. Consult prison authorities<br /></br>• Incarceration does not strip a competent patient of medical autonomy. Authorities cannot overrule a capable inmate’s informed refusal.  </p></br><p>D. Continue treatment per brother’s wishes<br /></br>• Surrogates are used only when the patient lacks capacity. A brother’s preference cannot override the competent patient’s decision.  </p></br><p>E. Perform complete neurocognitive testing<br /></br>• No evidence of cognitive impairment. Testing would only delay honoring an autonomous, informed choice and could be construed as coercive.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>A competent adult—regardless of prognosis, family objection, or prisoner status—retains the right to refuse life-sustaining therapy. Honor the patient’s request.</p>
178. A 12-year-old girl is brought to the emergency department by her parents because of a 2-hour history of severe weakness involving the right side of her face. The weakness has been present since the patient awoke from sleep. She is now drooling from the right side of her mouth. She has had an upper respiratory illness for the past 3 days, with symptoms of nasal congestion, cough, and muffling of sounds and pain in her right ear. Medical history is otherwise unremarkable. She has been taking acetaminophen as needed for the ear pain. Vital signs are normal. The patient is awake and alert. Her speech is dysarthric. The right side of her face is severely weakened, with inability to sustain eye closure against resistance. The remainder of the physical examination discloses no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely natural evolution of her condition if left untreated?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Gradual improvement over several months</br>b. Improvement with normalization in 2 to 3 days</br>c. Progression to involve the left limbs</br>d. Progression to involve the left side of the face</br>e. Progression to involve the right limbs</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Gradual improvement over several months  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Sudden onset overnight (“since the patient awoke”) unilateral facial weakness and drooling → points to an acute lower-motor-neuron facial palsy.<br /></br>• Inability to wrinkle forehead / sustain eye closure on the affected side → confirms it is a peripheral (not central) CN VII lesion.<br /></br>• Recent URI + otalgia / muffled hearing on the same side → classic trigger for Bell palsy (edema of CN VII in the facial canal, often post-viral or associated with otitis media).<br /></br>• No limb weakness, normal vital signs, awake/alert → rules out stroke or brain-stem mass/abscess.  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: “Previously healthy child + URI/ear pain + complete unilateral facial weakness including forehead = Bell palsy.”</p></br><hr /></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (why choice A is correct)<br /></br>1. Bell palsy is usually idiopathic inflammation or reactivation of HSV sitting in the geniculate ganglion.<br /></br>2. Natural history: ~85 % of children (and adults) recover spontaneously; function returns gradually over weeks with complete recovery by 3–6 mo (sometimes up to 1 y).<br /></br>3. Therefore, if absolutely no treatment were given, the most likely course is slow but steady improvement over several months.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Bell palsy almost always resolves spontaneously; steroids given within 72 h speed recovery, but natural evolution is gradual recovery over months.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: inflammatory edema of CN VII (idiopathic, HSV-1 most common, VZV, Lyme, post-URI, otitis media).<br /></br>• Edema → compression in the narrow facial canal → ischemia/demyelination → LMN paralysis of all ipsilateral facial muscles.<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations: inability to close eye, loss of forehead creases, drooping mouth, decreased lacrimation, hyperacusis, loss of anterior 2/3 tongue taste.  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostic Highlights<br /></br>First-line (if presenting within 72 h):<br /></br>• Prednisone 1 mg/kg/day (max 60 mg) × 5–7 d ± taper.<br /></br>• Eye protection: artificial tears, nighttime eye-patch to prevent exposure keratitis.<br /></br>• Antivirals (acyclovir/valacyclovir) are optional in children; evidence is weaker than in adults.<br /></br>Work-up: only if atypical (gradual onset, bilateral, recurrent) → Lyme titers, MRI, etc.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Memory Tips<br /></br>“Bell rings for MONTHS” → Bell palsy gets better over months, not days.<br /></br>LMN vs UMN face: “UMN spares Upper face” (stroke = forehead OK), “Lower Motor hits the Lower + Upper.”</p></br><hr /></br><p>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Improvement in 2–3 days<br /></br>• Bell palsy rarely resolves that quickly; edema and remyelination take weeks. Rapid resolution would be more typical of transient neuropraxia (e.g., local anesthesia, hypoglycemia).  </p></br><p>c. Progression to involve the left limbs<br /></br>• Would suggest evolving stroke or brain-stem lesion; inconsistent with isolated peripheral CN VII findings and intact neuro exam.  </p></br><p>d. Progression to involve the left side of the face<br /></br>• Contralateral progression would imply a systemic demyelinating process (e.g., Guillain-Barré) or sarcoidosis; not supported by isolated unilateral findings.  </p></br><p>e. Progression to involve the right limbs<br /></br>• Would require extension into corticospinal tracts (pontine infarct, abscess). Normal vitals, no risk factors, forehead involvement, and ear pain argue strongly against CNS pathology.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: mistaking complete unilateral facial weakness for stroke. Key discriminator: lower-motor-neuron palsy = forehead involved; stroke = forehead spared + additional focal deficits.  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Bottom Line<br /></br>Isolated, complete unilateral facial paralysis after a viral prodrome in a child = Bell palsy. Left untreated, Bell palsy almost always resolves gradually over several months.</p>
179. A 56-year-old woman comes to the clinic for examination prior to undergoing a dental procedure. She will be visiting her dentist frequently for multiple procedures to have braces placed on her teeth because of severe temporomandibular joint pain; she is concerned about her risk for infection. She has mitral valve prolapse and intermittent palpitations. She also has hypertension and osteopenia. Her only medication is atenolol. Her blood pressure is 110/60 mm Hg. Examination shows no abnormalities. Antibiotic prophylaxis is indicated for this patient for which of the following procedures?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Orthodontic appliance adjustment</br>b. Placement of orthodontic brackets</br>c. Teeth cleaning</br>d. Tooth extraction</br>e. No prophylaxis is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. No prophylaxis is indicated  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Clue #1 – Cardiac history: “mitral valve prolapse.”<br /></br>  – MVP used to be on the prophylaxis list but was removed in the 2007 AHA guideline update and remains off the current list.<br /></br>• Clue #2 – Type of procedure(s): “multiple procedures to have braces placed … orthodontic appliance adjustment / brackets.”<br /></br>  – Orthodontic work generally does NOT enter gingival sulcus or perforate oral mucosa.<br /></br>• Clue #3 – Patient diagnostic concern: asking specifically about infective endocarditis prophylaxis.<br /></br>Combine: She has a low-risk cardiac lesion and is undergoing low-risk dental work ⇒ no antibiotics.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Ask “Does the HEART qualify?”<br /></br>   High-risk lesions =<br /></br>   • Prosthetic heart valve or material<br /></br>   • Prior infective endocarditis<br /></br>   • Certain unrepaired/partially repaired cyanotic congenital heart disease<br /></br>   • Repaired CHD with prosthetic material within 6 mo<br /></br>   • Cardiac transplant with valvulopathy<br /></br>   ──&gt; MVP is NOT on the list → patient does NOT qualify.  </p></br><ol></br><li>If the heart qualified, then ask “Does the PROCEDURE qualify?”<br /></br>   Procedures needing coverage = manipulation of gingival tissue, periapical region, or oral mucosal perforation (e.g., extraction, scaling).<br /></br>   Orthodontic adjustments/bracket placement do not.<br /></br>   But we never reach Step 2 because Step 1 already said “no.”  </li></br></ol></br><p>Core takeaway: Endocarditis prophylaxis = High-risk heart lesion + high-risk dental work. Both must be present.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Endocarditis results when bacteremia (usually viridans streptococci during dental work) seeds damaged endocardium or prosthetic material.<br /></br>• Guidelines narrowed prophylaxis because:<br /></br>  – Very low absolute risk in most patients.<br /></br>  – Antibiotic-related adverse effects and resistance outweigh benefit.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>If prophylaxis WERE indicated:<br /></br>• Give amoxicillin 2 g PO 30–60 min before procedure (adults).<br /></br>• Alternatives: clindamycin 600 mg, azithromycin 500 mg if pen-allergic.<br /></br>No additional imaging or labs are required just to decide on prophylaxis.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>Mnemonic “P P C T” = People &amp; Procedures Covered<br /></br>People: “Previous endocarditis, Prosthetic valve, Congenital cyanotic (unrepaired), Transplant valve lesion.”<br /></br>Procedures: “Gum, Root, Cut” → gingiva, root (periapical), cut oral mucosa.<br /></br>If your patient doesn’t have BOTH a ‘P’ and a ‘GRC’, skip the meds.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Orthodontic appliance adjustment – Does not manipulate gingiva/mucosa; even in high-risk hearts, no prophylaxis.  </p></br><p>b. Placement of orthodontic brackets – Same reasoning as (a). Merely bonding brackets to enamel surfaces.  </p></br><p>c. Teeth cleaning (scaling/polishing) – WOULD meet the procedural criterion (manipulates gingiva); however, the patient lacks a high-risk cardiac condition → no antibiotics.  </p></br><p>d. Tooth extraction – Also a qualifying procedure, but again her cardiac lesion is low-risk → no antibiotics.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Students memorize “heart disease = antibiotics” without pairing heart lesion risk level with procedure type. Always apply the two-step rule.</p>
180. A 62-year-old man comes to the physician for a preoperative evaluation before undergoing elective cataract removal. He has a 3-month history of progressive mild shortness of breath with exertion. He takes no medications. He drinks three beers daily. His blood pressure is 150/92 mm Hg. Examination shows no other abnormalities. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hematocrit: 26 percent</br>Mean corpuscular volume: 75 µm3</br>Leukocyte count: 6,000 /mm3</br>Platelet count: 700,000 /mm3</br>Prothrombin time: 11 sec INR = 1</br>Serum</br>Glucose: 89 mg/dL</br>Creatinine: 1.6 mg/dL</br>Alkaline phosphatase: 142 U/L</br>AST: 55 U/L</br>Urine</br>Glucose: 2+</br>Protein: 1+</br>Which of the following is most likely to have prevented this patient's hematologic condition?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Annual blood pressure measurement</br>b. Avoidance of alcohol</br>c. Avoidance of radiation exposure</br>d. Colorectal cancer screening</br>e. Measurement of serum vitamin 812 cobalamin concentration every 2 years</br>F. Thyroid function tests every 2 years</br>g. Daily multivitamin supplementation</br>h. Subcutaneous erythropoietin therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>d. Colorectal cancer screening</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis – Pulling out the clues</br>• 62-year-old man (screening age for colorectal cancer).<br /></br>• Slowly progressive exertional dyspnea → suggests chronic anemia rather than an acute process.<br /></br>• Hematocrit 26% with MCV 75 µm³ → microcytic anemia → almost always iron-deficiency in an adult male until proven otherwise.<br /></br>• Platelets 700 000/mm³ → reactive thrombocytosis, a common accompaniment of iron-deficiency anemia.<br /></br>• No overt bleeding reported, so think occult blood loss from the GI tract (peptic ulcer, colon cancer/polyps, angiodysplasia, etc.).<br /></br>• Mild Cr elevation and trace protein/glucose in urine are incidental and don’t explain microcytosis.<br /></br>Take-home pattern: Older patient + microcytic anemia + thrombocytosis = occult GI bleed → colorectal carcinoma until proved otherwise.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)</br>1. Identify the anemia type (MCV 75 = microcytic → iron deficiency).<br /></br>2. In men and post-menopausal women, chronic GI blood loss is the #1 source of iron loss.<br /></br>3. Most common occult source in this age group = right-sided colorectal cancer/polyps.<br /></br>4. Detecting &amp; removing premalignant adenomas (or early cancer) with routine screening colonoscopy would prevent the chronic bleeding → would prevent the iron-deficiency anemia noted today.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Iron-deficiency anemia in an older adult mandates evaluation for colorectal malignancy; adherence to recommended colon-cancer screening (starting at age 45–50) prevents both the cancer and its hematologic sequelae.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Iron-deficiency anemia → ↓ iron stores → ↓ heme synthesis → smaller, paler RBCs (microcytic, hypochromic).<br /></br>• Chronic slow bleeding from a colonic adenocarcinoma/polyp causes continuous iron loss.<br /></br>• Thrombocytosis is driven by ↑ thrombopoietin and IL-6 in iron-deficient states.  </p></br><p>Relevant Diagnostics / Treatments</br>Diagnostic work-up of microcytosis in this scenario:<br /></br>• CBC &amp; iron studies → low ferritin, high TIBC, low serum Fe.<br /></br>• Colonoscopy (and often EGD) to locate bleeding source.<br /></br>Treatment: resect tumor/polyp, iron repletion (oral ferrous sulfate 325 mg TID).  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“Old + Cold (micro-Cytic) = Colon Cancer until proven otherwise.”<br /></br>OR<br /></br>MICRO mnemonic for microcytosis:<br /></br>M – Malignancy bleed (colon),<br /></br>I – Iron deficiency,<br /></br>C – Chronic disease,<br /></br>R – Reduced globin (thalassemia),<br /></br>O – Others (lead, sideroblastic).  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Rundown</br>a. Annual blood-pressure measurement – Prevents cardiovascular and renal disease, not iron-deficiency anemia.<br /></br>b. Avoidance of alcohol – Alcohol causes macrocytosis (folate deficiency), not microcytosis.<br /></br>c. Avoidance of radiation exposure – Would reduce risk of aplastic anemia/leukemia, usually normocytic.<br /></br>e. Serum vitamin B12 every 2 yrs – B12 deficiency → macrocytic anemia.<br /></br>f. Thyroid tests every 2 yrs – Hypothyroidism gives normo- or macrocytic anemia.<br /></br>g. Daily multivitamin – Routine multivitamins do not supply enough iron to counter chronic GI blood loss.<br /></br>h. Subcutaneous erythropoietin – Helpful in anemia of CKD (normocytic), but does not address iron loss; moreover, microcytosis wouldn’t correct without iron.</p></br><p>Avoid the trap: many students see renal insufficiency and think “anemia of chronic disease.” Always check the MCV; microcytosis in an older adult = look for iron-loss source, most importantly colorectal cancer.</p>
181. A 27-year-old man with asthma comes to the emergency department because of progressive shortness of breath and wheezing for 24 hours. His symptoms began 1 week ago with increasing wheezing. Treatment with inhaled J3-adrenergic agonists and corticosteroids has been ineffective. He appears mildly dehydrated and in moderate to severe respiratory distress with labored breathing and use of accessory muscles. His pulse is 90/min, respirations are 30/min, and blood pressure is 130/80 mm Hg. Arterial blood gas analysis on room air shows: </br>pH: 7.25</br>PCO2: 51 mm Hg</br>PO2: 65 mm Hg</br>HCO3⁻: 20 mEq/L</br>Which of the following is the most likely cause of these findings?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Metabolic acidosis with a compensatory respiratory alkalosis</br>b. Metabolic alkalosis with a compensatory respiratory acidosis</br>c. Mixed respiratory and metabolic acidosis</br>d. Pure respiratory acidosis</br>e. Respiratory acidosis with a compensatory metabolic alkalosis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Mixed respiratory and metabolic acidosis  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues: “progressive shortness of breath,” severe asthma attack despite β-agonist + steroids → impending respiratory failure (↓ alveolar ventilation → CO₂ retention).<br /></br>• ABG: pH 7.25 (acidemia), PCO₂ 51 mm Hg (↑), HCO₃⁻ 20 mEq/L (↓). A single primary disorder cannot simultaneously raise CO₂ and lower HCO₃⁻.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Decide acid-base status: pH &lt; 7.35 → acidemia.<br /></br>2. Determine primary process:<br /></br>   • Respiratory component: PCO₂ 51 (&gt;40) → respiratory acidosis.<br /></br>   • Metabolic component: HCO₃⁻ 20 (&lt;22) → metabolic acidosis.<br /></br>3. Check expected compensation to prove it’s mixed, not compensated.<br /></br>   • For acute respiratory acidosis, HCO₃⁻ should rise 1 mEq/L for every 10 mm Hg ↑ in CO₂.<br /></br>     – ΔPCO₂ ≈ +11 → expected HCO₃⁻ ≈ 24 + 1 ≈ 25 mEq/L.<br /></br>   • Actual HCO₃⁻ = 20 (lower, not higher) → there is <em>additional</em> metabolic acidosis.<br /></br>4. Conclusion: mixed respiratory (CO₂ retention from status asthmaticus) <em>and</em> metabolic (likely lactic acidosis from hypoxia/dehydration) acidosis.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: To diagnose a mixed disorder, compare measured HCO₃⁻ or PCO₂ to the value predicted by simple compensatory rules; a marked deviation signals a second primary disturbance.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Severe asthma → airway obstruction → hypoventilation → CO₂ retention → respiratory acidosis.<br /></br>• Hypoxia + increased work of breathing → anaerobic metabolism → lactic acid accumulation; dehydration → decreased perfusion, worsening lactic acidosis → metabolic acidosis.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostics (context)<br /></br>• Immediate: aggressive bronchodilation (continuous nebulized albuterol, ipratropium), systemic corticosteroids, ± magnesium sulfate.<br /></br>• Impending respiratory failure: prepare for intubation &amp; mechanical ventilation.<br /></br>• Correct dehydration &amp; lactic acidosis with IV fluids; monitor serial ABGs.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ROME”: Respiratory Opposite (pH ↓, CO₂ ↑), Metabolic Equal (pH ↓, HCO₃⁻ ↓).<br /></br>Compensation cheatsheet (easy numbers):<br /></br>• Acute Resp Acidosis: HCO₃⁻ ↑ 1 for every 10 CO₂ ↑<br /></br>• Chronic Resp Acidosis: HCO₃⁻ ↑ 3–4 for every 10 CO₂ ↑<br /></br>If the measured HCO₃⁻ deviates &gt;2 mEq/L from expected → suspect mixed disorder.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Metabolic acidosis with compensatory respiratory alkalosis<br /></br>   – Would show low pH, low HCO₃⁻, <em>low</em> PCO₂ (hyperventilation). Here PCO₂ is elevated.  </p></br><p>b. Metabolic alkalosis with compensatory respiratory acidosis<br /></br>   – Would have high pH and high HCO₃⁻, not the acidosis seen here.  </p></br><p>d. Pure respiratory acidosis<br /></br>   – HCO₃⁻ should be normal or slightly elevated (≥24 in acute). The low HCO₃⁻ rules this out.  </p></br><p>e. Respiratory acidosis with compensatory metabolic alkalosis<br /></br>   – Compensation for respiratory acidosis is <em>metabolic acidosis</em> (rise in HCO₃⁻), not alkalosis. Moreover HCO₃⁻ would be high, not low.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: forgetting the magnitude of expected compensation—always calculate!</p>
182. A 16-year-old girl comes to the office because of a 1-month history of green, sticky discharge from the right nipple. She initially noticed the discharge on her bra and was also able to express it manually. She has not had breast pain or skin changes. During the past 3 months, she has had a 4.5-kg 10-lb weight gain despite no change in appetite. She also has had frequent frontal headaches since beginning oral contraceptive therapy 3 months ago. Six months ago, she was diagnosed with major depressive disorder and was started on fluoxetine therapy. Menses occur at regular 28-day intervals. Her last menstrual period was 2 weeks ago. She is sexually active with one partner and uses condoms inconsistently. On examination, scant greenish discharge can be expressed from the right breast. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Ductal ectasia</br>b. Hypothyroid ism</br>c. Medication adverse effect</br>d. Pregnancy</br>e. Prolactinoma</br></br></br><p>Correct answer: A. Ductal ectasia</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Unilateral: only the right breast produces discharge → favors a local ductal process rather than systemic endocrine stimulation.<br /></br>• Color/consistency: “green, sticky” discharge is classic for duct ectasia (degenerating lipids in a dilated sub-areolar duct give the discharge a dark green or brown, toothpaste-like character).<br /></br>• No galactorrhea features: physiologic/endocrine causes (pregnancy, prolactinoma, hypothyroidism, drug-induced) produce thin, milky or clear bilateral discharge.<br /></br>• Regular menses and normal breast exam: argues against pregnancy, thyroid disease, or a macroprolactinoma.<br /></br>• Age: duct ectasia is most common in perimenopausal women but can occur at any age; the discharge characteristics trump the age “typicality” on exams.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (stepwise)<br /></br>1. Determine if discharge is physiologic vs pathologic.<br /></br>   – Physiologic: bilateral, milky/serous, associated with pregnancy/lactation/endocrine.<br /></br>   – Pathologic: unilateral, colored (green, brown, bloody), +/- mass.<br /></br>2. Characterize discharge color. Green sticky = duct ectasia (degenerative secretions).<br /></br>3. Exclude endocrine/drug causes (would be bilateral, milky).<br /></br>4. Arrive at most likely diagnosis: ductal ectasia.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: On NBME/USMLE, unilateral green/brown viscous discharge without systemic findings = ductal ectasia.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Obstruction &amp; dilation of a subareolar milk duct → chronic inflammation → breakdown of lipid-laden debris → thick, green/brown discharge ± nipple retraction or periareolar mass.<br /></br>• Risk factors: smoking, multiparity, aging, but can be idiopathic in adolescents.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Work-up<br /></br>1. Ultrasound ± ductogram to confirm dilated ducts (not always required on exams).<br /></br>2. If bothersome or recurrent: surgical excision of the involved duct.<br /></br>3. Otherwise reassurance and local care.</p></br><p>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“Ec-TASTE-ia” → discharge tastes/looks like old green toothpaste (green, pasty).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Hypothyroidism<br /></br>• TRH ↑ → prolactin ↑ → bilateral milky galactorrhea, menstrual irregularities, fatigue, cold intolerance. Weight gain alone is not enough, and discharge should be white/milky, not green.</p></br><p>C. Medication adverse effect (fluoxetine, OCP)<br /></br>• SSRIs/OCPs can elevate prolactin or cause estrogen stimulation → bilateral milky discharge. Again, color and laterality are wrong.</p></br><p>D. Pregnancy<br /></br>• Colostrum is bilateral, yellowish-white, occurs mid- to late pregnancy. Regular periods, last period 2 weeks ago, and unilateral green discharge make pregnancy unlikely.</p></br><p>E. Prolactinoma<br /></br>• Produces bilateral milky galactorrhea + amenorrhea, visual field defects, severe headaches. Patient has regular menses, unilateral green discharge.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: automatically blaming medications or prolactin for any nipple discharge. Always first ask, “Milky &amp; bilateral (endocrine) or colored &amp; unilateral (ductal lesion)?”</p>
183. A 14-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her mother because she has never had a menstrual period. The patient has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Thelarche began 18 months ago. Her mother's menarche was at the age of 13 years. The patient plays soccer and runs 3 miles weekly. She is at the 50th percentile for height, 10th percentile for weight, and 25th percentile for BMI. Breast and pubic hair development are sexual maturity rating stage 4. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Karyotype analysis</br>b. Measurement of serum gonadotropin concentrations</br>c. Measurement of serum prolactin concentration</br>d. Thyroid function testing</br>e. Reassurance only</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>e. Reassurance only</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Age = 14 years with no menses yet.<br /></br>• Secondary sexual characteristics present: breasts and pubic hair Tanner stage 4 (normal estrogen exposure).<br /></br>• Time since thelarche = 18 months (≈1.5 yr). Menarche usually follows thelarche by 2–2.5 years.<br /></br>• No systemic disease, normal activity level, only mildly low weight/BMI (10th–25th percentile).<br /></br>These data place her squarely in the NORMAL physiologic window for menarche; she has not yet met any definition of primary amenorrhea that warrants a work-up.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Primary-amenorrhea work-up is triggered when either:<br /></br>1. No menses by age 15 in a girl who has normal secondary sexual development, OR<br /></br>2. No menses by age 13 in a girl who lacks any secondary sexual development, OR<br /></br>3. Failure to menstruate within 3 years of thelarche.</p></br><p>The patient is 14 y (not yet 15) and only 1.5 y post-thelarche (&lt;3 y). Therefore she does NOT meet criteria for immediate evaluation; the proper next step is simple reassurance and observation.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Always check the age AND time since thelarche before starting an amenorrhea work-up.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (brief)</br>Menarche is the final stage of puberty. After pulsatile GnRH → ↑LH/FSH → ovarian estrogen → breast growth, the HPO axis continues to mature; progesterone-primed cycles and regular menses often lag behind thelarche by up to 30 months.</p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests</br>First-line “treatment” here is no treatment: reassure, educate about expected timing, and schedule routine follow-up. No labs or imaging are indicated unless she surpasses the age/time thresholds or develops other concerning signs (galactorrhea, hirsutism, systemic illness, etc.).</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“1-3-5 Rule” for primary amenorrhea:<br /></br>• 1 = no menses 1 yr after breast buds? (rare)<br /></br>• 3 = &gt;3 yrs after thelarche → evaluate.<br /></br>• 5 = age 15 with breasts but no menses → evaluate.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Karyotype analysis – Done when primary amenorrhea plus absent uterus, streak gonads, or phenotypic clues (Turner 45,X). She has normal secondary sex development → estrogen present → functional ovaries; no sign of Mullerian agenesis.</p></br><p>b. Serum gonadotropins (LH/FSH) – First test once the evaluation threshold is met to distinguish central vs. primary ovarian failure. She has not met the threshold.</p></br><p>c. Serum prolactin – Ordered for galactorrhea or headache/visual symptoms suggesting pituitary adenoma; none are present.</p></br><p>d. Thyroid function tests – Hypothyroidism can cause oligomenorrhea/amenorrhea, but there are no symptoms (fatigue, weight gain, cold intolerance, goiter).
