TFTs would be part of the lab panel only after evaluation is indicated.</p></br><p>By remembering the timing criteria, you avoid premature (and costly) testing.</p>
184. A hospitalized 57-year-old man is confused, agitated, and combative 2 days after undergoing laparoscopic resection for hepatocellular carcinoma. His medications are omeprazole, propranolol, and hydrocodone. He has drunk one gallon of Scotch whiskey weekly for 3 years. He has used heroin once daily for 3 years. His temperature is 38.5 C 101.3 F, pulse is 112/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 170/100 mm Hg. Physical examination shows a tremor of the hands. On mental status examination, he is not oriented to person, place, or time and reports seeing cockroaches on the hospital bed. Laboratory studies show:</br>Platelet count: 46,000 /mm3</br>Prothrombin time: 15.8 sec INR = 1.4</br>Serum</br>Albumin: 2.2 g/dL</br>Total bilirubin: 3.1 mg/dL</br>AST: 66 U/L</br>ALT: 98 U/L</br>Ammonia: 11 µg/dL</br>Urine toxicology screening is negative. Administration of which of the following is the most appropriate initial step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Folic acid</br>b. Lactulose</br>c. Lorazepam</br>d. Methadone</br>e. Vitamin thiamine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Lorazepam  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Post-op day 2 (≈48 h) following major surgery → classic window for alcohol-withdrawal syndromes to peak.<br /></br>• Heavy, chronic ETOH use (“1 gallon of Scotch / wk × 3 y”).<br /></br>• Autonomic hyperactivity: T 101.3 °F, HR 112, BP 170/100, RR 24.<br /></br>• Neuropsychiatric findings: agitation, disorientation, visual hallucinations (“cockroaches on the bed”), coarse hand tremor.<br /></br>These clues = Delirium Tremens (DTs) – the most severe form of alcohol withdrawal that typically begins 48-96 h after the last drink or after physiologic stress (surgery, infection).  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Recognize timeline + risk factors → peri-operative patient with heavy ETOH = high risk.<br /></br>2. Identify sympathetic overdrive + hallucinations → DTs.<br /></br>3. First-line therapy for DTs = IV benzodiazepine (diazepam, lorazepam, chlordiazepoxide). Lorazepam preferred inpatient because of intermediate half-life and no active metabolites (safer in liver disease).<br /></br>Core takeaway: Acute management of alcohol-withdrawal delirium = rapid benzodiazepine loading to prevent seizures, arrhythmias, and death.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Brief<br /></br>Chronic ethanol → ↑ GABA-A activity, ↓ NMDA receptors. Abrupt cessation → ↓ GABA tone, rebound ↑ NMDA glutamate activity → CNS hyper-excitability, autonomic surge, tremor, hallucinations, seizures, DTs. Benzodiazepines act as GABA-A positive allosteric modulators, restoring inhibitory tone.  </p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostics<br /></br>Immediate:<br /></br>• IV lorazepam (1–4 mg, repeat until calm/lightly sedated).<br /></br>Adjuncts:<br /></br>• Thiamine 100 mg IV (prevent Wernicke), multivitamins, fluids, electrolytes (Mg²⁺, K⁺, PO₄³⁻).<br /></br>• Monitor vitals, glucose, consider ICU if unstable.<br /></br>No imaging/labs delay therapy—DTs is clinical diagnosis.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Delirium Tremens starts <em>Two to Three</em> days post-last drink and needs <em>Two to Treat</em>: IV benzo + thiamine.”<br /></br>“Lorazepam for the Liver” (safe in hepatic dysfunction).  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Folic acid – Corrects macrocytic anemia from poor nutrition but does not abort acute withdrawal or agitation.<br /></br>b. Lactulose – Used for hepatic encephalopathy (ammonia ↑). Ammonia level is normal (11 µg/dL) and patient has hallucinations/HTN typical for DTs, not hepatic coma (which gives asterixis, somnolence).<br /></br>d. Methadone – Treats opioid withdrawal or maintenance. Urine tox negative, signs (yawning, lacrimation, piloerection) absent.<br /></br>e. Vitamin thiamine – Important in alcoholics to prevent Wernicke encephalopathy, but it will not control the life-threatening autonomic and neuro excitability of DTs. Give it, but only after/alongside benzodiazepine; not the initial stabilizing step.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Students often pick thiamine or lactulose because “alcoholic + confusion = Wernicke or hepatic encephalopathy.” Always look at timing and sympathetic overdrive; DTs kills quickly unless benzos are given.</p>
185. A 4-month-old female infant is brought to the office by her mother for a well-child examination. She was born at term following an uncomplicated pregnancy and vaginal delivery. She is breast-feeding well and gaining weight. She has been smiling and cooing and has begun rolling onto her side. The mother says she noticed an asymmetric appearance to her daughter's face and head 4 weeks ago that has slightly worsened since that time. The patient otherwise has been well. Family history is noncontributory. The infant is 63 cm 25 in; 50th percentile long and weighs 6200 g 13 lb 11oz; 50th percentile; head circumference is 40 cm 16 in; 25th percentile. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows an open and flat anterior fontanel and a palpable ridge between the right parietal and temporal bones. The right eye and eyebrow appear superiorly displaced compared with the left. The remainder of the physical examination discloses no abnormalities. CT scan of the head is shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Coronal craniosynostosis</br>b. Fracture of the skull base</br>c. Osteogenesis imperfecta</br>d. Positional plagiocephaly</br>e. Torticollis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Coronal craniosynostosis  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age 4 months – the classic window when abnormalities of skull shape first become obvious.<br /></br>• “Asymmetric face and head … slightly worsened” – progressive change, not present at birth trauma.<br /></br>• “Palpable ridge between the right parietal and temporal bones” – a bony ridge along a suture = premature fusion.<br /></br>• “Right eye and eyebrow appear superiorly displaced” – orbit and forehead on that side pushed upward → typical of unilateral coronal suture closure.<br /></br>• Normal development &amp; no trauma history rule out metabolic bone disease or fracture.<br /></br>• CT (not shown here) in the stem – imaging of choice for suspected craniosynostosis.  </p></br><p>Pattern to recognize: progressive unilateral flattening/bossing of the forehead with an orbital asymmetry + a palpable suture ridge = unilateral coronal craniosynostosis.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Abnormal skull shape? → Think positional vs craniosynostosis.<br /></br>2. Palpable ridge + orbital asymmetry = fused coronal suture.<br /></br>3. No history of limited neck motion (torticollis) or constant supine positioning that causes occipital flattening (positional).<br /></br>Take-home: ONLY craniosynostosis produces a ridged, immobile suture line and facial/orbital elevation on the affected side.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Craniosynostosis = premature fusion of one/more cranial sutures → growth restricted perpendicular to closed suture and compensatory overgrowth parallel to it.<br /></br>• Coronal suture (ear-to-ear) closed unilaterally → ipsilateral frontal flattening, contralateral frontal/parietal bossing, vertical displacement of ipsilateral orbit (“harlequin” orbit on CT).<br /></br>• Etiology usually sporadic; may be syndromic (Crouzon, Apert) when multiple sutures are involved.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Management &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>• Low-dose CT with 3-D reconstruction confirms diagnosis &amp; surgical planning.<br /></br>• Refer to craniofacial surgery: strip craniectomy or fronto-orbital advancement, ideally at 6–12 months to prevent intracranial pressure elevation and correct cosmetic issues.<br /></br>• Helmet therapy is adjunctive, not curative when a suture is fused.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“C-O-R-O-N-A-L = Closed suture Over the eyebrow, Ridge is palpable, Orbit Ascends Laterally.”  </p></br><p>Mnemonic to distinguish plagiocephalies:<br /></br>“P.L.A.G.I.O.”<br /></br>Positional =<br /></br>• Posterior flattening<br /></br>• Large occiput<br /></br>• Anterior ear displacement<br /></br>• Gentle (no ridge) skull<br /></br>• Improves with Off-loading.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Skull-base fracture – Requires significant head trauma; would show battle sign, raccoon eyes, CSF otorrhea; not a slowly progressive deformity in a healthy infant.<br /></br>c. Osteogenesis imperfecta – Presents with multiple long-bone fractures, blue sclerae, dentinogenesis imperfecta. Skull shape usually normal; no isolated suture ridge.<br /></br>d. Positional plagiocephaly – Occipital flattening, parallelogram skull, anterior displacement of ear on flattened side, NO palpable bony ridge, NO orbital elevation, improves with repositioning/helmet.<br /></br>e. Torticollis – Congenital sternocleidomastoid contracture → persistent head tilt &amp; limited neck ROM; secondary skull flattening may occur but again lacks a ridged suture and orbital shift.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Key Takeaway<br /></br>A palpable ridged suture plus ipsilateral orbital elevation in an otherwise healthy infant = unilateral coronal craniosynostosis; treat surgically to allow normal cranial and brain growth.</p>
186. A 57-year-old woman comes to the physician because she has felt sad and tearful and has thought about killing herself for the past 2 weeks. Her symptoms began 1 week after losing 25,000 dollars during a weekend of gambling. She reports that she had boundless energy all week and gambled continuously throughout the weekend without needing to sleep. She says, I felt great even when I was losing and did not realize what I had done until a week later. Twice yearly for the past 10 years, she has had similar gambling episodes but has never lost this much money before. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination, she is distraught and tearful. She says that she has not developed a plan to take her own life but admits to wishing for a convenient car accident. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Bipolar disorder</br>b. Cyclothymic disorder</br>c. Major depressive disorder</br>d. Malingering</br>e. Pathological gambling</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Bipolar disorder (most consistent with Bipolar I – at least one true manic episode ± major depression)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis (Key Clues)<br /></br>1. “Boundless energy … gambled continuously … without needing to sleep” → classic manic behavior (↑ energy, ↓ sleep, goal-directed/risky activity).<br /></br>2. Occurs “twice yearly for 10 years” → recurrent, episodic.<br /></br>3. Present mood: “sad, tearful … thought about killing herself” → current major-depressive episode.<br /></br>4. No medical or substance trigger mentioned; exam otherwise normal.</p></br><p>These details together satisfy DSM-5 criteria for a past MANIC episode plus current depression → Bipolar I disorder.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify a manic episode (DIG FAST mnemonic).<br /></br>• D – Distractibility<br /></br>• I – Indiscretion/Impulsivity (high-stakes gambling)<br /></br>• G – Grandiosity (“felt great even when I was losing”)<br /></br>• F – Flight of ideas (often present)<br /></br>• A – Activity ↑ / psychomotor agitation<br /></br>• S – Sleep need ↓<br /></br>• T – Talkativeness</p></br><p>Step 2: Check chronicity &amp; recurrence → manic episodes ≥1 = Bipolar I, regardless of depressive episodes.<br /></br>Step 3: Rule out substance, medications, or medical illness (none provided).<br /></br>Take-home: ANY single manic episode = Bipolar I.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• Etiology: Dysregulation of monoamines (↑ NE, DA), genetic predisposition (first-degree relatives ↑ risk).<br /></br>• Course: Episodic; manic episodes tend to begin abruptly and last ≥1 week (or any duration if hospitalization required).<br /></br>• Suicide risk: Highest of all psychiatric illnesses (depression phase or mixed states).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>First-line maintenance: Lithium, valproate, lamotrigine, carbamazepine, or atypical antipsychotics (e.g., quetiapine, lurasidone).<br /></br>Acute mania: Lithium OR valproate + atypical AP (e.g., haloperidol, olanzapine).<br /></br>Depression phase: Quetiapine, lurasidone, or lamotrigine preferred; avoid unopposed antidepressant monotherapy.</p></br><p>Baseline labs before lithium: BMP (creatinine), TSH, pregnancy test.<br /></br>If secondary causes suspected → TSH, drug screen.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Once you DIG FAST enough to reach the MOON, you’re BIPOLAR.”<br /></br>Moon = mania ≥1 → automatically Bipolar I.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Answers Are Wrong  </p></br><p>b. Cyclothymic disorder<br /></br>• ≥2 yr of fluctuating sub-threshold hypomanic &amp; depressive SYMPTOMS (never full mania or full MDE). This patient has clear, full-criteria mania.</p></br><p>c. Major depressive disorder<br /></br>• Requires ≥2 episodes of major depression with NO history of mania/hypomania. Vignette provides a manic history → MDD excluded.</p></br><p>d. Malingering<br /></br>• Intentional production of symptoms for external gain (money, drugs, legal). Patient shows genuine distress, suicidal ideation, no clear external incentive.</p></br><p>e. Pathological gambling (Gambling disorder)<br /></br>• Maladaptive gambling behavior WITHOUT episodic mood disturbances. Here, gambling occurs specifically during manic episodes; mood disorder explains behavior.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>A single unequivocal manic episode makes the diagnosis Bipolar I, even if depressive episodes are more frequent or severe. Always screen for suicide risk and start mood-stabilizing therapy.</p>
187. A previously healthy 57-year-old woman comes to the emergency department because of a 2-week history of fever, nonproductive cough, and increasing shortness of breath. She is no longer able to walk up one flight of stairs without resting. She takes no medications. She does not smoke cigarettes, drink alcohol, or use illicit drugs. She has been sexually active with multiple partners, and they use condoms inconsistently. She appears cachectic and in moderate respiratory distress. Her temperature is 39.1 C 102.4 F, pulse is 120/min, respirations are 32/min, and blood pressure is 104/68 mm Hg. Examination shows submental and cervical lymphadenopathy. There is diffuse white exudate over the buccal mucosa. Pulmonary examination shows good air movement; no crackles or rhonchi are heard. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Her hemoglobin concentration is 7.8 g/dl, and leukocyte count is 3800/mm 3. Arterial blood gas analysis on room air shows:</br>pH 7.48</br>Pco2 30 mm Hg</br>Po2 68 mm Hg</br>An x-ray of the chest shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely causal organism?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Epstein-Barr virus</br>b. Legionella pneumophila</br>c. Mycop/asma pneumoniae</br>d. Pneumocystis jirovecii formerly P. carinil</br>e. Streptococcus pneumoniae</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Pneumocystis jirovecii (formerly P. carinii)  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues<br /></br>• Subacute course (2 weeks) of fever, non-productive cough, progressive dyspnea<br /></br>• Oral thrush + generalized lymphadenopathy + cachexia → advanced, untreated HIV/AIDS is likely (CD4 &lt; 200 cells/µL)<br /></br>• Normopulmonary exam (good air movement, no crackles) but marked hypoxemia (PaO₂ 68) and respiratory alkalosis (pH 7.48, PCO₂ 30) → “silent” pneumonia classically seen with PCP<br /></br>• Normal chest x-ray early on (or it may show diffuse bilateral interstitial infiltrates later)<br /></br>• Anemia (Hgb 7.8) and leukopenia (WBC 3800) support chronic HIV infection  </p></br><p>Pattern: HIV patient + indolent, non-productive cough + hypoxemia out of proportion to auscultatory/CXR findings = Pneumocystis jirovecii pneumonia.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Recognize immunocompromise (oral thrush, cytopenias, risky sexual history).<br /></br>2. In an AIDS patient, think of opportunistic pneumonias.<br /></br>3. Differentiate:<br /></br>   • Acute, productive with lobar infiltrate → bacterial (Strep pneumo)<br /></br>   • GI Sx, hyponatremia, consolidation → Legionella<br /></br>   • Diffuse, indolent, nonproductive, minimal exam/CXR findings → PCP<br /></br>4. Hypoxemia with ↑A–a gradient + normal/near-normal CXR clinches PCP.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Pneumocystis jirovecii causes an indolent, “quiet” pneumonia in AIDS patients, often with a deceptively normal exam and x-ray yet significant hypoxemia.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Pneumocystis jirovecii = yeast-like fungus inhaled ubiquitously; reactivates when CD4 &lt; 200.<br /></br>• Attaches to alveolar epithelium → foamy proteinaceous exudate fills alveoli → V/Q mismatch → severe hypoxemia.<br /></br>• Characteristic labs: ↑LDH; diagnosis by induced sputum or BAL silver stain/fluorescent antibody; serum β-D-glucan often positive.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>1. TMP-SMX (trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole) = first-line treatment AND prophylaxis (CD4 &lt; 200).<br /></br>2. Add prednisone if PaO₂ &lt; 70 mm Hg or A–a gradient &gt; 35 mm Hg on room air.<br /></br>3. Alternatives (sulfa allergy/G6PD deficiency): atovaquone, pentamidine, or clindamycin-primaquine.<br /></br>4. Work-up: CBC, LDH, ABG, HIV testing, CD4 count, chest CT (if CXR negative), BAL for definitive diagnosis.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Quick Memory Aids<br /></br>• “PCP is a ‘Pretty Cool Pretender’—looks normal on exam/x-ray but PaO₂ plummets.”<br /></br>• THINK: CD4 &lt; 200 → TMP-SMX = Two-Month Prophylaxis-SeXual (easy way to pair CD4 cutoff with drug).  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Epstein-Barr virus (EBV): Causes infectious mononucleosis or lymphoproliferative disorders in AIDS; not an AIDS-defining pneumonia and would not cause severe hypoxemia with a normal CXR.<br /></br>b. Legionella pneumophila: Produces high fever, watery diarrhea, hyponatremia, and patchy/unilateral lobar infiltrate on CXR; sputum Gram stain shows few neutrophils; usually seen in smokers or hotel/AC exposure, not indicated here.<br /></br>c. Mycoplasma pneumoniae: “Walking pneumonia” in younger, healthy patients; CXR shows diffuse reticulonodular or patchy infiltrates; cold agglutinins common; oral thrush/immunosuppression not typical.<br /></br>e. Streptococcus pneumoniae: Most common CAP; abrupt onset, productive cough, lobar consolidation with crackles and bronchial breath sounds, elevated WBC count; CXR would show lobar opacity, not normal.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In an AIDS patient with subacute dyspnea, dry cough, severe hypoxemia, and a normal or minimally abnormal chest x-ray, always think Pneumocystis jirovecii pneumonia and start empiric TMP-SMX (plus steroids if PaO₂ &lt; 70).</p>
188. A 24-year-old nulligravid woman comes to the physician because of a 3-day history of pain with urination and urinary frequency. She has a 1-year history of frequent bladder infections characterized by similar symptoms. The infections occur after sexual intercourse and resolve with antibiotic therapy. She has no other history of serious illness and takes no medications. She is currently sexually active with one male partner, and they use condoms consistently. Examination shows bladder tenderness and urethral elevation; no other abnormalities are noted. Urine culture grows Escherichia coli. Which of the following is most likely to prevent recurrence of these symptoms?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Obtain a swab of the urethra of the patient's partner</br>b. Prescribe an antibiotic to be taken following sexual intercourse</br>c. Perform cystoscopy to rule out detrusor instability</br>d. Perform cystoscopy to rule out anatomic cause</br>e. Recommend weekly self-catheterization to determine postvoid residual volume</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Prescribe an antibiotic to be taken following sexual intercourse  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “24-year-old nulligravid woman” – young, healthy, no anatomic/neurologic RFs<br /></br>• “1-year history of frequent bladder infections … occur after sexual intercourse” – classic post-coital cystitis pattern<br /></br>• “Resolve with antibiotic therapy” – confirms true bacterial (E coli) UTIs, not interstitial cystitis<br /></br>• “Uses condoms consistently” – barrier contraception does NOT eliminate mechanical inoculation from intercourse<br /></br>Key takeaway: Recurrent, culture-proven, uncomplicated cystitis temporally linked to intercourse → post-coital antibiotic prophylaxis is the USMLE-tested prevention strategy.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Stepwise)<br /></br>1. Establish “recurrent” UTI: ≥2 infections in 6 mo or ≥3 in 1 yr.<br /></br>2. Identify clear trigger (sex) and absence of complicating factors (anatomic abnormality, nephrolithiasis, neurogenic bladder, pregnancy).<br /></br>3. Guideline-directed prevention:<br /></br>   • Behavioral: void pre/post intercourse, increase fluids (patient very likely already counseled).<br /></br>   • Pharmacologic: single, low-dose antibiotic within 2 hr after sex (TMP-SMX 80/400 mg, nitrofurantoin 50-100 mg, or cephalexin 250 mg).<br /></br>   • Daily prophylaxis considered only when infections are not temporally linked to intercourse.</p></br><p>Core concept: Post-coital antibiotic prophylaxis is first-line for sexually-associated recurrent cystitis in otherwise healthy women.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• During intercourse, peri-urethral/vaginal E coli are mechanically propelled into the bladder.<br /></br>• Short female urethra and peri-urethral colonization → rapid ascent.<br /></br>• Recurrent infections occur because inoculum re-introduces bacteria faster than host defenses can clear.<br /></br>• Single post-coital antibiotic dose sterilizes urine before bacterial replication reaches symptomatic threshold.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line regimens<br /></br>• TMP-SMX SS 1 tab<br /></br>• Nitrofurantoin 50-100 mg<br /></br>• Cephalexin 250 mg<br /></br>Alternative strategies<br /></br>• Daily prophylaxis if not intercourse-related.<br /></br>• Vaginal estrogen in post-menopausal women.<br /></br>Diagnostics not required unless red flags (hematuria, stones, neuro defects) appear.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Pee, Pill, Protection” for post-coital cystitis:<br /></br>1. Pee after sex<br /></br>2. Pill (single antibiotic)<br /></br>3. Protection already used (condom, diaphragm removal, etc.)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Obtain a swab of the urethra of the patient’s partner<br /></br>• Asymptomatic bacteriuria in men is not treated; partner treatment does not prevent female UTIs.  </p></br><p>c. Perform cystoscopy to rule out detrusor instability<br /></br>• Detrusor overactivity causes urgency incontinence, not infection; cystoscopy evaluates mucosa, not bladder dynamics. Urodynamics, not cystoscopy, assesses instability.  </p></br><p>d. Perform cystoscopy to rule out anatomic cause<br /></br>• Indicated for complicated UTIs (stones, hematuria, obstruction) or structural lesions. Young woman with culture-proven, straightforward infections lacks red-flag features.  </p></br><p>e. Recommend weekly self-catheterization to determine postvoid residual volume<br /></br>• Elevated PVR predisposes to infections in neurogenic bladder or obstruction. No voiding difficulty or neurologic disease is described. Self-catheterization introduces infection risk.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In healthy women with recurrent, post-coital, culture-proven cystitis, the most effective and guideline-endorsed preventive measure is a single prophylactic antibiotic dose taken immediately after intercourse.</p>
189. An asymptomatic 32-year-old man comes to the physician 2 days after his male sexual partner was diagnosed with gonorrhea. He has not had penile discharge or dysuria. Over the past 4 months, he and his partner have had unprotected sexual intercourse several times weekly. Four months ago, HIV testing of the patient and his partner was negative. Examination shows no abnormalities. A Gram stain of mucus from a urethral swab is negative for diplococci. A urethral culture is ordered. He gives written consent for HIV antibody testing and serologic testing for syphilis. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in therapy?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Withhold any treatment until results of the culture are known</br>b. Ceftriaxone only</br>c. Ceftriaxone and doxycycline</br>d. Ceftriaxone and metronidazole</br>e. Ciprofloxacin only</br>F. Ciprofloxacin and doxycycline</br>g. Doxycycline only</br>h. Doxycycline and metronidazole</br></br></br><p>Correct answer:  “Withhold any treatment until results of the culture are known”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Asymptomatic … no penile discharge or dysuria” –‐ he does NOT currently have urethritis.<br /></br>• “Gram stain … negative for diplococci” –‐ a rapid bedside test did not demonstrate N. gonorrhoeae.<br /></br>• “Urethral culture is ordered” –‐ definitive diagnostic test is pending.<br /></br>• “Sexual partner recently diagnosed with gonorrhoea” –‐ he is a contact, but not (yet) a proven case.<br /></br>Key point the NBME is testing: When the patient is asymptomatic and the immediate smear is negative, wait for the culture before treating to avoid unnecessary antibiotics.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach the examiners expect:<br /></br>1. Ask: Does the patient currently meet criteria for empiric therapy?<br /></br>   ‣ Empiric therapy is indicated if the contact is within 30 d <strong>and</strong> the index case is proven, <strong>unless</strong> a reliable test (culture/NAAT) will be available before the partner is likely to be lost to follow-up.<br /></br>2. Here, you already have the partner in the office, have obtained consent, and a culture will be back in 24–48 h; therefore you can safely defer.<br /></br>3. Give no antibiotics now → re-evaluate once the culture returns.</p></br><p>Core takeaway:  In asymptomatic contacts whose definitive test is pending and follow-up is assured, defer treatment until culture results are known.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (concise)<br /></br>• Neisseria gonorrhoeae colonises urethral mucosa → acute urethritis (dysuria, purulent discharge).<br /></br>• Gram-negative diplococci may be seen on smear; culture or NAAT is confirmatory.<br /></br>• Why you might withhold therapy: unnecessary ceftriaxone fosters resistance and exposes patient to side-effects; chlamydial co-infection prevalence is lower in truly asymptomatic contacts who ultimately test negative for gonorrhoea.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Current (2024 CDC) practice actually treats all sexual contacts empirically (ceftriaxone ± doxycycline). However, the NBME item is keyed to an <strong>older guideline</strong>:<br /></br>— Symptomatic or smear-positive: treat immediately.<br /></br>— Asymptomatic, smear-negative and guaranteed follow-up: wait for culture.<br /></br>Diagnostic confirmation: urethral culture or NAAT; also screen for HIV, syphilis, chlamydia.</p></br><p>Memorisation Tips<br /></br>“Smear shows spheres? Shoot (treat) now.<br /></br>Smear is clear &amp; you can steer (follow-up)? Sit tight.”  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis (distractors)<br /></br>B. Ceftriaxone only – would be correct if you had already confirmed gonorrhoea or elected empiric therapy; premature here.<br /></br>C. Ceftriaxone + doxycycline – guideline-driven empiric regimen for presumed N. gonorrhoeae/Chlamydia coinfection, but overtreatment given the negative smear and pending culture.<br /></br>D. Ceftriaxone + metronidazole – metronidazole is for Trichomonas or anaerobes, not indicated.<br /></br>E. Ciprofloxacin only – fluoroquinolones are no longer recommended because of resistance.<br /></br>F. Ciprofloxacin + doxycycline – same resistance issue plus unnecessary therapy.<br /></br>G. Doxycycline only – covers Chlamydia, not gonorrhoea; would miss N. gonorrhoeae if present.<br /></br>H. Doxycycline + metronidazole – treats Chlamydia and Trichomonas, misses gonorrhoea.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: believing all contacts always require immediate empiric therapy. The NBME expects you to recognise the scenario in which reliable follow-up allows culture confirmation first.</p>
190. An unconscious 32-year-old woman is found by a nurse outside the emergency department. There is a gunshot wound to her right temple. She does not have an advance directive or an organ donor card among her possessions. On arrival, she is unresponsive and has a Glasgow Coma Scale score of 3. Her pulse is 48/min, respirations are 8/min, and blood pressure is 180/100 mm Hg. Pupils are dilated and nonreactive to light. She is intubated, and mechanical ventilation is started. After fluid resuscitation, her hemodynamic status is stabilized. A CT scan of the head shows a large intracranial hematoma with a massive midline shift. After consulting with a neurosurgeon, the most appropriate next step is to contact which of the following?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Hospital ethics committee</br>b. Local police department</br>c. Medical examiner</br>d. Municipal court</br>e. Organ procurement specialist</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Hospital ethics committee  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Unconscious … no advance directive … no organ-donor card” → patient lacks decision-making capacity and has no readily identifiable surrogate decision-maker.<br /></br>• “Massive midline shift … pupils dilated, non-reactive … GCS 3” → devastating neurologic injury, but the patient is still alive (has a pulse and respirations after intubation).<br /></br>• “After consulting with a neurosurgeon” → a decision about pursuing (or foregoing) heroic neurosurgical intervention must now be made.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Determine capacity: The patient is clearly incapacitated.<br /></br>Step 2 – Look for an advance directive or surrogate: None is available.<br /></br>Step 3 – Identify next best surrogate mechanism:<br /></br> • Most states follow a statutory hierarchy (spouse → adult children → parents → siblings → …); when nobody in that chain is immediately available, the clinician should not make unilateral end-of-life decisions.<br /></br> • A hospital ethics committee (or ethics consult service) is specifically designed to advise on complex cases without a surrogate, help establish a patient-centered “best-interest” standard, and document the rationale for the final plan of care.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: When an incapacitated patient has no advance directive and no identifiable surrogate, hospital policy (backed by state law) generally requires an ethics consultation before life-sustaining therapy is withdrawn or before proceeding with high-risk invasive procedures.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (brief)<br /></br>Penetrating head trauma with midline shift suggests uncontrolled intracranial hypertension and herniation → poor prognosis for meaningful neurologic recovery. Whether to attempt decompressive craniectomy or transition to comfort care hinges on values-based decisions, not purely medical facts—hence the ethics consultation.  </p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Emergent options: decompressive craniectomy, hyperosmolar therapy, hypothermia trials, etc.—all invasive and resource-intensive.<br /></br>• Ethics committee involvement guides whether those treatments align with the presumed wishes/best interests of the patient.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“No capacity + No directive + No surrogate = ‘Call the Committee.’”<br /></br>Think: C-C-C (Capacity lost, Chart empty of directives, Caregivers absent → Committee).  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Local police department<br /></br> • Mandatory reporting of gunshot wounds is required, but it does not take priority over addressing the immediate surrogate-decision-making vacuum that will dictate urgent neurosurgical care. Notification can occur in parallel or afterward; it does not help resolve the current ethical dilemma.  </p></br><p>c. Medical examiner<br /></br> • The medical examiner is notified after a patient dies from a violent injury. The patient is still alive.  </p></br><p>d. Municipal court<br /></br> • A court-appointed guardian is possible, but courts are slow; hospital ethics committees exist precisely to avoid delays in emergent situations.  </p></br><p>e. Organ procurement specialist<br /></br> • Federal law mandates notifying the OPO once death is imminent or life-support withdrawal is planned, but organ donation discussions begin only after the decision to withdraw care is made—not before treatment goals have been clarified.  </p></br><p>By recognizing the absence of both capacity and a surrogate, the ethics committee becomes the appropriate immediate resource for guiding time-sensitive, value-laden clinical decisions.</p>
191. A 45-year-old woman comes to the office because of a wound on her right foot that she first noticed 8 weeks ago. During the past 3 days, it has increased in size and depth and has become moderately painful. She does not recall any injury to her foot. She has type 2 diabetes mellitus and hypertension. Her medications are insulin, atorvastatin, lisinopril, and daily aspirin. She is 163 cm 5 ft 4 in tall and weighs 95 kg 210 lb; BMI is 36 kg/m2. Temperature is 38.9 C 102.0 F, pulse is 80/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 140/95 mm Hg. Examination shows a 3 x 3-cm full-thickness wound over the second and third metatarsal heads of the plantar aspect of the right foot; there is necrotic tissue and eschar but no exposed tendon or bone. The plantar aspect of the right forefoot is tender to palpation. Dorsalis pedis and posterior tibial pulses in the right lower extremity are 1+. Semmes-Weinstein 5.07 monofilament testing shows absent sensation over the right foot. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Arterial Doppler ultrasonography of the lower extremities</br>b. Arteriography of the lower extremities</br>c. Electromyography and nerve conduction studies</br>d. MRI of the right foot</br>e. Triple-phase bone scan</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>a. Arterial Doppler ultrasonography of the lower extremities (ankle-brachial index ± segmental wave-form analysis)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis – Key Diagnostic Clues</br>• Long-standing type 2 diabetes mellitus → high risk for both neuropathy and peripheral arterial disease (PAD).<br /></br>• Full-thickness plantar ulcer with necrotic tissue/eschar → typical “neuropathic/ischemic” diabetic foot ulcer.<br /></br>• Absent monofilament sensation → confirms peripheral neuropathy (explains why she did not feel trauma).<br /></br>• Dorsalis pedis &amp; posterior tibial pulses only 1+ and foot is tender → suggests marginal arterial perfusion in addition to infection.<br /></br>• Fever 38.9 °C → infected ulcer, but successful treatment (debridement, antibiotics) will fail unless adequate blood flow is documented.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise approach to a diabetic foot ulcer:</br>1. Identify/treat infection (empiric antibiotics, wound cultures).<br /></br>2. Determine if the ulcer has adequate arterial inflow; debridement and healing require it.<br /></br>   • First-line, non-invasive test = ankle-brachial index (ABI) with duplex Doppler.<br /></br>   • If ABI ≤0.9 (or waveform abnormal), then proceed to CT/MR angiography or formal arteriography for possible revascularization.<br /></br>3. Only after confirming perfusion do you consider extensive surgical debridement, amputation level, or advanced imaging for osteomyelitis.</p></br><p>Therefore the most appropriate next step in diagnosis is an arterial Doppler study.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Diabetes accelerates atherosclerosis → PAD (macrovascular disease).<br /></br>• Neuropathy → loss of protective sensation + foot deformities → plantar pressure points → ulceration.<br /></br>• Local ischemia impairs leukocyte function &amp; tissue oxygenation → rapid infection &amp; poor healing.<br /></br>ABI &lt;0.9 = PAD; &lt;0.4 = critical ischemia. Revascularization (angioplasty, bypass) markedly improves ulcer healing and limb salvage.</p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests</br>First-line<br /></br>• Wound culture, broad-spectrum IV antibiotics.<br /></br>• Off-loading (total-contact cast, special shoe).<br /></br>• Doppler ABI.<br /></br>Second-line (if ABI abnormal)<br /></br>• CTA/MRA or conventional arteriography → endovascular vs. open bypass.<br /></br>• After perfusion optimized → sharp debridement, possible skin graft, long-term antibiotics if osteomyelitis.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“Diabetic FOOT”<br /></br>F – Feel pulses (vascular) → Doppler ABI first<br /></br>O – Off-load pressure<br /></br>O – Osteomyelitis search (MRI) only after pulses checked<br /></br>T – Treat infection / Tight glucose</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>b. Arteriography of the lower extremities – Invasive, iodine contrast; reserved for planning an intervention after non-invasive Doppler shows significant PAD.</p></br><p>c. Electromyography &amp; nerve conduction – Would confirm neuropathy already evident by monofilament test; does not influence immediate ulcer management.</p></br><p>d. MRI of the right foot – Best test for osteomyelitis (high sensitivity/specificity), but ischemia must be ruled out first; MRI is often nondiagnostic if severe ischemia and will not guide revascularization.</p></br><p>e. Triple-phase bone scan – Less specific than MRI, high false-positives; same issue regarding ordering sequence and inferior modality for bone infection.</p></br><p>Take-Home Message</br>In an infected diabetic foot ulcer, ALWAYS assess arterial perfusion non-invasively (ABI/Doppler) before advanced imaging or surgical debridement—adequate blood flow is the cornerstone of successful healing and limb salvage.</p>
192. A 27-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 6-month history of increasing pain and progressive swelling and stiffness of her ankles. Despite nightly application of ice to her ankles, ibuprofen therapy, and decreased activity during the past 3 months, her pain has worsened, she has had swelling of her ankles throughout the day, and she has had morning stiffness of her ankles. She has sustained no recent trauma to her ankles. She had a severe right ankle sprain in college. Her only other medication is an oral contraceptive. She is an avid tennis player and plays twice weekly. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows swelling and moderate effusion of both ankles. Range of motion of both ankles is full but produces mild pain. X-rays of the ankles are shown. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's symptoms?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Gonococcal arthritis</br>b. Gouty arthritis</br>c. Lyme disease</br>d. Osteomyelitis</br>e. Rheumatoid arthritis</br>F. Transient osteoporosis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Rheumatoid arthritis  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Chronic course – 6-month history that is steadily progressive despite rest and NSAIDs → points to a chronic inflammatory arthropathy rather than an acute infectious or crystal-induced process.<br /></br>• Symmetry – bilateral ankle swelling/effusions. Symmetric involvement of small/medium joints is classic for rheumatoid arthritis (RA).<br /></br>• Inflammatory features – “morning stiffness” that lasts (not relieved within a few minutes) + pain that improves a bit with activity are hallmark clues for RA.<br /></br>• Young woman – peak incidence of RA is in women age 20-40.<br /></br>• Imaging – although the radiograph is not shown here, typical early RA ankle films are either normal or show peri-articular osteopenia and soft-tissue swelling; late films show marginal erosions. Absence of bony overgrowth or calcifications argues against osteoarthritis or gout.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify that the pain is inflammatory rather than mechanical (morning stiffness, swelling, effusion).<br /></br>2. Determine joint pattern: symmetric, peripheral, &gt;1 joint → think RA or seronegative spondyloarthropathy.<br /></br>3. Age &amp; sex fit RA; lack of back pain, dactylitis, uveitis, psoriasis, or bowel disease makes seronegative spondyloarthropathies unlikely.<br /></br>4. Duration &gt;6 weeks satisfies the chronicity criterion for RA.  </p></br><p>Core concept takeaway: Symmetric, chronic, inflammatory arthritis with morning stiffness in a young woman = rheumatoid arthritis until proven otherwise.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Autoimmune synovitis driven by autoantibodies (rheumatoid factor, anti-cyclic citrullinated peptide).<br /></br>• Persistent synovial inflammation (pannus) → cartilage destruction, marginal bone erosions, and joint space narrowing.<br /></br>• Systemic manifestations: fatigue, weight loss, anemia of chronic disease, nodules, pleural effusion, vasculitis.  </p></br><p>Diagnostics (board level)<br /></br>• Best initial lab tests: ESR/CRP (↑), rheumatoid factor (IgM against Fc of IgG), anti-CCP (most specific).<br /></br>• Imaging: X-ray or ultrasound shows peri-articular osteopenia early; erosions and joint space loss later.<br /></br>• Arthrocentesis: inflammatory (2,000-50,000 WBCs) but sterile fluid.  </p></br><p>Treatment (testable hierarchy)<br /></br>1. Immediate symptom relief: NSAID or short taper of oral prednisone.<br /></br>2. Disease-modifying therapy started as soon as diagnosis is suspected:<br /></br>   – First-line synthetic DMARD: methotrexate (add folic acid).<br /></br>   – Alternatives/add-ons: leflunomide, sulfasalazine, hydroxychloroquine.<br /></br>3. Biologic DMARDs for inadequate response or aggressive disease (eg, TNF-α inhibitors such as etanercept, adalimumab; IL-6 inhibitor tocilizumab; CTLA-4-Ig abatacept; anti-CD20 rituximab).<br /></br>4. Non-pharmacologic: physical therapy, smoking cessation, vaccinations (pneumococcal, influenza, hepatitis B, zoster).  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Morning ankle pain that walks away” → think RA (pain/stiffness improves as the patient “walks away” during the day).<br /></br>RA = 3 S’s: Symmetric, Small joints, Stiff in the morning.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Gonococcal arthritis – Usually presents acutely with migratory polyarthralgia, tenosynovitis, or single hot purulent joint; systemic features (fever, pustular rash) common. Not a chronic 6-month symmetric process.<br /></br>b. Gouty arthritis – Acute, episodic flares; classically monoarticular (1st MTP), very painful with red, swollen joint. Rare in young women (estrogen increases uric acid excretion). X-ray would show punched-out erosions with overhanging edges in chronic disease.<br /></br>c. Lyme disease – Mono- or oligoarticular (knee &gt; ankle), usually months after tick exposure, may have erythema migrans, facial palsy. Serology would be positive; course can be chronic but typically not symmetric.<br /></br>d. Osteomyelitis – Bone infection causes localized pain, fever, elevated WBC/ESR; radiographs may show lytic lesions or periosteal reaction. Not bilateral symmetric ankle effusions.<br /></br>f. Transient osteoporosis – Self-limited hip pain in late pregnancy or middle-aged men; MRI shows diffuse marrow edema, plain films show osteopenia of the femoral head, NOT ankle effusions.  </p></br><p>Bottom line: The combination of (1) young woman, (2) chronic symmetrical inflammatory ankle involvement, (3) morning stiffness points squarely to rheumatoid arthritis.</p>
193. A previously healthy 24-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 2-week history of fatigue and easy bruising. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 100/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 100/60 mm Hg. Examination shows ecchymoses over the lower extremities. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin: 9.2 g/dL</br>Leukocyte count: 53,000 /mm3</br>Lymphocytes: 10 percent</br>Myeloblasts: 90 percent</br>Platelet count: 63,000 /mm3</br>Serum</br>Creatinine: 1.9 mg/dL</br>Lactate dehydrogenase: 2,233 U/L</br>A bone marrow biopsy specimen confirms acute myelogenous leukemia. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Hydration and urine alkalinization</br>b. Renal ultrasonography</br>c. Transfusion of platelets</br>d. Transfusion of 2 units of packed red blood cells</br>e. Induction chemotherapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>Hydration and urine alkalinization</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Young adult with de novo acute myelogenous leukemia (AML)</br>  – WBC 53,000/mm³ with 90 % myeloblasts → huge circulating tumor burden<br /></br>  – LDH 2,233 U/L (very high) → marker of rapid cell turnover<br /></br>  – Cr 1.9 mg/dL → early renal injury<br /></br>• Two-week history, no prior treatment → risk of spontaneous and imminent treatment-related tumor lysis syndrome (TLS).<br /></br>• No active bleeding, platelets 63 K (not &lt;10 K), Hb 9.2 (mild, stable), vitals OK.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Identify emergency threats in AML.</br>  a. Leukostasis (usually WBC &gt;100 K with dyspnea/neuro deficits) – not present.<br /></br>  b. DIC (classically APL) – no bleeding, no labs provided.<br /></br>  c. Tumor lysis syndrome → hyperuricemia → obstructive uric-acid nephropathy; already sees rising Cr &amp; high LDH.</p></br><p>Step 2: Prevent TLS before giving cytotoxic chemotherapy.</br>  • Saturate the kidneys with isotonic fluids (≥2-3 L/m²/day).<br /></br>  • Alkalinize urine (target pH &gt;7) + give xanthine-oxidase inhibitor (allopurinol or febuxostat) ± rasburicase.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Massive lysis of blasts releases K⁺, PO₄³⁻, and nucleic acids → purines → uric acid.<br /></br>• Uric acid is poorly soluble in acidic distal tubules/collecting ducts, precipitating crystals → acute obstructive nephropathy and further renal failure.<br /></br>• Alkalinization (IV NaHCO₃) converts uric acid → soluble urate; aggressive hydration maintains high urine flow, flushing crystals.</p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests</br>1. Immediate: IV crystalloids + urine alkalinization + allopurinol/rasburicase.<br /></br>2. Monitor chemistries (K⁺, Ca²⁺, PO₄³⁻, uric acid) every 4–6 h.<br /></br>3. Once kidney protected, start induction chemotherapy (e.g., “7+3” cytarabine + anthracycline).<br /></br>4. Platelet transfusion if &lt;10 K or active bleed; PRBCs if symptomatic or Hb &lt;7–8 g/dL.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“Lush FLUIDS Before Lush BLASTS”<br /></br>FLUIDS/alkaline urine (+Allopurinol) must flow before you blast the marrow with chemo.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>b. Renal ultrasonography – Useful for obstructive uropathy or renal mass; does nothing to prevent TLS or correct rising creatinine from uric-acid precipitation.<br /></br>c. Platelet transfusion – Indicated if &lt;10 K or bleeding; 63 K with only ecchymoses = observe. Does not address impending renal failure.<br /></br>d. Packed red cells – Hb 9.2 g/dL in a stable young woman rarely requires transfusion.<br /></br>e. Induction chemotherapy – Definitive therapy for AML but will precipitate fulminant TLS given current renal compromise. Always implement TLS prophylaxis first.</p></br><p>Take-Home Message</br>In high-burden leukemias (AML, ALL) with elevated LDH and/or rising creatinine, the FIRST lifesaving step is TLS prophylaxis—aggressive hydration and urine alkalinization (plus allopurinol/rasburicase). Chemotherapy can wait a few hours; kidneys cannot.</p>
194. A 20-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 3-year history of bruising without any trauma. He has not had bleeding from his nose or mouth. He has no histo [Y of serious illness and takes no medications. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows ecchymoses in various stages of healing over the upper extremities. Results of a complete blood count are within the reference range. His prothrombin time is 20 seconds INR=2, and partial thromboplastin time is 25 seconds. This patient most likely has a deficiency of which of the following?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Factor V</br>b. Factor VII</br>c. Factor VIII</br>d. Factor IX</br>e. Factor X</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>B. Factor VII  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key laboratory clue: Prothrombin time (PT) 20 s, INR 2 (↑) with a normal activated partial thromboplastin time (aPTT) 25 s (nl).<br /></br>• Normal platelets &amp; CBC ⇒ not a quantitative platelet disorder.<br /></br>• Easy bruising over several years ⇒ mild–moderate bleeding diathesis rather than life-threatening hemophilia.  </p></br><p>Pattern to recognize:<br /></br>Isolated prolongation of PT (extrinsic pathway) with a normal aPTT almost always localizes the defect to Factor VII.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. PT assesses extrinsic (Factor VII) + common pathway (Factors I, II, V, X).<br /></br>2. If a common-pathway factor were missing (V or X), BOTH PT &amp; aPTT would be prolonged.<br /></br>3. If an intrinsic-pathway factor (VIII, IX, XI, XII) were missing, ONLY aPTT would be prolonged.<br /></br>4. Therefore the only single-factor abnormality that yields ↑PT with normal aPTT is Factor VII deficiency.  </p></br><p>Take-home message:<br /></br>Isolated ↑PT → think Factor VII deficiency (or very early vitamin K deficiency/warfarin effect). On exams, chronic presentation in a young adult with no drug use or malabsorption = inherited Factor VII deficiency.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Factor VII: Vitamin K–dependent serine protease that complexes with tissue factor to initiate coagulation (extrinsic arm).<br /></br>• Shortest half-life of all clotting factors ⇒ first to drop with vit K deficiency or warfarin, but congenital quantitative/qualitative deficiencies also exist (rare, AR).<br /></br>• Clinical picture: easy bruising, mucocutaneous bleeding; severe hemarthroses less common than in hemophilia A/B.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>• Confirm with specific Factor VII activity assay.<br /></br>• Acute bleeding or peri-operative coverage:<br /></br>  – Recombinant activated Factor VIIa (rFVIIa)<br /></br>  – Prothrombin complex concentrate (PCC) or fresh-frozen plasma (FFP) if rFVIIa unavailable<br /></br>• Long-term: prophylactic rFVIIa in severe cases.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“SEVEN is the single Stranger”:<br /></br>• S = Single pathway abnormality (extrinsic only)<br /></br>• T = prolongs PT (“T” for Tissue factor arm)<br /></br>• rANGE (aPTT) = Normal<br /></br>• ER = Extrinsic Ranger  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Factor V<br /></br>• Common pathway factor → would prolong BOTH PT &amp; aPTT. Normal aPTT here rules it out.  </p></br><p>C. Factor VIII (Hemophilia A)<br /></br>• Intrinsic pathway → isolated ↑aPTT, normal PT. Patient’s PT, not aPTT, is abnormal.  </p></br><p>D. Factor IX (Hemophilia B)<br /></br>• Same reasoning as VIII: intrinsic defect causes ↑aPTT only.  </p></br><p>E. Factor X<br /></br>• Common pathway → ↑PT AND ↑aPTT.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Thinking “vitamin K deficiency” whenever PT alone is high. Early vit K deficiency can indeed appear this way, but a chronic 3-year history in an otherwise healthy young adult with normal diet/absorption and no drug exposure points toward congenital Factor VII deficiency.</p>
195. A 42-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 3-year history of ringing in her ears. The sound gradually intensified during the first year and has been unchanged since that time. The ringing is more noticeable when she is in a quiet environment but otherwise does not fluctuate throughout the day. She reports that her hearing is otherwise normal. She has hypertension treated with lisinopril. She has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 25 years and drinks one glass of wine daily. Her blood pressure is 130/80 mm Hg; other vital signs are within normal limits. Examination of the external auditory canals shows no abnormalities. Finger-rub testing shows no abnormalities. Audiometry shows mild high-frequency hearing loss bilaterally. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. MRI of the brain and internal auditory canals</br>b. Brain stem auditory evoked response</br>c. Electronystagmography</br>d. MR angiography of the brain</br>e. No further testing is necessary</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>MRI of the brain and internal auditory canals (with gadolinium)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Ring­ing in her ears” for years = chronic, non-pulsatile tinnitus.<br /></br>• Audiometry: “mild high-frequency hearing loss” = sensorineural pattern.<br /></br>• Middle-aged patient (42 yr) – too young for presbycusis; acoustic neuroma (vestibular schwannoma) must be excluded.<br /></br>• No cerumen, infection, or conductive deficit on exam; therefore the lesion is retro-cochlear.<br /></br>Key testable pattern: Non-pulsatile tinnitus + sensorineural hearing loss (especially if unilateral or asymmetric) → image the cerebellopontine angle/internal auditory canal.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1  Identify that the hearing loss is sensorineural (high-frequency, normal external canal/tympanic membrane).<br /></br>Step 2  Recall red-flag indications for imaging in tinnitus:<br /></br>   – Unilateral or asymmetric tinnitus<br /></br>   – Any sensorineural loss not clearly explained (young age, progressive)<br /></br>   – Associated focal neurologic signs<br /></br>Step 3  MRI with contrast is the most sensitive study for a vestibular schwannoma. It visualizes the nerve, cerebellopontine angle, and brainstem in one scan.<br /></br>Take-home message: Chronic non-pulsatile tinnitus with unexplained sensorineural loss → get an MRI of the brain &amp; internal auditory canals to rule out vestibular schwannoma.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Vestibular schwannoma<br /></br>• Benign Schwann-cell tumor on CN VIII (vestibular portion).<br /></br>• Grows in the internal auditory canal → cerebellopontine angle.<br /></br>• Classic triad: unilateral sensorineural hearing loss, tinnitus, imbalance.<br /></br>• Can compress CN V (face numbness) or CN VII (facial weakness) with growth.<br /></br>• Sporadic (95%) or associated with NF2 (bilateral tumors).</p></br><p>Treatments / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Diagnostic hierarchy<br /></br>1. Audiometry → detects sensorineural pattern.<br /></br>2. MRI with gadolinium of brain &amp; IAC → gold standard (≈ 100% sensitivity).<br /></br>3. BAER (brain-stem auditory evoked response) – historical screen; lower sensitivity.<br /></br>Management (after diagnosis)<br /></br>• &lt; 2 cm &amp; few symptoms → serial MRI (“wait-and-scan”) ± stereotactic radiosurgery.<br /></br>• Symptomatic or &gt; 2–3 cm → microsurgical resection.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Ring on ONE side, image the NERVE inside.” (Unilateral ringing → MRI IAC)<br /></br>“Pulsatile? Think pipes (angiography). Non-pulsatile? Think nerves (MRI).”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Brain-stem auditory evoked response (BAER)<br /></br>• Could detect retro-cochlear delay but misses small tumors; MRI is now preferred.</p></br><p>C. Electronystagmography<br /></br>• Evaluates vestibular end-organ function in vertigo (e.g., Ménière, BPPV), not isolated tinnitus.</p></br><p>D. MR angiography of the brain<br /></br>• Indicated for pulsatile (“whooshing”) tinnitus suggesting vascular malformation, dural AV fistula, carotid dissection—none present here.</p></br><p>E. No further testing necessary<br /></br>• Appropriate for bilateral, symmetric high-frequency loss in an elderly patient with benign tinnitus. At age 42 with unexplained sensorineural loss, imaging is required.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Any unexplained sensorineural hearing loss or unilateral/non-pulsatile tinnitus warrants MRI of the brain and internal auditory canals to rule out vestibular schwannoma.</p>
196. An 18-year-old basketball player is brought to the emergency department 1 hour after collapsing during a game. He was successfully resuscitated at the scene. On arrival, he is awake and alert. His temperature is 37.1  C 98.8 F, pulse is 80/min and regular, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 120/80 mm Hg. Examination shows no abnormalities. An ECG shows left ventricular hypertrophy. Echocardiography shows systolic anterior motion of the anterior leaflet of the mitral valve causing a subaortic obstruction. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Adrenergic blocking agent therapy</br>b. Placement of a permanent ventricular pacemaker</br>c. Aortic valve replacement</br>d. Mitral valve anuloplasty</br>e. Surgical excision of the endocardium of the left ventricular free wall</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Adrenergic-blocking agent therapy (i.e., a β-blocker such as propranolol, atenolol)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Young competitive athlete (18 y) who had exertional syncope/cardiac arrest ➜ classic presentation of hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy (HOCM).<br /></br>• Key echo finding: “systolic anterior motion (SAM) of the anterior mitral valve leaflet causing a subaortic obstruction” = pathognomonic for HOCM.<br /></br>• ECG: left-ventricular hypertrophy supports the diagnosis.<br /></br>The stem is therefore asking: what is the FIRST-LINE treatment once the diagnosis is made in a now-stable patient?</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Recognize HOCM.<br /></br>Step 2 – Know therapeutic hierarchy:<br /></br>  1. Negative inotropy/chronotropy (β-blockers first; non-DHP CCBs second)<br /></br>  2. If symptoms or gradient persist → invasive therapies (septal myectomy or alcohol ablation; ICD for sudden-death prevention).<br /></br>Thus, initial management in an otherwise stable patient = start β-blocker.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: β-blockers blunt sympathetic stimulation, slow HR (↑ diastolic filling time), and decrease LV contractility → outflow gradient falls and symptoms improve.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• Autosomal-dominant mutations in sarcomeric proteins (β-myosin heavy chain, myosin-binding protein-C, etc.).<br /></br>• Asymmetric septal hypertrophy + SAM of mitral valve = dynamic LVOT obstruction that worsens with ↓ preload/afterload or ↑ contractility (e.g., exercise, dehydration, standing, Valsalva).<br /></br>• Risk: ventricular tachyarrhythmias → sudden death in young athletes.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line (symptomatic or post-arrest but hemodynamically stable)<br /></br>• β-blocker: propranolol, atenolol, metoprolol.<br /></br>  – ↓ HR &amp; contractility, ↑ LV end-diastolic volume → ↓ obstruction.<br /></br>• Alternatives if β-blocker not tolerated: verapamil or disopyramide.<br /></br>Additional measures<br /></br>• Strict avoidance of high-intensity athletics.<br /></br>• Screen 1° relatives with echo/ECG.<br /></br>• ICD placement for secondary prevention in anyone who has survived cardiac arrest or sustained VT (USMLE often tests this; however, the “NEXT STEP” in a vignette that focuses on symptomatic relief usually remains pharmacologic unless explicitly asking about sudden-death prevention devices).<br /></br>Invasive options (refractory cases)<br /></br>• Surgical septal myectomy or percutaneous alcohol septal ablation.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Hypertrophic heart likes to B-BLOCK”<br /></br>• HOCM → β-Blocker first; if can’t → “Very Difficult” = Verapamil, Disopyramide.<br /></br>Dynamic obstruction gets Better with Beta-Blocker and Bad with exertion, standing, Valsalva.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Permanent ventricular pacemaker<br /></br>• Dual-chamber pacing may modestly reduce gradient but is NOT first-line; considered only when drugs fail or concomitant conduction disease exists.</p></br><p>c. Aortic valve replacement<br /></br>• Obstruction is below (subaortic), not the valve itself; valve leaflets are normal.</p></br><p>d. Mitral valve annuloplasty<br /></br>• SAM gives functional MR but the mitral apparatus is structurally normal; fixing the annulus does not relieve LVOT obstruction.</p></br><p>e. Surgical excision of the endocardium of the LV free wall<br /></br>• The hypertrophied interventricular septum, not the free wall, causes obstruction. Correct surgery (if needed) would be septal (Morrow) myectomy, pursued only after failure of medical therapy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>First therapeutic step for a stable patient with HOCM is to start a β-blocker (adrenergic-blocking agent) to decrease LVOT obstruction and prevent further exertional events.</p>
197. A 1-year-old boy is brought to the office because his parents are concerned that his birthmarks have been increasing in size and number during the past 6 months. Growth and dewelopment are appropriate for age. Immunizations are up-to-date. He has no histo [Y of serious illness and receives no medications. Examination shows ten 6- to 10-mm tan-brown maculae over the patient's trunk, axillae, and all extremities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. CT scan of the abdomen</br>b. Echocardiography</br>c. EEG</br>d. Ophthalmologic examination</br>e. Renal ultrasonography</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selected<br /></br>d. Ophthalmologic examination  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – “Ten 6- to 10-mm tan-brown maculae” → café-au-lait spots<br /></br>  – Axillary/truncal distribution; number ≥6 and diameter &gt;5 mm in a pre-pubertal child → meets NIH diagnostic criterion for Neurofibromatosis type 1 (NF1).<br /></br>• No other concerning symptoms yet (vision loss, neurologic deficits), so this is an asymptomatic 1-year-old who has just met clinical criteria for NF1.  </p></br><p>Recognizing the Pattern<br /></br>Café-au-lait spots + axillary freckling ± Lisch nodules ± neurofibromas = NF1.<br /></br>First evaluation after the skin findings are noted is an eye exam to look for:<br /></br>1. Optic pathway gliomas (most serious early complication)<br /></br>2. Iris hamartomas (Lisch nodules) – diagnostic but benign  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning / Take-away<br /></br>Step-wise approach in a newly diagnosed NF1 toddler:<br /></br>1. Confirm diagnostic criteria (≥6 café-au-lait spots, etc.)<br /></br>2. Screen for silent but vision-threatening complications → comprehensive ophthalmologic examination (including dilated fundus) by an ophthalmologist.<br /></br>3. Establish longitudinal surveillance (annual BP, yearly vision check, developmental monitoring).  </p></br><p>Core concept tested: NF1 requires early and regular ophthalmologic screening; optic gliomas can be present before symptoms appear, and early detection improves outcomes.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology in 60 sec<br /></br>• Gene: NF1 tumor-suppressor gene on chromosome 17 → codes neurofibromin (RAS-GAP).<br /></br>• Inheritance: Autosomal dominant, complete penetrance, variable expressivity; ~50 % de novo.<br /></br>• Major manifestations<br /></br>  – Pigmentary: café-au-lait spots, intertriginous freckling<br /></br>  – Ophthalmologic: Lisch nodules, optic pathway glioma<br /></br>  – Neurocutaneous: cutaneous / plexiform neurofibromas<br /></br>  – Skeletal: sphenoid wing dysplasia, tibial bowing<br /></br>  – Others: learning disabilities, ADHD, pheochromocytoma (later).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostic &amp; Management Pearls<br /></br>Initial work-up once diagnostic criteria met:<br /></br>• Ophthalmology annually (start immediately)<br /></br>• Annual blood pressure (screen for renal artery stenosis / pheo)<br /></br>• Developmental assessment + educational support<br /></br>• Imaging (MRI brain/orbits) ONLY if concerning eye exam or neurologic findings; routine baseline MRI not recommended.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Quick Mnemonic<br /></br>C A F É<br /></br>C – Café-au-lait spots<br /></br>A – Axillary freckling<br /></br>F – Fibromas (neurofibromas)<br /></br>É – Eye exam (look for Lisch nodules/optic glioma)  </p></br><p>“See the café? Check the eyes!”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. CT scan of the abdomen<br /></br>   • Would screen for abdominal tumors (e.g., pheochromocytoma, neuroblastoma), but these arise later; not first-line and exposes child to radiation.  </p></br><p>b. Echocardiography<br /></br>   • Indicated in tuberous sclerosis (cardiac rhabdomyoma) or congenital heart disease, not for asymptomatic NF1.  </p></br><p>c. EEG<br /></br>   • Used for seizure work-up; seizures are uncommon in NF1 compared to tuberous sclerosis. No history of seizures here.  </p></br><p>e. Renal ultrasonography<br /></br>   • Used for Wilms tumor screening in Beckwith-Wiedemann/WAGR or renal angiomyolipomas in tuberous sclerosis; not routine in NF1.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Jumping to brain MRI or abdominal imaging before basic, low-risk screening; USMLE loves to test the simple, guideline-driven first step—here, an eye exam.</p>
198. Three hours after undergoing placement of an intravenous line using routine universal precautions and beginning intravenous fluid therapy, a hospitalized 2-year-old girl has the onset of a generalized itching rash. She was admitted to the hospital today because of a 3-day history of fever and diarrhea. As a newborn, she underwent surgical repair of a myelomeningocele and creation of a ventriculoperitoneal shunt. She is not currently receiving any medications. Vital signs 3 hours ago and now are:</br>3 Hours Ago – Temperature: 38  C 100.4  F; Pulse: 110 /min; Respirations: 24 /min; Blood pressure: 105/65 mm Hg</br>Now – Temperature: 37.8  C 100  F; Pulse: 160 /min; Respirations: 40 /min; Blood pressure: 80/50 mm Hg</br>Examination shows diffuse, raised, erythematous lesions with pale centers. The upper and lower extremities appear mottled. Capillary refill time is 5 to 6 seconds. The remainder of the examination, including funduscopic and neurologic examinations, shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is most likely to have prevented this patient's current condition?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Avoidance of latex gloves</br>b. Earlier administration of fluids</br>c. Intravenous administration of a corticosteroid</br>d. Intravenous administration of dopamine</br>e. Revision of the shunt</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a.  Avoidance of latex gloves  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Generalized itching rash … diffuse, raised, erythematous lesions with pale centers” = urticaria (wheal-and-flare).<br /></br>• Rapid progression to hypotension (80/50 mm Hg), tachycardia (160/min), prolonged cap refill → distributive shock from anaphylaxis.<br /></br>• Temporal clue: began “3 hours after” an IV was started by staff wearing “routine universal precautions” (standard = latex gloves).<br /></br>• Major past history: myelomeningocele + VP shunt ⇒ multiple surgeries and catheterizations → repeated latex exposure. Children with spina bifida develop IgE-mediated (type I) latex hypersensitivity in up to 70 %.  </p></br><p>Pattern: Child with spina bifida + new urticaria + hypotension shortly after a procedure involving latex → think “latex-induced anaphylaxis”.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-by-step)<br /></br>1. Identify shock type: warm → distributive; cold/mottled extremities here but still distributive early. Presence of hives points strongly to anaphylaxis.<br /></br>2. Find trigger: the only new exposure is HCW gloves and IV tubing ports—common hospital latex sources.<br /></br>3. High-risk host: spina bifida = classic board association with latex allergy.<br /></br>4. Prevention, not treatment, is asked: eliminate latex from the environment before any contact.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Patients with neural tube defects (esp. myelomeningocele) must be managed in a latex-free environment to prevent IgE-mediated anaphylaxis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Latex proteins act as allergens → cross-link preformed surface IgE on mast cells/basophils → massive histamine, tryptase, leukotriene release → vasodilation, ↑vascular permeability (urticaria, angioedema, hypotension, bronchospasm).<br /></br>• Risk factors:<br /></br>  – Spina bifida / urologic anomalies (repeated bladder cath)<br /></br>  – Multiple childhood surgeries<br /></br>  – Healthcare workers<br /></br>• Cross-reactive foods: banana, avocado, kiwi, chestnut (“BACK”).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (for real-life management)<br /></br>Acute reaction: 1) IM epinephrine 0.01 mg/kg, 2) airway support, 3) aggressive IV crystalloids, 4) adjuncts (H1/H2 blockers, corticosteroids).<br /></br>Prevention: strict latex-free protocol (non-latex gloves, IV ports, foley caths, stoppers on meds, etc.). Skin testing / serum specific-IgE can confirm allergy but are not needed in high-risk patients; simply avoid exposure.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Spine + Latex = Shock”: Kids born with an open spine (spina bifida) + latex exposure → anaphylactic shock.<br /></br>BACK foods cross-react with latex: Banana - Avocado - Chestnut - Kiwi.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Earlier administration of fluids<br /></br>   • Would help hypovolemic or septic shock, not prevent an IgE-mediated reaction. Fluids treat, not prevent, hypotension.<br /></br>c. Intravenous corticosteroid<br /></br>   • Steroids may blunt late-phase allergic symptoms but do not prevent immediate mast-cell degranulation when antigen is present.<br /></br>d. Intravenous dopamine<br /></br>   • Vasopressor for refractory shock; has no preventive role.<br /></br>e. Revision of the shunt<br /></br>   • VP shunt malfunction causes ↑ICP (vomiting, papilledema), not urticaria/hypotension. No temporal link with current event.</p></br><p>Key difference: Only latex avoidance removes the antigen and therefore truly prevents anaphylaxis in this high-risk child.</p>
199. A previously healthy 29-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 6-week history of intermittent, increasingly severe upper abdominal pain. He has not had fever, chills, weight loss, or intolerance to fatty food. Over-the-counter famotidine has provided minimal relief. He has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 10 years. His temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 68/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 120/82 mm Hg. Abdominal examination shows epigastric tenderness to palpation. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Test of the stool for occult blood is positive. Esophagogastroduodenoscopy shows a 1.5-cm ulcer along the lesser curvature of the stomach; there is no active bleeding. Biopsy results are shown. Smoking cessation is recommended. In addition to lansoprazole therapy, which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Amoxicillin and metronidazole</br>b. Bismuth subsalicylate and ciprofloxacin</br>c. Bismuth subsalicylate and tetracycline</br>d. Ciprofloxacin and metronidazole</br>e. Clarithromycin and amoxicillin</br>F. Tetracycline and clarithromycin</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Clarithromycin and amoxicillin  (given together with a PPI = standard “triple therapy” for H. pylori)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 6-week history of gnawing epigastric pain + stool guaiac (+) → suspect peptic ulcer disease.<br /></br>• Ulcer located on the lesser curvature of the stomach → most gastric ulcers and virtually all duodenal ulcers are linked to Helicobacter pylori.<br /></br>• “Biopsy results are shown” (not printed in stem) almost certainly depicts curved, flagellated, Gram-negative organisms (H. pylori) or positive rapid urease test.<br /></br>• No red-flag features (weight loss, anemia, obstructive symptoms) suggesting malignancy; therefore focus is eradication of H. pylori.  </p></br><p>Key pattern: symptomatic gastric ulcer in a young adult + biopsy evidence of H. pylori → initiate eradication regimen (PPI + two antibiotics).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1  Confirm ulcer is H. pylori–positive.<br /></br>Step 2  Choose a regimen that includes:<br /></br>  • potent acid suppression (lansoprazole given in stem)<br /></br>  • two antibiotics—standard first-line is clarithromycin + amoxicillin (or + metronidazole if pen-allergic).<br /></br>Step 3  Counsel on risk-factor modification (smoking cessation already recommended).</p></br><p>Core takeaway: On NBME/Step 2, “PPI + clarithromycin + amoxicillin” is first-line triple therapy when local clarithromycin resistance &lt;15%.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• H. pylori colonizes the gastric antrum (most commonly) → urease‐mediated NH₃ production buffers gastric acid → local inflammation, ↑gastrin → mucosal damage and ulcer formation.<br /></br>• Eradication heals ulcers and markedly lowers recurrence &amp; bleeding risk.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. First-line (classic) triple therapy x 14 days:<br /></br>   • PPI (omeprazole, lansoprazole, etc.)<br /></br>   • Clarithromycin 500 mg BID<br /></br>   • Amoxicillin 1 g BID (or metronidazole 500 mg TID if PCN allergy)</p></br><ol></br><li></br><p>Second-line/quadruple therapy (used for regions of high clarithro resistance or after failure):<br /></br>   • PPI + bismuth subsalicylate + metronidazole + tetracycline.</p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Verify eradication (urea breath test or stool antigen) ≥4 wks after therapy in complicated ulcers.  </p></br></li></br></ol></br><p>────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CAP the pylori” → Clarithromycin + Amoxicillin + PPI.<br /></br>If allergic to penicillin, “CMP” → Clarithro + Metronidazole + PPI.<br /></br>Quadruple = “PBMT” → PPI, Bismuth, Metronidazole, Tetracycline.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Amoxicillin + metronidazole<br /></br>   • Missing clarithromycin → not recommended first-line unless clarithro resistance very high AND a second agent (usually bismuth or tetracycline) is added. Inadequate eradication rate.</p></br><p>b. Bismuth subsalicylate + ciprofloxacin<br /></br>   • Ciprofloxacin is not part of any accepted H. pylori regimen; bismuth without metronidazole + tetracycline is incomplete.</p></br><p>c. Bismuth subsalicylate + tetracycline<br /></br>   • Quadruple therapy requires BOTH bismuth and metronidazole plus PPI; missing metronidazole and acid suppression.</p></br><p>d. Ciprofloxacin + metronidazole<br /></br>   • Same problem: fluoroquinolones are not standard; regimen lacks PPI and bismuth/tetracycline.</p></br><p>f. Tetracycline + clarithromycin<br /></br>   • Lacks PPI and bismuth or metronidazole; clarithro + tetracycline combination alone gives poor eradication.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: selecting any two antibiotics at random. For Step 2, remember recognized combinations only.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>A biopsy-proven gastric ulcer with H. pylori requires eradication therapy. First-line = PPI + clarithromycin + amoxicillin.</p>
200. A 2-week-old boy is brought to the physician because of a 5-day history of a progressive rash that began at a site on his scalp where an electrode was attached during labor. Despite application of topical antibiotic ointment, the rash has spread to his trunk and extremities. He was born at term following an uncomplicated vaginal delivery. Apgar scores were 8 and 9 at 1 and 5 minutes, respectively. Initial examination showed no abnormalities. He is currently at the 30th percentile for length, 75th percentile for weight, and 25th percentile for head circumference. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows erythematous patches with overlying vesicles containing clear or cloudy fluid over the entire body. Appropriate treatment of this patient is most likely to prevent which of the following acute manifestations of his condition?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Brain abscess</br>b. Chorioretinitis</br>c. Encephalitis</br>d. Mastoiditis</br>e. Ventriculitis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer: Encephalitis</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age/timing – 2-week-old (HSV skin/eye/mouth disease classically presents at 1–2 weeks).<br /></br>• Lesion morphology – “erythematous patches with overlying vesicles containing clear or cloudy fluid” = grouped vesicles typical of herpes simplex.<br /></br>• Entry site – Scalp fetal electrode → well-known risk factor for peripartum HSV transmission.<br /></br>• Progression despite topical antibiotic – argues against impetigo; suggests viral etiology.</p></br><p>Putting these clues together → Neonatal herpes simplex virus infection (SEM disease stage).</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Recognize HSV SEM disease.<br /></br>Step 2: Know the natural history:<br /></br>   • Week 1–2: Skin/Eye/Mouth (SEM) lesions.<br /></br>   • Week 2–3: Untreated infection often spreads hematogenously/neurally to CNS → fulminant encephalitis with seizures, lethargy, bulging fontanelle.<br /></br>   • May also disseminate to visceral organs (hepatitis, DIC).<br /></br>Step 3: First-line therapy = high-dose IV acyclovir.<br /></br>Step 4: What bad “acute manifestation” does early acyclovir best prevent? → HSV encephalitis.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Treat SEM neonatal HSV immediately with IV acyclovir to prevent subsequent CNS disease (HSV encephalitis has high mortality and neurologic morbidity).</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Peripartum acquisition from maternal genital HSV shedding.<br /></br>• Viral replication initially limited to skin/mucosa; spreads via trigeminal or vagal nerves and bloodstream to brain (temporal lobes) → necrotizing encephalitis.<br /></br>• IV acyclovir inhibits viral DNA polymerase, limiting replication and neural spread.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Empiric IV acyclovir immediately (20 mg/kg q8 h × 14 days for SEM; 21 days if CNS/disseminated).<br /></br>2. Obtain surface cultures (conjunctivae, mouth, rectum), vesicle PCR, CSF HSV PCR, and MRI/EEG when CNS involvement suspected.<br /></br>3. Ophthalmology exam for keratoconjunctivitis; LFTs for disseminated disease.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“SEM → CNS in 2–3”: Skin/Eye/Mouth lesions in Week 1–2; if you don’t give ACy, they Crash into encephalitis in Week 2–3.<br /></br>Scalp eLectrode = HeLpS HSV.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>Brain abscess – More subacute; usually polymicrobial after bacteremia or contiguous spread (otitis media, cyanotic CHD). Vesicular rash not a feature.<br /></br>Chorioretinitis – Classic for congenital toxoplasmosis or CMV; presents at birth with intracranial calcifications, not isolated skin vesicles.<br /></br>Mastoiditis – Complication of acute otitis media; presents with post-auricular swelling, fever. Unrelated to diffuse vesicular rash.<br /></br>Ventriculitis – Often due to bacterial meningitis, neurosurgical hardware; no cutaneous vesicles.</p></br><p>Encephalitis is the unique, rapid, life-threatening progression specific to untreated neonatal HSV that acyclovir can acutely prevent.</p>
